110 "<CHAPTER I>"
110 ""
110 "On an evening in the latter part of May a middle-aged man was walking"
110 "homeward from Shaston to the village of Marlott, in the adjoining Vale"
110 "of Blakemore, or Blackmoor. The pair of legs that carried him were"
110 "rickety, and there was a bias in his gait which inclined him somewhat"
110 "to the left of a straight line. He occasionally gave a smart nod, as if"
110 "in confirmation of some opinion, though he was not thinking of anything"
110 "in particular. An empty egg-basket was slung upon his arm, the nap of"
110 "his hat was ruffled, a patch being quite worn away at its brim where"
110 "his thumb came in taking it off. Presently he was met by an elderly"
110 "parson astride on a gray mare, who, as he rode, hummed a wandering"
110 "tune."
110 ""
110 "“Good night t’ee,” said the man with the basket."
110 ""
110 "“Good night, Sir John,” said the parson."
110 ""
110 "The pedestrian, after another pace or two, halted, and turned round."
110 ""
110 "“Now, sir, begging your pardon; we met last market-day on this road"
110 "about this time, and I said ‘Good night,’ and you made reply ‘_Good"
110 "night, Sir John_,’ as now.”"
110 ""
110 "“I did,” said the parson."
110 ""
110 "“And once before that—near a month ago.”"
110 ""
110 "“I may have.”"
110 ""
110 "“Then what might your meaning be in calling me ‘Sir John’ these"
110 "different times, when I be plain Jack Durbeyfield, the haggler?”"
110 ""
110 "The parson rode a step or two nearer."
110 ""
110 "“It was only my whim,” he said; and, after a moment’s hesitation: “It"
110 "was on account of a discovery I made some little time ago, whilst I was"
110 "hunting up pedigrees for the new county history. I am Parson Tringham,"
110 "the antiquary, of Stagfoot Lane. Don’t you really know, Durbeyfield,"
110 "that you are the lineal representative of the ancient and knightly"
110 "family of the d’Urbervilles, who derive their descent from Sir Pagan"
110 "d’Urberville, that renowned knight who came from Normandy with William"
110 "the Conqueror, as appears by Battle Abbey Roll?”"
110 ""
110 "“Never heard it before, sir!”"
110 ""
110 "“Well it’s true. Throw up your chin a moment, so that I may catch the"
110 "profile of your face better. Yes, that’s the d’Urberville nose and"
110 "chin—a little debased. Your ancestor was one of the twelve knights who"
110 "assisted the Lord of Estremavilla in Normandy in his conquest of"
110 "Glamorganshire. Branches of your family held manors over all this part"
110 "of England; their names appear in the Pipe Rolls in the time of King"
110 "Stephen. In the reign of King John one of them was rich enough to give"
110 "a manor to the Knights Hospitallers; and in Edward the Second’s time"
110 "your forefather Brian was summoned to Westminster to attend the great"
110 "Council there. You declined a little in Oliver Cromwell’s time, but to"
110 "no serious extent, and in Charles the Second’s reign you were made"
110 "Knights of the Royal Oak for your loyalty. Aye, there have been"
110 "generations of Sir Johns among you, and if knighthood were hereditary,"
110 "like a baronetcy, as it practically was in old times, when men were"
110 "knighted from father to son, you would be Sir John now.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ye don’t say so!”"
110 ""
110 "“In short,” concluded the parson, decisively smacking his leg with his"
110 "switch, “there’s hardly such another family in England.”"
110 ""
110 "“Daze my eyes, and isn’t there?” said Durbeyfield. “And here have I"
110 "been knocking about, year after year, from pillar to post, as if I was"
110 "no more than the commonest feller in the parish.... And how long hev"
110 "this news about me been knowed, Pa’son Tringham?”"
110 ""
110 "The clergyman explained that, as far as he was aware, it had quite died"
110 "out of knowledge, and could hardly be said to be known at all. His own"
110 "investigations had begun on a day in the preceding spring when, having"
110 "been engaged in tracing the vicissitudes of the d’Urberville family, he"
110 "had observed Durbeyfield’s name on his waggon, and had thereupon been"
110 "led to make inquiries about his father and grandfather till he had no"
110 "doubt on the subject."
110 ""
110 "“At first I resolved not to disturb you with such a useless piece of"
110 "information,” said he. “However, our impulses are too strong for our"
110 "judgement sometimes. I thought you might perhaps know something of it"
110 "all the while.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, I have heard once or twice, ’tis true, that my family had seen"
110 "better days afore they came to Blackmoor. But I took no notice o’t,"
110 "thinking it to mean that we had once kept two horses where we now keep"
110 "only one. I’ve got a wold silver spoon, and a wold graven seal at home,"
110 "too; but, Lord, what’s a spoon and seal?... And to think that I and"
110 "these noble d’Urbervilles were one flesh all the time. ’Twas said that"
110 "my gr’t-granfer had secrets, and didn’t care to talk of where he came"
110 "from.... And where do we raise our smoke, now, parson, if I may make so"
110 "bold; I mean, where do we d’Urbervilles live?”"
110 ""
110 "“You don’t live anywhere. You are extinct—as a county family.”"
110 ""
110 "“That’s bad.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes—what the mendacious family chronicles call extinct in the male"
110 "line—that is, gone down—gone under.”"
110 ""
110 "“Then where do we lie?”"
110 ""
110 "“At Kingsbere-sub-Greenhill: rows and rows of you in your vaults, with"
110 "your effigies under Purbeck-marble canopies.”"
110 ""
110 "“And where be our family mansions and estates?”"
110 ""
110 "“You haven’t any.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh? No lands neither?”"
110 ""
110 "“None; though you once had ’em in abundance, as I said, for your family"
110 "consisted of numerous branches. In this county there was a seat of"
110 "yours at Kingsbere, and another at Sherton, and another in Millpond,"
110 "and another at Lullstead, and another at Wellbridge.”"
110 ""
110 "“And shall we ever come into our own again?”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah—that I can’t tell!”"
110 ""
110 "“And what had I better do about it, sir?” asked Durbeyfield, after a"
110 "pause."
110 ""
110 "“Oh—nothing, nothing; except chasten yourself with the thought of ‘how"
110 "are the mighty fallen.’ It is a fact of some interest to the local"
110 "historian and genealogist, nothing more. There are several families"
110 "among the cottagers of this county of almost equal lustre. Good night.”"
110 ""
110 "“But you’ll turn back and have a quart of beer wi’ me on the strength"
110 "o’t, Pa’son Tringham? There’s a very pretty brew in tap at The Pure"
110 "Drop—though, to be sure, not so good as at Rolliver’s.”"
110 ""
110 "“No, thank you—not this evening, Durbeyfield. You’ve had enough"
110 "already.” Concluding thus, the parson rode on his way, with doubts as"
110 "to his discretion in retailing this curious bit of lore."
110 ""
110 "When he was gone, Durbeyfield walked a few steps in a profound reverie,"
110 "and then sat down upon the grassy bank by the roadside, depositing his"
110 "basket before him. In a few minutes a youth appeared in the distance,"
110 "walking in the same direction as that which had been pursued by"
110 "Durbeyfield. The latter, on seeing him, held up his hand, and the lad"
110 "quickened his pace and came near."
110 ""
110 "“Boy, take up that basket! I want ’ee to go on an errand for me.”"
110 ""
110 "The lath-like stripling frowned. “Who be you, then, John Durbeyfield,"
110 "to order me about and call me ‘boy’? You know my name as well as I know"
110 "yours!”"
110 ""
110 "“Do you, do you? That’s the secret—that’s the secret! Now obey my"
110 "orders, and take the message I’m going to charge ’ee wi’... Well, Fred,"
110 "I don’t mind telling you that the secret is that I’m one of a noble"
110 "race—it has been just found out by me this present afternoon, p.m.” And"
110 "as he made the announcement, Durbeyfield, declining from his sitting"
110 "position, luxuriously stretched himself out upon the bank among the"
110 "daisies."
110 ""
110 "The lad stood before Durbeyfield, and contemplated his length from"
110 "crown to toe."
110 ""
110 "“Sir John d’Urberville—that’s who I am,” continued the prostrate man."
110 "“That is if knights were baronets—which they be. ’Tis recorded in"
110 "history all about me. Dost know of such a place, lad, as"
110 "Kingsbere-sub-Greenhill?”"
110 ""
110 "“Ees. I’ve been there to Greenhill Fair.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, under the church of that city there lie—”"
110 ""
110 "“’Tisn’t a city, the place I mean; leastwise ’twaddn’ when I was"
110 "there—’twas a little one-eyed, blinking sort o’ place.”"
110 ""
110 "“Never you mind the place, boy, that’s not the question before us."
110 "Under the church of that there parish lie my ancestors—hundreds of"
110 "’em—in coats of mail and jewels, in gr’t lead coffins weighing tons and"
110 "tons. There’s not a man in the county o’ South-Wessex that’s got"
110 "grander and nobler skillentons in his family than I.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh?”"
110 ""
110 "“Now take up that basket, and goo on to Marlott, and when you’ve come"
110 "to The Pure Drop Inn, tell ’em to send a horse and carriage to me"
110 "immed’ately, to carry me hwome. And in the bottom o’ the carriage they"
110 "be to put a noggin o’ rum in a small bottle, and chalk it up to my"
110 "account. And when you’ve done that goo on to my house with the basket,"
110 "and tell my wife to put away that washing, because she needn’t finish"
110 "it, and wait till I come hwome, as I’ve news to tell her.”"
110 ""
110 "As the lad stood in a dubious attitude, Durbeyfield put his hand in his"
110 "pocket, and produced a shilling, one of the chronically few that he"
110 "possessed."
110 ""
110 "“Here’s for your labour, lad.”"
110 ""
110 "This made a difference in the young man’s estimate of the position."
110 ""
110 "“Yes, Sir John. Thank ’ee. Anything else I can do for ’ee, Sir John?”"
110 ""
110 "“Tell ’em at hwome that I should like for supper,—well, lamb’s fry if"
110 "they can get it; and if they can’t, black-pot; and if they can’t get"
110 "that, well chitterlings will do.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, Sir John.”"
110 ""
110 "The boy took up the basket, and as he set out the notes of a brass band"
110 "were heard from the direction of the village."
110 ""
110 "“What’s that?” said Durbeyfield. “Not on account o’ I?”"
110 ""
110 "“’Tis the women’s club-walking, Sir John. Why, your da’ter is one o’"
110 "the members.”"
110 ""
110 "“To be sure—I’d quite forgot it in my thoughts of greater things! Well,"
110 "vamp on to Marlott, will ye, and order that carriage, and maybe I’ll"
110 "drive round and inspect the club.”"
110 ""
110 "The lad departed, and Durbeyfield lay waiting on the grass and daisies"
110 "in the evening sun. Not a soul passed that way for a long while, and"
110 "the faint notes of the band were the only human sounds audible within"
110 "the rim of blue hills."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER II>"
110 ""
110 "The village of Marlott lay amid the north-eastern undulations of the"
110 "beautiful Vale of Blakemore, or Blackmoor, aforesaid, an engirdled and"
110 "secluded region, for the most part untrodden as yet by tourist or"
110 "landscape-painter, though within a four hours’ journey from London."
110 ""
110 "It is a vale whose acquaintance is best made by viewing it from the"
110 "summits of the hills that surround it—except perhaps during the"
110 "droughts of summer. An unguided ramble into its recesses in bad weather"
110 "is apt to engender dissatisfaction with its narrow, tortuous, and miry"
110 "ways."
110 ""
110 "This fertile and sheltered tract of country, in which the fields are"
110 "never brown and the springs never dry, is bounded on the south by the"
110 "bold chalk ridge that embraces the prominences of Hambledon Hill,"
110 "Bulbarrow, Nettlecombe-Tout, Dogbury, High Stoy, and Bubb Down. The"
110 "traveller from the coast, who, after plodding northward for a score of"
110 "miles over calcareous downs and corn-lands, suddenly reaches the verge"
110 "of one of these escarpments, is surprised and delighted to behold,"
110 "extended like a map beneath him, a country differing absolutely from"
110 "that which he has passed through. Behind him the hills are open, the"
110 "sun blazes down upon fields so large as to give an unenclosed character"
110 "to the landscape, the lanes are white, the hedges low and plashed, the"
110 "atmosphere colourless. Here, in the valley, the world seems to be"
110 "constructed upon a smaller and more delicate scale; the fields are mere"
110 "paddocks, so reduced that from this height their hedgerows appear a"
110 "network of dark green threads overspreading the paler green of the"
110 "grass. The atmosphere beneath is languorous, and is so tinged with"
110 "azure that what artists call the middle distance partakes also of that"
110 "hue, while the horizon beyond is of the deepest ultramarine. Arable"
110 "lands are few and limited; with but slight exceptions the prospect is a"
110 "broad rich mass of grass and trees, mantling minor hills and dales"
110 "within the major. Such is the Vale of Blackmoor."
110 ""
110 "The district is of historic, no less than of topographical interest."
110 "The Vale was known in former times as the Forest of White Hart, from a"
110 "curious legend of King Henry III’s reign, in which the killing by a"
110 "certain Thomas de la Lynd of a beautiful white hart which the king had"
110 "run down and spared, was made the occasion of a heavy fine. In those"
110 "days, and till comparatively recent times, the country was densely"
110 "wooded. Even now, traces of its earlier condition are to be found in"
110 "the old oak copses and irregular belts of timber that yet survive upon"
110 "its slopes, and the hollow-trunked trees that shade so many of its"
110 "pastures."
110 ""
110 "The forests have departed, but some old customs of their shades remain."
110 "Many, however, linger only in a metamorphosed or disguised form. The"
110 "May-Day dance, for instance, was to be discerned on the afternoon under"
110 "notice, in the guise of the club revel, or “club-walking,” as it was"
110 "there called."
110 ""
110 "It was an interesting event to the younger inhabitants of Marlott,"
110 "though its real interest was not observed by the participators in the"
110 "ceremony. Its singularity lay less in the retention of a custom of"
110 "walking in procession and dancing on each anniversary than in the"
110 "members being solely women. In men’s clubs such celebrations were,"
110 "though expiring, less uncommon; but either the natural shyness of the"
110 "softer sex, or a sarcastic attitude on the part of male relatives, had"
110 "denuded such women’s clubs as remained (if any other did) or this their"
110 "glory and consummation. The club of Marlott alone lived to uphold the"
110 "local Cerealia. It had walked for hundreds of years, if not as"
110 "benefit-club, as votive sisterhood of some sort; and it walked still."
110 ""
110 "The banded ones were all dressed in white gowns—a gay survival from Old"
110 "Style days, when cheerfulness and May-time were synonyms—days before"
110 "the habit of taking long views had reduced emotions to a monotonous"
110 "average. Their first exhibition of themselves was in a processional"
110 "march of two and two round the parish. Ideal and real clashed slightly"
110 "as the sun lit up their figures against the green hedges and"
110 "creeper-laced house-fronts; for, though the whole troop wore white"
110 "garments, no two whites were alike among them. Some approached pure"
110 "blanching; some had a bluish pallor; some worn by the older characters"
110 "(which had possibly lain by folded for many a year) inclined to a"
110 "cadaverous tint, and to a Georgian style."
110 ""
110 "In addition to the distinction of a white frock, every woman and girl"
110 "carried in her right hand a peeled willow wand, and in her left a bunch"
110 "of white flowers. The peeling of the former, and the selection of the"
110 "latter, had been an operation of personal care."
110 ""
110 "There were a few middle-aged and even elderly women in the train, their"
110 "silver-wiry hair and wrinkled faces, scourged by time and trouble,"
110 "having almost a grotesque, certainly a pathetic, appearance in such a"
110 "jaunty situation. In a true view, perhaps, there was more to be"
110 "gathered and told of each anxious and experienced one, to whom the"
110 "years were drawing nigh when she should say, “I have no pleasure in"
110 "them,” than of her juvenile comrades. But let the elder be passed over"
110 "here for those under whose bodices the life throbbed quick and warm."
110 ""
110 "The young girls formed, indeed, the majority of the band, and their"
110 "heads of luxuriant hair reflected in the sunshine every tone of gold,"
110 "and black, and brown. Some had beautiful eyes, others a beautiful nose,"
110 "others a beautiful mouth and figure: few, if any, had all. A difficulty"
110 "of arranging their lips in this crude exposure to public scrutiny, an"
110 "inability to balance their heads, and to dissociate self-consciousness"
110 "from their features, was apparent in them, and showed that they were"
110 "genuine country girls, unaccustomed to many eyes."
110 ""
110 "And as each and all of them were warmed without by the sun, so each had"
110 "a private little sun for her soul to bask in; some dream, some"
110 "affection, some hobby, at least some remote and distant hope which,"
110 "though perhaps starving to nothing, still lived on, as hopes will. They"
110 "were all cheerful, and many of them merry."
110 ""
110 "They came round by The Pure Drop Inn, and were turning out of the high"
110 "road to pass through a wicket-gate into the meadows, when one of the"
110 "women said—"
110 ""
110 "“The Load-a-Lord! Why, Tess Durbeyfield, if there isn’t thy father"
110 "riding hwome in a carriage!”"
110 ""
110 "A young member of the band turned her head at the exclamation. She was"
110 "a fine and handsome girl—not handsomer than some others, possibly—but"
110 "her mobile peony mouth and large innocent eyes added eloquence to"
110 "colour and shape. She wore a red ribbon in her hair, and was the only"
110 "one of the white company who could boast of such a pronounced"
110 "adornment. As she looked round Durbeyfield was seen moving along the"
110 "road in a chaise belonging to The Pure Drop, driven by a frizzle-headed"
110 "brawny damsel with her gown-sleeves rolled above her elbows. This was"
110 "the cheerful servant of that establishment, who, in her part of"
110 "factotum, turned groom and ostler at times. Durbeyfield, leaning back,"
110 "and with his eyes closed luxuriously, was waving his hand above his"
110 "head, and singing in a slow recitative—"
110 ""
110 "“I’ve-got-a-gr’t-family-vault-at-Kingsbere—and"
110 "knighted-forefathers-in-lead-coffins-there!”"
110 ""
110 "The clubbists tittered, except the girl called Tess—in whom a slow heat"
110 "seemed to rise at the sense that her father was making himself foolish"
110 "in their eyes."
110 ""
110 "“He’s tired, that’s all,” she said hastily, “and he has got a lift"
110 "home, because our own horse has to rest to-day.”"
110 ""
110 "“Bless thy simplicity, Tess,” said her companions. “He’s got his"
110 "market-nitch. Haw-haw!”"
110 ""
110 "“Look here; I won’t walk another inch with you, if you say any jokes"
110 "about him!” Tess cried, and the colour upon her cheeks spread over her"
110 "face and neck. In a moment her eyes grew moist, and her glance drooped"
110 "to the ground. Perceiving that they had really pained her they said no"
110 "more, and order again prevailed. Tess’s pride would not allow her to"
110 "turn her head again, to learn what her father’s meaning was, if he had"
110 "any; and thus she moved on with the whole body to the enclosure where"
110 "there was to be dancing on the green. By the time the spot was reached"
110 "she had recovered her equanimity, and tapped her neighbour with her"
110 "wand and talked as usual."
110 ""
110 "Tess Durbeyfield at this time of her life was a mere vessel of emotion"
110 "untinctured by experience. The dialect was on her tongue to some"
110 "extent, despite the village school: the characteristic intonation of"
110 "that dialect for this district being the voicing approximately rendered"
110 "by the syllable UR, probably as rich an utterance as any to be found in"
110 "human speech. The pouted-up deep red mouth to which this syllable was"
110 "native had hardly as yet settled into its definite shape, and her lower"
110 "lip had a way of thrusting the middle of her top one upward, when they"
110 "closed together after a word."
110 ""
110 "Phases of her childhood lurked in her aspect still. As she walked along"
110 "to-day, for all her bouncing handsome womanliness, you could sometimes"
110 "see her twelfth year in her cheeks, or her ninth sparkling from her"
110 "eyes; and even her fifth would flit over the curves of her mouth now"
110 "and then."
110 ""
110 "Yet few knew, and still fewer considered this. A small minority, mainly"
110 "strangers, would look long at her in casually passing by, and grow"
110 "momentarily fascinated by her freshness, and wonder if they would ever"
110 "see her again: but to almost everybody she was a fine and picturesque"
110 "country girl, and no more."
110 ""
110 "Nothing was seen or heard further of Durbeyfield in his triumphal"
110 "chariot under the conduct of the ostleress, and the club having entered"
110 "the allotted space, dancing began. As there were no men in the company,"
110 "the girls danced at first with each other, but when the hour for the"
110 "close of labour drew on, the masculine inhabitants of the village,"
110 "together with other idlers and pedestrians, gathered round the spot,"
110 "and appeared inclined to negotiate for a partner."
110 ""
110 "Among these on-lookers were three young men of a superior class,"
110 "carrying small knapsacks strapped to their shoulders, and stout sticks"
110 "in their hands. Their general likeness to each other, and their"
110 "consecutive ages, would almost have suggested that they might be, what"
110 "in fact they were, brothers. The eldest wore the white tie, high"
110 "waistcoat, and thin-brimmed hat of the regulation curate; the second"
110 "was the normal undergraduate; the appearance of the third and youngest"
110 "would hardly have been sufficient to characterize him; there was an"
110 "uncribbed, uncabined aspect in his eyes and attire, implying that he"
110 "had hardly as yet found the entrance to his professional groove. That"
110 "he was a desultory tentative student of something and everything might"
110 "only have been predicted of him."
110 ""
110 "These three brethren told casual acquaintance that they were spending"
110 "their Whitsun holidays in a walking tour through the Vale of Blackmoor,"
110 "their course being south-westerly from the town of Shaston on the"
110 "north-east."
110 ""
110 "They leant over the gate by the highway, and inquired as to the meaning"
110 "of the dance and the white-frocked maids. The two elder of the brothers"
110 "were plainly not intending to linger more than a moment, but the"
110 "spectacle of a bevy of girls dancing without male partners seemed to"
110 "amuse the third, and make him in no hurry to move on. He unstrapped his"
110 "knapsack, put it, with his stick, on the hedge-bank, and opened the"
110 "gate."
110 ""
110 "“What are you going to do, Angel?” asked the eldest."
110 ""
110 "“I am inclined to go and have a fling with them. Why not all of us—just"
110 "for a minute or two—it will not detain us long?”"
110 ""
110 "“No—no; nonsense!” said the first. “Dancing in public with a troop of"
110 "country hoydens—suppose we should be seen! Come along, or it will be"
110 "dark before we get to Stourcastle, and there’s no place we can sleep at"
110 "nearer than that; besides, we must get through another chapter of _A"
110 "Counterblast to Agnosticism_ before we turn in, now I have taken the"
110 "trouble to bring the book.”"
110 ""
110 "“All right—I’ll overtake you and Cuthbert in five minutes; don’t stop;"
110 "I give my word that I will, Felix.”"
110 ""
110 "The two elder reluctantly left him and walked on, taking their"
110 "brother’s knapsack to relieve him in following, and the youngest"
110 "entered the field."
110 ""
110 "“This is a thousand pities,” he said gallantly, to two or three of the"
110 "girls nearest him, as soon as there was a pause in the dance. “Where"
110 "are your partners, my dears?”"
110 ""
110 "“They’ve not left off work yet,” answered one of the boldest. “They’ll"
110 "be here by and by. Till then, will you be one, sir?”"
110 ""
110 "“Certainly. But what’s one among so many!”"
110 ""
110 "“Better than none. ’Tis melancholy work facing and footing it to one of"
110 "your own sort, and no clipsing and colling at all. Now, pick and"
110 "choose.”"
110 ""
110 "“’Ssh—don’t be so for’ard!” said a shyer girl."
110 ""
110 "The young man, thus invited, glanced them over, and attempted some"
110 "discrimination; but, as the group were all so new to him, he could not"
110 "very well exercise it. He took almost the first that came to hand,"
110 "which was not the speaker, as she had expected; nor did it happen to be"
110 "Tess Durbeyfield. Pedigree, ancestral skeletons, monumental record, the"
110 "d’Urberville lineaments, did not help Tess in her life’s battle as yet,"
110 "even to the extent of attracting to her a dancing-partner over the"
110 "heads of the commonest peasantry. So much for Norman blood unaided by"
110 "Victorian lucre."
110 ""
110 "The name of the eclipsing girl, whatever it was, has not been handed"
110 "down; but she was envied by all as the first who enjoyed the luxury of"
110 "a masculine partner that evening. Yet such was the force of example"
110 "that the village young men, who had not hastened to enter the gate"
110 "while no intruder was in the way, now dropped in quickly, and soon the"
110 "couples became leavened with rustic youth to a marked extent, till at"
110 "length the plainest woman in the club was no longer compelled to foot"
110 "it on the masculine side of the figure."
110 ""
110 "The church clock struck, when suddenly the student said that he must"
110 "leave—he had been forgetting himself—he had to join his companions. As"
110 "he fell out of the dance his eyes lighted on Tess Durbeyfield, whose"
110 "own large orbs wore, to tell the truth, the faintest aspect of reproach"
110 "that he had not chosen her. He, too, was sorry then that, owing to her"
110 "backwardness, he had not observed her; and with that in his mind he"
110 "left the pasture."
110 ""
110 "On account of his long delay he started in a flying-run down the lane"
110 "westward, and had soon passed the hollow and mounted the next rise. He"
110 "had not yet overtaken his brothers, but he paused to get breath, and"
110 "looked back. He could see the white figures of the girls in the green"
110 "enclosure whirling about as they had whirled when he was among them."
110 "They seemed to have quite forgotten him already."
110 ""
110 "All of them, except, perhaps, one. This white shape stood apart by the"
110 "hedge alone. From her position he knew it to be the pretty maiden with"
110 "whom he had not danced. Trifling as the matter was, he yet"
110 "instinctively felt that she was hurt by his oversight. He wished that"
110 "he had asked her; he wished that he had inquired her name. She was so"
110 "modest, so expressive, she had looked so soft in her thin white gown"
110 "that he felt he had acted stupidly."
110 ""
110 "However, it could not be helped, and turning, and bending himself to a"
110 "rapid walk, he dismissed the subject from his mind."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER III>"
110 ""
110 "As for Tess Durbeyfield, she did not so easily dislodge the incident"
110 "from her consideration. She had no spirit to dance again for a long"
110 "time, though she might have had plenty of partners; but ah! they did"
110 "not speak so nicely as the strange young man had done. It was not till"
110 "the rays of the sun had absorbed the young stranger’s retreating figure"
110 "on the hill that she shook off her temporary sadness and answered her"
110 "would-be partner in the affirmative."
110 ""
110 "She remained with her comrades till dusk, and participated with a"
110 "certain zest in the dancing; though, being heart-whole as yet, she"
110 "enjoyed treading a measure purely for its own sake; little divining"
110 "when she saw “the soft torments, the bitter sweets, the pleasing pains,"
110 "and the agreeable distresses” of those girls who had been wooed and"
110 "won, what she herself was capable of in that kind. The struggles and"
110 "wrangles of the lads for her hand in a jig were an amusement to her—no"
110 "more; and when they became fierce she rebuked them."
110 ""
110 "She might have stayed even later, but the incident of her father’s odd"
110 "appearance and manner returned upon the girl’s mind to make her"
110 "anxious, and wondering what had become of him she dropped away from the"
110 "dancers and bent her steps towards the end of the village at which the"
110 "parental cottage lay."
110 ""
110 "While yet many score yards off, other rhythmic sounds than those she"
110 "had quitted became audible to her; sounds that she knew well—so well."
110 "They were a regular series of thumpings from the interior of the house,"
110 "occasioned by the violent rocking of a cradle upon a stone floor, to"
110 "which movement a feminine voice kept time by singing, in a vigorous"
110 "gallopade, the favourite ditty of “The Spotted Cow”—"
110 ""
110 "    I saw her lie do′-own in yon′-der green gro′-ove;"
110 "    Come, love!′ and I'll tell′ you where!′"
110 ""
110 "The cradle-rocking and the song would cease simultaneously for a"
110 "moment, and an exclamation at highest vocal pitch would take the place"
110 "of the melody."
110 ""
110 "“God bless thy diment eyes! And thy waxen cheeks! And thy cherry mouth!"
110 "And thy Cubit’s thighs! And every bit o’ thy blessed body!”"
110 ""
110 "After this invocation the rocking and the singing would recommence, and"
110 "the “Spotted Cow” proceed as before. So matters stood when Tess opened"
110 "the door and paused upon the mat within it, surveying the scene."
110 ""
110 "The interior, in spite of the melody, struck upon the girl’s senses"
110 "with an unspeakable dreariness. From the holiday gaieties of the"
110 "field—the white gowns, the nosegays, the willow-wands, the whirling"
110 "movements on the green, the flash of gentle sentiment towards the"
110 "stranger—to the yellow melancholy of this one-candled spectacle, what a"
110 "step! Besides the jar of contrast there came to her a chill"
110 "self-reproach that she had not returned sooner, to help her mother in"
110 "these domesticities, instead of indulging herself out-of-doors."
110 ""
110 "There stood her mother amid the group of children, as Tess had left"
110 "her, hanging over the Monday washing-tub, which had now, as always,"
110 "lingered on to the end of the week. Out of that tub had come the day"
110 "before—Tess felt it with a dreadful sting of remorse—the very white"
110 "frock upon her back which she had so carelessly greened about the skirt"
110 "on the damping grass—which had been wrung up and ironed by her mother’s"
110 "own hands."
110 ""
110 "As usual, Mrs Durbeyfield was balanced on one foot beside the tub, the"
110 "other being engaged in the aforesaid business of rocking her youngest"
110 "child. The cradle-rockers had done hard duty for so many years, under"
110 "the weight of so many children, on that flagstone floor, that they were"
110 "worn nearly flat, in consequence of which a huge jerk accompanied each"
110 "swing of the cot, flinging the baby from side to side like a weaver’s"
110 "shuttle, as Mrs Durbeyfield, excited by her song, trod the rocker with"
110 "all the spring that was left in her after a long day’s seething in the"
110 "suds."
110 ""
110 "Nick-knock, nick-knock, went the cradle; the candle-flame stretched"
110 "itself tall, and began jigging up and down; the water dribbled from the"
110 "matron’s elbows, and the song galloped on to the end of the verse, Mrs"
110 "Durbeyfield regarding her daughter the while. Even now, when burdened"
110 "with a young family, Joan Durbeyfield was a passionate lover of tune."
110 "No ditty floated into Blackmoor Vale from the outer world but Tess’s"
110 "mother caught up its notation in a week."
110 ""
110 "There still faintly beamed from the woman’s features something of the"
110 "freshness, and even the prettiness, of her youth; rendering it probable"
110 "that the personal charms which Tess could boast of were in main part"
110 "her mother’s gift, and therefore unknightly, unhistorical."
110 ""
110 "“I’ll rock the cradle for ’ee, mother,” said the daughter gently. “Or"
110 "I’ll take off my best frock and help you wring up? I thought you had"
110 "finished long ago.”"
110 ""
110 "Her mother bore Tess no ill-will for leaving the housework to her"
110 "single-handed efforts for so long; indeed, Joan seldom upbraided her"
110 "thereon at any time, feeling but slightly the lack of Tess’s assistance"
110 "whilst her instinctive plan for relieving herself of her labours lay in"
110 "postponing them. To-night, however, she was even in a blither mood than"
110 "usual. There was a dreaminess, a pre-occupation, an exaltation, in the"
110 "maternal look which the girl could not understand."
110 ""
110 "“Well, I’m glad you’ve come,” her mother said, as soon as the last note"
110 "had passed out of her. “I want to go and fetch your father; but what’s"
110 "more’n that, I want to tell ’ee what have happened. Y’ll be fess"
110 "enough, my poppet, when th’st know!” (Mrs Durbeyfield habitually spoke"
110 "the dialect; her daughter, who had passed the Sixth Standard in the"
110 "National School under a London-trained mistress, spoke two languages:"
110 "the dialect at home, more or less; ordinary English abroad and to"
110 "persons of quality.)"
110 ""
110 "“Since I’ve been away?” Tess asked."
110 ""
110 "“Ay!”"
110 ""
110 "“Had it anything to do with father’s making such a mommet of himself in"
110 "thik carriage this afternoon? Why did ’er? I felt inclined to sink into"
110 "the ground with shame!”"
110 ""
110 "“That wer all a part of the larry! We’ve been found to be the greatest"
110 "gentlefolk in the whole county—reaching all back long before Oliver"
110 "Grumble’s time—to the days of the Pagan Turks—with monuments, and"
110 "vaults, and crests, and ’scutcheons, and the Lord knows what all. In"
110 "Saint Charles’s days we was made Knights o’ the Royal Oak, our real"
110 "name being d’Urberville!... Don’t that make your bosom plim? ’Twas on"
110 "this account that your father rode home in the vlee; not because he’d"
110 "been drinking, as people supposed.”"
110 ""
110 "“I’m glad of that. Will it do us any good, mother?”"
110 ""
110 "“O yes! ’Tis thoughted that great things may come o’t. No doubt a"
110 "mampus of volk of our own rank will be down here in their carriages as"
110 "soon as ’tis known. Your father learnt it on his way hwome from"
110 "Shaston, and he has been telling me the whole pedigree of the matter.”"
110 ""
110 "“Where is father now?” asked Tess suddenly."
110 ""
110 "Her mother gave irrelevant information by way of answer: “He called to"
110 "see the doctor to-day in Shaston. It is not consumption at all, it"
110 "seems. It is fat round his heart, ’a says. There, it is like this.”"
110 "Joan Durbeyfield, as she spoke, curved a sodden thumb and forefinger to"
110 "the shape of the letter C, and used the other forefinger as a pointer."
110 "“‘At the present moment,’ he says to your father, ‘your heart is"
110 "enclosed all round there, and all round there; this space is still"
110 "open,’ ’a says. ‘As soon as it do meet, so,’”—Mrs Durbeyfield closed"
110 "her fingers into a circle complete—“‘off you will go like a shadder, Mr"
110 "Durbeyfield,’ ’a says. ‘You mid last ten years; you mid go off in ten"
110 "months, or ten days.’”"
110 ""
110 "Tess looked alarmed. Her father possibly to go behind the eternal cloud"
110 "so soon, notwithstanding this sudden greatness!"
110 ""
110 "“But where _is_ father?” she asked again."
110 ""
110 "Her mother put on a deprecating look. “Now don’t you be bursting out"
110 "angry! The poor man—he felt so rafted after his uplifting by the"
110 "pa’son’s news—that he went up to Rolliver’s half an hour ago. He do"
110 "want to get up his strength for his journey to-morrow with that load of"
110 "beehives, which must be delivered, family or no. He’ll have to start"
110 "shortly after twelve to-night, as the distance is so long.”"
110 ""
110 "“Get up his strength!” said Tess impetuously, the tears welling to her"
110 "eyes. “O my God! Go to a public-house to get up his strength! And you"
110 "as well agreed as he, mother!”"
110 ""
110 "Her rebuke and her mood seemed to fill the whole room, and to impart a"
110 "cowed look to the furniture, and candle, and children playing about,"
110 "and to her mother’s face."
110 ""
110 "“No,” said the latter touchily, “I be not agreed. I have been waiting"
110 "for ’ee to bide and keep house while I go fetch him.”"
110 ""
110 "“I’ll go.”"
110 ""
110 "“O no, Tess. You see, it would be no use.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess did not expostulate. She knew what her mother’s objection meant."
110 "Mrs Durbeyfield’s jacket and bonnet were already hanging slily upon a"
110 "chair by her side, in readiness for this contemplated jaunt, the reason"
110 "for which the matron deplored more than its necessity."
110 ""
110 "“And take the _Compleat Fortune-Teller_ to the outhouse,” Joan"
110 "continued, rapidly wiping her hands, and donning the garments."
110 ""
110 "The _Compleat Fortune-Teller_ was an old thick volume, which lay on a"
110 "table at her elbow, so worn by pocketing that the margins had reached"
110 "the edge of the type. Tess took it up, and her mother started."
110 ""
110 "This going to hunt up her shiftless husband at the inn was one of Mrs"
110 "Durbeyfield’s still extant enjoyments in the muck and muddle of rearing"
110 "children. To discover him at Rolliver’s, to sit there for an hour or"
110 "two by his side and dismiss all thought and care of the children during"
110 "the interval, made her happy. A sort of halo, an occidental glow, came"
110 "over life then. Troubles and other realities took on themselves a"
110 "metaphysical impalpability, sinking to mere mental phenomena for serene"
110 "contemplation, and no longer stood as pressing concretions which chafed"
110 "body and soul. The youngsters, not immediately within sight, seemed"
110 "rather bright and desirable appurtenances than otherwise; the incidents"
110 "of daily life were not without humorousness and jollity in their aspect"
110 "there. She felt a little as she had used to feel when she sat by her"
110 "now wedded husband in the same spot during his wooing, shutting her"
110 "eyes to his defects of character, and regarding him only in his ideal"
110 "presentation as lover."
110 ""
110 "Tess, being left alone with the younger children, went first to the"
110 "outhouse with the fortune-telling book, and stuffed it into the thatch."
110 "A curious fetishistic fear of this grimy volume on the part of her"
110 "mother prevented her ever allowing it to stay in the house all night,"
110 "and hither it was brought back whenever it had been consulted. Between"
110 "the mother, with her fast-perishing lumber of superstitions, folk-lore,"
110 "dialect, and orally transmitted ballads, and the daughter, with her"
110 "trained National teachings and Standard knowledge under an infinitely"
110 "Revised Code, there was a gap of two hundred years as ordinarily"
110 "understood. When they were together the Jacobean and the Victorian ages"
110 "were juxtaposed."
110 ""
110 "Returning along the garden path Tess mused on what the mother could"
110 "have wished to ascertain from the book on this particular day. She"
110 "guessed the recent ancestral discovery to bear upon it, but did not"
110 "divine that it solely concerned herself. Dismissing this, however, she"
110 "busied herself with sprinkling the linen dried during the day-time, in"
110 "company with her nine-year-old brother Abraham, and her sister"
110 "Eliza-Louisa of twelve and a half, called “’Liza-Lu,” the youngest ones"
110 "being put to bed. There was an interval of four years and more between"
110 "Tess and the next of the family, the two who had filled the gap having"
110 "died in their infancy, and this lent her a deputy-maternal attitude"
110 "when she was alone with her juniors. Next in juvenility to Abraham came"
110 "two more girls, Hope and Modesty; then a boy of three, and then the"
110 "baby, who had just completed his first year."
110 ""
110 "All these young souls were passengers in the Durbeyfield ship—entirely"
110 "dependent on the judgement of the two Durbeyfield adults for their"
110 "pleasures, their necessities, their health, even their existence. If"
110 "the heads of the Durbeyfield household chose to sail into difficulty,"
110 "disaster, starvation, disease, degradation, death, thither were these"
110 "half-dozen little captives under hatches compelled to sail with"
110 "them—six helpless creatures, who had never been asked if they wished"
110 "for life on any terms, much less if they wished for it on such hard"
110 "conditions as were involved in being of the shiftless house of"
110 "Durbeyfield. Some people would like to know whence the poet whose"
110 "philosophy is in these days deemed as profound and trustworthy as his"
110 "song is breezy and pure, gets his authority for speaking of “Nature’s"
110 "holy plan.”"
110 ""
110 "It grew later, and neither father nor mother reappeared. Tess looked"
110 "out of the door, and took a mental journey through Marlott. The village"
110 "was shutting its eyes. Candles and lamps were being put out everywhere:"
110 "she could inwardly behold the extinguisher and the extended hand."
110 ""
110 "Her mother’s fetching simply meant one more to fetch. Tess began to"
110 "perceive that a man in indifferent health, who proposed to start on a"
110 "journey before one in the morning, ought not to be at an inn at this"
110 "late hour celebrating his ancient blood."
110 ""
110 "“Abraham,” she said to her little brother, “do you put on your hat—you"
110 "bain’t afraid?—and go up to Rolliver’s, and see what has gone wi’"
110 "father and mother.”"
110 ""
110 "The boy jumped promptly from his seat, and opened the door, and the"
110 "night swallowed him up. Half an hour passed yet again; neither man,"
110 "woman, nor child returned. Abraham, like his parents, seemed to have"
110 "been limed and caught by the ensnaring inn."
110 ""
110 "“I must go myself,” she said."
110 ""
110 "’Liza-Lu then went to bed, and Tess, locking them all in, started on"
110 "her way up the dark and crooked lane or street not made for hasty"
110 "progress; a street laid out before inches of land had value, and when"
110 "one-handed clocks sufficiently subdivided the day."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER IV>"
110 ""
110 "Rolliver’s inn, the single alehouse at this end of the long and broken"
110 "village, could only boast of an off-licence; hence, as nobody could"
110 "legally drink on the premises, the amount of overt accommodation for"
110 "consumers was strictly limited to a little board about six inches wide"
110 "and two yards long, fixed to the garden palings by pieces of wire, so"
110 "as to form a ledge. On this board thirsty strangers deposited their"
110 "cups as they stood in the road and drank, and threw the dregs on the"
110 "dusty ground to the pattern of Polynesia, and wished they could have a"
110 "restful seat inside."
110 ""
110 "Thus the strangers. But there were also local customers who felt the"
110 "same wish; and where there’s a will there’s a way."
110 ""
110 "In a large bedroom upstairs, the window of which was thickly curtained"
110 "with a great woollen shawl lately discarded by the landlady, Mrs"
110 "Rolliver, were gathered on this evening nearly a dozen persons, all"
110 "seeking beatitude; all old inhabitants of the nearer end of Marlott,"
110 "and frequenters of this retreat. Not only did the distance to the The"
110 "Pure Drop, the fully-licensed tavern at the further part of the"
110 "dispersed village, render its accommodation practically unavailable for"
110 "dwellers at this end; but the far more serious question, the quality of"
110 "the liquor, confirmed the prevalent opinion that it was better to drink"
110 "with Rolliver in a corner of the housetop than with the other landlord"
110 "in a wide house."
110 ""
110 "A gaunt four-post bedstead which stood in the room afforded"
110 "sitting-space for several persons gathered round three of its sides; a"
110 "couple more men had elevated themselves on a chest of drawers; another"
110 "rested on the oak-carved “cwoffer”; two on the wash-stand; another on"
110 "the stool; and thus all were, somehow, seated at their ease. The stage"
110 "of mental comfort to which they had arrived at this hour was one"
110 "wherein their souls expanded beyond their skins, and spread their"
110 "personalities warmly through the room. In this process the chamber and"
110 "its furniture grew more and more dignified and luxurious; the shawl"
110 "hanging at the window took upon itself the richness of tapestry; the"
110 "brass handles of the chest of drawers were as golden knockers; and the"
110 "carved bedposts seemed to have some kinship with the magnificent"
110 "pillars of Solomon’s temple."
110 ""
110 "Mrs Durbeyfield, having quickly walked hitherward after parting from"
110 "Tess, opened the front door, crossed the downstairs room, which was in"
110 "deep gloom, and then unfastened the stair-door like one whose fingers"
110 "knew the tricks of the latches well. Her ascent of the crooked"
110 "staircase was a slower process, and her face, as it rose into the light"
110 "above the last stair, encountered the gaze of all the party assembled"
110 "in the bedroom."
110 ""
110 "“—Being a few private friends I’ve asked in to keep up club-walking at"
110 "my own expense,” the landlady exclaimed at the sound of footsteps, as"
110 "glibly as a child repeating the Catechism, while she peered over the"
110 "stairs. “Oh, ’tis you, Mrs Durbeyfield—Lard—how you frightened me!—I"
110 "thought it might be some gaffer sent by Gover’ment.”"
110 ""
110 "Mrs Durbeyfield was welcomed with glances and nods by the remainder of"
110 "the conclave, and turned to where her husband sat. He was humming"
110 "absently to himself, in a low tone: “I be as good as some folks here"
110 "and there! I’ve got a great family vault at Kingsbere-sub-Greenhill,"
110 "and finer skillentons than any man in Wessex!”"
110 ""
110 "“I’ve something to tell ’ee that’s come into my head about that—a grand"
110 "projick!” whispered his cheerful wife. “Here, John, don’t ’ee see me?”"
110 "She nudged him, while he, looking through her as through a window-pane,"
110 "went on with his recitative."
110 ""
110 "“Hush! Don’t ’ee sing so loud, my good man,” said the landlady; “in"
110 "case any member of the Gover’ment should be passing, and take away my"
110 "licends.”"
110 ""
110 "“He’s told ’ee what’s happened to us, I suppose?” asked Mrs"
110 "Durbeyfield."
110 ""
110 "“Yes—in a way. D’ye think there’s any money hanging by it?”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah, that’s the secret,” said Joan Durbeyfield sagely. “However, ’tis"
110 "well to be kin to a coach, even if you don’t ride in ’en.” She dropped"
110 "her public voice, and continued in a low tone to her husband: “I’ve"
110 "been thinking since you brought the news that there’s a great rich lady"
110 "out by Trantridge, on the edge o’ The Chase, of the name of"
110 "d’Urberville.”"
110 ""
110 "“Hey—what’s that?” said Sir John."
110 ""
110 "She repeated the information. “That lady must be our relation,” she"
110 "said. “And my projick is to send Tess to claim kin.”"
110 ""
110 "“There _is_ a lady of the name, now you mention it,” said Durbeyfield."
110 "“Pa’son Tringham didn’t think of that. But she’s nothing beside we—a"
110 "junior branch of us, no doubt, hailing long since King Norman’s day.”"
110 ""
110 "While this question was being discussed neither of the pair noticed, in"
110 "their preoccupation, that little Abraham had crept into the room, and"
110 "was awaiting an opportunity of asking them to return."
110 ""
110 "“She is rich, and she’d be sure to take notice o’ the maid,” continued"
110 "Mrs Durbeyfield; “and ’twill be a very good thing. I don’t see why two"
110 "branches o’ one family should not be on visiting terms.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes; and we’ll all claim kin!” said Abraham brightly from under the"
110 "bedstead. “And we’ll all go and see her when Tess has gone to live with"
110 "her; and we’ll ride in her coach and wear black clothes!”"
110 ""
110 "“How do you come here, child? What nonsense be ye talking! Go away, and"
110 "play on the stairs till father and mother be ready!... Well, Tess ought"
110 "to go to this other member of our family. She’d be sure to win the"
110 "lady—Tess would; and likely enough ’twould lead to some noble gentleman"
110 "marrying her. In short, I know it.”"
110 ""
110 "“How?”"
110 ""
110 "“I tried her fate in the _Fortune-Teller_, and it brought out that very"
110 "thing!... You should ha’ seen how pretty she looked to-day; her skin is"
110 "as sumple as a duchess’.”"
110 ""
110 "“What says the maid herself to going?”"
110 ""
110 "“I’ve not asked her. She don’t know there is any such lady-relation"
110 "yet. But it would certainly put her in the way of a grand marriage, and"
110 "she won’t say nay to going.”"
110 ""
110 "“Tess is queer.”"
110 ""
110 "“But she’s tractable at bottom. Leave her to me.”"
110 ""
110 "Though this conversation had been private, sufficient of its import"
110 "reached the understandings of those around to suggest to them that the"
110 "Durbeyfields had weightier concerns to talk of now than common folks"
110 "had, and that Tess, their pretty eldest daughter, had fine prospects in"
110 "store."
110 ""
110 "“Tess is a fine figure o’ fun, as I said to myself to-day when I zeed"
110 "her vamping round parish with the rest,” observed one of the elderly"
110 "boozers in an undertone. “But Joan Durbeyfield must mind that she don’t"
110 "get green malt in floor.” It was a local phrase which had a peculiar"
110 "meaning, and there was no reply."
110 ""
110 "The conversation became inclusive, and presently other footsteps were"
110 "heard crossing the room below."
110 ""
110 "“—Being a few private friends asked in to-night to keep up club-walking"
110 "at my own expense.” The landlady had rapidly re-used the formula she"
110 "kept on hand for intruders before she recognized that the newcomer was"
110 "Tess."
110 ""
110 "Even to her mother’s gaze the girl’s young features looked sadly out of"
110 "place amid the alcoholic vapours which floated here as no unsuitable"
110 "medium for wrinkled middle-age; and hardly was a reproachful flash from"
110 "Tess’s dark eyes needed to make her father and mother rise from their"
110 "seats, hastily finish their ale, and descend the stairs behind her, Mrs"
110 "Rolliver’s caution following their footsteps."
110 ""
110 "“No noise, please, if ye’ll be so good, my dears; or I mid lose my"
110 "licends, and be summons’d, and I don’t know what all! ’Night t’ye!”"
110 ""
110 "They went home together, Tess holding one arm of her father, and Mrs"
110 "Durbeyfield the other. He had, in truth, drunk very little—not a fourth"
110 "of the quantity which a systematic tippler could carry to church on a"
110 "Sunday afternoon without a hitch in his eastings or genuflections; but"
110 "the weakness of Sir John’s constitution made mountains of his petty"
110 "sins in this kind. On reaching the fresh air he was sufficiently"
110 "unsteady to incline the row of three at one moment as if they were"
110 "marching to London, and at another as if they were marching to"
110 "Bath—which produced a comical effect, frequent enough in families on"
110 "nocturnal homegoings; and, like most comical effects, not quite so"
110 "comic after all. The two women valiantly disguised these forced"
110 "excursions and countermarches as well as they could from Durbeyfield,"
110 "their cause, and from Abraham, and from themselves; and so they"
110 "approached by degrees their own door, the head of the family bursting"
110 "suddenly into his former refrain as he drew near, as if to fortify his"
110 "soul at sight of the smallness of his present residence—"
110 ""
110 "“I’ve got a fam—ily vault at Kingsbere!”"
110 ""
110 "“Hush—don’t be so silly, Jacky,” said his wife. “Yours is not the only"
110 "family that was of ’count in wold days. Look at the Anktells, and"
110 "Horseys, and the Tringhams themselves—gone to seed a’most as much as"
110 "you—though you was bigger folks than they, that’s true. Thank God, I"
110 "was never of no family, and have nothing to be ashamed of in that way!”"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t you be so sure o’ that. From your nater ’tis my belief you’ve"
110 "disgraced yourselves more than any o’ us, and was kings and queens"
110 "outright at one time.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess turned the subject by saying what was far more prominent in her"
110 "own mind at the moment than thoughts of her ancestry—“I am afraid"
110 "father won’t be able to take the journey with the beehives to-morrow so"
110 "early.”"
110 ""
110 "“I? I shall be all right in an hour or two,” said Durbeyfield."
110 ""
110 "It was eleven o’clock before the family were all in bed, and two"
110 "o’clock next morning was the latest hour for starting with the beehives"
110 "if they were to be delivered to the retailers in Casterbridge before"
110 "the Saturday market began, the way thither lying by bad roads over a"
110 "distance of between twenty and thirty miles, and the horse and waggon"
110 "being of the slowest. At half-past one Mrs Durbeyfield came into the"
110 "large bedroom where Tess and all her little brothers and sisters slept."
110 ""
110 "“The poor man can’t go,” she said to her eldest daughter, whose great"
110 "eyes had opened the moment her mother’s hand touched the door."
110 ""
110 "Tess sat up in bed, lost in a vague interspace between a dream and this"
110 "information."
110 ""
110 "“But somebody must go,” she replied. “It is late for the hives already."
110 "Swarming will soon be over for the year; and it we put off taking ’em"
110 "till next week’s market the call for ’em will be past, and they’ll be"
110 "thrown on our hands.”"
110 ""
110 "Mrs Durbeyfield looked unequal to the emergency. “Some young feller,"
110 "perhaps, would go? One of them who were so much after dancing with ’ee"
110 "yesterday,” she presently suggested."
110 ""
110 "“O no—I wouldn’t have it for the world!” declared Tess proudly. “And"
110 "letting everybody know the reason—such a thing to be ashamed of! I"
110 "think _I_ could go if Abraham could go with me to kip me company.”"
110 ""
110 "Her mother at length agreed to this arrangement. Little Abraham was"
110 "aroused from his deep sleep in a corner of the same apartment, and made"
110 "to put on his clothes while still mentally in the other world."
110 "Meanwhile Tess had hastily dressed herself; and the twain, lighting a"
110 "lantern, went out to the stable. The rickety little waggon was already"
110 "laden, and the girl led out the horse, Prince, only a degree less"
110 "rickety than the vehicle."
110 ""
110 "The poor creature looked wonderingly round at the night, at the"
110 "lantern, at their two figures, as if he could not believe that at that"
110 "hour, when every living thing was intended to be in shelter and at"
110 "rest, he was called upon to go out and labour. They put a stock of"
110 "candle-ends into the lantern, hung the latter to the off-side of the"
110 "load, and directed the horse onward, walking at his shoulder at first"
110 "during the uphill parts of the way, in order not to overload an animal"
110 "of so little vigour. To cheer themselves as well as they could, they"
110 "made an artificial morning with the lantern, some bread and butter, and"
110 "their own conversation, the real morning being far from come. Abraham,"
110 "as he more fully awoke (for he had moved in a sort of trance so far),"
110 "began to talk of the strange shapes assumed by the various dark objects"
110 "against the sky; of this tree that looked like a raging tiger springing"
110 "from a lair; of that which resembled a giant’s head."
110 ""
110 "When they had passed the little town of Stourcastle, dumbly somnolent"
110 "under its thick brown thatch, they reached higher ground. Still higher,"
110 "on their left, the elevation called Bulbarrow, or Bealbarrow, well-nigh"
110 "the highest in South Wessex, swelled into the sky, engirdled by its"
110 "earthen trenches. From hereabout the long road was fairly level for"
110 "some distance onward. They mounted in front of the waggon, and Abraham"
110 "grew reflective."
110 ""
110 "“Tess!” he said in a preparatory tone, after a silence."
110 ""
110 "“Yes, Abraham.”"
110 ""
110 "“Bain’t you glad that we’ve become gentlefolk?”"
110 ""
110 "“Not particular glad.”"
110 ""
110 "“But you be glad that you ’m going to marry a gentleman?”"
110 ""
110 "“What?” said Tess, lifting her face."
110 ""
110 "“That our great relation will help ’ee to marry a gentleman.”"
110 ""
110 "“I? Our great relation? We have no such relation. What has put that"
110 "into your head?”"
110 ""
110 "“I heard ’em talking about it up at Rolliver’s when I went to find"
110 "father. There’s a rich lady of our family out at Trantridge, and mother"
110 "said that if you claimed kin with the lady, she’d put ’ee in the way of"
110 "marrying a gentleman.”"
110 ""
110 "His sister became abruptly still, and lapsed into a pondering silence."
110 "Abraham talked on, rather for the pleasure of utterance than for"
110 "audition, so that his sister’s abstraction was of no account. He leant"
110 "back against the hives, and with upturned face made observations on the"
110 "stars, whose cold pulses were beating amid the black hollows above, in"
110 "serene dissociation from these two wisps of human life. He asked how"
110 "far away those twinklers were, and whether God was on the other side of"
110 "them. But ever and anon his childish prattle recurred to what impressed"
110 "his imagination even more deeply than the wonders of creation. If Tess"
110 "were made rich by marrying a gentleman, would she have money enough to"
110 "buy a spyglass so large that it would draw the stars as near to her as"
110 "Nettlecombe-Tout?"
110 ""
110 "The renewed subject, which seemed to have impregnated the whole family,"
110 "filled Tess with impatience."
110 ""
110 "“Never mind that now!” she exclaimed."
110 ""
110 "“Did you say the stars were worlds, Tess?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes.”"
110 ""
110 "“All like ours?”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t know; but I think so. They sometimes seem to be like the"
110 "apples on our stubbard-tree. Most of them splendid and sound—a few"
110 "blighted.”"
110 ""
110 "“Which do we live on—a splendid one or a blighted one?”"
110 ""
110 "“A blighted one.”"
110 ""
110 "“’Tis very unlucky that we didn’t pitch on a sound one, when there were"
110 "so many more of ’em!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes.”"
110 ""
110 "“Is it like that _really_, Tess?” said Abraham, turning to her much"
110 "impressed, on reconsideration of this rare information. “How would it"
110 "have been if we had pitched on a sound one?”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, father wouldn’t have coughed and creeped about as he does, and"
110 "wouldn’t have got too tipsy to go on this journey; and mother wouldn’t"
110 "have been always washing, and never getting finished.”"
110 ""
110 "“And you would have been a rich lady ready-made, and not have had to be"
110 "made rich by marrying a gentleman?”"
110 ""
110 "“O Aby, don’t—don’t talk of that any more!”"
110 ""
110 "Left to his reflections Abraham soon grew drowsy. Tess was not skilful"
110 "in the management of a horse, but she thought that she could take upon"
110 "herself the entire conduct of the load for the present and allow"
110 "Abraham to go to sleep if he wished to do so. She made him a sort of"
110 "nest in front of the hives, in such a manner that he could not fall,"
110 "and, taking the reins into her own hands, jogged on as before."
110 ""
110 "Prince required but slight attention, lacking energy for superfluous"
110 "movements of any sort. With no longer a companion to distract her, Tess"
110 "fell more deeply into reverie than ever, her back leaning against the"
110 "hives. The mute procession past her shoulders of trees and hedges"
110 "became attached to fantastic scenes outside reality, and the occasional"
110 "heave of the wind became the sigh of some immense sad soul,"
110 "conterminous with the universe in space, and with history in time."
110 ""
110 "Then, examining the mesh of events in her own life, she seemed to see"
110 "the vanity of her father’s pride; the gentlemanly suitor awaiting"
110 "herself in her mother’s fancy; to see him as a grimacing personage,"
110 "laughing at her poverty and her shrouded knightly ancestry. Everything"
110 "grew more and more extravagant, and she no longer knew how time passed."
110 "A sudden jerk shook her in her seat, and Tess awoke from the sleep into"
110 "which she, too, had fallen."
110 ""
110 "They were a long way further on than when she had lost consciousness,"
110 "and the waggon had stopped. A hollow groan, unlike anything she had"
110 "ever heard in her life, came from the front, followed by a shout of"
110 "“Hoi there!”"
110 ""
110 "The lantern hanging at her waggon had gone out, but another was shining"
110 "in her face—much brighter than her own had been. Something terrible had"
110 "happened. The harness was entangled with an object which blocked the"
110 "way."
110 ""
110 "In consternation Tess jumped down, and discovered the dreadful truth."
110 "The groan had proceeded from her father’s poor horse Prince. The"
110 "morning mail-cart, with its two noiseless wheels, speeding along these"
110 "lanes like an arrow, as it always did, had driven into her slow and"
110 "unlighted equipage. The pointed shaft of the cart had entered the"
110 "breast of the unhappy Prince like a sword, and from the wound his"
110 "life’s blood was spouting in a stream, and falling with a hiss into the"
110 "road."
110 ""
110 "In her despair Tess sprang forward and put her hand upon the hole, with"
110 "the only result that she became splashed from face to skirt with the"
110 "crimson drops. Then she stood helplessly looking on. Prince also stood"
110 "firm and motionless as long as he could; till he suddenly sank down in"
110 "a heap."
110 ""
110 "By this time the mail-cart man had joined her, and began dragging and"
110 "unharnessing the hot form of Prince. But he was already dead, and,"
110 "seeing that nothing more could be done immediately, the mail-cart man"
110 "returned to his own animal, which was uninjured."
110 ""
110 "“You was on the wrong side,” he said. “I am bound to go on with the"
110 "mail-bags, so that the best thing for you to do is bide here with your"
110 "load. I’ll send somebody to help you as soon as I can. It is getting"
110 "daylight, and you have nothing to fear.”"
110 ""
110 "He mounted and sped on his way; while Tess stood and waited. The"
110 "atmosphere turned pale, the birds shook themselves in the hedges,"
110 "arose, and twittered; the lane showed all its white features, and Tess"
