110 ""
110 "“’Tis all my doing—all mine!” the girl cried, gazing at the spectacle."
110 "“No excuse for me—none. What will mother and father live on now? Aby,"
110 "Aby!” She shook the child, who had slept soundly through the whole"
110 "disaster. “We can’t go on with our load—Prince is killed!”"
110 ""
110 "When Abraham realized all, the furrows of fifty years were extemporized"
110 "on his young face."
110 ""
110 "“Why, I danced and laughed only yesterday!” she went on to herself. “To"
110 "think that I was such a fool!”"
110 ""
110 "“’Tis because we be on a blighted star, and not a sound one, isn’t it,"
110 "Tess?” murmured Abraham through his tears."
110 ""
110 "In silence they waited through an interval which seemed endless. At"
110 "length a sound, and an approaching object, proved to them that the"
110 "driver of the mail-car had been as good as his word. A farmer’s man"
110 "from near Stourcastle came up, leading a strong cob. He was harnessed"
110 "to the waggon of beehives in the place of Prince, and the load taken on"
110 "towards Casterbridge."
110 ""
110 "The evening of the same day saw the empty waggon reach again the spot"
110 "of the accident. Prince had lain there in the ditch since the morning;"
110 "but the place of the blood-pool was still visible in the middle of the"
110 "road, though scratched and scraped over by passing vehicles. All that"
110 "was left of Prince was now hoisted into the waggon he had formerly"
110 "hauled, and with his hoofs in the air, and his shoes shining in the"
110 "setting sunlight, he retraced the eight or nine miles to Marlott."
110 ""
110 "Tess had gone back earlier. How to break the news was more than she"
110 "could think. It was a relief to her tongue to find from the faces of"
110 "her parents that they already knew of their loss, though this did not"
110 "lessen the self-reproach which she continued to heap upon herself for"
110 "her negligence."
110 ""
110 "But the very shiftlessness of the household rendered the misfortune a"
110 "less terrifying one to them than it would have been to a thriving"
110 "family, though in the present case it meant ruin, and in the other it"
110 "would only have meant inconvenience. In the Durbeyfield countenances"
110 "there was nothing of the red wrath that would have burnt upon the girl"
110 "from parents more ambitious for her welfare. Nobody blamed Tess as she"
110 "blamed herself."
110 ""
110 "When it was discovered that the knacker and tanner would give only a"
110 "very few shillings for Prince’s carcase because of his decrepitude,"
110 "Durbeyfield rose to the occasion."
110 ""
110 "“No,” said he stoically, “I won’t sell his old body. When we"
110 "d’Urbervilles was knights in the land, we didn’t sell our chargers for"
110 "cat’s meat. Let ’em keep their shillings! He’ve served me well in his"
110 "lifetime, and I won’t part from him now.”"
110 ""
110 "He worked harder the next day in digging a grave for Prince in the"
110 "garden than he had worked for months to grow a crop for his family."
110 "When the hole was ready, Durbeyfield and his wife tied a rope round the"
110 "horse and dragged him up the path towards it, the children following in"
110 "funeral train. Abraham and ’Liza-Lu sobbed, Hope and Modesty discharged"
110 "their griefs in loud blares which echoed from the walls; and when"
110 "Prince was tumbled in they gathered round the grave. The bread-winner"
110 "had been taken away from them; what would they do?"
110 ""
110 "“Is he gone to heaven?” asked Abraham, between the sobs."
110 ""
110 "Then Durbeyfield began to shovel in the earth, and the children cried"
110 "anew. All except Tess. Her face was dry and pale, as though she"
110 "regarded herself in the light of a murderess."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER V>"
110 ""
110 "The haggling business, which had mainly depended on the horse, became"
110 "disorganized forthwith. Distress, if not penury, loomed in the"
110 "distance. Durbeyfield was what was locally called a slack-twisted"
110 "fellow; he had good strength to work at times; but the times could not"
110 "be relied on to coincide with the hours of requirement; and, having"
110 "been unaccustomed to the regular toil of the day-labourer, he was not"
110 "particularly persistent when they did so coincide."
110 ""
110 "Tess, meanwhile, as the one who had dragged her parents into this"
110 "quagmire, was silently wondering what she could do to help them out of"
110 "it; and then her mother broached her scheme."
110 ""
110 "“We must take the ups wi’ the downs, Tess,” said she; “and never could"
110 "your high blood have been found out at a more called-for moment. You"
110 "must try your friends. Do ye know that there is a very rich Mrs"
110 "d’Urberville living on the outskirts o’ The Chase, who must be our"
110 "relation? You must go to her and claim kin, and ask for some help in"
110 "our trouble.”"
110 ""
110 "“I shouldn’t care to do that,” says Tess. “If there is such a lady,"
110 "’twould be enough for us if she were friendly—not to expect her to give"
110 "us help.”"
110 ""
110 "“You could win her round to do anything, my dear. Besides, perhaps"
110 "there’s more in it than you know of. I’ve heard what I’ve heard,"
110 "good-now.”"
110 ""
110 "The oppressive sense of the harm she had done led Tess to be more"
110 "deferential than she might otherwise have been to the maternal wish;"
110 "but she could not understand why her mother should find such"
110 "satisfaction in contemplating an enterprise of, to her, such doubtful"
110 "profit. Her mother might have made inquiries, and have discovered that"
110 "this Mrs d’Urberville was a lady of unequalled virtues and charity. But"
110 "Tess’s pride made the part of poor relation one of particular distaste"
110 "to her."
110 ""
110 "“I’d rather try to get work,” she murmured."
110 ""
110 "“Durbeyfield, you can settle it,” said his wife, turning to where he"
110 "sat in the background. “If you say she ought to go, she will go.”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t like my children going and making themselves beholden to"
110 "strange kin,” murmured he. “I’m the head of the noblest branch o’ the"
110 "family, and I ought to live up to it.”"
110 ""
110 "His reasons for staying away were worse to Tess than her own objections"
110 "to going. “Well, as I killed the horse, mother,” she said mournfully,"
110 "“I suppose I ought to do something. I don’t mind going and seeing her,"
110 "but you must leave it to me about asking for help. And don’t go"
110 "thinking about her making a match for me—it is silly.”"
110 ""
110 "“Very well said, Tess!” observed her father sententiously."
110 ""
110 "“Who said I had such a thought?” asked Joan."
110 ""
110 "“I fancy it is in your mind, mother. But I’ll go.”"
110 ""
110 "Rising early next day she walked to the hill-town called Shaston, and"
110 "there took advantage of a van which twice in the week ran from Shaston"
110 "eastward to Chaseborough, passing near Trantridge, the parish in which"
110 "the vague and mysterious Mrs d’Urberville had her residence."
110 ""
110 "Tess Durbeyfield’s route on this memorable morning lay amid the"
110 "north-eastern undulations of the Vale in which she had been born, and"
110 "in which her life had unfolded. The Vale of Blackmoor was to her the"
110 "world, and its inhabitants the races thereof. From the gates and stiles"
110 "of Marlott she had looked down its length in the wondering days of"
110 "infancy, and what had been mystery to her then was not much less than"
110 "mystery to her now. She had seen daily from her chamber-window towers,"
110 "villages, faint white mansions; above all, the town of Shaston standing"
110 "majestically on its height; its windows shining like lamps in the"
110 "evening sun. She had hardly ever visited the place, only a small tract"
110 "even of the Vale and its environs being known to her by close"
110 "inspection. Much less had she been far outside the valley. Every"
110 "contour of the surrounding hills was as personal to her as that of her"
110 "relatives’ faces; but for what lay beyond, her judgment was dependent"
110 "on the teaching of the village school, where she had held a leading"
110 "place at the time of her leaving, a year or two before this date."
110 ""
110 "In those early days she had been much loved by others of her own sex"
110 "and age, and had used to be seen about the village as one of three—all"
110 "nearly of the same year—walking home from school side by side; Tess the"
110 "middle one—in a pink print pinafore, of a finely reticulated pattern,"
110 "worn over a stuff frock that had lost its original colour for a"
110 "nondescript tertiary—marching on upon long stalky legs, in tight"
110 "stockings which had little ladder-like holes at the knees, torn by"
110 "kneeling in the roads and banks in search of vegetable and mineral"
110 "treasures; her then earth-coloured hair hanging like pot-hooks; the"
110 "arms of the two outside girls resting round the waist of Tess; her arms"
110 "on the shoulders of the two supporters."
110 ""
110 "As Tess grew older, and began to see how matters stood, she felt quite"
110 "a Malthusian towards her mother for thoughtlessly giving her so many"
110 "little sisters and brothers, when it was such a trouble to nurse and"
110 "provide for them. Her mother’s intelligence was that of a happy child:"
110 "Joan Durbeyfield was simply an additional one, and that not the eldest,"
110 "to her own long family of waiters on Providence."
110 ""
110 "However, Tess became humanely beneficent towards the small ones, and to"
110 "help them as much as possible she used, as soon as she left school, to"
110 "lend a hand at haymaking or harvesting on neighbouring farms; or, by"
110 "preference, at milking or butter-making processes, which she had learnt"
110 "when her father had owned cows; and being deft-fingered it was a kind"
110 "of work in which she excelled."
110 ""
110 "Every day seemed to throw upon her young shoulders more of the family"
110 "burdens, and that Tess should be the representative of the Durbeyfields"
110 "at the d’Urberville mansion came as a thing of course. In this instance"
110 "it must be admitted that the Durbeyfields were putting their fairest"
110 "side outward."
110 ""
110 "She alighted from the van at Trantridge Cross, and ascended on foot a"
110 "hill in the direction of the district known as The Chase, on the"
110 "borders of which, as she had been informed, Mrs d’Urberville’s seat,"
110 "The Slopes, would be found. It was not a manorial home in the ordinary"
110 "sense, with fields, and pastures, and a grumbling farmer, out of whom"
110 "the owner had to squeeze an income for himself and his family by hook"
110 "or by crook. It was more, far more; a country-house built for enjoyment"
110 "pure and simple, with not an acre of troublesome land attached to it"
110 "beyond what was required for residential purposes, and for a little"
110 "fancy farm kept in hand by the owner, and tended by a bailiff."
110 ""
110 "The crimson brick lodge came first in sight, up to its eaves in dense"
110 "evergreens. Tess thought this was the mansion itself till, passing"
110 "through the side wicket with some trepidation, and onward to a point at"
110 "which the drive took a turn, the house proper stood in full view. It"
110 "was of recent erection—indeed almost new—and of the same rich red"
110 "colour that formed such a contrast with the evergreens of the lodge."
110 "Far behind the corner of the house—which rose like a geranium bloom"
110 "against the subdued colours around—stretched the soft azure landscape"
110 "of The Chase—a truly venerable tract of forest land, one of the few"
110 "remaining woodlands in England of undoubted primaeval date, wherein"
110 "Druidical mistletoe was still found on aged oaks, and where enormous"
110 "yew-trees, not planted by the hand of man grew as they had grown when"
110 "they were pollarded for bows. All this sylvan antiquity, however,"
110 "though visible from The Slopes, was outside the immediate boundaries of"
110 "the estate."
110 ""
110 "Everything on this snug property was bright, thriving, and well kept;"
110 "acres of glass-houses stretched down the inclines to the copses at"
110 "their feet. Everything looked like money—like the last coin issued from"
110 "the Mint. The stables, partly screened by Austrian pines and evergreen"
110 "oaks, and fitted with every late appliance, were as dignified as"
110 "Chapels-of-Ease. On the extensive lawn stood an ornamental tent, its"
110 "door being towards her."
110 ""
110 "Simple Tess Durbeyfield stood at gaze, in a half-alarmed attitude, on"
110 "the edge of the gravel sweep. Her feet had brought her onward to this"
110 "point before she had quite realized where she was; and now all was"
110 "contrary to her expectation."
110 ""
110 "“I thought we were an old family; but this is all new!” she said, in"
110 "her artlessness. She wished that she had not fallen in so readily with"
110 "her mother’s plans for “claiming kin,” and had endeavoured to gain"
110 "assistance nearer home."
110 ""
110 "The d’Urbervilles—or Stoke-d’Urbervilles, as they at first called"
110 "themselves—who owned all this, were a somewhat unusual family to find"
110 "in such an old-fashioned part of the country. Parson Tringham had"
110 "spoken truly when he said that our shambling John Durbeyfield was the"
110 "only really lineal representative of the old d’Urberville family"
110 "existing in the county, or near it; he might have added, what he knew"
110 "very well, that the Stoke-d’Urbervilles were no more d’Urbervilles of"
110 "the true tree then he was himself. Yet it must be admitted that this"
110 "family formed a very good stock whereon to regraft a name which sadly"
110 "wanted such renovation."
110 ""
110 "When old Mr Simon Stoke, latterly deceased, had made his fortune as an"
110 "honest merchant (some said money-lender) in the North, he decided to"
110 "settle as a county man in the South of England, out of hail of his"
110 "business district; and in doing this he felt the necessity of"
110 "recommencing with a name that would not too readily identify him with"
110 "the smart tradesman of the past, and that would be less commonplace"
110 "than the original bald, stark words. Conning for an hour in the British"
110 "Museum the pages of works devoted to extinct, half-extinct, obscured,"
110 "and ruined families appertaining to the quarter of England in which he"
110 "proposed to settle, he considered that _d’Urberville_ looked and"
110 "sounded as well as any of them: and d’Urberville accordingly was"
110 "annexed to his own name for himself and his heirs eternally. Yet he was"
110 "not an extravagant-minded man in this, and in constructing his family"
110 "tree on the new basis was duly reasonable in framing his"
110 "inter-marriages and aristocratic links, never inserting a single title"
110 "above a rank of strict moderation."
110 ""
110 "Of this work of imagination poor Tess and her parents were naturally in"
110 "ignorance—much to their discomfiture; indeed, the very possibility of"
110 "such annexations was unknown to them; who supposed that, though to be"
110 "well-favoured might be the gift of fortune, a family name came by"
110 "nature."
110 ""
110 "Tess still stood hesitating like a bather about to make his plunge,"
110 "hardly knowing whether to retreat or to persevere, when a figure came"
110 "forth from the dark triangular door of the tent. It was that of a tall"
110 "young man, smoking."
110 ""
110 "He had an almost swarthy complexion, with full lips, badly moulded,"
110 "though red and smooth, above which was a well-groomed black moustache"
110 "with curled points, though his age could not be more than three- or"
110 "four-and-twenty. Despite the touches of barbarism in his contours,"
110 "there was a singular force in the gentleman’s face, and in his bold"
110 "rolling eye."
110 ""
110 "“Well, my Beauty, what can I do for you?” said he, coming forward. And"
110 "perceiving that she stood quite confounded: “Never mind me. I am Mr"
110 "d’Urberville. Have you come to see me or my mother?”"
110 ""
110 "This embodiment of a d’Urberville and a namesake differed even more"
110 "from what Tess had expected than the house and grounds had differed."
110 "She had dreamed of an aged and dignified face, the sublimation of all"
110 "the d’Urberville lineaments, furrowed with incarnate memories"
110 "representing in hieroglyphic the centuries of her family’s and"
110 "England’s history. But she screwed herself up to the work in hand,"
110 "since she could not get out of it, and answered—"
110 ""
110 "“I came to see your mother, sir.”"
110 ""
110 "“I am afraid you cannot see her—she is an invalid,” replied the present"
110 "representative of the spurious house; for this was Mr Alec, the only"
110 "son of the lately deceased gentleman. “Cannot I answer your purpose?"
110 "What is the business you wish to see her about?”"
110 ""
110 "“It isn’t business—it is—I can hardly say what!”"
110 ""
110 "“Pleasure?”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh no. Why, sir, if I tell you, it will seem—”"
110 ""
110 "Tess’s sense of a certain ludicrousness in her errand was now so strong"
110 "that, notwithstanding her awe of him, and her general discomfort at"
110 "being here, her rosy lips curved towards a smile, much to the"
110 "attraction of the swarthy Alexander."
110 ""
110 "“It is so very foolish,” she stammered; “I fear I can’t tell you!”"
110 ""
110 "“Never mind; I like foolish things. Try again, my dear,” said he"
110 "kindly."
110 ""
110 "“Mother asked me to come,” Tess continued; “and, indeed, I was in the"
110 "mind to do so myself likewise. But I did not think it would be like"
110 "this. I came, sir, to tell you that we are of the same family as you.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ho! Poor relations?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes.”"
110 ""
110 "“Stokes?”"
110 ""
110 "“No; d’Urbervilles.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ay, ay; I mean d’Urbervilles.”"
110 ""
110 "“Our names are worn away to Durbeyfield; but we have several proofs"
110 "that we are d’Urbervilles. Antiquarians hold we are,—and—and we have an"
110 "old seal, marked with a ramping lion on a shield, and a castle over"
110 "him. And we have a very old silver spoon, round in the bowl like a"
110 "little ladle, and marked with the same castle. But it is so worn that"
110 "mother uses it to stir the pea-soup.”"
110 ""
110 "“A castle argent is certainly my crest,” said he blandly. “And my arms"
110 "a lion rampant.”"
110 ""
110 "“And so mother said we ought to make ourselves beknown to you—as we’ve"
110 "lost our horse by a bad accident, and are the oldest branch o’ the"
110 "family.”"
110 ""
110 "“Very kind of your mother, I’m sure. And I, for one, don’t regret her"
110 "step.” Alec looked at Tess as he spoke, in a way that made her blush a"
110 "little. “And so, my pretty girl, you’ve come on a friendly visit to us,"
110 "as relations?”"
110 ""
110 "“I suppose I have,” faltered Tess, looking uncomfortable again."
110 ""
110 "“Well—there’s no harm in it. Where do you live? What are you?”"
110 ""
110 "She gave him brief particulars; and responding to further inquiries"
110 "told him that she was intending to go back by the same carrier who had"
110 "brought her."
110 ""
110 "“It is a long while before he returns past Trantridge Cross. Supposing"
110 "we walk round the grounds to pass the time, my pretty Coz?”"
110 ""
110 "Tess wished to abridge her visit as much as possible; but the young man"
110 "was pressing, and she consented to accompany him. He conducted her"
110 "about the lawns, and flower-beds, and conservatories; and thence to the"
110 "fruit-garden and greenhouses, where he asked her if she liked"
110 "strawberries."
110 ""
110 "“Yes,” said Tess, “when they come.”"
110 ""
110 "“They are already here.” D’Urberville began gathering specimens of the"
110 "fruit for her, handing them back to her as he stooped; and, presently,"
110 "selecting a specially fine product of the “British Queen” variety, he"
110 "stood up and held it by the stem to her mouth."
110 ""
110 "“No—no!” she said quickly, putting her fingers between his hand and her"
110 "lips. “I would rather take it in my own hand.”"
110 ""
110 "“Nonsense!” he insisted; and in a slight distress she parted her lips"
110 "and took it in."
110 ""
110 "They had spent some time wandering desultorily thus, Tess eating in a"
110 "half-pleased, half-reluctant state whatever d’Urberville offered her."
110 "When she could consume no more of the strawberries he filled her little"
110 "basket with them; and then the two passed round to the rose-trees,"
110 "whence he gathered blossoms and gave her to put in her bosom. She"
110 "obeyed like one in a dream, and when she could affix no more he himself"
110 "tucked a bud or two into her hat, and heaped her basket with others in"
110 "the prodigality of his bounty. At last, looking at his watch, he said,"
110 "“Now, by the time you have had something to eat, it will be time for"
110 "you to leave, if you want to catch the carrier to Shaston. Come here,"
110 "and I’ll see what grub I can find.”"
110 ""
110 "Stoke d’Urberville took her back to the lawn and into the tent, where"
110 "he left her, soon reappearing with a basket of light luncheon, which he"
110 "put before her himself. It was evidently the gentleman’s wish not to be"
110 "disturbed in this pleasant _tête-à-tête_ by the servantry."
110 ""
110 "“Do you mind my smoking?” he asked."
110 ""
110 "“Oh, not at all, sir.”"
110 ""
110 "He watched her pretty and unconscious munching through the skeins of"
110 "smoke that pervaded the tent, and Tess Durbeyfield did not divine, as"
110 "she innocently looked down at the roses in her bosom, that there behind"
110 "the blue narcotic haze was potentially the “tragic mischief” of her"
110 "drama—one who stood fair to be the blood-red ray in the spectrum of her"
110 "young life. She had an attribute which amounted to a disadvantage just"
110 "now; and it was this that caused Alec d’Urberville’s eyes to rivet"
110 "themselves upon her. It was a luxuriance of aspect, a fulness of"
110 "growth, which made her appear more of a woman than she really was. She"
110 "had inherited the feature from her mother without the quality it"
110 "denoted. It had troubled her mind occasionally, till her companions had"
110 "said that it was a fault which time would cure."
110 ""
110 "She soon had finished her lunch. “Now I am going home, sir,” she said,"
110 "rising."
110 ""
110 "“And what do they call you?” he asked, as he accompanied her along the"
110 "drive till they were out of sight of the house."
110 ""
110 "“Tess Durbeyfield, down at Marlott.”"
110 ""
110 "“And you say your people have lost their horse?”"
110 ""
110 "“I—killed him!” she answered, her eyes filling with tears as she gave"
110 "particulars of Prince’s death. “And I don’t know what to do for father"
110 "on account of it!”"
110 ""
110 "“I must think if I cannot do something. My mother must find a berth for"
110 "you. But, Tess, no nonsense about ‘d’Urberville’;—‘Durbeyfield’ only,"
110 "you know—quite another name.”"
110 ""
110 "“I wish for no better, sir,” said she with something of dignity."
110 ""
110 "For a moment—only for a moment—when they were in the turning of the"
110 "drive, between the tall rhododendrons and conifers, before the lodge"
110 "became visible, he inclined his face towards her as if—but, no: he"
110 "thought better of it, and let her go."
110 ""
110 "Thus the thing began. Had she perceived this meeting’s import she might"
110 "have asked why she was doomed to be seen and coveted that day by the"
110 "wrong man, and not by some other man, the right and desired one in all"
110 "respects—as nearly as humanity can supply the right and desired; yet to"
110 "him who amongst her acquaintance might have approximated to this kind,"
110 "she was but a transient impression, half forgotten."
110 ""
110 "In the ill-judged execution of the well-judged plan of things the call"
110 "seldom produces the comer, the man to love rarely coincides with the"
110 "hour for loving. Nature does not often say “See!” to her poor creature"
110 "at a time when seeing can lead to happy doing; or reply “Here!” to a"
110 "body’s cry of “Where?” till the hide-and-seek has become an irksome,"
110 "outworn game. We may wonder whether at the acme and summit of the human"
110 "progress these anachronisms will be corrected by a finer intuition, a"
110 "closer interaction of the social machinery than that which now jolts us"
110 "round and along; but such completeness is not to be prophesied, or even"
110 "conceived as possible. Enough that in the present case, as in millions,"
110 "it was not the two halves of a perfect whole that confronted each other"
110 "at the perfect moment; a missing counterpart wandered independently"
110 "about the earth waiting in crass obtuseness till the late time came."
110 "Out of which maladroit delay sprang anxieties, disappointments, shocks,"
110 "catastrophes, and passing-strange destinies."
110 ""
110 "When d’Urberville got back to the tent he sat down astride on a chair,"
110 "reflecting, with a pleased gleam in his face. Then he broke into a loud"
110 "laugh."
110 ""
110 "“Well, I’m damned! What a funny thing! Ha-ha-ha! And what a crumby"
110 "girl!”"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER VI>"
110 ""
110 "Tess went down the hill to Trantridge Cross, and inattentively waited"
110 "to take her seat in the van returning from Chaseborough to Shaston. She"
110 "did not know what the other occupants said to her as she entered,"
110 "though she answered them; and when they had started anew she rode along"
110 "with an inward and not an outward eye."
110 ""
110 "One among her fellow-travellers addressed her more pointedly than any"
110 "had spoken before: “Why, you be quite a posy! And such roses in early"
110 "June!”"
110 ""
110 "Then she became aware of the spectacle she presented to their surprised"
110 "vision: roses at her breasts; roses in her hat; roses and strawberries"
110 "in her basket to the brim. She blushed, and said confusedly that the"
110 "flowers had been given to her. When the passengers were not looking she"
110 "stealthily removed the more prominent blooms from her hat and placed"
110 "them in the basket, where she covered them with her handkerchief. Then"
110 "she fell to reflecting again, and in looking downwards a thorn of the"
110 "rose remaining in her breast accidentally pricked her chin. Like all"
110 "the cottagers in Blackmoor Vale, Tess was steeped in fancies and"
110 "prefigurative superstitions; she thought this an ill omen—the first she"
110 "had noticed that day."
110 ""
110 "The van travelled only so far as Shaston, and there were several miles"
110 "of pedestrian descent from that mountain-town into the vale to Marlott."
110 "Her mother had advised her to stay here for the night, at the house of"
110 "a cottage-woman they knew, if she should feel too tired to come on; and"
110 "this Tess did, not descending to her home till the following afternoon."
110 ""
110 "When she entered the house she perceived in a moment from her mother’s"
110 "triumphant manner that something had occurred in the interim."
110 ""
110 "“Oh yes; I know all about it! I told ’ee it would be all right, and now"
110 "’tis proved!”"
110 ""
110 "“Since I’ve been away? What has?” said Tess rather wearily."
110 ""
110 "Her mother surveyed the girl up and down with arch approval, and went"
110 "on banteringly: “So you’ve brought ’em round!”"
110 ""
110 "“How do you know, mother?”"
110 ""
110 "“I’ve had a letter.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess then remembered that there would have been time for this."
110 ""
110 "“They say—Mrs d’Urberville says—that she wants you to look after a"
110 "little fowl-farm which is her hobby. But this is only her artful way of"
110 "getting ’ee there without raising your hopes. She’s going to own ’ee as"
110 "kin—that’s the meaning o’t.”"
110 ""
110 "“But I didn’t see her.”"
110 ""
110 "“You zid somebody, I suppose?”"
110 ""
110 "“I saw her son.”"
110 ""
110 "“And did he own ’ee?”"
110 ""
110 "“Well—he called me Coz.”"
110 ""
110 "“An’ I knew it! Jacky—he called her Coz!” cried Joan to her husband."
110 "“Well, he spoke to his mother, of course, and she do want ’ee there.”"
110 ""
110 "“But I don’t know that I am apt at tending fowls,” said the dubious"
110 "Tess."
110 ""
110 "“Then I don’t know who is apt. You’ve be’n born in the business, and"
110 "brought up in it. They that be born in a business always know more"
110 "about it than any ’prentice. Besides, that’s only just a show of"
110 "something for you to do, that you midn’t feel beholden.”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t altogether think I ought to go,” said Tess thoughtfully. “Who"
110 "wrote the letter? Will you let me look at it?”"
110 ""
110 "“Mrs d’Urberville wrote it. Here it is.”"
110 ""
110 "The letter was in the third person, and briefly informed Mrs"
110 "Durbeyfield that her daughter’s services would be useful to that lady"
110 "in the management of her poultry-farm, that a comfortable room would be"
110 "provided for her if she could come, and that the wages would be on a"
110 "liberal scale if they liked her."
110 ""
110 "“Oh—that’s all!” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "“You couldn’t expect her to throw her arms round ’ee, an’ to kiss and"
110 "to coll ’ee all at once.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess looked out of the window."
110 ""
110 "“I would rather stay here with father and you,” she said."
110 ""
110 "“But why?”"
110 ""
110 "“I’d rather not tell you why, mother; indeed, I don’t quite know why.”"
110 ""
110 "A week afterwards she came in one evening from an unavailing search for"
110 "some light occupation in the immediate neighbourhood. Her idea had been"
110 "to get together sufficient money during the summer to purchase another"
110 "horse. Hardly had she crossed the threshold before one of the children"
110 "danced across the room, saying, “The gentleman’s been here!”"
110 ""
110 "Her mother hastened to explain, smiles breaking from every inch of her"
110 "person. Mrs d’Urberville’s son had called on horseback, having been"
110 "riding by chance in the direction of Marlott. He had wished to know,"
110 "finally, in the name of his mother, if Tess could really come to manage"
110 "the old lady’s fowl-farm or not; the lad who had hitherto superintended"
110 "the birds having proved untrustworthy. “Mr d’Urberville says you must"
110 "be a good girl if you are at all as you appear; he knows you must be"
110 "worth your weight in gold. He is very much interested in ’ee—truth to"
110 "tell.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess seemed for the moment really pleased to hear that she had won such"
110 "high opinion from a stranger when, in her own esteem, she had sunk so"
110 "low."
110 ""
110 "“It is very good of him to think that,” she murmured; “and if I was"
110 "quite sure how it would be living there, I would go any-when.”"
110 ""
110 "“He is a mighty handsome man!”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t think so,” said Tess coldly."
110 ""
110 "“Well, there’s your chance, whether or no; and I’m sure he wears a"
110 "beautiful diamond ring!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes,” said little Abraham, brightly, from the window-bench; “and I"
110 "seed it! and it did twinkle when he put his hand up to his mistarshers."
110 "Mother, why did our grand relation keep on putting his hand up to his"
110 "mistarshers?”"
110 ""
110 "“Hark at that child!” cried Mrs Durbeyfield, with parenthetic"
110 "admiration."
110 ""
110 "“Perhaps to show his diamond ring,” murmured Sir John, dreamily, from"
110 "his chair."
110 ""
110 "“I’ll think it over,” said Tess, leaving the room."
110 ""
110 "“Well, she’s made a conquest o’ the younger branch of us, straight"
110 "off,” continued the matron to her husband, “and she’s a fool if she"
110 "don’t follow it up.”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t quite like my children going away from home,” said the"
110 "haggler. “As the head of the family, the rest ought to come to me.”"
110 ""
110 "“But do let her go, Jacky,” coaxed his poor witless wife. “He’s struck"
110 "wi’ her—you can see that. He called her Coz! He’ll marry her, most"
110 "likely, and make a lady of her; and then she’ll be what her forefathers"
110 "was.”"
110 ""
110 "John Durbeyfield had more conceit than energy or health, and this"
110 "supposition was pleasant to him."
110 ""
110 "“Well, perhaps that’s what young Mr d’Urberville means,” he admitted;"
110 "“and sure enough he mid have serious thoughts about improving his blood"
110 "by linking on to the old line. Tess, the little rogue! And have she"
110 "really paid ’em a visit to such an end as this?”"
110 ""
110 "Meanwhile Tess was walking thoughtfully among the gooseberry-bushes in"
110 "the garden, and over Prince’s grave. When she came in her mother"
110 "pursued her advantage."
110 ""
110 "“Well, what be you going to do?” she asked."
110 ""
110 "“I wish I had seen Mrs d’Urberville,” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "“I think you mid as well settle it. Then you’ll see her soon enough.”"
110 ""
110 "Her father coughed in his chair."
110 ""
110 "“I don’t know what to say!” answered the girl restlessly. “It is for"
110 "you to decide. I killed the old horse, and I suppose I ought to do"
110 "something to get ye a new one. But—but—I don’t quite like Mr"
110 "d’Urberville being there!”"
110 ""
110 "The children, who had made use of this idea of Tess being taken up by"
110 "their wealthy kinsfolk (which they imagined the other family to be) as"
110 "a species of dolorifuge after the death of the horse, began to cry at"
110 "Tess’s reluctance, and teased and reproached her for hesitating."
110 ""
110 "“Tess won’t go-o-o and be made a la-a-dy of!—no, she says she"
110 "wo-o-on’t!” they wailed, with square mouths. “And we shan’t have a nice"
110 "new horse, and lots o’ golden money to buy fairlings! And Tess won’t"
110 "look pretty in her best cloze no mo-o-ore!”"
110 ""
110 "Her mother chimed in to the same tune: a certain way she had of making"
110 "her labours in the house seem heavier than they were by prolonging them"
110 "indefinitely, also weighed in the argument. Her father alone preserved"
110 "an attitude of neutrality."
110 ""
110 "“I will go,” said Tess at last."
110 ""
110 "Her mother could not repress her consciousness of the nuptial vision"
110 "conjured up by the girl’s consent."
110 ""
110 "“That’s right! For such a pretty maid as ’tis, this is a fine chance!”"
110 ""
110 "Tess smiled crossly."
110 ""
110 "“I hope it is a chance for earning money. It is no other kind of"
110 "chance. You had better say nothing of that silly sort about parish.”"
110 ""
110 "Mrs Durbeyfield did not promise. She was not quite sure that she did"
110 "not feel proud enough, after the visitor’s remarks, to say a good deal."
110 ""
110 "Thus it was arranged; and the young girl wrote, agreeing to be ready to"
110 "set out on any day on which she might be required. She was duly"
110 "informed that Mrs d’Urberville was glad of her decision, and that a"
110 "spring-cart should be sent to meet her and her luggage at the top of"
110 "the Vale on the day after the morrow, when she must hold herself"
110 "prepared to start. Mrs d’Urberville’s handwriting seemed rather"
110 "masculine."
110 ""
110 "“A cart?” murmured Joan Durbeyfield doubtingly. “It might have been a"
110 "carriage for her own kin!”"
110 ""
110 "Having at last taken her course Tess was less restless and abstracted,"
110 "going about her business with some self-assurance in the thought of"
110 "acquiring another horse for her father by an occupation which would not"
110 "be onerous. She had hoped to be a teacher at the school, but the fates"
110 "seemed to decide otherwise. Being mentally older than her mother she"
110 "did not regard Mrs Durbeyfield’s matrimonial hopes for her in a serious"
110 "aspect for a moment. The light-minded woman had been discovering good"
110 "matches for her daughter almost from the year of her birth."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER VII>"
110 ""
110 "On the morning appointed for her departure Tess was awake before"
110 "dawn—at the marginal minute of the dark when the grove is still mute,"
110 "save for one prophetic bird who sings with a clear-voiced conviction"
110 "that he at least knows the correct time of day, the rest preserving"
110 "silence as if equally convinced that he is mistaken. She remained"
110 "upstairs packing till breakfast-time, and then came down in her"
110 "ordinary week-day clothes, her Sunday apparel being carefully folded in"
110 "her box."
110 ""
110 "Her mother expostulated. “You will never set out to see your folks"
110 "without dressing up more the dand than that?”"
110 ""
110 "“But I am going to work!” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "“Well, yes,” said Mrs Durbeyfield; and in a private tone, “at first"
110 "there mid be a little pretence o’t.... But I think it will be wiser of"
110 "’ee to put your best side outward,” she added."
110 ""
110 "“Very well; I suppose you know best,” replied Tess with calm"
110 "abandonment."
110 ""
110 "And to please her parent the girl put herself quite in Joan’s hands,"
110 "saying serenely—“Do what you like with me, mother.”"
110 ""
110 "Mrs Durbeyfield was only too delighted at this tractability. First she"
110 "fetched a great basin, and washed Tess’s hair with such thoroughness"
110 "that when dried and brushed it looked twice as much as at other times."
110 "She tied it with a broader pink ribbon than usual. Then she put upon"
110 "her the white frock that Tess had worn at the club-walking, the airy"
110 "fulness of which, supplementing her enlarged _coiffure_, imparted to"
110 "her developing figure an amplitude which belied her age, and might"
110 "cause her to be estimated as a woman when she was not much more than a"
110 "child."
110 ""
110 "“I declare there’s a hole in my stocking-heel!” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "“Never mind holes in your stockings—they don’t speak! When I was a"
110 "maid, so long as I had a pretty bonnet the devil might ha’ found me in"
110 "heels.”"
110 ""
110 "Her mother’s pride in the girl’s appearance led her to step back, like"
110 "a painter from his easel, and survey her work as a whole."
110 ""
110 "“You must zee yourself!” she cried. “It is much better than you was"
110 "t’other day.”"
110 ""
110 "As the looking-glass was only large enough to reflect a very small"
110 "portion of Tess’s person at one time, Mrs Durbeyfield hung a black"
110 "cloak outside the casement, and so made a large reflector of the panes,"
110 "as it is the wont of bedecking cottagers to do. After this she went"
110 "downstairs to her husband, who was sitting in the lower room."
110 ""
110 "“I’ll tell ’ee what ’tis, Durbeyfield,” said she exultingly; “he’ll"
110 "never have the heart not to love her. But whatever you do, don’t zay"
110 "too much to Tess of his fancy for her, and this chance she has got. She"
110 "is such an odd maid that it mid zet her against him, or against going"
110 "there, even now. If all goes well, I shall certainly be for making some"
110 "return to pa’son at Stagfoot Lane for telling us—dear, good man!”"
110 ""
110 "However, as the moment for the girl’s setting out drew nigh, when the"
110 "first excitement of the dressing had passed off, a slight misgiving"
110 "found place in Joan Durbeyfield’s mind. It prompted the matron to say"
110 "that she would walk a little way—as far as to the point where the"
110 "acclivity from the valley began its first steep ascent to the outer"
110 "world. At the top Tess was going to be met with the spring-cart sent by"
110 "the Stoke-d’Urbervilles, and her box had already been wheeled ahead"
110 "towards this summit by a lad with trucks, to be in readiness."
110 ""
110 "Seeing their mother put on her bonnet, the younger children clamoured"
110 "to go with her."
110 ""
110 "“I do want to walk a little-ways wi’ Sissy, now she’s going to marry"
110 "our gentleman-cousin, and wear fine cloze!”"
110 ""
110 "“Now,” said Tess, flushing and turning quickly, “I’ll hear no more o’"
110 "that! Mother, how could you ever put such stuff into their heads?”"
110 ""
110 "“Going to work, my dears, for our rich relation, and help get enough"
110 "money for a new horse,” said Mrs Durbeyfield pacifically."
110 ""
110 "“Goodbye, father,” said Tess, with a lumpy throat."
110 ""
110 "“Goodbye, my maid,” said Sir John, raising his head from his breast as"
110 "he suspended his nap, induced by a slight excess this morning in honour"
110 "of the occasion. “Well, I hope my young friend will like such a comely"
110 "sample of his own blood. And tell’n, Tess, that being sunk, quite, from"
110 "our former grandeur, I’ll sell him the title—yes, sell it—and at no"
110 "onreasonable figure.”"
110 ""
110 "“Not for less than a thousand pound!” cried Lady Durbeyfield."
110 ""
110 "“Tell’n—I’ll take a thousand pound. Well, I’ll take less, when I come"
110 "to think o’t. He’ll adorn it better than a poor lammicken feller like"
110 "myself can. Tell’n he shall hae it for a hundred. But I won’t stand"
110 "upon trifles—tell’n he shall hae it for fifty—for twenty pound! Yes,"
110 "twenty pound—that’s the lowest. Dammy, family honour is family honour,"
110 "and I won’t take a penny less!”"
110 ""
110 "Tess’s eyes were too full and her voice too choked to utter the"
110 "sentiments that were in her. She turned quickly, and went out."
110 ""
110 "So the girls and their mother all walked together, a child on each side"
110 "of Tess, holding her hand and looking at her meditatively from time to"
110 "time, as at one who was about to do great things; her mother just"
110 "behind with the smallest; the group forming a picture of honest beauty"
110 "flanked by innocence, and backed by simple-souled vanity. They followed"
110 "the way till they reached the beginning of the ascent, on the crest of"
110 "which the vehicle from Trantridge was to receive her, this limit having"
110 "been fixed to save the horse the labour of the last slope. Far away"
110 "behind the first hills the cliff-like dwellings of Shaston broke the"
110 "line of the ridge. Nobody was visible in the elevated road which"
110 "skirted the ascent save the lad whom they had sent on before them,"
110 "sitting on the handle of the barrow that contained all Tess’s worldly"
110 "possessions."
110 ""
110 "“Bide here a bit, and the cart will soon come, no doubt,” said Mrs"
110 "Durbeyfield. “Yes, I see it yonder!”"
110 ""
110 "It had come—appearing suddenly from behind the forehead of the nearest"
110 "upland, and stopping beside the boy with the barrow. Her mother and the"
110 "children thereupon decided to go no farther, and bidding them a hasty"
110 "goodbye, Tess bent her steps up the hill."
110 ""
110 "They saw her white shape draw near to the spring-cart, on which her box"
110 "was already placed. But before she had quite reached it another vehicle"
110 "shot out from a clump of trees on the summit, came round the bend of"
110 "the road there, passed the luggage-cart, and halted beside Tess, who"
110 "looked up as if in great surprise."
110 ""
110 "Her mother perceived, for the first time, that the second vehicle was"
110 "not a humble conveyance like the first, but a spick-and-span gig or"
110 "dog-cart, highly varnished and equipped. The driver was a young man of"
110 "three- or four-and-twenty, with a cigar between his teeth; wearing a"
110 "dandy cap, drab jacket, breeches of the same hue, white neckcloth,"
110 "stick-up collar, and brown driving-gloves—in short, he was the"
110 "handsome, horsey young buck who had visited Joan a week or two before"
110 "to get her answer about Tess."
110 ""
110 "Mrs Durbeyfield clapped her hands like a child. Then she looked down,"
110 "then stared again. Could she be deceived as to the meaning of this?"
110 ""
110 "“Is dat the gentleman-kinsman who’ll make Sissy a lady?” asked the"
110 "youngest child."
110 ""
110 "Meanwhile the muslined form of Tess could be seen standing still,"
110 "undecided, beside this turn-out, whose owner was talking to her. Her"
110 "seeming indecision was, in fact, more than indecision: it was"
110 "misgiving. She would have preferred the humble cart. The young man"
110 "dismounted, and appeared to urge her to ascend. She turned her face"
110 "down the hill to her relatives, and regarded the little group."
110 "Something seemed to quicken her to a determination; possibly the"
110 "thought that she had killed Prince. She suddenly stepped up; he mounted"
110 "beside her, and immediately whipped on the horse. In a moment they had"
110 "passed the slow cart with the box, and disappeared behind the shoulder"
110 "of the hill."
110 ""
110 "Directly Tess was out of sight, and the interest of the matter as a"
110 "drama was at an end, the little ones’ eyes filled with tears. The"
110 "youngest child said, “I wish poor, poor Tess wasn’t gone away to be a"
110 "lady!” and, lowering the corners of his lips, burst out crying. The new"
110 "point of view was infectious, and the next child did likewise, and then"
110 "the next, till the whole three of them wailed loud."
110 ""
110 "There were tears also in Joan Durbeyfield’s eyes as she turned to go"
110 "home. But by the time she had got back to the village she was passively"
110 "trusting to the favour of accident. However, in bed that night she"
110 "sighed, and her husband asked her what was the matter."
110 ""
110 "“Oh, I don’t know exactly,” she said. “I was thinking that perhaps it"
110 "would ha’ been better if Tess had not gone.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oughtn’t ye to have thought of that before?”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, ’tis a chance for the maid—Still, if ’twere the doing again, I"
110 "wouldn’t let her go till I had found out whether the gentleman is"
110 "really a good-hearted young man and choice over her as his kinswoman.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, you ought, perhaps, to ha’ done that,” snored Sir John."
110 ""
110 "Joan Durbeyfield always managed to find consolation somewhere: “Well,"
110 "as one of the genuine stock, she ought to make her way with ’en, if she"
110 "plays her trump card aright. And if he don’t marry her afore he will"
110 "after. For that he’s all afire wi’ love for her any eye can see.”"
110 ""
110 "“What’s her trump card? Her d’Urberville blood, you mean?”"
110 ""
110 "“No, stupid; her face—as ’twas mine.”"

110 "<CHAPTER VIII>"
110 ""
110 "Having mounted beside her, Alec d’Urberville drove rapidly along the"
110 "crest of the first hill, chatting compliments to Tess as they went, the"
110 "cart with her box being left far behind. Rising still, an immense"
110 "landscape stretched around them on every side; behind, the green valley"
110 "of her birth, before, a gray country of which she knew nothing except"
110 "from her first brief visit to Trantridge. Thus they reached the verge"
110 "of an incline down which the road stretched in a long straight descent"
110 "of nearly a mile."
110 ""
110 "Ever since the accident with her father’s horse Tess Durbeyfield,"
110 "courageous as she naturally was, had been exceedingly timid on wheels;"
110 "the least irregularity of motion startled her. She began to get uneasy"
110 "at a certain recklessness in her conductor’s driving."
110 ""
110 "“You will go down slow, sir, I suppose?” she said with attempted"
110 "unconcern."
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville looked round upon her, nipped his cigar with the tips of"
110 "his large white centre-teeth, and allowed his lips to smile slowly of"
110 "themselves."
110 ""
110 "“Why, Tess,” he answered, after another whiff or two, “it isn’t a brave"
110 "bouncing girl like you who asks that? Why, I always go down at full"
110 "gallop. There’s nothing like it for raising your spirits.”"
110 ""
110 "“But perhaps you need not now?”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah,” he said, shaking his head, “there are two to be reckoned with. It"
110 "is not me alone. Tib has to be considered, and she has a very queer"
110 "temper.”"
110 ""
110 "“Who?”"
110 ""
110 "“Why, this mare. I fancy she looked round at me in a very grim way just"
110 "then. Didn’t you notice it?”"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t try to frighten me, sir,” said Tess stiffly."
110 ""
110 "“Well, I don’t. If any living man can manage this horse I can: I won’t"
110 "say any living man can do it—but if such has the power, I am he.”"
110 ""
110 "“Why do you have such a horse?”"
110 ""

110 "chap; and just after I bought her she nearly killed me. And then, take"
110 "my word for it, I nearly killed her. But she’s touchy still, very"
110 "touchy; and one’s life is hardly safe behind her sometimes.”"
110 ""
110 "They were just beginning to descend; and it was evident that the horse,"
110 "whether of her own will or of his (the latter being the more likely),"
110 "knew so well the reckless performance expected of her that she hardly"
110 "required a hint from behind."
110 ""
110 "Down, down, they sped, the wheels humming like a top, the dog-cart"
110 "rocking right and left, its axis acquiring a slightly oblique set in"
110 "relation to the line of progress; the figure of the horse rising and"
110 "falling in undulations before them. Sometimes a wheel was off the"
110 "ground, it seemed, for many yards; sometimes a stone was sent spinning"
110 "over the hedge, and flinty sparks from the horse’s hoofs outshone the"
110 "daylight. The aspect of the straight road enlarged with their advance,"
110 "the two banks dividing like a splitting stick; one rushing past at each"
110 "shoulder."
110 ""
110 "The wind blew through Tess’s white muslin to her very skin, and her"
110 "washed hair flew out behind. She was determined to show no open fear,"
110 "but she clutched d’Urberville’s rein-arm."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t touch my arm! We shall be thrown out if you do! Hold on round my"
110 "waist!”"
110 ""
110 "She grasped his waist, and so they reached the bottom."
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Tess—fie! that’s temper!” said d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "“’Tis truth.”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 "woman, stick or stone, in her involuntary hold on him. Recovering her"
110 "reserve, she sat without replying, and thus they reached the summit of"
110 "another declivity."
110 ""
110 "“Now then, again!” said d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "“No, no!” said Tess. “Show more sense, do, please.”"
110 ""
110 "“But when people find themselves on one of the highest points in the"
110 "county, they must get down again,” he retorted."
110 ""

110 "his face to her as they rocked, and said, in playful raillery: “Now"

110 ""
110 "“Never!” said Tess independently, holding on as well as she could"

110 ""

110 ""

110 "Her strategic silence confirmed his suspicion."

110 "A few minutes later the chimneys of The Slopes appeared in view, and in"
110 "a snug nook to the right the poultry-farm and cottage of Tess’s"
110 "destination."

110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER IX>"
110 ""
110 "The community of fowls to which Tess had been appointed as supervisor,"
110 "purveyor, nurse, surgeon, and friend made its headquarters in an old"
110 "thatched cottage standing in an enclosure that had once been a garden,"
110 "but was now a trampled and sanded square. The house was overrun with"
110 "ivy, its chimney being enlarged by the boughs of the parasite to the"
110 "aspect of a ruined tower. The lower rooms were entirely given over to"
110 "the birds, who walked about them with a proprietary air, as though the"
110 "place had been built by themselves, and not by certain dusty"
110 "copyholders who now lay east and west in the churchyard. The"
110 "descendants of these bygone owners felt it almost as a slight to their"
110 "family when the house which had so much of their affection, had cost so"
110 "much of their forefathers’ money, and had been in their possession for"
110 "several generations before the d’Urbervilles came and built here, was"
110 "indifferently turned into a fowl-house by Mrs Stoke-d’Urberville as"
110 "soon as the property fell into hand according to law. “’Twas good"
110 "enough for Christians in grandfather’s time,” they said."
110 ""
110 "The rooms wherein dozens of infants had wailed at their nursing now"
110 "resounded with the tapping of nascent chicks. Distracted hens in coops"
110 "occupied spots where formerly stood chairs supporting sedate"
110 "agriculturists. The chimney-corner and once-blazing hearth was now"
110 "filled with inverted beehives, in which the hens laid their eggs; while"
110 "out of doors the plots that each succeeding householder had carefully"
110 "shaped with his spade were torn by the cocks in wildest fashion."
110 ""
110 "The garden in which the cottage stood was surrounded by a wall, and"
110 "could only be entered through a door."
110 ""
110 "When Tess had occupied herself about an hour the next morning in"
110 "altering and improving the arrangements, according to her skilled ideas"
110 "as the daughter of a professed poulterer, the door in the wall opened"
110 "and a servant in white cap and apron entered. She had come from the"
110 "manor-house."
110 ""
110 "“Mrs d’Urberville wants the fowls as usual,” she said; but perceiving"
110 "that Tess did not quite understand, she explained, “Mis’ess is a old"
110 "lady, and blind.”"
110 ""
110 "“Blind!” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "Almost before her misgiving at the news could find time to shape itself"
110 "she took, under her companion’s direction, two of the most beautiful of"
110 "the Hamburghs in her arms, and followed the maid-servant, who had"
110 "likewise taken two, to the adjacent mansion, which, though ornate and"
110 "imposing, showed traces everywhere on this side that some occupant of"
110 "its chambers could bend to the love of dumb creatures—feathers floating"
110 "within view of the front, and hen-coops standing on the grass."
110 ""
110 "In a sitting-room on the ground-floor, ensconced in an armchair with"
110 "her back to the light, was the owner and mistress of the estate, a"
110 "white-haired woman of not more than sixty, or even less, wearing a"
110 "large cap. She had the mobile face frequent in those whose sight has"
110 "decayed by stages, has been laboriously striven after, and reluctantly"
110 "let go, rather than the stagnant mien apparent in persons long"
110 "sightless or born blind. Tess walked up to this lady with her feathered"
110 "charges—one sitting on each arm."
110 ""
110 "“Ah, you are the young woman come to look after my birds?” said Mrs"
110 "d’Urberville, recognizing a new footstep. “I hope you will be kind to"
110 "them. My bailiff tells me you are quite the proper person. Well, where"
110 "are they? Ah, this is Strut! But he is hardly so lively to-day, is he?"
110 "He is alarmed at being handled by a stranger, I suppose. And Phena"
110 "too—yes, they are a little frightened—aren’t you, dears? But they will"
110 "soon get used to you.”"
110 ""
110 "While the old lady had been speaking Tess and the other maid, in"
110 "obedience to her gestures, had placed the fowls severally in her lap,"
110 "and she had felt them over from head to tail, examining their beaks,"
110 "their combs, the manes of the cocks, their wings, and their claws. Her"
110 "touch enabled her to recognize them in a moment, and to discover if a"
110 "single feather were crippled or draggled. She handled their crops, and"
110 "knew what they had eaten, and if too little or too much; her face"
110 "enacting a vivid pantomime of the criticisms passing in her mind."
110 ""
110 "The birds that the two girls had brought in were duly returned to the"
110 "yard, and the process was repeated till all the pet cocks and hens had"
110 "been submitted to the old woman—Hamburghs, Bantams, Cochins, Brahmas,"
110 "Dorkings, and such other sorts as were in fashion just then—her"
110 "perception of each visitor being seldom at fault as she received the"
110 "bird upon her knees."
110 ""
110 "It reminded Tess of a Confirmation, in which Mrs d’Urberville was the"
110 "bishop, the fowls the young people presented, and herself and the"
110 "maid-servant the parson and curate of the parish bringing them up. At"
110 "the end of the ceremony Mrs d’Urberville abruptly asked Tess, wrinkling"
110 "and twitching her face into undulations, “Can you whistle?”"
110 ""
110 "“Whistle, Ma’am?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, whistle tunes.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess could whistle like most other country-girls, though the"
110 "accomplishment was one which she did not care to profess in genteel"
110 "company. However, she blandly admitted that such was the fact."
110 ""
110 "“Then you will have to practise it every day. I had a lad who did it"
110 "very well, but he has left. I want you to whistle to my bullfinches; as"
110 "I cannot see them, I like to hear them, and we teach ’em airs that way."
110 "Tell her where the cages are, Elizabeth. You must begin to-morrow, or"
110 "they will go back in their piping. They have been neglected these"
110 "several days.”"
110 ""
110 "“Mr d’Urberville whistled to ’em this morning, ma’am,” said Elizabeth."
110 ""
110 "“He! Pooh!”"
110 ""
110 "The old lady’s face creased into furrows of repugnance, and she made no"
110 "further reply."
110 ""
110 "Thus the reception of Tess by her fancied kinswoman terminated, and the"
110 "birds were taken back to their quarters. The girl’s surprise at Mrs"
110 "d’Urberville’s manner was not great; for since seeing the size of the"
110 "house she had expected no more. But she was far from being aware that"
110 "the old lady had never heard a word of the so-called kinship. She"
110 "gathered that no great affection flowed between the blind woman and her"
110 "son. But in that, too, she was mistaken. Mrs d’Urberville was not the"
110 "first mother compelled to love her offspring resentfully, and to be"
110 "bitterly fond."
110 ""
110 "In spite of the unpleasant initiation of the day before, Tess inclined"
110 "to the freedom and novelty of her new position in the morning when the"
110 "sun shone, now that she was once installed there; and she was curious"
110 "to test her powers in the unexpected direction asked of her, so as to"
110 "ascertain her chance of retaining her post. As soon as she was alone"
110 "within the walled garden she sat herself down on a coop, and seriously"
110 "screwed up her mouth for the long-neglected practice. She found her"
110 "former ability to have degenerated to the production of a hollow rush"
110 "of wind through the lips, and no clear note at all."
110 ""
110 "She remained fruitlessly blowing and blowing, wondering how she could"
110 "have so grown out of the art which had come by nature, till she became"
110 "aware of a movement among the ivy-boughs which cloaked the garden-wall"
110 "no less then the cottage. Looking that way she beheld a form springing"
110 "from the coping to the plot. It was Alec d’Urberville, whom she had not"
110 "set eyes on since he had conducted her the day before to the door of"
110 "the gardener’s cottage where she had lodgings."
110 ""
110 "“Upon my honour!” cried he, “there was never before such a beautiful"
110 "thing in Nature or Art as you look, ‘Cousin’ Tess (‘Cousin’ had a faint"
110 "ring of mockery). I have been watching you from over the wall—sitting"
110 "like _Im_-patience on a monument, and pouting up that pretty red mouth"
110 "to whistling shape, and whooing and whooing, and privately swearing,"
110 "and never being able to produce a note. Why, you are quite cross"
110 "because you can’t do it.”"
110 ""
110 "“I may be cross, but I didn’t swear.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah! I understand why you are trying—those bullies! My mother wants you"
110 "to carry on their musical education. How selfish of her! As if"
110 "attending to these curst cocks and hens here were not enough work for"
110 "any girl. I would flatly refuse, if I were you.”"
110 ""
110 "“But she wants me particularly to do it, and to be ready by to-morrow"
110 "morning.”"
110 ""
110 "“Does she? Well then—I’ll give you a lesson or two.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh no, you won’t!” said Tess, withdrawing towards the door."
110 ""
110 "“Nonsense; I don’t want to touch you. See—I’ll stand on this side of"
110 "the wire-netting, and you can keep on the other; so you may feel quite"
110 "safe. Now, look here; you screw up your lips too harshly. There"
110 "’tis—so.”"
110 ""
110 "He suited the action to the word, and whistled a line of “Take, O take"
110 "those lips away.” But the allusion was lost upon Tess."
110 ""
110 "“Now try,” said d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "She attempted to look reserved; her face put on a sculptural severity."
110 "But he persisted in his demand, and at last, to get rid of him, she did"
110 "put up her lips as directed for producing a clear note; laughing"
110 "distressfully, however, and then blushing with vexation that she had"
110 "laughed."
110 ""
110 "He encouraged her with “Try again!”"
110 ""
110 "Tess was quite serious, painfully serious by this time; and she"
110 "tried—ultimately and unexpectedly emitting a real round sound. The"
110 "momentary pleasure of success got the better of her; her eyes enlarged,"
110 "and she involuntarily smiled in his face."
110 ""
110 "“That’s it! Now I have started you—you’ll go on beautifully. There—I"
110 "said I would not come near you; and, in spite of such temptation as"
110 "never before fell to mortal man, I’ll keep my word.... Tess, do you"
110 "think my mother a queer old soul?”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t know much of her yet, sir.”"
110 ""
110 "“You’ll find her so; she must be, to make you learn to whistle to her"
110 "bullfinches. I am rather out of her books just now, but you will be"
110 "quite in favour if you treat her live-stock well. Good morning. If you"
110 "meet with any difficulties and want help here, don’t go to the bailiff,"
110 "come to me.”"
110 ""
110 "It was in the economy of this _régime_ that Tess Durbeyfield had"
110 "undertaken to fill a place. Her first day’s experiences were fairly"
110 "typical of those which followed through many succeeding days. A"
110 "familiarity with Alec d’Urberville’s presence—which that young man"
110 "carefully cultivated in her by playful dialogue, and by jestingly"
110 "calling her his cousin when they were alone—removed much of her"
110 "original shyness of him, without, however, implanting any feeling which"
110 "could engender shyness of a new and tenderer kind. But she was more"
110 "pliable under his hands than a mere companionship would have made her,"
110 "owing to her unavoidable dependence upon his mother, and, through that"
110 "lady’s comparative helplessness, upon him."
110 ""
110 "She soon found that whistling to the bullfinches in Mrs d’Urberville’s"
110 "room was no such onerous business when she had regained the art, for"
110 "she had caught from her musical mother numerous airs that suited those"
110 "songsters admirably. A far more satisfactory time than when she"
110 "practised in the garden was this whistling by the cages each morning."
110 "Unrestrained by the young man’s presence she threw up her mouth, put"
110 "her lips near the bars, and piped away in easeful grace to the"
110 "attentive listeners."
110 ""
110 "Mrs d’Urberville slept in a large four-post bedstead hung with heavy"
110 "damask curtains, and the bullfinches occupied the same apartment, where"
110 "they flitted about freely at certain hours, and made little white spots"
110 "on the furniture and upholstery. Once while Tess was at the window"
110 "where the cages were ranged, giving her lesson as usual, she thought"
110 "she heard a rustling behind the bed. The old lady was not present, and"
110 "turning round the girl had an impression that the toes of a pair of"
110 "boots were visible below the fringe of the curtains. Thereupon her"
110 "whistling became so disjointed that the listener, if such there were,"
110 "must have discovered her suspicion of his presence. She searched the"
110 "curtains every morning after that, but never found anybody within them."
110 "Alec d’Urberville had evidently thought better of his freak to terrify"
110 "her by an ambush of that kind."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER X>"
110 ""
110 "Every village has its idiosyncrasy, its constitution, often its own"
110 "code of morality. The levity of some of the younger women in and about"
110 "Trantridge was marked, and was perhaps symptomatic of the choice spirit"
110 "who ruled The Slopes in that vicinity. The place had also a more"
110 "abiding defect; it drank hard. The staple conversation on the farms"
110 "around was on the uselessness of saving money; and smock-frocked"
110 "arithmeticians, leaning on their ploughs or hoes, would enter into"
110 "calculations of great nicety to prove that parish relief was a fuller"
110 "provision for a man in his old age than any which could result from"
110 "savings out of their wages during a whole lifetime."
110 ""
110 "The chief pleasure of these philosophers lay in going every Saturday"
110 "night, when work was done, to Chaseborough, a decayed market-town two"
110 "or three miles distant; and, returning in the small hours of the next"
110 "morning, to spend Sunday in sleeping off the dyspeptic effects of the"
110 "curious compounds sold to them as beer by the monopolizers of the"
110 "once-independent inns."
110 ""
110 "For a long time Tess did not join in the weekly pilgrimages. But under"
110 "pressure from matrons not much older than herself—for a field-man’s"
110 "wages being as high at twenty-one as at forty, marriage was early"
110 "here—Tess at length consented to go. Her first experience of the"
110 "journey afforded her more enjoyment than she had expected, the"
110 "hilariousness of the others being quite contagious after her monotonous"
110 "attention to the poultry-farm all the week. She went again and again."
110 "Being graceful and interesting, standing moreover on the momentary"
110 "threshold of womanhood, her appearance drew down upon her some sly"
110 "regards from loungers in the streets of Chaseborough; hence, though"
110 "sometimes her journey to the town was made independently, she always"
110 "searched for her fellows at nightfall, to have the protection of their"
110 "companionship homeward."
110 ""
110 "This had gone on for a month or two when there came a Saturday in"
110 "September, on which a fair and a market coincided; and the pilgrims"
110 "from Trantridge sought double delights at the inns on that account."
110 "Tess’s occupations made her late in setting out, so that her comrades"
110 "reached the town long before her. It was a fine September evening, just"
110 "before sunset, when yellow lights struggle with blue shades in hairlike"
110 "lines, and the atmosphere itself forms a prospect without aid from more"
110 "solid objects, except the innumerable winged insects that dance in it."
110 "Through this low-lit mistiness Tess walked leisurely along."
110 ""
110 "She did not discover the coincidence of the market with the fair till"
110 "she had reached the place, by which time it was close upon dusk. Her"
110 "limited marketing was soon completed; and then as usual she began to"
110 "look about for some of the Trantridge cottagers."
110 ""
110 "At first she could not find them, and she was informed that most of"
110 "them had gone to what they called a private little jig at the house of"
110 "a hay-trusser and peat-dealer who had transactions with their farm. He"
110 "lived in an out-of-the-way nook of the townlet, and in trying to find"
110 "her course thither her eyes fell upon Mr d’Urberville standing at a"
110 "street corner."
110 ""
110 "“What—my Beauty? You here so late?” he said."
110 ""
110 "She told him that she was simply waiting for company homeward."
110 ""
110 "“I’ll see you again,” said he over her shoulder as she went on down the"
110 "back lane."
110 ""
110 "Approaching the hay-trussers, she could hear the fiddled notes of a"
110 "reel proceeding from some building in the rear; but no sound of dancing"
110 "was audible—an exceptional state of things for these parts, where as a"
110 "rule the stamping drowned the music. The front door being open she"
110 "could see straight through the house into the garden at the back as far"
110 "as the shades of night would allow; and nobody appearing to her knock,"
110 "she traversed the dwelling and went up the path to the outhouse whence"
110 "the sound had attracted her."
110 ""
110 "It was a windowless erection used for storage, and from the open door"
110 "there floated into the obscurity a mist of yellow radiance, which at"
110 "first Tess thought to be illuminated smoke. But on drawing nearer she"
110 "perceived that it was a cloud of dust, lit by candles within the"
110 "outhouse, whose beams upon the haze carried forward the outline of the"
110 "doorway into the wide night of the garden."
110 ""
110 "When she came close and looked in she beheld indistinct forms racing up"
110 "and down to the figure of the dance, the silence of their footfalls"
110 "arising from their being overshoe in “scroff”—that is to say, the"
110 "powdery residuum from the storage of peat and other products, the"
110 "stirring of which by their turbulent feet created the nebulosity that"
110 "involved the scene. Through this floating, fusty _débris_ of peat and"
110 "hay, mixed with the perspirations and warmth of the dancers, and"
110 "forming together a sort of vegeto-human pollen, the muted fiddles"
110 "feebly pushed their notes, in marked contrast to the spirit with which"
110 "the measure was trodden out. They coughed as they danced, and laughed"
110 "as they coughed. Of the rushing couples there could barely be discerned"
110 "more than the high lights—the indistinctness shaping them to satyrs"
110 "clasping nymphs—a multiplicity of Pans whirling a multiplicity of"
110 "Syrinxes; Lotis attempting to elude Priapus, and always failing."
110 ""
110 "At intervals a couple would approach the doorway for air, and the haze"
110 "no longer veiling their features, the demigods resolved themselves into"
110 "the homely personalities of her own next-door neighbours. Could"
110 "Trantridge in two or three short hours have metamorphosed itself thus"
110 "madly!"
110 ""
110 "Some Sileni of the throng sat on benches and hay-trusses by the wall;"
110 "and one of them recognized her."
110 ""
110 "“The maids don’t think it respectable to dance at The Flower-de-Luce,”"
110 "he explained. “They don’t like to let everybody see which be their"
110 "fancy-men. Besides, the house sometimes shuts up just when their jints"
110 "begin to get greased. So we come here and send out for liquor.”"
110 ""
110 "“But when be any of you going home?” asked Tess with some anxiety."
110 ""
110 "“Now—a’most directly. This is all but the last jig.”"
110 ""
110 "She waited. The reel drew to a close, and some of the party were in the"
110 "mind of starting. But others would not, and another dance was formed."
110 "This surely would end it, thought Tess. But it merged in yet another."
110 "She became restless and uneasy; yet, having waited so long, it was"
110 "necessary to wait longer; on account of the fair the roads were dotted"
110 "with roving characters of possibly ill intent; and, though not fearful"
110 "of measurable dangers, she feared the unknown. Had she been near"
110 "Marlott she would have had less dread."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t ye be nervous, my dear good soul,” expostulated, between his"
110 "coughs, a young man with a wet face and his straw hat so far back upon"
110 "his head that the brim encircled it like the nimbus of a saint. “What’s"
110 "yer hurry? To-morrow is Sunday, thank God, and we can sleep it off in"
110 "church-time. Now, have a turn with me?”"
110 ""
110 "She did not abhor dancing, but she was not going to dance here. The"
110 "movement grew more passionate: the fiddlers behind the luminous pillar"
110 "of cloud now and then varied the air by playing on the wrong side of"
110 "the bridge or with the back of the bow. But it did not matter; the"
110 "panting shapes spun onwards."
110 ""
110 "They did not vary their partners if their inclination were to stick to"
110 "previous ones. Changing partners simply meant that a satisfactory"
110 "choice had not as yet been arrived at by one or other of the pair, and"
110 "by this time every couple had been suitably matched. It was then that"
110 "the ecstasy and the dream began, in which emotion was the matter of the"
110 "universe, and matter but an adventitious intrusion likely to hinder you"
110 "from spinning where you wanted to spin."
110 ""
110 "Suddenly there was a dull thump on the ground: a couple had fallen, and"
110 "lay in a mixed heap. The next couple, unable to check its progress,"
110 "came toppling over the obstacle. An inner cloud of dust rose around the"
110 "prostrate figures amid the general one of the room, in which a"
110 "twitching entanglement of arms and legs was discernible."
110 ""
110 "“You shall catch it for this, my gentleman, when you get home!” burst"
110 "in female accents from the human heap—those of the unhappy partner of"
110 "the man whose clumsiness had caused the mishap; she happened also to be"
110 "his recently married wife, in which assortment there was nothing"
110 "unusual at Trantridge as long as any affection remained between wedded"
110 "couples; and, indeed, it was not uncustomary in their later lives, to"
110 "avoid making odd lots of the single people between whom there might be"
110 "a warm understanding."
110 ""
110 "A loud laugh from behind Tess’s back, in the shade of the garden,"
110 "united with the titter within the room. She looked round, and saw the"
110 "red coal of a cigar: Alec d’Urberville was standing there alone. He"
110 "beckoned to her, and she reluctantly retreated towards him."
110 ""
110 "“Well, my Beauty, what are you doing here?”"
110 ""
110 "She was so tired after her long day and her walk that she confided her"
110 "trouble to him—that she had been waiting ever since he saw her to have"
110 "their company home, because the road at night was strange to her. “But"
110 "it seems they will never leave off, and I really think I will wait no"
110 "longer.”"
110 ""
110 "“Certainly do not. I have only a saddle-horse here to-day; but come to"
110 "The Flower-de-Luce, and I’ll hire a trap, and drive you home with me.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess, though flattered, had never quite got over her original mistrust"
110 "of him, and, despite their tardiness, she preferred to walk home with"
110 "the work-folk. So she answered that she was much obliged to him, but"
110 "would not trouble him. “I have said that I will wait for ’em, and they"
110 "will expect me to now.”"
110 ""
110 "“Very well, Miss Independence. Please yourself... Then I shall not"
110 "hurry... My good Lord, what a kick-up they are having there!”"
110 ""
110 "He had not put himself forward into the light, but some of them had"
110 "perceived him, and his presence led to a slight pause and a"
110 "consideration of how the time was flying. As soon as he had re-lit a"
110 "cigar and walked away the Trantridge people began to collect themselves"
110 "from amid those who had come in from other farms, and prepared to leave"

110 "later, when the clock-chime sounded a quarter past eleven, they were"
110 "straggling along the lane which led up the hill towards their homes."
110 ""
110 "It was a three-mile walk, along a dry white road, made whiter to-night"
110 "by the light of the moon."
110 ""
110 "Tess soon perceived as she walked in the flock, sometimes with this"
110 "one, sometimes with that, that the fresh night air was producing"
110 "staggerings and serpentine courses among the men who had partaken too"
110 "freely; some of the more careless women also were wandering in their"
110 "gait—to wit, a dark virago, Car Darch, dubbed Queen of Spades, till"
110 "lately a favourite of d’Urberville’s; Nancy, her sister, nicknamed the"
110 "Queen of Diamonds; and the young married woman who had already tumbled"
110 "down. Yet however terrestrial and lumpy their appearance just now to"
110 "the mean unglamoured eye, to themselves the case was different. They"
110 "followed the road with a sensation that they were soaring along in a"
110 "supporting medium, possessed of original and profound thoughts,"
110 "themselves and surrounding nature forming an organism of which all the"
110 "parts harmoniously and joyously interpenetrated each other. They were"
110 "as sublime as the moon and stars above them, and the moon and stars"
110 "were as ardent as they."
110 ""
110 "Tess, however, had undergone such painful experiences of this kind in"
110 "her father’s house that the discovery of their condition spoilt the"
110 "pleasure she was beginning to feel in the moonlight journey. Yet she"
110 "stuck to the party, for reasons above given."
110 ""
110 "In the open highway they had progressed in scattered order; but now"
110 "their route was through a field-gate, and the foremost finding a"
110 "difficulty in opening it, they closed up together."
110 ""
110 "This leading pedestrian was Car the Queen of Spades, who carried a"
110 "wicker-basket containing her mother’s groceries, her own draperies, and"
110 "other purchases for the week. The basket being large and heavy, Car had"
110 "placed it for convenience of porterage on the top of her head, where it"
110 "rode on in jeopardized balance as she walked with arms akimbo."
110 ""
110 "“Well—whatever is that a-creeping down thy back, Car Darch?” said one"
110 "of the group suddenly."
110 ""
110 "All looked at Car. Her gown was a light cotton print, and from the back"
110 "of her head a kind of rope could be seen descending to some distance"
110 "below her waist, like a Chinaman’s queue."
110 ""
110 "“’Tis her hair falling down,” said another."
110 ""
110 "No; it was not her hair: it was a black stream of something oozing from"
110 "her basket, and it glistened like a slimy snake in the cold still rays"
110 "of the moon."
110 ""
110 "“’Tis treacle,” said an observant matron."
110 ""
110 "Treacle it was. Car’s poor old grandmother had a weakness for the sweet"
110 "stuff. Honey she had in plenty out of her own hives, but treacle was"
110 "what her soul desired, and Car had been about to give her a treat of"
110 "surprise. Hastily lowering the basket the dark girl found that the"
110 "vessel containing the syrup had been smashed within."
110 ""
110 "By this time there had arisen a shout of laughter at the extraordinary"
110 "appearance of Car’s back, which irritated the dark queen into getting"
110 "rid of the disfigurement by the first sudden means available, and"
110 "independently of the help of the scoffers. She rushed excitedly into"
110 "the field they were about to cross, and flinging herself flat on her"

110 "spinning horizontally on the herbage and dragging herself over it upon"
110 "her elbows."
110 ""
110 "The laughter rang louder; they clung to the gate, to the posts, rested"
110 "on their staves, in the weakness engendered by their convulsions at the"
110 "spectacle of Car. Our heroine, who had hitherto held her peace, at this"
110 "wild moment could not help joining in with the rest."
110 ""
110 "It was a misfortune—in more ways than one. No sooner did the dark queen"
110 "hear the soberer richer note of Tess among those of the other"
110 "work-people than a long-smouldering sense of rivalry inflamed her to"
110 "madness. She sprang to her feet and closely faced the object of her"
110 "dislike."
110 ""
110 "“How darest th’ laugh at me, hussy!” she cried."
110 ""
110 "“I couldn’t really help it when t’others did,” apologized Tess, still"
110 "tittering."
110 ""
110 "“Ah, th’st think th’ beest everybody, dostn’t, because th’ beest first"
110 "favourite with He just now! But stop a bit, my lady, stop a bit! I’m as"
110 "good as two of such! Look here—here’s at ’ee!”"
110 ""
110 "To Tess’s horror the dark queen began stripping off the bodice of her"
110 "gown—which for the added reason of its ridiculed condition she was only"

110 "and arms to the moonshine, under which they looked as luminous and"
