110 ""
110 "“Indeed, then, I shall not fight!” said the latter majestically; “and"
110 "if I had known you was of that sort, I wouldn’t have so let myself down"
110 "as to come with such a whorage as this is!”"
110 ""
110 "The rather too inclusive speech brought down a torrent of vituperation"
110 "from other quarters upon fair Tess’s unlucky head, particularly from"
110 "the Queen of Diamonds, who having stood in the relations to"
110 "d’Urberville that Car had also been suspected of, united with the"

110 "with an animus which none of them would have been so fatuous as to show"

110 "unfairly browbeaten, the husbands and lovers tried to make peace by"
110 "defending her; but the result of that attempt was directly to increase"
110 "the war."
110 ""
110 "Tess was indignant and ashamed. She no longer minded the loneliness of"
110 "the way and the lateness of the hour; her one object was to get away"

110 "better among them would repent of their passion next day. They were all"
110 "now inside the field, and she was edging back to rush off alone when a"
110 "horseman emerged almost silently from the corner of the hedge that"
110 "screened the road, and Alec d’Urberville looked round upon them."
110 ""
110 "“What the devil is all this row about, work-folk?” he asked."
110 ""
110 "The explanation was not readily forthcoming; and, in truth, he did not"
110 "require any. Having heard their voices while yet some way off he had"
110 "ridden creepingly forward, and learnt enough to satisfy himself."
110 ""
110 "Tess was standing apart from the rest, near the gate. He bent over"
110 "towards her. “Jump up behind me,” he whispered, “and we’ll get shot of"
110 "the screaming cats in a jiffy!”"
110 ""
110 "She felt almost ready to faint, so vivid was her sense of the crisis."
110 "At almost any other moment of her life she would have refused such"
110 "proffered aid and company, as she had refused them several times"
110 "before; and now the loneliness would not of itself have forced her to"
110 "do otherwise. But coming as the invitation did at the particular"
110 "juncture when fear and indignation at these adversaries could be"
110 "transformed by a spring of the foot into a triumph over them, she"
110 "abandoned herself to her impulse, climbed the gate, put her toe upon"
110 "his instep, and scrambled into the saddle behind him. The pair were"
110 "speeding away into the distant gray by the time that the contentious"
110 "revellers became aware of what had happened."
110 ""
110 "The Queen of Spades forgot the stain on her bodice, and stood beside"
110 "the Queen of Diamonds and the new-married, staggering young woman—all"
110 "with a gaze of fixity in the direction in which the horse’s tramp was"
110 "diminishing into silence on the road."
110 ""
110 "“What be ye looking at?” asked a man who had not observed the incident."
110 ""
110 "“Ho-ho-ho!” laughed dark Car."
110 ""
110 "“Hee-hee-hee!” laughed the tippling bride, as she steadied herself on"
110 "the arm of her fond husband."
110 ""
110 "“Heu-heu-heu!” laughed dark Car’s mother, stroking her moustache as she"
110 "explained laconically: “Out of the frying-pan into the fire!”"
110 ""
110 "Then these children of the open air, whom even excess of alcohol could"
110 "scarce injure permanently, betook themselves to the field-path; and as"
110 "they went there moved onward with them, around the shadow of each one’s"
110 "head, a circle of opalized light, formed by the moon’s rays upon the"
110 "glistening sheet of dew. Each pedestrian could see no halo but his or"
110 "her own, which never deserted the head-shadow, whatever its vulgar"
110 "unsteadiness might be; but adhered to it, and persistently beautified"
110 "it; till the erratic motions seemed an inherent part of the"
110 "irradiation, and the fumes of their breathing a component of the"
110 "night’s mist; and the spirit of the scene, and of the moonlight, and of"
110 "Nature, seemed harmoniously to mingle with the spirit of wine."

110 "<CHAPTER XI>"
110 ""
110 "The twain cantered along for some time without speech, Tess as she"
110 "clung to him still panting in her triumph, yet in other respects"
110 "dubious. She had perceived that the horse was not the spirited one he"
110 "sometimes rose, and felt no alarm on that score, though her seat was"
110 "precarious enough despite her tight hold of him. She begged him to slow"
110 "the animal to a walk, which Alec accordingly did."
110 ""
110 "“Neatly done, was it not, dear Tess?” he said by and by."
110 ""
110 "“Yes!” said she. “I am sure I ought to be much obliged to you.”"
110 ""
110 "“And are you?”"
110 ""
110 "She did not reply."
110 ""
110 "“Tess, why do you always dislike my kissing you?”"
110 ""
110 "“I suppose—because I don’t love you.”"
110 ""
110 "“You are quite sure?”"
110 ""
110 "“I am angry with you sometimes!”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah, I half feared as much.” Nevertheless, Alec did not object to that"
110 "confession. He knew that anything was better then frigidity. “Why"
110 "haven’t you told me when I have made you angry?”"
110 ""
110 "“You know very well why. Because I cannot help myself here.”"
110 ""
110 "“I haven’t offended you often by love-making?”"
110 ""
110 "“You have sometimes.”"
110 ""
110 "“How many times?”"
110 ""
110 "“You know as well as I—too many times.”"
110 ""
110 "“Every time I have tried?”"
110 ""
110 "She was silent, and the horse ambled along for a considerable distance,"
110 "till a faint luminous fog, which had hung in the hollows all the"
110 "evening, became general and enveloped them. It seemed to hold the"
110 "moonlight in suspension, rendering it more pervasive than in clear air."
110 "Whether on this account, or from absent-mindedness, or from sleepiness,"
110 "she did not perceive that they had long ago passed the point at which"
110 "the lane to Trantridge branched from the highway, and that her"
110 "conductor had not taken the Trantridge track."
110 ""
110 "She was inexpressibly weary. She had risen at five o’clock every"
110 "morning of that week, had been on foot the whole of each day, and on"
110 "this evening had in addition walked the three miles to Chaseborough,"
110 "waited three hours for her neighbours without eating or drinking, her"
110 "impatience to start them preventing either; she had then walked a mile"
110 "of the way home, and had undergone the excitement of the quarrel, till,"
110 "with the slow progress of their steed, it was now nearly one o’clock."
110 "Only once, however, was she overcome by actual drowsiness. In that"
110 "moment of oblivion her head sank gently against him."
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville stopped the horse, withdrew his feet from the stirrups,"
110 "turned sideways on the saddle, and enclosed her waist with his arm to"
110 "support her."
110 ""
110 "This immediately put her on the defensive, and with one of those sudden"
110 "impulses of reprisal to which she was liable she gave him a little push"
110 "from her. In his ticklish position he nearly lost his balance and only"
110 "just avoided rolling over into the road, the horse, though a powerful"
110 "one, being fortunately the quietest he rode."
110 ""
110 "“That is devilish unkind!” he said. “I mean no harm—only to keep you"
110 "from falling.”"
110 ""
110 "She pondered suspiciously, till, thinking that this might after all be"
110 "true, she relented, and said quite humbly, “I beg your pardon, sir.”"
110 ""
110 "“I won’t pardon you unless you show some confidence in me. Good God!”"
110 "he burst out, “what am I, to be repulsed so by a mere chit like you?"
110 "For near three mortal months have you trifled with my feelings, eluded"
110 "me, and snubbed me; and I won’t stand it!”"
110 ""
110 "“I’ll leave you to-morrow, sir.”"
110 ""
110 "“No, you will not leave me to-morrow! Will you, I ask once more, show"
110 "your belief in me by letting me clasp you with my arm? Come, between us"
110 "two and nobody else, now. We know each other well; and you know that I"
110 "love you, and think you the prettiest girl in the world, which you are."
110 "Mayn’t I treat you as a lover?”"
110 ""
110 "She drew a quick pettish breath of objection, writhing uneasily on her"
110 "seat, looked far ahead, and murmured, “I don’t know—I wish—how can I"
110 "say yes or no when—”"
110 ""
110 "He settled the matter by clasping his arm round her as he desired, and"
110 "Tess expressed no further negative. Thus they sidled slowly onward till"
110 "it struck her they had been advancing for an unconscionable time—far"
110 "longer than was usually occupied by the short journey from"
110 "Chaseborough, even at this walking pace, and that they were no longer"
110 "on hard road, but in a mere trackway."
110 ""
110 "“Why, where be we?” she exclaimed."
110 ""
110 "“Passing by a wood.”"
110 ""
110 "“A wood—what wood? Surely we are quite out of the road?”"
110 ""
110 "“A bit of The Chase—the oldest wood in England. It is a lovely night,"
110 "and why should we not prolong our ride a little?”"
110 ""
110 "“How could you be so treacherous!” said Tess, between archness and real"
110 "dismay, and getting rid of his arm by pulling open his fingers one by"
110 "one, though at the risk of slipping off herself. “Just when I’ve been"
110 "putting such trust in you, and obliging you to please you, because I"
110 "thought I had wronged you by that push! Please set me down, and let me"
110 "walk home.”"
110 ""
110 "“You cannot walk home, darling, even if the air were clear. We are"
110 "miles away from Trantridge, if I must tell you, and in this growing fog"
110 "you might wander for hours among these trees.”"
110 ""
110 "“Never mind that,” she coaxed. “Put me down, I beg you. I don’t mind"
110 "where it is; only let me get down, sir, please!”"
110 ""
110 "“Very well, then, I will—on one condition. Having brought you here to"
110 "this out-of-the-way place, I feel myself responsible for your"
110 "safe-conduct home, whatever you may yourself feel about it. As to your"
110 "getting to Trantridge without assistance, it is quite impossible; for,"
110 "to tell the truth, dear, owing to this fog, which so disguises"
110 "everything, I don’t quite know where we are myself. Now, if you will"
110 "promise to wait beside the horse while I walk through the bushes till I"
110 "come to some road or house, and ascertain exactly our whereabouts, I’ll"
110 "deposit you here willingly. When I come back I’ll give you full"
110 "directions, and if you insist upon walking you may; or you may ride—at"
110 "your pleasure.”"
110 ""
110 "She accepted these terms, and slid off on the near side, though not"
110 "till he had stolen a cursory kiss. He sprang down on the other side."
110 ""
110 "“I suppose I must hold the horse?” said she."
110 ""
110 "“Oh no; it’s not necessary,” replied Alec, patting the panting"
110 "creature. “He’s had enough of it for to-night.”"
110 ""
110 "He turned the horse’s head into the bushes, hitched him on to a bough,"
110 "and made a sort of couch or nest for her in the deep mass of dead"
110 "leaves."
110 ""
110 "“Now, you sit there,” he said. “The leaves have not got damp as yet."
110 "Just give an eye to the horse—it will be quite sufficient.”"
110 ""
110 "He took a few steps away from her, but, returning, said, “By the bye,"
110 "Tess, your father has a new cob to-day. Somebody gave it to him.”"
110 ""
110 "“Somebody? You!”"
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville nodded."
110 ""
110 "“O how very good of you that is!” she exclaimed, with a painful sense"
110 "of the awkwardness of having to thank him just then."
110 ""
110 "“And the children have some toys.”"
110 ""
110 "“I didn’t know—you ever sent them anything!” she murmured, much moved."
110 "“I almost wish you had not—yes, I almost wish it!”"
110 ""
110 "“Why, dear?”"
110 ""
110 "“It—hampers me so.”"
110 ""
110 "“Tessy—don’t you love me ever so little now?”"
110 ""

110 "sudden vision of his passion for herself as a factor in this result so"
110 "distressed her that, beginning with one slow tear, and then following"
110 "with another, she wept outright."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t cry, dear, dear one! Now sit down here, and wait till I come.”"
110 "She passively sat down amid the leaves he had heaped, and shivered"
110 "slightly. “Are you cold?” he asked."
110 ""
110 "“Not very—a little.”"
110 ""
110 "He touched her with his fingers, which sank into her as into down. “You"
110 "have only that puffy muslin dress on—how’s that?”"
110 ""
110 "“It’s my best summer one. ’Twas very warm when I started, and I didn’t"
110 "know I was going to ride, and that it would be night.”"
110 ""
110 "“Nights grow chilly in September. Let me see.” He pulled off a light"
110 "overcoat that he had worn, and put it round her tenderly. “That’s"
110 "it—now you’ll feel warmer,” he continued. “Now, my pretty, rest there;"
110 "I shall soon be back again.”"
110 ""
110 "Having buttoned the overcoat round her shoulders he plunged into the"
110 "webs of vapour which by this time formed veils between the trees. She"
110 "could hear the rustling of the branches as he ascended the adjoining"
110 "slope, till his movements were no louder than the hopping of a bird,"
110 "and finally died away. With the setting of the moon the pale light"
110 "lessened, and Tess became invisible as she fell into reverie upon the"
110 "leaves where he had left her."
110 ""
110 "In the meantime Alec d’Urberville had pushed on up the slope to clear"
110 "his genuine doubt as to the quarter of The Chase they were in. He had,"
110 "in fact, ridden quite at random for over an hour, taking any turning"
110 "that came to hand in order to prolong companionship with her, and"
110 "giving far more attention to Tess’s moonlit person than to any wayside"
110 "object. A little rest for the jaded animal being desirable, he did not"
110 "hasten his search for landmarks. A clamber over the hill into the"
110 "adjoining vale brought him to the fence of a highway whose contours he"
110 "recognized, which settled the question of their whereabouts."
110 "D’Urberville thereupon turned back; but by this time the moon had quite"
110 "gone down, and partly on account of the fog The Chase was wrapped in"
110 "thick darkness, although morning was not far off. He was obliged to"
110 "advance with outstretched hands to avoid contact with the boughs, and"
110 "discovered that to hit the exact spot from which he had started was at"
110 "first entirely beyond him. Roaming up and down, round and round, he at"
110 "length heard a slight movement of the horse close at hand; and the"
110 "sleeve of his overcoat unexpectedly caught his foot."
110 ""
110 "“Tess!” said d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "There was no answer. The obscurity was now so great that he could see"
110 "absolutely nothing but a pale nebulousness at his feet, which"
110 "represented the white muslin figure he had left upon the dead leaves."
110 "Everything else was blackness alike. D’Urberville stooped; and heard a"
110 "gentle regular breathing. He knelt and bent lower, till her breath"
110 "warmed his face, and in a moment his cheek was in contact with hers."
110 "She was sleeping soundly, and upon her eyelashes there lingered tears."
110 ""
110 "Darkness and silence ruled everywhere around. Above them rose the"
110 "primaeval yews and oaks of The Chase, in which there poised gentle"
110 "roosting birds in their last nap; and about them stole the hopping"
110 "rabbits and hares. But, might some say, where was Tess’s guardian"
110 "angel? where was the providence of her simple faith? Perhaps, like that"
110 "other god of whom the ironical Tishbite spoke, he was talking, or he"
110 "was pursuing, or he was in a journey, or he was sleeping and not to be"
110 "awaked."
110 ""
110 "Why it was that upon this beautiful feminine tissue, sensitive as"
110 "gossamer, and practically blank as snow as yet, there should have been"
110 "traced such a coarse pattern as it was doomed to receive; why so often"
110 "the coarse appropriates the finer thus, the wrong man the woman, the"
110 "wrong woman the man, many thousand years of analytical philosophy have"
110 "failed to explain to our sense of order. One may, indeed, admit the"
110 "possibility of a retribution lurking in the present catastrophe."
110 "Doubtless some of Tess d’Urberville’s mailed ancestors rollicking home"
110 "from a fray had dealt the same measure even more ruthlessly towards"
110 "peasant girls of their time. But though to visit the sins of the"
110 "fathers upon the children may be a morality good enough for divinities,"
110 "it is scorned by average human nature; and it therefore does not mend"
110 "the matter."
110 ""
110 "As Tess’s own people down in those retreats are never tired of saying"
110 "among each other in their fatalistic way: “It was to be.” There lay the"
110 "pity of it. An immeasurable social chasm was to divide our heroine’s"
110 "personality thereafter from that previous self of hers who stepped from"
110 "her mother’s door to try her fortune at Trantridge poultry-farm."
110 ""
110 ""

110 "<CHAPTER XII>"
110 ""
110 "The basket was heavy and the bundle was large, but she lugged them"
110 "along like a person who did not find her especial burden in material"
110 "things. Occasionally she stopped to rest in a mechanical way by some"
110 "gate or post; and then, giving the baggage another hitch upon her full"
110 "round arm, went steadily on again."
110 ""
110 "It was a Sunday morning in late October, about four months after Tess"
110 "Durbeyfield’s arrival at Trantridge, and some few weeks subsequent to"
110 "the night ride in The Chase. The time was not long past daybreak, and"
110 "the yellow luminosity upon the horizon behind her back lighted the"
110 "ridge towards which her face was set—the barrier of the vale wherein"
110 "she had of late been a stranger—which she would have to climb over to"
110 "reach her birthplace. The ascent was gradual on this side, and the soil"
110 "and scenery differed much from those within Blakemore Vale. Even the"
110 "character and accent of the two peoples had shades of difference,"
110 "despite the amalgamating effects of a roundabout railway; so that,"
110 "though less than twenty miles from the place of her sojourn at"
110 "Trantridge, her native village had seemed a far-away spot. The"
110 "field-folk shut in there traded northward and westward, travelled,"
110 "courted, and married northward and westward, thought northward and"
110 "westward; those on this side mainly directed their energies and"
110 "attention to the east and south."
110 ""
110 "The incline was the same down which d’Urberville had driven her so"
110 "wildly on that day in June. Tess went up the remainder of its length"
110 "without stopping, and on reaching the edge of the escarpment gazed over"
110 "the familiar green world beyond, now half-veiled in mist. It was always"
110 "beautiful from here; it was terribly beautiful to Tess to-day, for"
110 "since her eyes last fell upon it she had learnt that the serpent hisses"
110 "where the sweet birds sing, and her views of life had been totally"
110 "changed for her by the lesson. Verily another girl than the simple one"
110 "she had been at home was she who, bowed by thought, stood still here,"
110 "and turned to look behind her. She could not bear to look forward into"
110 "the Vale."
110 ""
110 "Ascending by the long white road that Tess herself had just laboured"
110 "up, she saw a two-wheeled vehicle, beside which walked a man, who held"
110 "up his hand to attract her attention."
110 ""

110 "a few minutes man and horse stopped beside her."

110 "like the deuce to overtake you. Just look at the mare. Why go off like"
110 "this? You know that nobody wished to hinder your going. And how"
110 "unnecessary it has been for you to toil along on foot, and encumber"

110 "drive you the rest of the distance, if you won’t come back.”"
110 ""
110 "“I shan’t come back,” said she."
110 ""

110 "let me help you on.”"
110 ""
110 "She listlessly placed her basket and bundle within the dog-cart, and"
110 "stepped up, and they sat side by side. She had no fear of him now, and"
110 "in the cause of her confidence her sorrow lay."
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“What are you crying for?” he coldly asked."
110 ""
110 "“I was only thinking that I was born over there,” murmured Tess."
110 ""
110 "“Well—we must all be born somewhere.”"
110 ""
110 "“I wish I had never been born—there or anywhere else!”"
110 ""

110 "come?”"
110 ""
110 "She did not reply."
110 ""
110 "“You didn’t come for love of me, that I’ll swear.”"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "He emitted a laboured breath, as if the scene were getting rather"
110 "oppressive to his heart, or to his conscience, or to his gentility."
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Then good morning, my four months’ cousin—good-bye!”"
110 ""
110 "He leapt up lightly, arranged the reins, and was gone between the tall"
110 "red-berried hedges."
110 ""
110 "Tess did not look after him, but slowly wound along the crooked lane."
110 "It was still early, and though the sun’s lower limb was just free of"
110 "the hill, his rays, ungenial and peering, addressed the eye rather than"

110 ""
110 "As she walked, however, some footsteps approached behind her, the"
110 "footsteps of a man; and owing to the briskness of his advance he was"
110 "close at her heels and had said “Good morning” before she had been long"
110 "aware of his propinquity. He appeared to be an artisan of some sort,"
110 "and carried a tin pot of red paint in his hand. He asked in a"
110 "business-like manner if he should take her basket, which she permitted"
110 "him to do, walking beside him."
110 ""
110 "“It is early to be astir this Sabbath morn!” he said cheerfully."
110 ""
110 "“Yes,” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "“When most people are at rest from their week’s work.”"
110 ""
110 "She also assented to this."
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Do you?”"
110 ""
110 "“All the week I work for the glory of man, and on Sunday for the glory"

110 "roadside leading into a pasture. “If you’ll wait a moment,” he added,"
110 "“I shall not be long.”"
110 ""
110 "As he had her basket she could not well do otherwise; and she waited,"

110 "paint with the brush that was in it began painting large square letters"

110 ""
110 "    THY, DAMNATION, SLUMBERETH, NOT."
110 "        2 Pet. ii. 3."
110 ""
110 "Against the peaceful landscape, the pale, decaying tints of the copses,"
110 "the blue air of the horizon, and the lichened stile-boards, these"
110 "staring vermilion words shone forth. They seemed to shout themselves"
110 "out and make the atmosphere ring. Some people might have cried “Alas,"
110 "poor Theology!” at the hideous defacement—the last grotesque phase of a"
110 "creed which had served mankind well in its time. But the words entered"
110 "Tess with accusatory horror. It was as if this man had known her recent"
110 "history; yet he was a total stranger."
110 ""
110 "Having finished his text he picked up her basket, and she mechanically"
110 "resumed her walk beside him."
110 ""
110 "“Do you believe what you paint?” she asked in low tones."
110 ""
110 "“Believe that tex? Do I believe in my own existence!”"
110 ""
110 "“But,” said she tremulously, “suppose your sin was not of your own"
110 "seeking?”"
110 ""
110 "He shook his head."
110 ""
110 "“I cannot split hairs on that burning query,” he said. “I have walked"
110 "hundreds of miles this past summer, painting these texes on every wall,"
110 "gate, and stile the length and breadth of this district. I leave their"
110 "application to the hearts of the people who read ’em.”"
110 ""
110 "“I think they are horrible,” said Tess. “Crushing! Killing!”"
110 ""
110 "“That’s what they are meant to be!” he replied in a trade voice. “But"
110 "you should read my hottest ones—them I kips for slums and seaports."
110 "They’d make ye wriggle! Not but what this is a very good tex for rural"
110 "districts.... Ah—there’s a nice bit of blank wall up by that barn"
110 "standing to waste. I must put one there—one that it will be good for"

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 "Get Alec d’Urberville in the mind to marry her! He marry _her_! On"
110 "matrimony he had never once said a word. And what if he had? How a"
110 "convulsive snatching at social salvation might have impelled her to"

110 "stirred to confused surrender awhile: had suddenly despised and"

110 "“You ought to have been more careful if you didn’t mean to get him to"
110 "make you his wife!”"
110 ""

110 "expected to know? I was a child when I left this house four months ago."

110 "novels that tell them of these tricks; but I never had the chance o’"

110 ""
110 "Her mother was subdued."
110 ""

110 "<CHAPTER XIII>"
110 ""
110 "The event of Tess Durbeyfield’s return from the manor of her bogus"
110 "kinsfolk was rumoured abroad, if rumour be not too large a word for a"
110 "space of a square mile. In the afternoon several young girls of"
110 "Marlott, former schoolfellows and acquaintances of Tess, called to see"
110 "her, arriving dressed in their best starched and ironed, as became"
110 "visitors to a person who had made a transcendent conquest (as they"
110 "supposed), and sat round the room looking at her with great curiosity."
110 "For the fact that it was this said thirty-first cousin, Mr"
110 "d’Urberville, who had fallen in love with her, a gentleman not"
110 "altogether local, whose reputation as a reckless gallant and"
110 "heartbreaker was beginning to spread beyond the immediate boundaries of"
110 "Trantridge, lent Tess’s supposed position, by its fearsomeness, a far"
110 "higher fascination that it would have exercised if unhazardous."
110 ""
110 "Their interest was so deep that the younger ones whispered when her"
110 "back was turned—"
110 ""
110 "“How pretty she is; and how that best frock do set her off! I believe"
110 "it cost an immense deal, and that it was a gift from him.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess, who was reaching up to get the tea-things from the"
110 "corner-cupboard, did not hear these commentaries. If she had heard"
110 "them, she might soon have set her friends right on the matter. But her"
110 "mother heard, and Joan’s simple vanity, having been denied the hope of"
110 "a dashing marriage, fed itself as well as it could upon the sensation"
110 "of a dashing flirtation. Upon the whole she felt gratified, even though"
110 "such a limited and evanescent triumph should involve her daughter’s"
110 "reputation; it might end in marriage yet, and in the warmth of her"
110 "responsiveness to their admiration she invited her visitors to stay to"
110 "tea."
110 ""
110 "Their chatter, their laughter, their good-humoured innuendoes, above"
110 "all, their flashes and flickerings of envy, revived Tess’s spirits"
110 "also; and, as the evening wore on, she caught the infection of their"
110 "excitement, and grew almost gay. The marble hardness left her face, she"
110 "moved with something of her old bounding step, and flushed in all her"
110 "young beauty."
110 ""
110 "At moments, in spite of thought, she would reply to their inquiries"
110 "with a manner of superiority, as if recognizing that her experiences in"
110 "the field of courtship had, indeed, been slightly enviable. But so far"
110 "was she from being, in the words of Robert South, “in love with her own"
110 "ruin,” that the illusion was transient as lightning; cold reason came"
110 "back to mock her spasmodic weakness; the ghastliness of her momentary"
110 "pride would convict her, and recall her to reserved listlessness again."
110 ""
110 "And the despondency of the next morning’s dawn, when it was no longer"
110 "Sunday, but Monday; and no best clothes; and the laughing visitors were"
110 "gone, and she awoke alone in her old bed, the innocent younger children"
110 "breathing softly around her. In place of the excitement of her return,"
110 "and the interest it had inspired, she saw before her a long and stony"
110 "highway which she had to tread, without aid, and with little sympathy."
110 "Her depression was then terrible, and she could have hidden herself in"
110 "a tomb."
110 ""
110 "In the course of a few weeks Tess revived sufficiently to show herself"
110 "so far as was necessary to get to church one Sunday morning. She liked"
110 "to hear the chanting—such as it was—and the old Psalms, and to join in"
110 "the Morning Hymn. That innate love of melody, which she had inherited"
110 "from her ballad-singing mother, gave the simplest music a power over"
110 "her which could well-nigh drag her heart out of her bosom at times."
110 ""
110 "To be as much out of observation as possible for reasons of her own,"
110 "and to escape the gallantries of the young men, she set out before the"
110 "chiming began, and took a back seat under the gallery, close to the"
110 "lumber, where only old men and women came, and where the bier stood on"
110 "end among the churchyard tools."
110 ""
110 "Parishioners dropped in by twos and threes, deposited themselves in"
110 "rows before her, rested three-quarters of a minute on their foreheads"
110 "as if they were praying, though they were not; then sat up, and looked"
110 "around. When the chants came on, one of her favourites happened to be"
110 "chosen among the rest—the old double chant “Langdon”—but she did not"
110 "know what it was called, though she would much have liked to know. She"
110 "thought, without exactly wording the thought, how strange and god-like"
110 "was a composer’s power, who from the grave could lead through sequences"
110 "of emotion, which he alone had felt at first, a girl like her who had"
110 "never heard of his name, and never would have a clue to his"
110 "personality."
110 ""
110 "The people who had turned their heads turned them again as the service"
110 "proceeded; and at last observing her, they whispered to each other. She"
110 "knew what their whispers were about, grew sick at heart, and felt that"
110 "she could come to church no more."
110 ""
110 "The bedroom which she shared with some of the children formed her"
110 "retreat more continually than ever. Here, under her few square yards of"
110 "thatch, she watched winds, and snows, and rains, gorgeous sunsets, and"
110 "successive moons at their full. So close kept she that at length almost"
110 "everybody thought she had gone away."
110 ""
110 "The only exercise that Tess took at this time was after dark; and it"
110 "was then, when out in the woods, that she seemed least solitary. She"
110 "knew how to hit to a hair’s-breadth that moment of evening when the"
110 "light and the darkness are so evenly balanced that the constraint of"
110 "day and the suspense of night neutralize each other, leaving absolute"
110 "mental liberty. It is then that the plight of being alive becomes"
110 "attenuated to its least possible dimensions. She had no fear of the"
110 "shadows; her sole idea seemed to be to shun mankind—or rather that cold"
110 "accretion called the world, which, so terrible in the mass, is so"
110 "unformidable, even pitiable, in its units."
110 ""
110 "On these lonely hills and dales her quiescent glide was of a piece with"
110 "the element she moved in. Her flexuous and stealthy figure became an"
110 "integral part of the scene. At times her whimsical fancy would"
110 "intensify natural processes around her till they seemed a part of her"
110 "own story. Rather they became a part of it; for the world is only a"
110 "psychological phenomenon, and what they seemed they were. The midnight"
110 "airs and gusts, moaning amongst the tightly-wrapped buds and bark of"
110 "the winter twigs, were formulae of bitter reproach. A wet day was the"
110 "expression of irremediable grief at her weakness in the mind of some"
110 "vague ethical being whom she could not class definitely as the God of"
110 "her childhood, and could not comprehend as any other."
110 ""
110 "But this encompassment of her own characterization, based on shreds of"
110 "convention, peopled by phantoms and voices antipathetic to her, was a"
110 "sorry and mistaken creation of Tess’s fancy—a cloud of moral hobgoblins"
110 "by which she was terrified without reason. It was they that were out of"
110 "harmony with the actual world, not she. Walking among the sleeping"
110 "birds in the hedges, watching the skipping rabbits on a moonlit warren,"
110 "or standing under a pheasant-laden bough, she looked upon herself as a"
110 "figure of Guilt intruding into the haunts of Innocence. But all the"
110 "while she was making a distinction where there was no difference."
110 "Feeling herself in antagonism, she was quite in accord. She had been"
110 "made to break an accepted social law, but no law known to the"
110 "environment in which she fancied herself such an anomaly."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XIV>"
110 ""
110 "It was a hazy sunrise in August. The denser nocturnal vapours, attacked"
110 "by the warm beams, were dividing and shrinking into isolated fleeces"
110 "within hollows and coverts, where they waited till they should be dried"
110 "away to nothing."
110 ""
110 "The sun, on account of the mist, had a curious sentient, personal look,"
110 "demanding the masculine pronoun for its adequate expression. His"
110 "present aspect, coupled with the lack of all human forms in the scene,"
110 "explained the old-time heliolatries in a moment. One could feel that a"
110 "saner religion had never prevailed under the sky. The luminary was a"
110 "golden-haired, beaming, mild-eyed, God-like creature, gazing down in"
110 "the vigour and intentness of youth upon an earth that was brimming with"
110 "interest for him."
110 ""
110 "His light, a little later, broke though chinks of cottage shutters,"
110 "throwing stripes like red-hot pokers upon cupboards, chests of drawers,"
110 "and other furniture within; and awakening harvesters who were not"
110 "already astir."
110 ""
110 "But of all ruddy things that morning the brightest were two broad arms"
110 "of painted wood, which rose from the margin of yellow cornfield hard by"
110 "Marlott village. They, with two others below, formed the revolving"
110 "Maltese cross of the reaping-machine, which had been brought to the"
110 "field on the previous evening to be ready for operations this day. The"
110 "paint with which they were smeared, intensified in hue by the sunlight,"
110 "imparted to them a look of having been dipped in liquid fire."
110 ""
110 "The field had already been “opened”; that is to say, a lane a few feet"
110 "wide had been hand-cut through the wheat along the whole circumference"
110 "of the field for the first passage of the horses and machine."
110 ""
110 "Two groups, one of men and lads, the other of women, had come down the"
110 "lane just at the hour when the shadows of the eastern hedge-top struck"
110 "the west hedge midway, so that the heads of the groups were enjoying"
110 "sunrise while their feet were still in the dawn. They disappeared from"
110 "the lane between the two stone posts which flanked the nearest"
110 "field-gate."
110 ""
110 "Presently there arose from within a ticking like the love-making of the"
110 "grasshopper. The machine had begun, and a moving concatenation of three"
110 "horses and the aforesaid long rickety machine was visible over the"
110 "gate, a driver sitting upon one of the hauling horses, and an attendant"
110 "on the seat of the implement. Along one side of the field the whole"
110 "wain went, the arms of the mechanical reaper revolving slowly, till it"
110 "passed down the hill quite out of sight. In a minute it came up on the"
110 "other side of the field at the same equable pace; the glistening brass"
110 "star in the forehead of the fore horse first catching the eye as it"
110 "rose into view over the stubble, then the bright arms, and then the"
110 "whole machine."
110 ""
110 "The narrow lane of stubble encompassing the field grew wider with each"
110 "circuit, and the standing corn was reduced to a smaller area as the"
110 "morning wore on. Rabbits, hares, snakes, rats, mice, retreated inwards"
110 "as into a fastness, unaware of the ephemeral nature of their refuge,"
110 "and of the doom that awaited them later in the day when, their covert"
110 "shrinking to a more and more horrible narrowness, they were huddled"
110 "together, friends and foes, till the last few yards of upright wheat"
110 "fell also under the teeth of the unerring reaper, and they were every"
110 "one put to death by the sticks and stones of the harvesters."
110 ""
110 "The reaping-machine left the fallen corn behind it in little heaps,"
110 "each heap being of the quantity for a sheaf; and upon these the active"
110 "binders in the rear laid their hands—mainly women, but some of them men"
110 "in print shirts, and trousers supported round their waists by leather"
110 "straps, rendering useless the two buttons behind, which twinkled and"
110 "bristled with sunbeams at every movement of each wearer, as if they"
110 "were a pair of eyes in the small of his back."
110 ""
110 "But those of the other sex were the most interesting of this company of"
110 "binders, by reason of the charm which is acquired by woman when she"
110 "becomes part and parcel of outdoor nature, and is not merely an object"
110 "set down therein as at ordinary times. A field-man is a personality"
110 "afield; a field-woman is a portion of the field; she had somehow lost"
110 "her own margin, imbibed the essence of her surrounding, and assimilated"
110 "herself with it."
110 ""
110 "The women—or rather girls, for they were mostly young—wore drawn cotton"
110 "bonnets with great flapping curtains to keep off the sun, and gloves to"
110 "prevent their hands being wounded by the stubble. There was one wearing"
110 "a pale pink jacket, another in a cream-coloured tight-sleeved gown,"
110 "another in a petticoat as red as the arms of the reaping-machine; and"
110 "others, older, in the brown-rough “wropper” or over-all—the"
110 "old-established and most appropriate dress of the field-woman, which"
110 "the young ones were abandoning. This morning the eye returns"
110 "involuntarily to the girl in the pink cotton jacket, she being the most"
110 "flexuous and finely-drawn figure of them all. But her bonnet is pulled"
110 "so far over her brow that none of her face is disclosed while she"
110 "binds, though her complexion may be guessed from a stray twine or two"
110 "of dark brown hair which extends below the curtain of her bonnet."
110 "Perhaps one reason why she seduces casual attention is that she never"
110 "courts it, though the other women often gaze around them."
110 ""
110 "Her binding proceeds with clock-like monotony. From the sheaf last"
110 "finished she draws a handful of ears, patting their tips with her left"
110 "palm to bring them even. Then, stooping low, she moves forward,"
110 "gathering the corn with both hands against her knees, and pushing her"
110 "left gloved hand under the bundle to meet the right on the other side,"
110 "holding the corn in an embrace like that of a lover. She brings the"
110 "ends of the bond together, and kneels on the sheaf while she ties it,"
110 "beating back her skirts now and then when lifted by the breeze. A bit"
110 "of her naked arm is visible between the buff leather of the gauntlet"
110 "and the sleeve of her gown; and as the day wears on its feminine"
110 "smoothness becomes scarified by the stubble and bleeds."
110 ""
110 "At intervals she stands up to rest, and to retie her disarranged apron,"
110 "or to pull her bonnet straight. Then one can see the oval face of a"
110 "handsome young woman with deep dark eyes and long heavy clinging"
110 "tresses, which seem to clasp in a beseeching way anything they fall"
110 "against. The cheeks are paler, the teeth more regular, the red lips"
110 "thinner than is usual in a country-bred girl."
110 ""
110 "It is Tess Durbeyfield, otherwise d’Urberville, somewhat changed—the"
110 "same, but not the same; at the present stage of her existence living as"
110 "a stranger and an alien here, though it was no strange land that she"
110 "was in. After a long seclusion she had come to a resolve to undertake"
110 "outdoor work in her native village, the busiest season of the year in"
110 "the agricultural world having arrived, and nothing that she could do"
110 "within the house being so remunerative for the time as harvesting in"
110 "the fields."
110 ""
110 "The movements of the other women were more or less similar to Tess’s,"
110 "the whole bevy of them drawing together like dancers in a quadrille at"
110 "the completion of a sheaf by each, every one placing her sheaf on end"
110 "against those of the rest, till a shock, or “stitch” as it was here"
110 "called, of ten or a dozen was formed."
110 ""
110 "They went to breakfast, and came again, and the work proceeded as"
110 "before. As the hour of eleven drew near a person watching her might"
110 "have noticed that every now and then Tess’s glance flitted wistfully to"
110 "the brow of the hill, though she did not pause in her sheafing. On the"
110 "verge of the hour the heads of a group of children, of ages ranging"
110 "from six to fourteen, rose over the stubbly convexity of the hill."
110 ""
110 "The face of Tess flushed slightly, but still she did not pause."
110 ""
110 "The eldest of the comers, a girl who wore a triangular shawl, its"
110 "corner draggling on the stubble, carried in her arms what at first"
110 "sight seemed to be a doll, but proved to be an infant in long clothes."
110 "Another brought some lunch. The harvesters ceased working, took their"
110 "provisions, and sat down against one of the shocks. Here they fell to,"
110 "the men plying a stone jar freely, and passing round a cup."
110 ""
110 "Tess Durbeyfield had been one of the last to suspend her labours. She"
110 "sat down at the end of the shock, her face turned somewhat away from"
110 "her companions. When she had deposited herself a man in a rabbit-skin"
110 "cap, and with a red handkerchief tucked into his belt, held the cup of"
110 "ale over the top of the shock for her to drink. But she did not accept"
110 "his offer. As soon as her lunch was spread she called up the big girl,"
110 "her sister, and took the baby of her, who, glad to be relieved of the"
110 "burden, went away to the next shock and joined the other children"
110 "playing there. Tess, with a curiously stealthy yet courageous movement,"
110 "and with a still rising colour, unfastened her frock and began suckling"
110 "the child."
110 ""
110 "The men who sat nearest considerately turned their faces towards the"
110 "other end of the field, some of them beginning to smoke; one, with"
110 "absent-minded fondness, regretfully stroking the jar that would no"
110 "longer yield a stream. All the women but Tess fell into animated talk,"
110 "and adjusted the disarranged knots of their hair."
110 ""
110 "When the infant had taken its fill, the young mother sat it upright in"
110 "her lap, and looking into the far distance, dandled it with a gloomy"
110 "indifference that was almost dislike; then all of a sudden she fell to"
110 "violently kissing it some dozens of times, as if she could never leave"
110 "off, the child crying at the vehemence of an onset which strangely"
110 "combined passionateness with contempt."
110 ""
110 "“She’s fond of that there child, though she mid pretend to hate en, and"
110 "say she wishes the baby and her too were in the churchyard,” observed"
110 "the woman in the red petticoat."
110 ""
110 "“She’ll soon leave off saying that,” replied the one in buff. “Lord,"
110 "’tis wonderful what a body can get used to o’ that sort in time!”"
110 ""
110 "“A little more than persuading had to do wi’ the coming o’t, I reckon."
110 "There were they that heard a sobbing one night last year in The Chase;"
110 "and it mid ha’ gone hard wi’ a certain party if folks had come along.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, a little more, or a little less, ’twas a thousand pities that it"
110 "should have happened to she, of all others. But ’tis always the"
110 "comeliest! The plain ones be as safe as churches—hey, Jenny?” The"
110 "speaker turned to one of the group who certainly was not ill-defined as"
110 "plain."
110 ""
110 "It was a thousand pities, indeed; it was impossible for even an enemy"
110 "to feel otherwise on looking at Tess as she sat there, with her"
110 "flower-like mouth and large tender eyes, neither black nor blue nor"
110 "grey nor violet; rather all those shades together, and a hundred"
110 "others, which could be seen if one looked into their irises—shade"
110 "behind shade—tint beyond tint—around pupils that had no bottom; an"
110 "almost standard woman, but for the slight incautiousness of character"
110 "inherited from her race."
110 ""
110 "A resolution which had surprised herself had brought her into the"
110 "fields this week for the first time during many months. After wearing"
110 "and wasting her palpitating heart with every engine of regret that"
110 "lonely inexperience could devise, common sense had illuminated her. She"
110 "felt that she would do well to be useful again—to taste anew sweet"
110 "independence at any price. The past was past; whatever it had been, it"
110 "was no more at hand. Whatever its consequences, time would close over"
110 "them; they would all in a few years be as if they had never been, and"
110 "she herself grassed down and forgotten. Meanwhile the trees were just"
110 "as green as before; the birds sang and the sun shone as clearly now as"
110 "ever. The familiar surroundings had not darkened because of her grief,"
110 "nor sickened because of her pain."
110 ""
110 "She might have seen that what had bowed her head so profoundly—the"
110 "thought of the world’s concern at her situation—was founded on an"
110 "illusion. She was not an existence, an experience, a passion, a"
110 "structure of sensations, to anybody but herself. To all humankind"
110 "besides, Tess was only a passing thought. Even to friends she was no"
110 "more than a frequently passing thought. If she made herself miserable"
110 "the livelong night and day it was only this much to them—“Ah, she makes"
110 "herself unhappy.” If she tried to be cheerful, to dismiss all care, to"
110 "take pleasure in the daylight, the flowers, the baby, she could only be"
110 "this idea to them—“Ah, she bears it very well.” Moreover, alone in a"
110 "desert island would she have been wretched at what had happened to her?"
110 "Not greatly. If she could have been but just created, to discover"
110 "herself as a spouseless mother, with no experience of life except as"
110 "the parent of a nameless child, would the position have caused her to"
110 "despair? No, she would have taken it calmly, and found pleasure"
110 "therein. Most of the misery had been generated by her conventional"
110 "aspect, and not by her innate sensations."
110 ""
110 "Whatever Tess’s reasoning, some spirit had induced her to dress herself"
110 "up neatly as she had formerly done, and come out into the fields,"
110 "harvest-hands being greatly in demand just then. This was why she had"
110 "borne herself with dignity, and had looked people calmly in the face at"
110 "times, even when holding the baby in her arms."
110 ""
110 "The harvest-men rose from the shock of corn, and stretched their limbs,"
110 "and extinguished their pipes. The horses, which had been unharnessed"
110 "and fed, were again attached to the scarlet machine. Tess, having"
110 "quickly eaten her own meal, beckoned to her eldest sister to come and"
110 "take away the baby, fastened her dress, put on the buff gloves again,"
110 "and stooped anew to draw a bond from the last completed sheaf for the"
110 "tying of the next."
110 ""
110 "In the afternoon and evening the proceedings of the morning were"
110 "continued, Tess staying on till dusk with the body of harvesters. Then"
110 "they all rode home in one of the largest wagons, in the company of a"
110 "broad tarnished moon that had risen from the ground to the eastwards,"
110 "its face resembling the outworn gold-leaf halo of some worm-eaten"
110 "Tuscan saint. Tess’s female companions sang songs, and showed"
110 "themselves very sympathetic and glad at her reappearance out of doors,"
110 "though they could not refrain from mischievously throwing in a few"
110 "verses of the ballad about the maid who went to the merry green wood"
110 "and came back a changed state. There are counterpoises and"
110 "compensations in life; and the event which had made of her a social"
110 "warning had also for the moment made her the most interesting personage"
110 "in the village to many. Their friendliness won her still farther away"
110 "from herself, their lively spirits were contagious, and she became"
110 "almost gay."
110 ""
110 "But now that her moral sorrows were passing away a fresh one arose on"
110 "the natural side of her which knew no social law. When she reached home"
110 "it was to learn to her grief that the baby had been suddenly taken ill"
110 "since the afternoon. Some such collapse had been probable, so tender"
110 "and puny was its frame; but the event came as a shock nevertheless."
110 ""
110 "The baby’s offence against society in coming into the world was"
110 "forgotten by the girl-mother; her soul’s desire was to continue that"
110 "offence by preserving the life of the child. However, it soon grew"
110 "clear that the hour of emancipation for that little prisoner of the"
110 "flesh was to arrive earlier than her worst misgiving had conjectured."
110 "And when she had discovered this she was plunged into a misery which"
110 "transcended that of the child’s simple loss. Her baby had not been"
110 "baptized."
110 ""
110 "Tess had drifted into a frame of mind which accepted passively the"
110 "consideration that if she should have to burn for what she had done,"
110 "burn she must, and there was an end of it. Like all village girls, she"
110 "was well grounded in the Holy Scriptures, and had dutifully studied the"
110 "histories of Aholah and Aholibah, and knew the inferences to be drawn"
110 "therefrom. But when the same question arose with regard to the baby, it"
110 "had a very different colour. Her darling was about to die, and no"
110 "salvation."
110 ""
110 "It was nearly bedtime, but she rushed downstairs and asked if she might"
110 "send for the parson. The moment happened to be one at which her"
110 "father’s sense of the antique nobility of his family was highest, and"
110 "his sensitiveness to the smudge which Tess had set upon that nobility"
110 "most pronounced, for he had just returned from his weekly booze at"
110 "Rolliver’s Inn. No parson should come inside his door, he declared,"
110 "prying into his affairs, just then, when, by her shame, it had become"
110 "more necessary than ever to hide them. He locked the door and put the"
110 "key in his pocket."
110 ""
110 "The household went to bed, and, distressed beyond measure, Tess retired"
110 "also. She was continually waking as she lay, and in the middle of the"
110 "night found that the baby was still worse. It was obviously"
110 "dying—quietly and painlessly, but none the less surely."
110 ""
110 "In her misery she rocked herself upon the bed. The clock struck the"
110 "solemn hour of one, that hour when fancy stalks outside reason, and"
110 "malignant possibilities stand rock-firm as facts. She thought of the"
110 "child consigned to the nethermost corner of hell, as its double doom"
110 "for lack of baptism and lack of legitimacy; saw the arch-fiend tossing"
110 "it with his three-pronged fork, like the one they used for heating the"
110 "oven on baking days; to which picture she added many other quaint and"
110 "curious details of torment sometimes taught the young in this Christian"
110 "country. The lurid presentment so powerfully affected her imagination"
110 "in the silence of the sleeping house that her nightgown became damp"
110 "with perspiration, and the bedstead shook with each throb of her heart."
110 ""
110 "The infant’s breathing grew more difficult, and the mother’s mental"
110 "tension increased. It was useless to devour the little thing with"
110 "kisses; she could stay in bed no longer, and walked feverishly about"
110 "the room."
110 ""
110 "“O merciful God, have pity; have pity upon my poor baby!” she cried."
110 "“Heap as much anger as you want to upon me, and welcome; but pity the"
110 "child!”"
110 ""
110 "She leant against the chest of drawers, and murmured incoherent"
110 "supplications for a long while, till she suddenly started up."
110 ""
110 "“Ah! perhaps baby can be saved! Perhaps it will be just the same!”"
110 ""
110 "She spoke so brightly that it seemed as though her face might have"
110 "shone in the gloom surrounding her. She lit a candle, and went to a"
110 "second and a third bed under the wall, where she awoke her young"
110 "sisters and brothers, all of whom occupied the same room. Pulling out"
110 "the washing-stand so that she could get behind it, she poured some"
110 "water from a jug, and made them kneel around, putting their hands"
110 "together with fingers exactly vertical. While the children, scarcely"
110 "awake, awe-stricken at her manner, their eyes growing larger and"
110 "larger, remained in this position, she took the baby from her bed—a"
110 "child’s child—so immature as scarce to seem a sufficient personality to"
110 "endow its producer with the maternal title. Tess then stood erect with"
110 "the infant on her arm beside the basin; the next sister held the"
110 "Prayer-Book open before her, as the clerk at church held it before the"
110 "parson; and thus the girl set about baptizing her child."
110 ""
110 "Her figure looked singularly tall and imposing as she stood in her long"
110 "white nightgown, a thick cable of twisted dark hair hanging straight"
110 "down her back to her waist. The kindly dimness of the weak candle"
110 "abstracted from her form and features the little blemishes which"
110 "sunlight might have revealed—the stubble scratches upon her wrists, and"
110 "the weariness of her eyes—her high enthusiasm having a transfiguring"
110 "effect upon the face which had been her undoing, showing it as a thing"
110 "of immaculate beauty, with a touch of dignity which was almost regal."
110 "The little ones kneeling round, their sleepy eyes blinking and red,"
110 "awaited her preparations full of a suspended wonder which their"
110 "physical heaviness at that hour would not allow to become active."
110 ""
110 "The most impressed of them said:"
110 ""
110 "“Be you really going to christen him, Tess?”"
110 ""
110 "The girl-mother replied in a grave affirmative."
110 ""
110 "“What’s his name going to be?”"
110 ""
110 "She had not thought of that, but a name suggested by a phrase in the"
110 "book of Genesis came into her head as she proceeded with the baptismal"
110 "service, and now she pronounced it:"
110 ""
110 "“SORROW, I baptize thee in the name of the Father, and of the Son, and"
110 "of the Holy Ghost.”"
110 ""
110 "She sprinkled the water, and there was silence."
110 ""
110 "“Say ‘Amen,’ children.”"
110 ""
110 "The tiny voices piped in obedient response, “Amen!”"
110 ""
110 "Tess went on:"
110 ""
110 "“We receive this child”—and so forth—“and do sign him with the sign of"
110 "the Cross.”"
110 ""
110 "Here she dipped her hand into the basin, and fervently drew an immense"
110 "cross upon the baby with her forefinger, continuing with the customary"
110 "sentences as to his manfully fighting against sin, the world, and the"
110 "devil, and being a faithful soldier and servant unto his life’s end."
110 "She duly went on with the Lord’s Prayer, the children lisping it after"
110 "her in a thin gnat-like wail, till, at the conclusion, raising their"
110 "voices to clerk’s pitch, they again piped into silence, “Amen!”"
110 ""
110 "Then their sister, with much augmented confidence in the efficacy of"
110 "the sacrament, poured forth from the bottom of her heart the"
110 "thanksgiving that follows, uttering it boldly and triumphantly in the"
110 "stopt-diapason note which her voice acquired when her heart was in her"
110 "speech, and which will never be forgotten by those who knew her. The"
110 "ecstasy of faith almost apotheosized her; it set upon her face a"
110 "glowing irradiation, and brought a red spot into the middle of each"
110 "cheek; while the miniature candle-flame inverted in her eye-pupils"
110 "shone like a diamond. The children gazed up at her with more and more"
110 "reverence, and no longer had a will for questioning. She did not look"
110 "like Sissy to them now, but as a being large, towering, and awful—a"
110 "divine personage with whom they had nothing in common."
110 ""
110 "Poor Sorrow’s campaign against sin, the world, and the devil was doomed"
110 "to be of limited brilliancy—luckily perhaps for himself, considering"
110 "his beginnings. In the blue of the morning that fragile soldier and"
110 "servant breathed his last, and when the other children awoke they cried"
110 "bitterly, and begged Sissy to have another pretty baby."
110 ""
110 "The calmness which had possessed Tess since the christening remained"
110 "with her in the infant’s loss. In the daylight, indeed, she felt her"
110 "terrors about his soul to have been somewhat exaggerated; whether well"
110 "founded or not, she had no uneasiness now, reasoning that if Providence"
110 "would not ratify such an act of approximation she, for one, did not"
110 "value the kind of heaven lost by the irregularity—either for herself or"
110 "for her child."
110 ""
110 "So passed away Sorrow the Undesired—that intrusive creature, that"
110 "bastard gift of shameless Nature, who respects not the social law; a"
110 "waif to whom eternal Time had been a matter of days merely, who knew"
110 "not that such things as years and centuries ever were; to whom the"
110 "cottage interior was the universe, the week’s weather climate, new-born"
110 "babyhood human existence, and the instinct to suck human knowledge."
110 ""
110 "Tess, who mused on the christening a good deal, wondered if it were"
110 "doctrinally sufficient to secure a Christian burial for the child."
110 "Nobody could tell this but the parson of the parish, and he was a"
110 "new-comer, and did not know her. She went to his house after dusk, and"
110 "stood by the gate, but could not summon courage to go in. The"
110 "enterprise would have been abandoned if she had not by accident met him"
110 "coming homeward as she turned away. In the gloom she did not mind"
110 "speaking freely."
110 ""
110 "“I should like to ask you something, sir.”"
110 ""
110 "He expressed his willingness to listen, and she told the story of the"
110 "baby’s illness and the extemporized ordinance. “And now, sir,” she"
110 "added earnestly, “can you tell me this—will it be just the same for him"
110 "as if you had baptized him?”"
110 ""
110 "Having the natural feelings of a tradesman at finding that a job he"
110 "should have been called in for had been unskilfully botched by his"
110 "customers among themselves, he was disposed to say no. Yet the dignity"
110 "of the girl, the strange tenderness in her voice, combined to affect"
110 "his nobler impulses—or rather those that he had left in him after ten"
110 "years of endeavour to graft technical belief on actual scepticism. The"
110 "man and the ecclesiastic fought within him, and the victory fell to the"
110 "man."
110 ""
110 "“My dear girl,” he said, “it will be just the same.”"
110 ""
110 "“Then will you give him a Christian burial?” she asked quickly."
110 ""
110 "The Vicar felt himself cornered. Hearing of the baby’s illness, he had"
110 "conscientiously gone to the house after nightfall to perform the rite,"
110 "and, unaware that the refusal to admit him had come from Tess’s father"
110 "and not from Tess, he could not allow the plea of necessity for its"
110 "irregular administration."
110 ""
110 "“Ah—that’s another matter,” he said."
110 ""
110 "“Another matter—why?” asked Tess, rather warmly."
110 ""
110 "“Well—I would willingly do so if only we two were concerned. But I must"
110 "not—for certain reasons.”"
110 ""
110 "“Just for once, sir!”"
110 ""
110 "“Really I must not.”"
110 ""
110 "“O sir!” She seized his hand as she spoke."
110 ""
110 "He withdrew it, shaking his head."
110 ""
110 "“Then I don’t like you!” she burst out, “and I’ll never come to your"
110 "church no more!”"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t talk so rashly.”"
110 ""
110 "“Perhaps it will be just the same to him if you don’t?... Will it be"
110 "just the same? Don’t for God’s sake speak as saint to sinner, but as"
110 "you yourself to me myself—poor me!”"
110 ""
110 "How the Vicar reconciled his answer with the strict notions he supposed"
110 "himself to hold on these subjects it is beyond a layman’s power to"
110 "tell, though not to excuse. Somewhat moved, he said in this case also—"
110 ""
110 "“It will be just the same.”"
110 ""
110 "So the baby was carried in a small deal box, under an ancient woman’s"
110 "shawl, to the churchyard that night, and buried by lantern-light, at"
110 "the cost of a shilling and a pint of beer to the sexton, in that shabby"
110 "corner of God’s allotment where He lets the nettles grow, and where all"
110 "unbaptized infants, notorious drunkards, suicides, and others of the"
110 "conjecturally damned are laid. In spite of the untoward surroundings,"
110 "however, Tess bravely made a little cross of two laths and a piece of"
110 "string, and having bound it with flowers, she stuck it up at the head"
110 "of the grave one evening when she could enter the churchyard without"
110 "being seen, putting at the foot also a bunch of the same flowers in a"
110 "little jar of water to keep them alive. What matter was it that on the"
110 "outside of the jar the eye of mere observation noted the words"
110 "“Keelwell’s Marmalade”? The eye of maternal affection did not see them"
110 "in its vision of higher things."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XV>"
110 ""
110 "“By experience,” says Roger Ascham, “we find out a short way by a long"
110 "wandering.” Not seldom that long wandering unfits us for further"
110 "travel, and of what use is our experience to us then? Tess"
110 "Durbeyfield’s experience was of this incapacitating kind. At last she"
110 "had learned what to do; but who would now accept her doing?"
110 ""
110 "If before going to the d’Urbervilles’ she had vigorously moved under"
110 "the guidance of sundry gnomic texts and phrases known to her and to the"
110 "world in general, no doubt she would never have been imposed on. But it"
110 "had not been in Tess’s power—nor is it in anybody’s power—to feel the"
110 "whole truth of golden opinions while it is possible to profit by them."
110 "She—and how many more—might have ironically said to God with Saint"
110 "Augustine: “Thou hast counselled a better course than Thou hast"
110 "permitted.”"
110 ""
110 "She remained at her father’s house during the winter months, plucking"
110 "fowls, or cramming turkeys and geese, or making clothes for her sisters"
110 "and brothers out of some finery which d’Urberville had given her, and"
110 "she had put by with contempt. Apply to him she would not. But she would"
110 "often clasp her hands behind her head and muse when she was supposed to"
110 "be working hard."
110 ""
110 "She philosophically noted dates as they came past in the revolution of"
110 "the year; the disastrous night of her undoing at Trantridge with its"
110 "dark background of The Chase; also the dates of the baby’s birth and"
110 "death; also her own birthday; and every other day individualized by"
110 "incidents in which she had taken some share. She suddenly thought one"
110 "afternoon, when looking in the glass at her fairness, that there was"
110 "yet another date, of greater importance to her than those; that of her"
110 "own death, when all these charms would have disappeared; a day which"
110 "lay sly and unseen among all the other days of the year, giving no sign"
110 "or sound when she annually passed over it; but not the less surely"
110 "there. When was it? Why did she not feel the chill of each yearly"
110 "encounter with such a cold relation? She had Jeremy Taylor’s thought"
110 "that some time in the future those who had known her would say: “It is"
110 "the ——th, the day that poor Tess Durbeyfield died”; and there would be"
110 "nothing singular to their minds in the statement. Of that day, doomed"
110 "to be her terminus in time through all the ages, she did not know the"
110 "place in month, week, season or year."
110 ""
110 "Almost at a leap Tess thus changed from simple girl to complex woman."
110 "Symbols of reflectiveness passed into her face, and a note of tragedy"
110 "at times into her voice. Her eyes grew larger and more eloquent. She"
110 "became what would have been called a fine creature; her aspect was fair"
110 "and arresting; her soul that of a woman whom the turbulent experiences"
110 "of the last year or two had quite failed to demoralize. But for the"
110 "world’s opinion those experiences would have been simply a liberal"
110 "education."
110 ""
110 "She had held so aloof of late that her trouble, never generally known,"
110 "was nearly forgotten in Marlott. But it became evident to her that she"
110 "could never be really comfortable again in a place which had seen the"
110 "collapse of her family’s attempt to “claim kin”—and, through her, even"
110 "closer union—with the rich d’Urbervilles. At least she could not be"
110 "comfortable there till long years should have obliterated her keen"
110 "consciousness of it. Yet even now Tess felt the pulse of hopeful life"
110 "still warm within her; she might be happy in some nook which had no"
110 "memories. To escape the past and all that appertained thereto was to"
110 "annihilate it, and to do that she would have to get away."
110 ""
110 "Was once lost always lost really true of chastity? she would ask"
110 "herself. She might prove it false if she could veil bygones. The"
110 "recuperative power which pervaded organic nature was surely not denied"
110 "to maidenhood alone."
110 ""
110 "She waited a long time without finding opportunity for a new departure."
110 "A particularly fine spring came round, and the stir of germination was"
110 "almost audible in the buds; it moved her, as it moved the wild animals,"
110 "and made her passionate to go. At last, one day in early May, a letter"
110 "reached her from a former friend of her mother’s, to whom she had"
110 "addressed inquiries long before—a person whom she had never seen—that a"
110 "skilful milkmaid was required at a dairy-house many miles to the"
110 "southward, and that the dairyman would be glad to have her for the"
110 "summer months."
110 ""
110 "It was not quite so far off as could have been wished; but it was"
110 "probably far enough, her radius of movement and repute having been so"
110 "small. To persons of limited spheres, miles are as geographical"
110 "degrees, parishes as counties, counties as provinces and kingdoms."
110 ""
110 "On one point she was resolved: there should be no more d’Urberville"
110 "air-castles in the dreams and deeds of her new life. She would be the"
110 "dairymaid Tess, and nothing more. Her mother knew Tess’s feeling on"
110 "this point so well, though no words had passed between them on the"
110 "subject, that she never alluded to the knightly ancestry now."
110 ""
110 "Yet such is human inconsistency that one of the interests of the new"
110 "place to her was the accidental virtues of its lying near her"
110 "forefathers’ country (for they were not Blakemore men, though her"
110 "mother was Blakemore to the bone). The dairy called Talbothays, for"
110 "which she was bound, stood not remotely from some of the former estates"
110 "of the d’Urbervilles, near the great family vaults of her granddames"
110 "and their powerful husbands. She would be able to look at them, and"
110 "think not only that d’Urberville, like Babylon, had fallen, but that"
110 "the individual innocence of a humble descendant could lapse as"
110 "silently. All the while she wondered if any strange good thing might"
110 "come of her being in her ancestral land; and some spirit within her"
110 "rose automatically as the sap in the twigs. It was unexpected youth,"
110 "surging up anew after its temporary check, and bringing with it hope,"
110 "and the invincible instinct towards self-delight."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "End of Phase the Second"
110 ""

110 "Phase the Third:"
110 ""
110 "The Rally"
