110 "name), “the prettiest milker I’ve got in my dairy; you mustn’t get so"
110 "fagged as this at the first breath of summer weather, or we shall be"
110 "finely put to for want of ’ee by dog-days, shan’t we, Mr Clare?”"
110 ""
110 "“I was faint—and—I think I am better out o’ doors,” she said"
110 "mechanically; and disappeared outside."
110 ""
110 "Fortunately for her the milk in the revolving churn at that moment"
110 "changed its squashing for a decided flick-flack."
110 ""
110 "“’Tis coming!” cried Mrs Crick, and the attention of all was called off"
110 "from Tess."
110 ""
110 "That fair sufferer soon recovered herself externally; but she remained"
110 "much depressed all the afternoon. When the evening milking was done she"
110 "did not care to be with the rest of them, and went out of doors,"
110 "wandering along she knew not whither. She was wretched—O so wretched—at"
110 "the perception that to her companions the dairyman’s story had been"
110 "rather a humorous narration than otherwise; none of them but herself"
110 "seemed to see the sorrow of it; to a certainty, not one knew how"
110 "cruelly it touched the tender place in her experience. The evening sun"
110 "was now ugly to her, like a great inflamed wound in the sky. Only a"
110 "solitary cracked-voice reed-sparrow greeted her from the bushes by the"
110 "river, in a sad, machine-made tone, resembling that of a past friend"
110 "whose friendship she had outworn."
110 ""
110 "In these long June days the milkmaids, and, indeed, most of the"
110 "household, went to bed at sunset or sooner, the morning work before"
110 "milking being so early and heavy at a time of full pails. Tess usually"
110 "accompanied her fellows upstairs. To-night, however, she was the first"
110 "to go to their common chamber; and she had dozed when the other girls"
110 "came in. She saw them undressing in the orange light of the vanished"
110 "sun, which flushed their forms with its colour; she dozed again, but"
110 "she was reawakened by their voices, and quietly turned her eyes towards"
110 "them."
110 ""
110 "Neither of her three chamber-companions had got into bed. They were"
110 "standing in a group, in their nightgowns, barefooted, at the window,"
110 "the last red rays of the west still warming their faces and necks and"
110 "the walls around them. All were watching somebody in the garden with"
110 "deep interest, their three faces close together: a jovial and round"
110 "one, a pale one with dark hair, and a fair one whose tresses were"
110 "auburn."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t push! You can see as well as I,” said Retty, the auburn-haired"
110 "and youngest girl, without removing her eyes from the window."
110 ""
110 "“’Tis no use for you to be in love with him any more than me, Retty"
110 "Priddle,” said jolly-faced Marian, the eldest, slily. “His thoughts be"
110 "of other cheeks than thine!”"
110 ""
110 "Retty Priddle still looked, and the others looked again."
110 ""
110 "“There he is again!” cried Izz Huett, the pale girl with dark damp hair"
110 "and keenly cut lips."
110 ""
110 "“You needn’t say anything, Izz,” answered Retty. “For I zid you kissing"
110 "his shade.”"
110 ""
110 "_”What_ did you see her doing?” asked Marian."
110 ""
110 "“Why—he was standing over the whey-tub to let off the whey, and the"
110 "shade of his face came upon the wall behind, close to Izz, who was"
110 "standing there filling a vat. She put her mouth against the wall and"
110 "kissed the shade of his mouth; I zid her, though he didn’t.”"
110 ""
110 "“O Izz Huett!” said Marian."
110 ""
110 "A rosy spot came into the middle of Izz Huett’s cheek."
110 ""
110 "“Well, there was no harm in it,” she declared, with attempted coolness."
110 "“And if I be in love wi’en, so is Retty, too; and so be you, Marian,"
110 "come to that.”"
110 ""
110 "Marian’s full face could not blush past its chronic pinkness."
110 ""
110 "“I!” she said. “What a tale! Ah, there he is again! Dear eyes—dear"
110 "face—dear Mr Clare!”"
110 ""
110 "“There—you’ve owned it!”"
110 ""
110 "“So have you—so have we all,” said Marian, with the dry frankness of"
110 "complete indifference to opinion. “It is silly to pretend otherwise"
110 "amongst ourselves, though we need not own it to other folks. I would"
110 "just marry ’n to-morrow!”"
110 ""
110 "“So would I—and more,” murmured Izz Huett."
110 ""
110 "“And I too,” whispered the more timid Retty."
110 ""
110 "The listener grew warm."
110 ""
110 "“We can’t all marry him,” said Izz."
110 ""
110 "“We shan’t, either of us; which is worse still,” said the eldest."
110 "“There he is again!”"
110 ""
110 "They all three blew him a silent kiss."
110 ""
110 "“Why?” asked Retty quickly."
110 ""
110 "“Because he likes Tess Durbeyfield best,” said Marian, lowering her"
110 "voice. “I have watched him every day, and have found it out.”"
110 ""
110 "There was a reflective silence."
110 ""
110 "“But she don’t care anything for ’n?” at length breathed Retty."
110 ""
110 "“Well—I sometimes think that too.”"
110 ""
110 "“But how silly all this is!” said Izz Huett impatiently. “Of course he"
110 "won’t marry any one of us, or Tess either—a gentleman’s son, who’s"
110 "going to be a great landowner and farmer abroad! More likely to ask us"
110 "to come wi’en as farm-hands at so much a year!”"
110 ""
110 "One sighed, and another sighed, and Marian’s plump figure sighed"
110 "biggest of all. Somebody in bed hard by sighed too. Tears came into the"
110 "eyes of Retty Priddle, the pretty red-haired youngest—the last bud of"
110 "the Paridelles, so important in the county annals. They watched"
110 "silently a little longer, their three faces still close together as"
110 "before, and the triple hues of their hair mingling. But the unconscious"
110 "Mr Clare had gone indoors, and they saw him no more; and, the shades"
110 "beginning to deepen, they crept into their beds. In a few minutes they"
110 "heard him ascend the ladder to his own room. Marian was soon snoring,"
110 "but Izz did not drop into forgetfulness for a long time. Retty Priddle"
110 "cried herself to sleep."
110 ""
110 "The deeper-passioned Tess was very far from sleeping even then. This"
110 "conversation was another of the bitter pills she had been obliged to"
110 "swallow that day. Scarce the least feeling of jealousy arose in her"
110 "breast. For that matter she knew herself to have the preference. Being"
110 "more finely formed, better educated, and, though the youngest except"
110 "Retty, more woman than either, she perceived that only the slightest"
110 "ordinary care was necessary for holding her own in Angel Clare’s heart"
110 "against these her candid friends. But the grave question was, ought she"
110 "to do this? There was, to be sure, hardly a ghost of a chance for"
110 "either of them, in a serious sense; but there was, or had been, a"
110 "chance of one or the other inspiring him with a passing fancy for her,"
110 "and enjoying the pleasure of his attentions while he stayed here. Such"
110 "unequal attachments had led to marriage; and she had heard from Mrs"
110 "Crick that Mr Clare had one day asked, in a laughing way, what would be"
110 "the use of his marrying a fine lady, and all the while ten thousand"
110 "acres of Colonial pasture to feed, and cattle to rear, and corn to"
110 "reap. A farm-woman would be the only sensible kind of wife for him. But"
110 "whether Mr Clare had spoken seriously or not, why should she, who could"
110 "never conscientiously allow any man to marry her now, and who had"
110 "religiously determined that she never would be tempted to do so, draw"
110 "off Mr Clare’s attention from other women, for the brief happiness of"
110 "sunning herself in his eyes while he remained at Talbothays?"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXII>"
110 ""
110 "They came downstairs yawning next morning; but skimming and milking"
110 "were proceeded with as usual, and they went indoors to breakfast."
110 "Dairyman Crick was discovered stamping about the house. He had received"
110 "a letter, in which a customer had complained that the butter had a"
110 "twang."
110 ""
110 "“And begad, so ’t have!” said the dairyman, who held in his left hand a"
110 "wooden slice on which a lump of butter was stuck. “Yes—taste for"
110 "yourself!”"
110 ""
110 "Several of them gathered round him; and Mr Clare tasted, Tess tasted,"
110 "also the other indoor milkmaids, one or two of the milking-men, and"
110 "last of all Mrs Crick, who came out from the waiting breakfast-table."
110 "There certainly was a twang."
110 ""
110 "The dairyman, who had thrown himself into abstraction to better realize"
110 "the taste, and so divine the particular species of noxious weed to"
110 "which it appertained, suddenly exclaimed—"
110 ""
110 "“’Tis garlic! and I thought there wasn’t a blade left in that mead!”"
110 ""
110 "Then all the old hands remembered that a certain dry mead, into which a"
110 "few of the cows had been admitted of late, had, in years gone by,"
110 "spoilt the butter in the same way. The dairyman had not recognized the"
110 "taste at that time, and thought the butter bewitched."
110 ""
110 "“We must overhaul that mead,” he resumed; “this mustn’t continny!”"
110 ""
110 "All having armed themselves with old pointed knives, they went out"
110 "together. As the inimical plant could only be present in very"
110 "microscopic dimensions to have escaped ordinary observation, to find it"
110 "seemed rather a hopeless attempt in the stretch of rich grass before"
110 "them. However, they formed themselves into line, all assisting, owing"
110 "to the importance of the search; the dairyman at the upper end with Mr"
110 "Clare, who had volunteered to help; then Tess, Marian, Izz Huett, and"
110 "Retty; then Bill Lewell, Jonathan, and the married dairywomen—Beck"
110 "Knibbs, with her wooly black hair and rolling eyes; and flaxen Frances,"
110 "consumptive from the winter damps of the water-meads—who lived in their"
110 "respective cottages."
110 ""
110 "With eyes fixed upon the ground they crept slowly across a strip of the"
110 "field, returning a little further down in such a manner that, when they"
110 "should have finished, not a single inch of the pasture but would have"
110 "fallen under the eye of some one of them. It was a most tedious"
110 "business, not more than half a dozen shoots of garlic being"
110 "discoverable in the whole field; yet such was the herb’s pungency that"
110 "probably one bite of it by one cow had been sufficient to season the"
110 "whole dairy’s produce for the day."
110 ""
110 "Differing one from another in natures and moods so greatly as they did,"
110 "they yet formed, bending, a curiously uniform row—automatic, noiseless;"
110 "and an alien observer passing down the neighbouring lane might well"
110 "have been excused for massing them as “Hodge”. As they crept along,"
110 "stooping low to discern the plant, a soft yellow gleam was reflected"
110 "from the buttercups into their shaded faces, giving them an elfish,"
110 "moonlit aspect, though the sun was pouring upon their backs in all the"
110 "strength of noon."
110 ""
110 "Angel Clare, who communistically stuck to his rule of taking part with"
110 "the rest in everything, glanced up now and then. It was not, of course,"
110 "by accident that he walked next to Tess."
110 ""
110 "“Well, how are you?” he murmured."
110 ""
110 "“Very well, thank you, sir,” she replied demurely."
110 ""
110 "As they had been discussing a score of personal matters only"
110 "half-an-hour before, the introductory style seemed a little"
110 "superfluous. But they got no further in speech just then. They crept"
110 "and crept, the hem of her petticoat just touching his gaiter, and his"
110 "elbow sometimes brushing hers. At last the dairyman, who came next,"
110 "could stand it no longer."
110 ""
110 "“Upon my soul and body, this here stooping do fairly make my back open"
110 "and shut!” he exclaimed, straightening himself slowly with an"
110 "excruciated look till quite upright. “And you, maidy Tess, you wasn’t"
110 "well a day or two ago—this will make your head ache finely! Don’t do"
110 "any more, if you feel fainty; leave the rest to finish it.”"
110 ""
110 "Dairyman Crick withdrew, and Tess dropped behind. Mr Clare also stepped"
110 "out of line, and began privateering about for the weed. When she found"
110 "him near her, her very tension at what she had heard the night before"
110 "made her the first to speak."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t they look pretty?” she said."
110 ""
110 "“Who?”"
110 ""
110 "“Izzy Huett and Retty.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess had moodily decided that either of these maidens would make a good"
110 "farmer’s wife, and that she ought to recommend them, and obscure her"
110 "own wretched charms."
110 ""
110 "“Pretty? Well, yes—they are pretty girls—fresh looking. I have often"
110 "thought so.”"
110 ""
110 "“Though, poor dears, prettiness won’t last long!”"
110 ""
110 "“O no, unfortunately.”"
110 ""
110 "“They are excellent dairywomen.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes: though not better than you.”"
110 ""
110 "“They skim better than I.”"
110 ""
110 "“Do they?”"
110 ""
110 "Clare remained observing them—not without their observing him."
110 ""
110 "“She is colouring up,” continued Tess heroically."
110 ""
110 "“Who?”"
110 ""
110 "“Retty Priddle.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh! Why it that?”"
110 ""
110 "“Because you are looking at her.”"
110 ""
110 "Self-sacrificing as her mood might be, Tess could not well go further"
110 "and cry, “Marry one of them, if you really do want a dairywoman and not"
110 "a lady; and don’t think of marrying me!” She followed Dairyman Crick,"
110 "and had the mournful satisfaction of seeing that Clare remained behind."
110 ""
110 "From this day she forced herself to take pains to avoid him—never"
110 "allowing herself, as formerly, to remain long in his company, even if"
110 "their juxtaposition were purely accidental. She gave the other three"
110 "every chance."
110 ""
110 "Tess was woman enough to realize from their avowals to herself that"
110 "Angel Clare had the honour of all the dairymaids in his keeping, and"
110 "her perception of his care to avoid compromising the happiness of"
110 "either in the least degree bred a tender respect in Tess for what she"
110 "deemed, rightly or wrongly, the self-controlling sense of duty shown by"
110 "him, a quality which she had never expected to find in one of the"
110 "opposite sex, and in the absence of which more than one of the simple"
110 "hearts who were his house-mates might have gone weeping on her"
110 "pilgrimage."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXIII>"
110 ""
110 "The hot weather of July had crept upon them unawares, and the"
110 "atmosphere of the flat vale hung heavy as an opiate over the"
110 "dairy-folk, the cows, and the trees. Hot steaming rains fell"
110 "frequently, making the grass where the cows fed yet more rank, and"
110 "hindering the late hay-making in the other meads."
110 ""
110 "It was Sunday morning; the milking was done; the outdoor milkers had"
110 "gone home. Tess and the other three were dressing themselves rapidly,"
110 "the whole bevy having agreed to go together to Mellstock Church, which"
110 "lay some three or four miles distant from the dairy-house. She had now"
110 "been two months at Talbothays, and this was her first excursion."
110 ""
110 "All the preceding afternoon and night heavy thunderstorms had hissed"
110 "down upon the meads, and washed some of the hay into the river; but"
110 "this morning the sun shone out all the more brilliantly for the deluge,"
110 "and the air was balmy and clear."
110 ""
110 "The crooked lane leading from their own parish to Mellstock ran along"
110 "the lowest levels in a portion of its length, and when the girls"
110 "reached the most depressed spot they found that the result of the rain"
110 "had been to flood the lane over-shoe to a distance of some fifty yards."
110 "This would have been no serious hindrance on a week-day; they would"
110 "have clicked through it in their high pattens and boots quite"
110 "unconcerned; but on this day of vanity, this Sun’s-day, when flesh went"
110 "forth to coquet with flesh while hypocritically affecting business with"
110 "spiritual things; on this occasion for wearing their white stockings"
110 "and thin shoes, and their pink, white, and lilac gowns, on which every"
110 "mud spot would be visible, the pool was an awkward impediment. They"
110 "could hear the church-bell calling—as yet nearly a mile off."
110 ""
110 "“Who would have expected such a rise in the river in summer-time!” said"
110 "Marian, from the top of the roadside bank on which they had climbed,"
110 "and were maintaining a precarious footing in the hope of creeping along"
110 "its slope till they were past the pool."
110 ""
110 "“We can’t get there anyhow, without walking right through it, or else"
110 "going round the Turnpike way; and that would make us so very late!”"
110 "said Retty, pausing hopelessly."
110 ""
110 "“And I do colour up so hot, walking into church late, and all the"
110 "people staring round,” said Marian, “that I hardly cool down again till"
110 "we get into the That-it-may-please-Thees.”"
110 ""
110 "While they stood clinging to the bank they heard a splashing round the"
110 "bend of the road, and presently appeared Angel Clare, advancing along"
110 "the lane towards them through the water."
110 ""
110 "Four hearts gave a big throb simultaneously."
110 ""
110 "His aspect was probably as un-Sabbatarian a one as a dogmatic parson’s"
110 "son often presented; his attire being his dairy clothes, long wading"
110 "boots, a cabbage-leaf inside his hat to keep his head cool, with a"
110 "thistle-spud to finish him off. “He’s not going to church,” said"
110 "Marian."
110 ""
110 "“No—I wish he was!” murmured Tess."
110 ""
110 "Angel, in fact, rightly or wrongly (to adopt the safe phrase of evasive"
110 "controversialists), preferred sermons in stones to sermons in churches"
110 "and chapels on fine summer days. This morning, moreover, he had gone"
110 "out to see if the damage to the hay by the flood was considerable or"
110 "not. On his walk he observed the girls from a long distance, though"
110 "they had been so occupied with their difficulties of passage as not to"
110 "notice him. He knew that the water had risen at that spot, and that it"
110 "would quite check their progress. So he had hastened on, with a dim"
110 "idea of how he could help them—one of them in particular."
110 ""
110 "The rosy-cheeked, bright-eyed quartet looked so charming in their light"
110 "summer attire, clinging to the roadside bank like pigeons on a"
110 "roof-slope, that he stopped a moment to regard them before coming"
110 "close. Their gauzy skirts had brushed up from the grass innumerable"
110 "flies and butterflies which, unable to escape, remained caged in the"
110 "transparent tissue as in an aviary. Angel’s eye at last fell upon Tess,"
110 "the hindmost of the four; she, being full of suppressed laughter at"
110 "their dilemma, could not help meeting his glance radiantly."
110 ""
110 "He came beneath them in the water, which did not rise over his long"
110 "boots; and stood looking at the entrapped flies and butterflies."
110 ""
110 "“Are you trying to get to church?” he said to Marian, who was in front,"
110 "including the next two in his remark, but avoiding Tess."
110 ""
110 "“Yes, sir; and ’tis getting late; and my colour do come up so—”"
110 ""
110 "“I’ll carry you through the pool—every Jill of you.”"
110 ""
110 "The whole four flushed as if one heart beat through them."
110 ""
110 "“I think you can’t, sir,” said Marian."
110 ""
110 "“It is the only way for you to get past. Stand still. Nonsense—you are"
110 "not too heavy! I’d carry you all four together. Now, Marian, attend,”"
110 "he continued, “and put your arms round my shoulders, so. Now! Hold on."
110 "That’s well done.”"
110 ""
110 "Marian had lowered herself upon his arm and shoulder as directed, and"
110 "Angel strode off with her, his slim figure, as viewed from behind,"
110 "looking like the mere stem to the great nosegay suggested by hers. They"
110 "disappeared round the curve of the road, and only his sousing footsteps"
110 "and the top ribbon of Marian’s bonnet told where they were. In a few"
110 "minutes he reappeared. Izz Huett was the next in order upon the bank."
110 ""
110 "“Here he comes,” she murmured, and they could hear that her lips were"
110 "dry with emotion. “And I have to put my arms round his neck and look"
110 "into his face as Marian did.”"
110 ""
110 "“There’s nothing in that,” said Tess quickly."
110 ""
110 "“There’s a time for everything,” continued Izz, unheeding. “A time to"
110 "embrace, and a time to refrain from embracing; the first is now going"
110 "to be mine.”"
110 ""
110 "“Fie—it is Scripture, Izz!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes,” said Izz, “I’ve always a’ ear at church for pretty verses.”"
110 ""
110 "Angel Clare, to whom three-quarters of this performance was a"
110 "commonplace act of kindness, now approached Izz. She quietly and"
110 "dreamily lowered herself into his arms, and Angel methodically marched"
110 "off with her. When he was heard returning for the third time Retty’s"
110 "throbbing heart could be almost seen to shake her. He went up to the"
110 "red-haired girl, and while he was seizing her he glanced at Tess. His"
110 "lips could not have pronounced more plainly, “It will soon be you and"
110 "I.” Her comprehension appeared in her face; she could not help it."
110 "There was an understanding between them."
110 ""
110 "Poor little Retty, though by far the lightest weight, was the most"
110 "troublesome of Clare’s burdens. Marian had been like a sack of meal, a"
110 "dead weight of plumpness under which he has literally staggered. Izz"
110 "had ridden sensibly and calmly. Retty was a bunch of hysterics."
110 ""
110 "However, he got through with the disquieted creature, deposited her,"
110 "and returned. Tess could see over the hedge the distant three in a"
110 "group, standing as he had placed them on the next rising ground. It was"
110 "now her turn. She was embarrassed to discover that excitement at the"
110 "proximity of Mr Clare’s breath and eyes, which she had contemned in her"
110 "companions, was intensified in herself; and as if fearful of betraying"
110 "her secret, she paltered with him at the last moment."
110 ""
110 "“I may be able to clim’ along the bank perhaps—I can clim’ better than"
110 "they. You must be so tired, Mr Clare!”"
110 ""
110 "“No, no, Tess,” said he quickly. And almost before she was aware, she"
110 "was seated in his arms and resting against his shoulder."
110 ""
110 "“Three Leahs to get one Rachel,” he whispered."
110 ""
110 "“They are better women than I,” she replied, magnanimously sticking to"
110 "her resolve."
110 ""
110 "“Not to me,” said Angel."
110 ""
110 "He saw her grow warm at this; and they went some steps in silence."
110 ""
110 "“I hope I am not too heavy?” she said timidly."
110 ""
110 "“O no. You should lift Marian! Such a lump. You are like an undulating"
110 "billow warmed by the sun. And all this fluff of muslin about you is the"
110 "froth.”"
110 ""
110 "“It is very pretty—if I seem like that to you.”"
110 ""
110 "“Do you know that I have undergone three-quarters of this labour"
110 "entirely for the sake of the fourth quarter?”"
110 ""
110 "“No.”"
110 ""
110 "“I did not expect such an event to-day.”"
110 ""
110 "“Nor I... The water came up so sudden.”"
110 ""
110 "That the rise in the water was what she understood him to refer to, the"
110 "state of breathing belied. Clare stood still and inclinced his face"
110 "towards hers."
110 ""
110 "“O Tessy!” he exclaimed."
110 ""
110 "The girl’s cheeks burned to the breeze, and she could not look into his"
110 "eyes for her emotion. It reminded Angel that he was somewhat unfairly"
110 "taking advantage of an accidental position; and he went no further with"
110 "it. No definite words of love had crossed their lips as yet, and"
110 "suspension at this point was desirable now. However, he walked slowly,"
110 "to make the remainder of the distance as long as possible; but at last"
110 "they came to the bend, and the rest of their progress was in full view"
110 "of the other three. The dry land was reached, and he set her down."
110 ""
110 "Her friends were looking with round thoughtful eyes at her and him, and"
110 "she could see that they had been talking of her. He hastily bade them"
110 "farewell, and splashed back along the stretch of submerged road."
110 ""
110 "The four moved on together as before, till Marian broke the silence by"
110 "saying—"
110 ""
110 "“No—in all truth; we have no chance against her!” She looked joylessly"
110 "at Tess."
110 ""
110 "“What do you mean?” asked the latter."
110 ""
110 "“He likes ’ee best—the very best! We could see it as he brought ’ee. He"
110 "would have kissed ’ee, if you had encouraged him to do it, ever so"
110 "little.”"
110 ""
110 "“No, no,” said she."
110 ""
110 "The gaiety with which they had set out had somehow vanished; and yet"
110 "there was no enmity or malice between them. They were generous young"
110 "souls; they had been reared in the lonely country nooks where fatalism"
110 "is a strong sentiment, and they did not blame her. Such supplanting was"
110 "to be."
110 ""
110 "Tess’s heart ached. There was no concealing from herself the fact that"
110 "she loved Angel Clare, perhaps all the more passionately from knowing"
110 "that the others had also lost their hearts to him. There is contagion"
110 "in this sentiment, especially among women. And yet that same hungry"
110 "nature had fought against this, but too feebly, and the natural result"
110 "had followed."
110 ""
110 "“I will never stand in your way, nor in the way of either of you!” she"
110 "declared to Retty that night in the bedroom (her tears running down)."
110 "“I can’t help this, my dear! I don’t think marrying is in his mind at"
110 "all; but if he were ever to ask me I should refuse him, as I should"
110 "refuse any man.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh! would you? Why?” said wondering Retty."
110 ""
110 "“It cannot be! But I will be plain. Putting myself quite on one side, I"
110 "don’t think he will choose either of you.”"
110 ""
110 "“I have never expected it—thought of it!” moaned Retty. “But O! I wish"
110 "I was dead!”"
110 ""
110 "The poor child, torn by a feeling which she hardly understood, turned"
110 "to the other two girls who came upstairs just then."
110 ""
110 "“We be friends with her again,” she said to them. “She thinks no more"
110 "of his choosing her than we do.”"
110 ""
110 "So the reserve went off, and they were confiding and warm."
110 ""
110 "“I don’t seem to care what I do now,” said Marian, whose mood was"
110 "turned to its lowest bass. “I was going to marry a dairyman at"
110 "Stickleford, who’s asked me twice; but—my soul—I would put an end to"
110 "myself rather’n be his wife now! Why don’t ye speak, Izz?”"
110 ""
110 "“To confess, then,” murmured Izz, “I made sure to-day that he was going"
110 "to kiss me as he held me; and I lay still against his breast, hoping"
110 "and hoping, and never moved at all. But he did not. I don’t like biding"
110 "here at Talbothays any longer! I shall go hwome.”"
110 ""
110 "The air of the sleeping-chamber seemed to palpitate with the hopeless"
110 "passion of the girls. They writhed feverishly under the oppressiveness"
110 "of an emotion thrust on them by cruel Nature’s law—an emotion which"
110 "they had neither expected nor desired. The incident of the day had"
110 "fanned the flame that was burning the inside of their hearts out, and"
110 "the torture was almost more than they could endure. The differences"
110 "which distinguished them as individuals were abstracted by this"
110 "passion, and each was but portion of one organism called sex. There was"
110 "so much frankness and so little jealousy because there was no hope."
110 "Each one was a girl of fair common sense, and she did not delude"
110 "herself with any vain conceits, or deny her love, or give herself airs,"
110 "in the idea of outshining the others. The full recognition of the"
110 "futility of their infatuation, from a social point of view; its"
110 "purposeless beginning; its self-bounded outlook; its lack of everything"
110 "to justify its existence in the eye of civilization (while lacking"
110 "nothing in the eye of Nature); the one fact that it did exist,"
110 "ecstasizing them to a killing joy—all this imparted to them a"
110 "resignation, a dignity, which a practical and sordid expectation of"
110 "winning him as a husband would have destroyed."
110 ""
110 "They tossed and turned on their little beds, and the cheese-wring"
110 "dripped monotonously downstairs."
110 ""
110 "“B’ you awake, Tess?” whispered one, half-an-hour later."
110 ""
110 "It was Izz Huett’s voice."
110 ""
110 "Tess replied in the affirmative, whereupon also Retty and Marian"
110 "suddenly flung the bedclothes off them, and sighed—"
110 ""
110 "“So be we!”"
110 ""
110 "“I wonder what she is like—the lady they say his family have looked out"
110 "for him!”"
110 ""
110 "“I wonder,” said Izz."
110 ""
110 "“Some lady looked out for him?” gasped Tess, starting. “I have never"
110 "heard o’ that!”"
110 ""
110 "“O yes—’tis whispered; a young lady of his own rank, chosen by his"
110 "family; a Doctor of Divinity’s daughter near his father’s parish of"
110 "Emminster; he don’t much care for her, they say. But he is sure to"
110 "marry her.”"
110 ""
110 "They had heard so very little of this; yet it was enough to build up"
110 "wretched dolorous dreams upon, there in the shade of the night. They"
110 "pictured all the details of his being won round to consent, of the"
110 "wedding preparations, of the bride’s happiness, of her dress and veil,"
110 "of her blissful home with him, when oblivion would have fallen upon"
110 "themselves as far as he and their love were concerned. Thus they"
110 "talked, and ached, and wept till sleep charmed their sorrow away."
110 ""
110 "After this disclosure Tess nourished no further foolish thought that"
110 "there lurked any grave and deliberate import in Clare’s attentions to"
110 "her. It was a passing summer love of her face, for love’s own temporary"
110 "sake—nothing more. And the thorny crown of this sad conception was that"
110 "she whom he really did prefer in a cursory way to the rest, she who"
110 "knew herself to be more impassioned in nature, cleverer, more beautiful"
110 "than they, was in the eyes of propriety far less worthy of him than the"
110 "homelier ones whom he ignored."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXIV>"
110 ""
110 "Amid the oozing fatness and warm ferments of the Froom Vale, at a"
110 "season when the rush of juices could almost be heard below the hiss of"
110 "fertilization, it was impossible that the most fanciful love should not"
110 "grow passionate. The ready bosoms existing there were impregnated by"
110 "their surroundings."
110 ""
110 "July passed over their heads, and the Thermidorean weather which came"
110 "in its wake seemed an effort on the part of Nature to match the state"
110 "of hearts at Talbothays Dairy. The air of the place, so fresh in the"
110 "spring and early summer, was stagnant and enervating now. Its heavy"
110 "scents weighed upon them, and at mid-day the landscape seemed lying in"
110 "a swoon. Ethiopic scorchings browned the upper slopes of the pastures,"
110 "but there was still bright green herbage here where the watercourses"
110 "purled. And as Clare was oppressed by the outward heats, so was he"
110 "burdened inwardly by waxing fervour of passion for the soft and silent"
110 "Tess."
110 ""
110 "The rains having passed, the uplands were dry. The wheels of the"
110 "dairyman’s spring-cart, as he sped home from market, licked up the"
110 "pulverized surface of the highway, and were followed by white ribands"
110 "of dust, as if they had set a thin powder-train on fire. The cows"
110 "jumped wildly over the five-barred barton-gate, maddened by the"
110 "gad-fly; Dairyman Crick kept his shirt-sleeves permanently rolled up"
110 "from Monday to Saturday; open windows had no effect in ventilation"
110 "without open doors, and in the dairy-garden the blackbirds and thrushes"
110 "crept about under the currant-bushes, rather in the manner of"
110 "quadrupeds than of winged creatures. The flies in the kitchen were"
110 "lazy, teasing, and familiar, crawling about in the unwonted places, on"
110 "the floors, into drawers, and over the backs of the milkmaids’ hands."
110 "Conversations were concerning sunstroke; while butter-making, and still"
110 "more butter-keeping, was a despair."
110 ""
110 "They milked entirely in the meads for coolness and convenience, without"
110 "driving in the cows. During the day the animals obsequiously followed"
110 "the shadow of the smallest tree as it moved round the stem with the"
110 "diurnal roll; and when the milkers came they could hardly stand still"
110 "for the flies."
110 ""
110 "On one of these afternoons four or five unmilked cows chanced to stand"
110 "apart from the general herd, behind the corner of a hedge, among them"
110 "being Dumpling and Old Pretty, who loved Tess’s hands above those of"
110 "any other maid. When she rose from her stool under a finished cow,"
110 "Angel Clare, who had been observing her for some time, asked her if she"
110 "would take the aforesaid creatures next. She silently assented, and"
110 "with her stool at arm’s length, and the pail against her knee, went"
110 "round to where they stood. Soon the sound of Old Pretty’s milk fizzing"
110 "into the pail came through the hedge, and then Angel felt inclined to"
110 "go round the corner also, to finish off a hard-yielding milcher who had"
110 "strayed there, he being now as capable of this as the dairyman himself."
110 ""
110 "All the men, and some of the women, when milking, dug their foreheads"
110 "into the cows and gazed into the pail. But a few—mainly the younger"
110 "ones—rested their heads sideways. This was Tess Durbeyfield’s habit,"
110 "her temple pressing the milcher’s flank, her eyes fixed on the far end"
110 "of the meadow with the quiet of one lost in meditation. She was milking"
110 "Old Pretty thus, and the sun chancing to be on the milking-side, it"
110 "shone flat upon her pink-gowned form and her white curtain-bonnet, and"
110 "upon her profile, rendering it keen as a cameo cut from the dun"
110 "background of the cow."
110 ""
110 "She did not know that Clare had followed her round, and that he sat"
110 "under his cow watching her. The stillness of her head and features was"
110 "remarkable: she might have been in a trance, her eyes open, yet"
110 "unseeing. Nothing in the picture moved but Old Pretty’s tail and Tess’s"
110 "pink hands, the latter so gently as to be a rhythmic pulsation only, as"
110 "if they were obeying a reflex stimulus, like a beating heart."
110 ""
110 "How very lovable her face was to him. Yet there was nothing ethereal"
110 "about it; all was real vitality, real warmth, real incarnation. And it"
110 "was in her mouth that this culminated. Eyes almost as deep and speaking"
110 "he had seen before, and cheeks perhaps as fair; brows as arched, a chin"
110 "and throat almost as shapely; her mouth he had seen nothing to equal on"
110 "the face of the earth. To a young man with the least fire in him that"
110 "little upward lift in the middle of her red top lip was distracting,"
110 "infatuating, maddening. He had never before seen a woman’s lips and"
110 "teeth which forced upon his mind with such persistent iteration the old"
110 "Elizabethan simile of roses filled with snow. Perfect, he, as a lover,"
110 "might have called them off-hand. But no—they were not perfect. And it"
110 "was the touch of the imperfect upon the would-be perfect that gave the"
110 "sweetness, because it was that which gave the humanity."
110 ""
110 "Clare had studied the curves of those lips so many times that he could"
110 "reproduce them mentally with ease: and now, as they again confronted"
110 "him, clothed with colour and life, they sent an _aura_ over his flesh,"
110 "a breeze through his nerves, which well nigh produced a qualm; and"
110 "actually produced, by some mysterious physiological process, a prosaic"
110 "sneeze."
110 ""
110 "She then became conscious that he was observing her; but she would not"
110 "show it by any change of position, though the curious dream-like fixity"
110 "disappeared, and a close eye might easily have discerned that the"
110 "rosiness of her face deepened, and then faded till only a tinge of it"
110 "was left."
110 ""
110 "The influence that had passed into Clare like an excitation from the"
110 "sky did not die down. Resolutions, reticences, prudences, fears, fell"
110 "back like a defeated battalion. He jumped up from his seat, and,"
110 "leaving his pail to be kicked over if the milcher had such a mind, went"
110 "quickly towards the desire of his eyes, and, kneeling down beside her,"
110 "clasped her in his arms."
110 ""
110 "Tess was taken completely by surprise, and she yielded to his embrace"
110 "with unreflecting inevitableness. Having seen that it was really her"
110 "lover who had advanced, and no one else, her lips parted, and she sank"
110 "upon him in her momentary joy, with something very like an ecstatic"
110 "cry."
110 ""
110 "He had been on the point of kissing that too tempting mouth, but he"
110 "checked himself, for tender conscience’ sake."
110 ""
110 "“Forgive me, Tess dear!” he whispered. “I ought to have asked. I—did"
110 "not know what I was doing. I do not mean it as a liberty. I am devoted"
110 "to you, Tessy, dearest, in all sincerity!”"
110 ""
110 "Old Pretty by this time had looked round, puzzled; and seeing two"
110 "people crouching under her where, by immemorial custom, there should"
110 "have been only one, lifted her hind leg crossly."
110 ""
110 "“She is angry—she doesn’t know what we mean—she’ll kick over the milk!”"
110 "exclaimed Tess, gently striving to free herself, her eyes concerned"
110 "with the quadruped’s actions, her heart more deeply concerned with"
110 "herself and Clare."
110 ""
110 "She slipped up from her seat, and they stood together, his arm still"
110 "encircling her. Tess’s eyes, fixed on distance, began to fill."
110 ""
110 "“Why do you cry, my darling?” he said."
110 ""
110 "“O—I don’t know!” she murmured."
110 ""
110 "As she saw and felt more clearly the position she was in she became"
110 "agitated and tried to withdraw."
110 ""
110 "“Well, I have betrayed my feeling, Tess, at last,” said he, with a"
110 "curious sigh of desperation, signifying unconsciously that his heart"
110 "had outrun his judgement. “That I—love you dearly and truly I need not"
110 "say. But I—it shall go no further now—it distresses you—I am as"
110 "surprised as you are. You will not think I have presumed upon your"
110 "defencelessness—been too quick and unreflecting, will you?”"
110 ""
110 "“N’—I can’t tell.”"
110 ""
110 "He had allowed her to free herself; and in a minute or two the milking"
110 "of each was resumed. Nobody had beheld the gravitation of the two into"
110 "one; and when the dairyman came round by that screened nook a few"
110 "minutes later, there was not a sign to reveal that the markedly"
110 "sundered pair were more to each other than mere acquaintance. Yet in"
110 "the interval since Crick’s last view of them something had occurred"
110 "which changed the pivot of the universe for their two natures;"
110 "something which, had he known its quality, the dairyman would have"
110 "despised, as a practical man; yet which was based upon a more stubborn"
110 "and resistless tendency than a whole heap of so-called practicalities."
110 "A veil had been whisked aside; the tract of each one’s outlook was to"
110 "have a new horizon thenceforward—for a short time or for a long."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "End of Phase the Third"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "Phase the Fourth:"
110 ""
110 "The Consequence"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXV>"
110 ""
110 "Clare, restless, went out into the dusk when evening drew on, she who"
110 "had won him having retired to her chamber."
110 ""
110 "The night was as sultry as the day. There was no coolness after dark"
110 "unless on the grass. Roads, garden-paths, the house-fronts, the"
110 "barton-walls were warm as hearths, and reflected the noontime"
110 "temperature into the noctambulist’s face."
110 ""
110 "He sat on the east gate of the dairy-yard, and knew not what to think"
110 "of himself. Feeling had indeed smothered judgement that day."
110 ""
110 "Since the sudden embrace, three hours before, the twain had kept apart."
110 "She seemed stilled, almost alarmed, at what had occurred, while the"
110 "novelty, unpremeditation, mastery of circumstance disquieted"
110 "him—palpitating, contemplative being that he was. He could hardly"
110 "realize their true relations to each other as yet, and what their"
110 "mutual bearing should be before third parties thenceforward."
110 ""
110 "Angel had come as pupil to this dairy in the idea that his temporary"
110 "existence here was to be the merest episode in his life, soon passed"
110 "through and early forgotten; he had come as to a place from which as"
110 "from a screened alcove he could calmly view the absorbing world"
110 "without, and, apostrophizing it with Walt Whitman—"
110 ""
110 "    Crowds of men and women attired in the usual costumes,"
110 "    How curious you are to me!—"
110 ""
110 "resolve upon a plan for plunging into that world anew. But behold, the"
110 "absorbing scene had been imported hither. What had been the engrossing"
110 "world had dissolved into an uninteresting outer dumb-show; while here,"
110 "in this apparently dim and unimpassioned place, novelty had"
110 "volcanically started up, as it had never, for him, started up"
110 "elsewhere."
110 ""
110 "Every window of the house being open, Clare could hear across the yard"
110 "each trivial sound of the retiring household. The dairy-house, so"
110 "humble, so insignificant, so purely to him a place of constrained"
110 "sojourn that he had never hitherto deemed it of sufficient importance"
110 "to be reconnoitred as an object of any quality whatever in the"
110 "landscape; what was it now? The aged and lichened brick gables breathed"
110 "forth “Stay!” The windows smiled, the door coaxed and beckoned, the"
110 "creeper blushed confederacy. A personality within it was so"
110 "far-reaching in her influence as to spread into and make the bricks,"
110 "mortar, and whole overhanging sky throb with a burning sensibility."
110 "Whose was this mighty personality? A milkmaid’s."
110 ""
110 "It was amazing, indeed, to find how great a matter the life of the"
110 "obscure dairy had become to him. And though new love was to be held"
110 "partly responsible for this, it was not solely so. Many besides Angel"
110 "have learnt that the magnitude of lives is not as to their external"
110 "displacements, but as to their subjective experiences. The"
110 "impressionable peasant leads a larger, fuller, more dramatic life than"
110 "the pachydermatous king. Looking at it thus, he found that life was to"
110 "be seen of the same magnitude here as elsewhere."
110 ""
110 "Despite his heterodoxy, faults, and weaknesses, Clare was a man with a"
110 "conscience. Tess was no insignificant creature to toy with and dismiss;"
110 "but a woman living her precious life—a life which, to herself who"
110 "endured or enjoyed it, possessed as great a dimension as the life of"
110 "the mightiest to himself. Upon her sensations the whole world depended"
110 "to Tess; through her existence all her fellow-creatures existed, to"
110 "her. The universe itself only came into being for Tess on the"
110 "particular day in the particular year in which she was born."
110 ""
110 "This consciousness upon which he had intruded was the single"
110 "opportunity of existence ever vouchsafed to Tess by an unsympathetic"
110 "First Cause—her all; her every and only chance. How then should he look"
110 "upon her as of less consequence than himself; as a pretty trifle to"
110 "caress and grow weary of; and not deal in the greatest seriousness with"
110 "the affection which he knew that he had awakened in her—so fervid and"
110 "so impressionable as she was under her reserve—in order that it might"
110 "not agonize and wreck her?"
110 ""
110 "To encounter her daily in the accustomed manner would be to develop"
110 "what had begun. Living in such close relations, to meet meant to fall"
110 "into endearment; flesh and blood could not resist it; and, having"
110 "arrived at no conclusion as to the issue of such a tendency, he decided"
110 "to hold aloof for the present from occupations in which they would be"
110 "mutually engaged. As yet the harm done was small."
110 ""
110 "But it was not easy to carry out the resolution never to approach her."
110 "He was driven towards her by every heave of his pulse."
110 ""
110 "He thought he would go and see his friends. It might be possible to"
110 "sound them upon this. In less than five months his term here would have"
110 "ended, and after a few additional months spent upon other farms he"
110 "would be fully equipped in agricultural knowledge and in a position to"
110 "start on his own account. Would not a farmer want a wife, and should a"
110 "farmer’s wife be a drawing-room wax-figure, or a woman who understood"
110 "farming? Notwithstanding the pleasing answer returned to him by the"
110 "silence, he resolved to go his journey."
110 ""
110 "One morning when they sat down to breakfast at Talbothays Dairy some"
110 "maid observed that she had not seen anything of Mr Clare that day."
110 ""
110 "“O no,” said Dairyman Crick. “Mr Clare has gone hwome to Emminster to"
110 "spend a few days wi’ his kinsfolk.”"
110 ""
110 "For four impassioned ones around that table the sunshine of the morning"
110 "went out at a stroke, and the birds muffled their song. But neither"
110 "girl by word or gesture revealed her blankness. “He’s getting on"
110 "towards the end of his time wi’ me,” added the dairyman, with a phlegm"
110 "which unconsciously was brutal; “and so I suppose he is beginning to"
110 "see about his plans elsewhere.”"
110 ""
110 "“How much longer is he to bide here?” asked Izz Huett, the only one of"
110 "the gloom-stricken bevy who could trust her voice with the question."
110 ""
110 "The others waited for the dairyman’s answer as if their lives hung upon"
110 "it; Retty, with parted lips, gazing on the tablecloth, Marian with heat"
110 "added to her redness, Tess throbbing and looking out at the meads."
110 ""
110 "“Well, I can’t mind the exact day without looking at my"
110 "memorandum-book,” replied Crick, with the same intolerable unconcern."
110 "“And even that may be altered a bit. He’ll bide to get a little"
110 "practice in the calving out at the straw-yard, for certain. He’ll hang"
110 "on till the end of the year I should say.”"
110 ""
110 "Four months or so of torturing ecstasy in his society—of “pleasure"
110 "girdled about with pain”. After that the blackness of unutterable"
110 "night."
110 ""
110 "At this moment of the morning Angel Clare was riding along a narrow"
110 "lane ten miles distant from the breakfasters, in the direction of his"
110 "father’s Vicarage at Emminster, carrying, as well as he could, a little"
110 "basket which contained some black-puddings and a bottle of mead, sent"
110 "by Mrs Crick, with her kind respects, to his parents. The white lane"
110 "stretched before him, and his eyes were upon it; but they were staring"
110 "into next year, and not at the lane. He loved her; ought he to marry"
110 "her? Dared he to marry her? What would his mother and his brothers say?"
110 "What would he himself say a couple of years after the event? That would"
110 "depend upon whether the germs of staunch comradeship underlay the"
110 "temporary emotion, or whether it were a sensuous joy in her form only,"
110 "with no substratum of everlastingness."
110 ""
110 "His father’s hill-surrounded little town, the Tudor church-tower of red"
110 "stone, the clump of trees near the Vicarage, came at last into view"
110 "beneath him, and he rode down towards the well-known gate. Casting a"
110 "glance in the direction of the church before entering his home, he"
110 "beheld standing by the vestry-door a group of girls, of ages between"
110 "twelve and sixteen, apparently awaiting the arrival of some other one,"
110 "who in a moment became visible; a figure somewhat older than the"
110 "school-girls, wearing a broad-brimmed hat and highly-starched cambric"
110 "morning-gown, with a couple of books in her hand."
110 ""
110 "Clare knew her well. He could not be sure that she observed him; he"
110 "hoped she did not, so as to render it unnecessary that he should go and"
110 "speak to her, blameless creature that she was. An overpowering"
110 "reluctance to greet her made him decide that she had not seen him. The"
110 "young lady was Miss Mercy Chant, the only daughter of his father’s"
110 "neighbour and friend, whom it was his parents’ quiet hope that he might"
110 "wed some day. She was great at Antinomianism and Bible-classes, and was"
110 "plainly going to hold a class now. Clare’s mind flew to the"
110 "impassioned, summer-steeped heathens in the Var Vale, their rosy faces"
110 "court-patched with cow-droppings; and to one the most impassioned of"
110 "them all."
110 ""
110 "It was on the impulse of the moment that he had resolved to trot over"
110 "to Emminster, and hence had not written to apprise his mother and"
110 "father, aiming, however, to arrive about the breakfast hour, before"
110 "they should have gone out to their parish duties. He was a little late,"
110 "and they had already sat down to the morning meal. The group at the"
110 "table jumped up to welcome him as soon as he entered. They were his"
110 "father and mother, his brother the Reverend Felix—curate at a town in"
110 "the adjoining county, home for the inside of a fortnight—and his other"
110 "brother, the Reverend Cuthbert, the classical scholar, and Fellow and"
110 "Dean of his College, down from Cambridge for the long vacation. His"
110 "mother appeared in a cap and silver spectacles, and his father looked"
110 "what in fact he was—an earnest, God-fearing man, somewhat gaunt, in"
110 "years about sixty-five, his pale face lined with thought and purpose."
110 "Over their heads hung the picture of Angel’s sister, the eldest of the"
110 "family, sixteen years his senior, who had married a missionary and gone"
110 "out to Africa."
110 ""
110 "Old Mr Clare was a clergyman of a type which, within the last twenty"
110 "years, has well nigh dropped out of contemporary life. A spiritual"
110 "descendant in the direct line from Wycliff, Huss, Luther, Calvin; an"
110 "Evangelical of the Evangelicals, a Conversionist, a man of Apostolic"
110 "simplicity in life and thought, he had in his raw youth made up his"
110 "mind once for all in the deeper questions of existence, and admitted no"
110 "further reasoning on them thenceforward. He was regarded even by those"
110 "of his own date and school of thinking as extreme; while, on the other"
110 "hand, those totally opposed to him were unwillingly won to admiration"
110 "for his thoroughness, and for the remarkable power he showed in"
110 "dismissing all question as to principles in his energy for applying"
110 "them. He loved Paul of Tarsus, liked St John, hated St James as much as"
110 "he dared, and regarded with mixed feelings Timothy, Titus, and"
110 "Philemon. The New Testament was less a Christiad then a Pauliad to his"
110 "intelligence—less an argument than an intoxication. His creed of"
110 "determinism was such that it almost amounted to a vice, and quite"
110 "amounted, on its negative side, to a renunciative philosophy which had"
110 "cousinship with that of Schopenhauer and Leopardi. He despised the"
110 "Canons and Rubric, swore by the Articles, and deemed himself consistent"
110 "through the whole category—which in a way he might have been. One thing"
110 "he certainly was—sincere."
110 ""
110 "To the aesthetic, sensuous, pagan pleasure in natural life and lush"
110 "womanhood which his son Angel had lately been experiencing in Var Vale,"
110 "his temper would have been antipathetic in a high degree, had he either"
110 "by inquiry or imagination been able to apprehend it. Once upon a time"
110 "Angel had been so unlucky as to say to his father, in a moment of"
110 "irritation, that it might have resulted far better for mankind if"
110 "Greece had been the source of the religion of modern civilization, and"
110 "not Palestine; and his father’s grief was of that blank description"
110 "which could not realize that there might lurk a thousandth part of a"
110 "truth, much less a half truth or a whole truth, in such a proposition."
110 "He had simply preached austerely at Angel for some time after. But the"
110 "kindness of his heart was such that he never resented anything for"
110 "long, and welcomed his son to-day with a smile which was as candidly"
110 "sweet as a child’s."
110 ""
110 "Angel sat down, and the place felt like home; yet he did not so much as"
110 "formerly feel himself one of the family gathered there. Every time that"
110 "he returned hither he was conscious of this divergence, and since he"
110 "had last shared in the Vicarage life it had grown even more distinctly"
110 "foreign to his own than usual. Its transcendental aspirations—still"
110 "unconsciously based on the geocentric view of things, a zenithal"
110 "paradise, a nadiral hell—were as foreign to his own as if they had been"
110 "the dreams of people on another planet. Latterly he had seen only Life,"
110 "felt only the great passionate pulse of existence, unwarped,"
110 "uncontorted, untrammelled by those creeds which futilely attempt to"
110 "check what wisdom would be content to regulate."
110 ""
110 "On their part they saw a great difference in him, a growing divergence"
110 "from the Angel Clare of former times. It was chiefly a difference in"
110 "his manner that they noticed just now, particularly his brothers. He"
110 "was getting to behave like a farmer; he flung his legs about; the"
110 "muscles of his face had grown more expressive; his eyes looked as much"
110 "information as his tongue spoke, and more. The manner of the scholar"
110 "had nearly disappeared; still more the manner of the drawing-room young"
110 "man. A prig would have said that he had lost culture, and a prude that"
110 "he had become coarse. Such was the contagion of domiciliary fellowship"
110 "with the Talbothays nymphs and swains."
110 ""
110 "After breakfast he walked with his two brothers, non-evangelical,"
110 "well-educated, hall-marked young men, correct to their remotest fibre,"
110 "such unimpeachable models as are turned out yearly by the lathe of a"
110 "systematic tuition. They were both somewhat short-sighted, and when it"
110 "was the custom to wear a single eyeglass and string they wore a single"
110 "eyeglass and string; when it was the custom to wear a double glass they"
110 "wore a double glass; when it was the custom to wear spectacles they"
110 "wore spectacles straightway, all without reference to the particular"
110 "variety of defect in their own vision. When Wordsworth was enthroned"
110 "they carried pocket copies; and when Shelley was belittled they allowed"
110 "him to grow dusty on their shelves. When Correggio’s Holy Families were"
110 "admired, they admired Correggio’s Holy Families; when he was decried in"
110 "favour of Velasquez, they sedulously followed suit without any personal"
110 "objection."
110 ""
110 "If these two noticed Angel’s growing social ineptness, he noticed their"
110 "growing mental limitations. Felix seemed to him all Church; Cuthbert"
110 "all College. His Diocesan Synod and Visitations were the mainsprings of"
110 "the world to the one; Cambridge to the other. Each brother candidly"
110 "recognized that there were a few unimportant score of millions of"
110 "outsiders in civilized society, persons who were neither University men"
110 "nor churchmen; but they were to be tolerated rather than reckoned with"
110 "and respected."
110 ""
110 "They were both dutiful and attentive sons, and were regular in their"
110 "visits to their parents. Felix, though an offshoot from a far more"
110 "recent point in the devolution of theology than his father, was less"
110 "self-sacrificing and disinterested. More tolerant than his father of a"
110 "contradictory opinion, in its aspect as a danger to its holder, he was"
110 "less ready than his father to pardon it as a slight to his own"
110 "teaching. Cuthbert was, upon the whole, the more liberal-minded,"
110 "though, with greater subtlety, he had not so much heart."
110 ""
110 "As they walked along the hillside Angel’s former feeling revived in"
110 "him—that whatever their advantages by comparison with himself, neither"
110 "saw or set forth life as it really was lived. Perhaps, as with many"
110 "men, their opportunities of observation were not so good as their"
110 "opportunities of expression. Neither had an adequate conception of the"
110 "complicated forces at work outside the smooth and gentle current in"
110 "which they and their associates floated. Neither saw the difference"
110 "between local truth and universal truth; that what the inner world said"
110 "in their clerical and academic hearing was quite a different thing from"
110 "what the outer world was thinking."
110 ""
110 "“I suppose it is farming or nothing for you now, my dear fellow,” Felix"
110 "was saying, among other things, to his youngest brother, as he looked"
110 "through his spectacles at the distant fields with sad austerity. “And,"
110 "therefore, we must make the best of it. But I do entreat you to"
110 "endeavour to keep as much as possible in touch with moral ideals."
110 "Farming, of course, means roughing it externally; but high thinking may"
110 "go with plain living, nevertheless.”"
110 ""
110 "“Of course it may,” said Angel. “Was it not proved nineteen hundred"
110 "years ago—if I may trespass upon your domain a little? Why should you"
110 "think, Felix, that I am likely to drop my high thinking and my moral"
110 "ideals?”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, I fancied, from the tone of your letters and our conversation—it"
110 "may be fancy only—that you were somehow losing intellectual grasp."
110 "Hasn’t it struck you, Cuthbert?”"
110 ""
110 "“Now, Felix,” said Angel drily, “we are very good friends, you know;"
110 "each of us treading our allotted circles; but if it comes to"
110 "intellectual grasp, I think you, as a contented dogmatist, had better"
110 "leave mine alone, and inquire what has become of yours.”"
110 ""
110 "They returned down the hill to dinner, which was fixed at any time at"
110 "which their father’s and mother’s morning work in the parish usually"
110 "concluded. Convenience as regarded afternoon callers was the last thing"
110 "to enter into the consideration of unselfish Mr and Mrs Clare; though"
110 "the three sons were sufficiently in unison on this matter to wish that"
110 "their parents would conform a little to modern notions."
110 ""
110 "The walk had made them hungry, Angel in particular, who was now an"
110 "outdoor man, accustomed to the profuse _dapes inemptae_ of the"
110 "dairyman’s somewhat coarsely-laden table. But neither of the old people"
110 "had arrived, and it was not till the sons were almost tired of waiting"
110 "that their parents entered. The self-denying pair had been occupied in"
110 "coaxing the appetites of some of their sick parishioners, whom they,"
110 "somewhat inconsistently, tried to keep imprisoned in the flesh, their"
110 "own appetites being quite forgotten."
110 ""
110 "The family sat down to table, and a frugal meal of cold viands was"
110 "deposited before them. Angel looked round for Mrs Crick’s"
110 "black-puddings, which he had directed to be nicely grilled as they did"
110 "them at the dairy, and of which he wished his father and mother to"
110 "appreciate the marvellous herbal savours as highly as he did himself."
110 ""
110 "“Ah! you are looking for the black-puddings, my dear boy,” observed"
110 "Clare’s mother. “But I am sure you will not mind doing without them as"
110 "I am sure your father and I shall not, when you know the reason. I"
110 "suggested to him that we should take Mrs Crick’s kind present to the"
110 "children of the man who can earn nothing just now because of his"
110 "attacks of delirium tremens; and he agreed that it would be a great"
110 "pleasure to them; so we did.”"
110 ""
110 "“Of course,” said Angel cheerfully, looking round for the mead."
110 ""
110 "“I found the mead so extremely alcoholic,” continued his mother, “that"
110 "it was quite unfit for use as a beverage, but as valuable as rum or"
110 "brandy in an emergency; so I have put it in my medicine-closet.”"
110 ""
110 "“We never drink spirits at this table, on principle,” added his father."
110 ""
110 "“But what shall I tell the dairyman’s wife?” said Angel."
110 ""
110 "“The truth, of course,” said his father."
110 ""
110 "“I rather wanted to say we enjoyed the mead and the black-puddings very"
110 "much. She is a kind, jolly sort of body, and is sure to ask me directly"
110 "I return.”"
110 ""
110 "“You cannot, if we did not,” Mr Clare answered lucidly."
110 ""
110 "“Ah—no; though that mead was a drop of pretty tipple.”"
110 ""
110 "“A what?” said Cuthbert and Felix both."
110 ""
110 "“Oh—’tis an expression they use down at Talbothays,” replied Angel,"
110 "blushing. He felt that his parents were right in their practice if"
110 "wrong in their want of sentiment, and said no more."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXVI>"
110 ""
110 "It was not till the evening, after family prayers, that Angel found"
110 "opportunity of broaching to his father one or two subjects near his"
110 "heart. He had strung himself up to the purpose while kneeling behind"
110 "his brothers on the carpet, studying the little nails in the heels of"
110 "their walking boots. When the service was over they went out of the"
110 "room with their mother, and Mr Clare and himself were left alone."
110 ""
110 "The young man first discussed with the elder his plans for the"
110 "attainment of his position as a farmer on an extensive scale—either in"
110 "England or in the Colonies. His father then told him that, as he had"
110 "not been put to the expense of sending Angel up to Cambridge, he had"
110 "felt it his duty to set by a sum of money every year towards the"
110 "purchase or lease of land for him some day, that he might not feel"
110 "himself unduly slighted."
110 ""
110 "“As far as worldly wealth goes,” continued his father, “you will no"
110 "doubt stand far superior to your brothers in a few years.”"
110 ""
110 "This considerateness on old Mr Clare’s part led Angel onward to the"
110 "other and dearer subject. He observed to his father that he was then"
110 "six-and-twenty, and that when he should start in the farming business"
110 "he would require eyes in the back of his head to see to all"
110 "matters—some one would be necessary to superintend the domestic labours"
110 "of his establishment whilst he was afield. Would it not be well,"
110 "therefore, for him to marry?"
110 ""
110 "His father seemed to think this idea not unreasonable; and then Angel"
110 "put the question—"
110 ""
110 "“What kind of wife do you think would be best for me as a thrifty"
110 "hard-working farmer?”"
110 ""
110 "“A truly Christian woman, who will be a help and a comfort to you in"
110 "your goings-out and your comings-in. Beyond that, it really matters"
110 "little. Such an one can be found; indeed, my earnest-minded friend and"
110 "neighbour, Dr Chant—”"
110 ""
110 "“But ought she not primarily to be able to milk cows, churn good"
110 "butter, make immense cheeses; know how to sit hens and turkeys and rear"
110 "chickens, to direct a field of labourers in an emergency, and estimate"
110 "the value of sheep and calves?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes; a farmer’s wife; yes, certainly. It would be desirable.” Mr"
110 "Clare, the elder, had plainly never thought of these points before. “I"
110 "was going to add,” he said, “that for a pure and saintly woman you will"
110 "not find one more to your true advantage, and certainly not more to"
110 "your mother’s mind and my own, than your friend Mercy, whom you used to"
110 "show a certain interest in. It is true that my neighbour Chant’s"
110 "daughter had lately caught up the fashion of the younger clergy round"
110 "about us for decorating the Communion-table—alter, as I was shocked to"
110 "hear her call it one day—with flowers and other stuff on festival"
110 "occasions. But her father, who is quite as opposed to such flummery as"
110 "I, says that can be cured. It is a mere girlish outbreak which, I am"
110 "sure, will not be permanent.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, yes; Mercy is good and devout, I know. But, father, don’t you"
110 "think that a young woman equally pure and virtuous as Miss Chant, but"
110 "one who, in place of that lady’s ecclesiastical accomplishments,"
110 "understands the duties of farm life as well as a farmer himself, would"
110 "suit me infinitely better?”"
110 ""
110 "His father persisted in his conviction that a knowledge of a farmer’s"
110 "wife’s duties came second to a Pauline view of humanity; and the"
110 "impulsive Angel, wishing to honour his father’s feelings and to advance"
110 "the cause of his heart at the same time, grew specious. He said that"
110 "fate or Providence had thrown in his way a woman who possessed every"
110 "qualification to be the helpmate of an agriculturist, and was decidedly"
110 "of a serious turn of mind. He would not say whether or not she had"
110 "attached herself to the sound Low Church School of his father; but she"
110 "would probably be open to conviction on that point; she was a regular"
110 "church-goer of simple faith; honest-hearted, receptive, intelligent,"
110 "graceful to a degree, chaste as a vestal, and, in personal appearance,"
110 "exceptionally beautiful."
110 ""
110 "“Is she of a family such as you would care to marry into—a lady, in"
110 "short?” asked his startled mother, who had come softly into the study"
110 "during the conversation."
110 ""
110 "“She is not what in common parlance is called a lady,” said Angel,"
110 "unflinchingly, “for she is a cottager’s daughter, as I am proud to say."
110 "But she _is_ a lady, nevertheless—in feeling and nature.”"
110 ""
110 "“Mercy Chant is of a very good family.”"
110 ""
110 "“Pooh!—what’s the advantage of that, mother?” said Angel quickly. “How"
110 "is family to avail the wife of a man who has to rough it as I have, and"
110 "shall have to do?”"
110 ""
110 "“Mercy is accomplished. And accomplishments have their charm,” returned"
110 "his mother, looking at him through her silver spectacles."
110 ""
110 "“As to external accomplishments, what will be the use of them in the"
110 "life I am going to lead?—while as to her reading, I can take that in"
110 "hand. She’ll be apt pupil enough, as you would say if you knew her."
110 "She’s brim full of poetry—actualized poetry, if I may use the"
110 "expression. She _lives_ what paper-poets only write... And she is an"
110 "unimpeachable Christian, I am sure; perhaps of the very tribe, genus,"
110 "and species you desire to propagate.”"
110 ""
110 "“O Angel, you are mocking!”"
110 ""
110 "“Mother, I beg pardon. But as she really does attend Church almost"
110 "every Sunday morning, and is a good Christian girl, I am sure you will"
110 "tolerate any social shortcomings for the sake of that quality, and feel"
110 "that I may do worse than choose her.” Angel waxed quite earnest on that"
110 "rather automatic orthodoxy in his beloved Tess which (never dreaming"
110 "that it might stand him in such good stead) he had been prone to slight"
110 "when observing it practised by her and the other milkmaids, because of"
110 "its obvious unreality amid beliefs essentially naturalistic."
110 ""
110 "In their sad doubts as to whether their son had himself any right"
110 "whatever to the title he claimed for the unknown young woman, Mr and"
110 "Mrs Clare began to feel it as an advantage not to be overlooked that"
110 "she at least was sound in her views; especially as the conjunction of"
110 "the pair must have arisen by an act of Providence; for Angel never"
110 "would have made orthodoxy a condition of his choice. They said finally"
110 "that it was better not to act in a hurry, but that they would not"
110 "object to see her."
110 ""
110 "Angel therefore refrained from declaring more particulars now. He felt"
110 "that, single-minded and self-sacrificing as his parents were, there yet"
110 "existed certain latent prejudices of theirs, as middle-class people,"
110 "which it would require some tact to overcome. For though legally at"
110 "liberty to do as he chose, and though their daughter-in-law’s"
110 "qualifications could make no practical difference to their lives, in"
110 "the probability of her living far away from them, he wished for"
110 "affection’s sake not to wound their sentiment in the most important"
110 "decision of his life."
110 ""
110 "He observed his own inconsistencies in dwelling upon accidents in"
110 "Tess’s life as if they were vital features. It was for herself that he"
110 "loved Tess; her soul, her heart, her substance—not for her skill in the"
110 "dairy, her aptness as his scholar, and certainly not for her simple"
110 "formal faith-professions. Her unsophisticated open-air existence"
110 "required no varnish of conventionality to make it palatable to him. He"
110 "held that education had as yet but little affected the beats of emotion"
110 "and impulse on which domestic happiness depends. It was probable that,"
110 "in the lapse of ages, improved systems of moral and intellectual"
110 "training would appreciably, perhaps considerably, elevate the"
110 "involuntary and even the unconscious instincts of human nature; but up"
110 "to the present day, culture, as far as he could see, might be said to"
110 "have affected only the mental epiderm of those lives which had been"
110 "brought under its influence. This belief was confirmed by his"
110 "experience of women, which, having latterly been extended from the"
110 "cultivated middle-class into the rural community, had taught him how"
110 "much less was the intrinsic difference between the good and wise woman"
110 "of one social stratum and the good and wise woman of another social"
110 "stratum, than between the good and bad, the wise and the foolish, of"
110 "the same stratum or class."
110 ""
110 "It was the morning of his departure. His brothers had already left the"
110 "Vicarage to proceed on a walking tour in the north, whence one was to"
110 "return to his college, and the other to his curacy. Angel might have"
110 "accompanied them, but preferred to rejoin his sweetheart at Talbothays."
110 "He would have been an awkward member of the party; for, though the most"
110 "appreciative humanist, the most ideal religionist, even the best-versed"
110 "Christologist of the three, there was alienation in the standing"
110 "consciousness that his squareness would not fit the round hole that had"
110 "been prepared for him. To neither Felix nor Cuthbert had he ventured to"
110 "mention Tess."
110 ""
110 "His mother made him sandwiches, and his father accompanied him, on his"
110 "own mare, a little way along the road. Having fairly well advanced his"
110 "own affairs, Angel listened in a willing silence, as they jogged on"
110 "together through the shady lanes, to his father’s account of his parish"
110 "difficulties, and the coldness of brother clergymen whom he loved,"
110 "because of his strict interpretations of the New Testament by the light"
110 "of what they deemed a pernicious Calvinistic doctrine."
110 ""
110 "“Pernicious!” said Mr Clare, with genial scorn; and he proceeded to"
110 "recount experiences which would show the absurdity of that idea. He"
110 "told of wondrous conversions of evil livers of which he had been the"
110 "instrument, not only amongst the poor, but amongst the rich and"
110 "well-to-do; and he also candidly admitted many failures."
110 ""
110 "As an instance of the latter, he mentioned the case of a young upstart"
110 "squire named d’Urberville, living some forty miles off, in the"
110 "neighbourhood of Trantridge."
110 ""
110 "“Not one of the ancient d’Urbervilles of Kingsbere and other places?”"
110 "asked his son. “That curiously historic worn-out family with its"
110 "ghostly legend of the coach-and-four?”"
110 ""
110 "“O no. The original d’Urbervilles decayed and disappeared sixty or"
110 "eighty years ago—at least, I believe so. This seems to be a new family"
110 "which had taken the name; for the credit of the former knightly line I"
110 "hope they are spurious, I’m sure. But it is odd to hear you express"
110 "interest in old families. I thought you set less store by them even"
110 "than I.”"
110 ""
110 "“You misapprehend me, father; you often do,” said Angel with a little"
110 "impatience. “Politically I am sceptical as to the virtue of their being"
110 "old. Some of the wise even among themselves ‘exclaim against their own"
110 "succession,’ as Hamlet puts it; but lyrically, dramatically, and even"
110 "historically, I am tenderly attached to them.”"
110 ""
110 "This distinction, though by no means a subtle one, was yet too subtle"
110 "for Mr Clare the elder, and he went on with the story he had been about"
110 "to relate; which was that after the death of the senior so-called"
110 "d’Urberville, the young man developed the most culpable passions,"
110 "though he had a blind mother, whose condition should have made him know"
110 "better. A knowledge of his career having come to the ears of Mr Clare,"
110 "when he was in that part of the country preaching missionary sermons,"
110 "he boldly took occasion to speak to the delinquent on his spiritual"
110 "state. Though he was a stranger, occupying another’s pulpit, he had"
110 "felt this to be his duty, and took for his text the words from St Luke:"
110 "“Thou fool, this night thy soul shall be required of thee!” The young"
110 "man much resented this directness of attack, and in the war of words"
110 "which followed when they met he did not scruple publicly to insult Mr"
110 "Clare, without respect for his gray hairs."
110 ""
110 "Angel flushed with distress."
110 ""
110 "“Dear father,” he said sadly, “I wish you would not expose yourself to"
110 "such gratuitous pain from scoundrels!”"
110 ""
110 "“Pain?” said his father, his rugged face shining in the ardour of"
110 "self-abnegation. “The only pain to me was pain on his account, poor,"
110 "foolish young man. Do you suppose his incensed words could give me any"
110 "pain, or even his blows? ‘Being reviled we bless; being persecuted we"
110 "suffer it; being defamed we entreat; we are made as the filth of the"
110 "world, and as the offscouring of all things unto this day.’ Those"
110 "ancient and noble words to the Corinthians are strictly true at this"
110 "present hour.”"
110 ""
110 "“Not blows, father? He did not proceed to blows?”"
110 ""
110 "“No, he did not. Though I have borne blows from men in a mad state of"
110 "intoxication.”"
110 ""
110 "“No!”"
110 ""
110 "“A dozen times, my boy. What then? I have saved them from the guilt of"
110 "murdering their own flesh and blood thereby; and they have lived to"
110 "thank me, and praise God.”"
110 ""
110 "“May this young man do the same!” said Angel fervently. “But I fear"
110 "otherwise, from what you say.”"
110 ""
110 "“We’ll hope, nevertheless,” said Mr Clare. “And I continue to pray for"
110 "him, though on this side of the grave we shall probably never meet"
110 "again. But, after all, one of those poor words of mine may spring up in"
110 "his heart as a good seed some day.”"
110 ""
110 "Now, as always, Clare’s father was sanguine as a child; and though the"
110 "younger could not accept his parent’s narrow dogma, he revered his"
110 "practice and recognized the hero under the pietist. Perhaps he revered"
110 "his father’s practice even more now than ever, seeing that, in the"
110 "question of making Tessy his wife, his father had not once thought of"
110 "inquiring whether she were well provided or penniless. The same"
110 "unworldliness was what had necessitated Angel’s getting a living as a"
110 "farmer, and would probably keep his brothers in the position of poor"
110 "parsons for the term of their activities; yet Angel admired it none the"
110 "less. Indeed, despite his own heterodoxy, Angel often felt that he was"
110 "nearer to his father on the human side than was either of his brethren."
