110 "<CHAPTER XXVII>"
110 ""
110 "An up-hill and down-hill ride of twenty-odd miles through a garish"
110 "mid-day atmosphere brought him in the afternoon to a detached knoll a"
110 "mile or two west of Talbothays, whence he again looked into that green"
110 "trough of sappiness and humidity, the valley of the Var or Froom."
110 "Immediately he began to descend from the upland to the fat alluvial"
110 "soil below, the atmosphere grew heavier; the languid perfume of the"
110 "summer fruits, the mists, the hay, the flowers, formed therein a vast"
110 "pool of odour which at this hour seemed to make the animals, the very"
110 "bees and butterflies drowsy. Clare was now so familiar with the spot"
110 "that he knew the individual cows by their names when, a long distance"
110 "off, he saw them dotted about the meads. It was with a sense of luxury"
110 "that he recognized his power of viewing life here from its inner side,"
110 "in a way that had been quite foreign to him in his student-days; and,"
110 "much as he loved his parents, he could not help being aware that to"
110 "come here, as now, after an experience of home-life, affected him like"
110 "throwing off splints and bandages; even the one customary curb on the"
110 "humours of English rural societies being absent in this place,"
110 "Talbothays having no resident landlord."
110 ""
110 "Not a human being was out of doors at the dairy. The denizens were all"
110 "enjoying the usual afternoon nap of an hour or so which the exceedingly"
110 "early hours kept in summer-time rendered a necessity. At the door the"
110 "wood-hooped pails, sodden and bleached by infinite scrubbings, hung"
110 "like hats on a stand upon the forked and peeled limb of an oak fixed"
110 "there for that purpose; all of them ready and dry for the evening"
110 "milking. Angel entered, and went through the silent passages of the"
110 "house to the back quarters, where he listened for a moment. Sustained"
110 "snores came from the cart-house, where some of the men were lying down;"
110 "the grunt and squeal of sweltering pigs arose from the still further"
110 "distance. The large-leaved rhubarb and cabbage plants slept too, their"
110 "broad limp surfaces hanging in the sun like half-closed umbrellas."
110 ""
110 "He unbridled and fed his horse, and as he re-entered the house the"
110 "clock struck three. Three was the afternoon skimming-hour; and, with"
110 "the stroke, Clare heard the creaking of the floor-boards above, and"
110 "then the touch of a descending foot on the stairs. It was Tess’s, who"
110 "in another moment came down before his eyes."
110 ""
110 "She had not heard him enter, and hardly realized his presence there."
110 "She was yawning, and he saw the red interior of her mouth as if it had"
110 "been a snake’s. She had stretched one arm so high above her coiled-up"
110 "cable of hair that he could see its satin delicacy above the sunburn;"
110 "her face was flushed with sleep, and her eyelids hung heavy over their"
110 "pupils. The brim-fulness of her nature breathed from her. It was a"
110 "moment when a woman’s soul is more incarnate than at any other time;"
110 "when the most spiritual beauty bespeaks itself flesh; and sex takes the"
110 "outside place in the presentation."
110 ""

110 ""
110 "There had not at first been time for her to think of the changed"

110 ""

110 ""

110 "help me to-day. Mrs Crick is gone to market with Mr Crick, and Retty is"
110 "not well, and the others are gone out somewhere, and won’t be home till"

110 ""
110 "As they retreated to the milk-house Deborah Fyander appeared on the"
110 "stairs."
110 ""
110 "“I have come back, Deborah,” said Mr Clare, upwards. “So I can help"

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 "<CHAPTER XXVIII>"
110 ""
110 "Her refusal, though unexpected, did not permanently daunt Clare. His"
110 "experience of women was great enough for him to be aware that the"
110 "negative often meant nothing more than the preface to the affirmative;"
110 "and it was little enough for him not to know that in the manner of the"
110 "present negative there lay a great exception to the dallyings of"
110 "coyness. That she had already permitted him to make love to her he read"
110 "as an additional assurance, not fully trowing that in the fields and"
110 "pastures to “sigh gratis” is by no means deemed waste; love-making"
110 "being here more often accepted inconsiderately and for its own sweet"
110 "sake than in the carking, anxious homes of the ambitious, where a"
110 "girl’s craving for an establishment paralyzes her healthy thought of a"
110 "passion as an end."
110 ""
110 "“Tess, why did you say ‘no’ in such a positive way?” he asked her in"
110 "the course of a few days."
110 ""
110 "She started."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t ask me. I told you why—partly. I am not good enough—not worthy"
110 "enough.”"
110 ""
110 "“How? Not fine lady enough?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes—something like that,” murmured she. “Your friends would scorn me.”"
110 ""
110 "“Indeed, you mistake them—my father and mother. As for my brothers, I"
110 "don’t care—” He clasped his fingers behind her back to keep her from"
110 "slipping away. “Now—you did not mean it, sweet?—I am sure you did not!"
110 "You have made me so restless that I cannot read, or play, or do"
110 "anything. I am in no hurry, Tess, but I want to know—to hear from your"
110 "own warm lips—that you will some day be mine—any time you may choose;"
110 "but some day?”"
110 ""
110 "She could only shake her head and look away from him."
110 ""
110 "Clare regarded her attentively, conned the characters of her face as if"
110 "they had been hieroglyphics. The denial seemed real."
110 ""
110 "“Then I ought not to hold you in this way—ought I? I have no right to"
110 "you—no right to seek out where you are, or walk with you! Honestly,"
110 "Tess, do you love any other man?”"
110 ""
110 "“How can you ask?” she said, with continued self-suppression."
110 ""
110 "“I almost know that you do not. But then, why do you repulse me?”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t repulse you. I like you to—tell me you love me; and you may"
110 "always tell me so as you go about with me—and never offend me.”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Ah—you think so, but you don’t know!”"
110 ""
110 "At such times as this, apprehending the grounds of her refusal to be"

110 "phlegmatic negative."
110 ""
110 "The struggle was so fearful; her own heart was so strongly on the side"
110 "of his—two ardent hearts against one poor little conscience—that she"
110 "tried to fortify her resolution by every means in her power. She had"
110 "come to Talbothays with a made-up mind. On no account could she agree"

110 "his blindness in wedding her. And she held that what her conscience had"
110 "decided for her when her mind was unbiassed ought not to be overruled"
110 "now."
110 ""
110 "“Why don’t somebody tell him all about me?” she said. “It was only"
110 "forty miles off—why hasn’t it reached here? Somebody must know!”"
110 ""
110 "Yet nobody seemed to know; nobody told him."
110 ""
110 "For two or three days no more was said. She guessed from the sad"

110 ""
110 "Tess had never before known a time in which the thread of her life was"

110 ""
110 "They were breaking up the masses of curd before putting them into the"

110 ""

110 "“Do you know why I did that, Tess?” he said."
110 ""
110 "“Because you love me very much!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, and as a preliminary to a new entreaty.”"

110 "“Not _again_!”"
110 ""
110 "She looked a sudden fear that her resistance might break down under her"

110 ""
110 "“O, Tessy!” he went on, “I _cannot_ think why you are so tantalizing."

110 "The stress now getting beyond endurance, her lip quivered, and she was"

110 ""
110 "“Tell me, tell me!” he said, passionately clasping her, in"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Your experiences, dear; yes, certainly; any number.” He expressed"
110 "assent in loving satire, looking into her face. “My Tess, no doubt,"

110 ""
110 "“I will try—not! And I’ll give you my reasons to-morrow—next week.”"
110 ""
110 "“Say on Sunday?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, on Sunday.”"
110 ""
110 "At last she got away, and did not stop in her retreat till she was in"
110 "the thicket of pollard willows at the lower side of the barton, where"
110 "she could be quite unseen. Here Tess flung herself down upon the"
110 "rustling undergrowth of spear-grass, as upon a bed, and remained"
110 "crouching in palpitating misery broken by momentary shoots of joy,"
110 "which her fears about the ending could not altogether suppress."
110 ""
110 "In reality, she was drifting into acquiescence. Every see-saw of her"
110 "breath, every wave of her blood, every pulse singing in her ears, was a"
110 "voice that joined with nature in revolt against her scrupulousness."
110 "Reckless, inconsiderate acceptance of him; to close with him at the"
110 "altar, revealing nothing, and chancing discovery; to snatch ripe"
110 "pleasure before the iron teeth of pain could have time to shut upon"
110 "her: that was what love counselled; and in almost a terror of ecstasy"
110 "Tess divined that, despite her many months of lonely self-chastisement,"
110 "wrestlings, communings, schemes to lead a future of austere isolation,"
110 "love’s counsel would prevail."
110 ""
110 "The afternoon advanced, and still she remained among the willows. She"
110 "heard the rattle of taking down the pails from the forked stands; the"
110 "“waow-waow!” which accompanied the getting together of the cows. But"
110 "she did not go to the milking. They would see her agitation; and the"
110 "dairyman, thinking the cause to be love alone, would good-naturedly"
110 "tease her; and that harassment could not be borne."
110 ""
110 "Her lover must have guessed her overwrought state, and invented some"
110 "excuse for her non-appearance, for no inquiries were made or calls"
110 "given. At half-past six the sun settled down upon the levels with the"
110 "aspect of a great forge in the heavens; and presently a monstrous"
110 "pumpkin-like moon arose on the other hand. The pollard willows,"
110 "tortured out of their natural shape by incessant choppings, became"
110 "spiny-haired monsters as they stood up against it. She went in and"
110 "upstairs without a light."
110 ""
110 "It was now Wednesday. Thursday came, and Angel looked thoughtfully at"
110 "her from a distance, but intruded in no way upon her. The indoor"
110 "milkmaids, Marian and the rest, seemed to guess that something definite"
110 "was afoot, for they did not force any remarks upon her in the"
110 "bedchamber. Friday passed; Saturday. To-morrow was the day."
110 ""
110 "“I shall give way—I shall say yes—I shall let myself marry him—I cannot"
110 "help it!” she jealously panted, with her hot face to the pillow that"
110 "night, on hearing one of the other girls sigh his name in her sleep. “I"
110 "can’t bear to let anybody have him but me! Yet it is a wrong to him,"
110 "and may kill him when he knows! O my heart—O—O—O!”"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXIX>"
110 ""
110 "“Now, who mid ye think I’ve heard news o’ this morning?” said Dairyman"
110 "Crick, as he sat down to breakfast next day, with a riddling gaze round"
110 "upon the munching men and maids. “Now, just who mid ye think?”"
110 ""
110 "One guessed, and another guessed. Mrs Crick did not guess, because she"
110 "knew already."
110 ""
110 "“Well,” said the dairyman, “’tis that slack-twisted ’hore’s-bird of a"
110 "feller, Jack Dollop. He’s lately got married to a widow-woman.”"
110 ""
110 "“Not Jack Dollop? A villain—to think o’ that!” said a milker."
110 ""
110 "The name entered quickly into Tess Durbeyfield’s consciousness, for it"
110 "was the name of the lover who had wronged his sweetheart, and had"
110 "afterwards been so roughly used by the young woman’s mother in the"
110 "butter-churn."
110 ""
110 "“And had he married the valiant matron’s daughter, as he promised?”"

110 "Crick, in her sense of his gentility."
110 ""
110 "“Not he, sir. Never meant to,” replied the dairyman. “As I say, ’tis a"
110 "widow-woman, and she had money, it seems—fifty poun’ a year or so; and"
110 "that was all he was after. They were married in a great hurry; and then"
110 "she told him that by marrying she had lost her fifty poun’ a year. Just"
110 "fancy the state o’ my gentleman’s mind at that news! Never such a"
110 "cat-and-dog life as they’ve been leading ever since! Serves him well"
110 "beright. But onluckily the poor woman gets the worst o’t.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, the silly body should have told en sooner that the ghost of her"
110 "first man would trouble him,” said Mrs Crick."
110 ""

110 ""
110 "He glanced towards the row of girls."
110 ""
110 "“She ought to ha’ told him just before they went to church, when he"

110 ""
110 "“Yes, she ought,” agreed Izz."
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "The laughter which followed this sally was supplemented only by a sorry"

110 ""
110 "Yes, there was the pain of it. This question of a woman telling her"
110 "story—the heaviest of crosses to herself—seemed but amusement to"
110 "others. It was as if people should laugh at martyrdom."
110 ""
110 "“Tessy!” came from behind her, and Clare sprang across the gully,"
110 "alighting beside her feet. “My wife—soon!”"

110 "“No, no; I cannot. For your sake, O Mr Clare; for your sake, I say no!”"
110 ""
110 "“Tess!”"
110 ""
110 "“Still I say no!” she repeated."
110 ""
110 "Not expecting this, he had put his arm lightly round her waist the"
110 "moment after speaking, beneath her hanging tail of hair. (The younger"
110 "dairymaids, including Tess, breakfasted with their hair loose on Sunday"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "Day after day they met—somewhat less constantly than before; and thus"

110 "could see in his eye that he might ask her again."
110 ""
110 "His plan of procedure was different now—as though he had made up his"
110 "mind that her negatives were, after all, only coyness and youth"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "Tess knew that she must break down. Neither a religious sense of a"
110 "certain moral validity in the previous union nor a conscientious wish"

110 "be his wife,” the words were vain. A proof of her weakness lay in the"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "She had run up in her bedgown to his door to call him as usual; then"

110 "In the afternoon several of the dairyman’s household and assistants"

110 ""

110 "Mr Clare volunteered to do so, though it was none of his business,"

110 "<CHAPTER XXX>"
110 ""
110 "In the diminishing daylight they went along the level roadway through"
110 "the meads, which stretched away into gray miles, and were backed in the"
110 "extreme edge of distance by the swarthy and abrupt slopes of Egdon"
110 "Heath. On its summit stood clumps and stretches of fir-trees, whose"
110 "notched tips appeared like battlemented towers crowning black-fronted"
110 "castles of enchantment."
110 ""
110 "They were so absorbed in the sense of being close to each other that"
110 "they did not begin talking for a long while, the silence being broken"
110 "only by the clucking of the milk in the tall cans behind them. The lane"
110 "they followed was so solitary that the hazel nuts had remained on the"
110 "boughs till they slipped from their shells, and the blackberries hung"
110 "in heavy clusters. Every now and then Angel would fling the lash of his"
110 "whip round one of these, pluck it off, and give it to his companion."
110 ""
110 "The dull sky soon began to tell its meaning by sending down"
110 "herald-drops of rain, and the stagnant air of the day changed into a"
110 "fitful breeze which played about their faces. The quick-silvery glaze"
110 "on the rivers and pools vanished; from broad mirrors of light they"
110 "changed to lustreless sheets of lead, with a surface like a rasp. But"
110 "that spectacle did not affect her preoccupation. Her countenance, a"
110 "natural carnation slightly embrowned by the season, had deepened its"
110 "tinge with the beating of the rain-drops; and her hair, which the"
110 "pressure of the cows’ flanks had, as usual, caused to tumble down from"

110 ""
110 "“I ought not to have come, I suppose,” she murmured, looking at the"
110 "sky."

110 "here!”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 "“Yes,” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "They crept along towards a point in the expanse of shade just at hand"

110 "touched had been uncongenial."
110 ""
110 "They reached the feeble light, which came from the smoky lamp of a"

110 ""
110 "“Londoners will drink it at their breakfasts to-morrow, won’t they?”"

110 "<CHAPTER XXXI>"
110 ""
110 "Tess wrote a most touching and urgent letter to her mother the very"
110 "next day, and by the end of the week a response to her communication"
110 "arrived in Joan Durbeyfield’s wandering last-century hand."
110 ""
110 "    Dear Tess,"
110 ""
110 "    J write these few lines Hoping they will find you well, as they"
110 "    leave me at Present, thank God for it. Dear Tess, we are all glad"
110 "    to Hear that you are going really to be married soon. But with"
110 "    respect to your question, Tess, J say between ourselves, quite"
110 "    private but very strong, that on no account do you say a word of"
110 "    your Bygone Trouble to him. J did not tell everything to your"
110 "    Father, he being so Proud on account of his Respectability, which,"
110 "    perhaps, your Intended is the same. Many a woman—some of the"
110 "    Highest in the Land—have had a Trouble in their time; and why"
110 "    should you Trumpet yours when others don’t Trumpet theirs? No girl"
110 "    would be such a Fool, specially as it is so long ago, and not your"
110 "    Fault at all. J shall answer the same if you ask me fifty times."
110 "    Besides, you must bear in mind that, knowing it to be your Childish"
110 "    Nature to tell all that’s in your heart—so simple!—J made you"
110 "    promise me never to let it out by Word or Deed, having your Welfare"
110 "    in my Mind; and you most solemnly did promise it going from this"
110 "    Door. J have not named either that Question or your coming marriage"
110 "    to your Father, as he would blab it everywhere, poor Simple Man."
110 ""
110 "    Dear Tess, keep up your Spirits, and we mean to send you a Hogshead"
110 "    of Cyder for you Wedding, knowing there is not much in your parts,"
110 "    and thin Sour Stuff what there is. So no more at present, and with"
110 "    kind love to your Young Man.—From your affectte. Mother,"
110 ""
110 "    J. Durbeyfield"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "“O mother, mother!” murmured Tess."
110 ""
110 "She was recognizing how light was the touch of events the most"
110 "oppressive upon Mrs Durbeyfield’s elastic spirit. Her mother did not"
110 "see life as Tess saw it. That haunting episode of bygone days was to"
110 "her mother but a passing accident. But perhaps her mother was right as"
110 "to the course to be followed, whatever she might be in her reasons."
110 "Silence seemed, on the face of it, best for her adored one’s happiness:"
110 "silence it should be."
110 ""
110 "Thus steadied by a command from the only person in the world who had"
110 "any shadow of right to control her action, Tess grew calmer. The"
110 "responsibility was shifted, and her heart was lighter than it had been"
110 "for weeks. The days of declining autumn which followed her assent,"
110 "beginning with the month of October, formed a season through which she"
110 "lived in spiritual altitudes more nearly approaching ecstasy than any"
110 "other period of her life."
110 ""
110 "There was hardly a touch of earth in her love for Clare. To her sublime"
110 "trustfulness he was all that goodness could be—knew all that a guide,"
110 "philosopher, and friend should know. She thought every line in the"
110 "contour of his person the perfection of masculine beauty, his soul the"
110 "soul of a saint, his intellect that of a seer. The wisdom of her love"
110 "for him, as love, sustained her dignity; she seemed to be wearing a"
110 "crown. The compassion of his love for her, as she saw it, made her lift"
110 "up her heart to him in devotion. He would sometimes catch her large,"
110 "worshipful eyes, that had no bottom to them looking at him from their"
110 "depths, as if she saw something immortal before her."
110 ""

110 "coal that is smouldering and dangerous."
110 ""
110 "She had not known that men could be so disinterested, chivalrous,"
110 "protective, in their love for women as he. Angel Clare was far from all"

110 "in truth, more spiritual than animal; he had himself well in hand, and"
110 "was singularly free from grossness. Though not cold-natured, he was"

110 "desperately, but with a love more especially inclined to the"

110 ""

110 "quality of her sex which attracts men in general might be distasteful"

110 ""
110 "The country custom of unreserved comradeship out of doors during"

110 "<CHAPTER XXXII>"
110 ""
110 "This penitential mood kept her from naming the wedding-day. The"
110 "beginning of November found its date still in abeyance, though he asked"
110 "her at the most tempting times. But Tess’s desire seemed to be for a"
110 "perpetual betrothal in which everything should remain as it was then."
110 ""
110 "The meads were changing now; but it was still warm enough in early"
110 "afternoons before milking to idle there awhile, and the state of"
110 "dairy-work at this time of year allowed a spare hour for idling."
110 "Looking over the damp sod in the direction of the sun, a glistening"
110 "ripple of gossamer webs was visible to their eyes under the luminary,"
110 "like the track of moonlight on the sea. Gnats, knowing nothing of their"
110 "brief glorification, wandered across the shimmer of this pathway,"
110 "irradiated as if they bore fire within them, then passed out of its"
110 "line, and were quite extinct. In the presence of these things he would"
110 "remind her that the date was still the question."
110 ""
110 "Or he would ask her at night, when he accompanied her on some mission"
110 "invented by Mrs Crick to give him the opportunity. This was mostly a"
110 "journey to the farmhouse on the slopes above the vale, to inquire how"
110 "the advanced cows were getting on in the straw-barton to which they"
110 "were relegated. For it was a time of the year that brought great"
110 "changes to the world of kine. Batches of the animals were sent away"
110 "daily to this lying-in hospital, where they lived on straw till their"
110 "calves were born, after which event, and as soon as the calf could"
110 "walk, mother and offspring were driven back to the dairy. In the"
110 "interval which elapsed before the calves were sold there was, of"
110 "course, little milking to be done, but as soon as the calf had been"
110 "taken away the milkmaids would have to set to work as usual."
110 ""
110 "Returning from one of these dark walks they reached a great"
110 "gravel-cliff immediately over the levels, where they stood still and"
110 "listened. The water was now high in the streams, squirting through the"
110 "weirs, and tinkling under culverts; the smallest gullies were all full;"
110 "there was no taking short cuts anywhere, and foot-passengers were"
110 "compelled to follow the permanent ways. From the whole extent of the"
110 "invisible vale came a multitudinous intonation; it forced upon their"
110 "fancy that a great city lay below them, and that the murmur was the"
110 "vociferation of its populace."
110 ""
110 "“It seems like tens of thousands of them,” said Tess; “holding"
110 "public-meetings in their market-places, arguing, preaching,"
110 "quarrelling, sobbing, groaning, praying, and cursing.”"
110 ""
110 "Clare was not particularly heeding."
110 ""
110 "“Did Crick speak to you to-day, dear, about his not wanting much"
110 "assistance during the winter months?”"
110 ""
110 "“No.”"
110 ""
110 "“The cows are going dry rapidly.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes. Six or seven went to the straw-barton yesterday, and three the"
110 "day before, making nearly twenty in the straw already. Ah—is it that"
110 "the farmer don’t want my help for the calving? O, I am not wanted here"
110 "any more! And I have tried so hard to—”"
110 ""
110 "“Crick didn’t exactly say that he would no longer require you. But,"
110 "knowing what our relations were, he said in the most good-natured and"
110 "respectful manner possible that he supposed on my leaving at Christmas"
110 "I should take you with me, and on my asking what he would do without"
110 "you he merely observed that, as a matter of fact, it was a time of year"
110 "when he could do with a very little female help. I am afraid I was"
110 "sinner enough to feel rather glad that he was in this way forcing your"
110 "hand.”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t think you ought to have felt glad, Angel. Because ’tis always"
110 "mournful not to be wanted, even if at the same time ’tis convenient.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, it is convenient—you have admitted that.” He put his finger upon"
110 "her cheek. “Ah!” he said."
110 ""
110 "“What?”"
110 ""
110 "“I feel the red rising up at her having been caught! But why should I"
110 "trifle so! We will not trifle—life is too serious.”"
110 ""
110 "“It is. Perhaps I saw that before you did.”"
110 ""
110 "She was seeing it then. To decline to marry him after all—in obedience"
110 "to her emotion of last night—and leave the dairy, meant to go to some"
110 "strange place, not a dairy; for milkmaids were not in request now"
110 "calving-time was coming on; to go to some arable farm where no divine"
110 "being like Angel Clare was. She hated the thought, and she hated more"
110 "the thought of going home."
110 ""
110 "“So that, seriously, dearest Tess,” he continued, “since you will"
110 "probably have to leave at Christmas, it is in every way desirable and"
110 "convenient that I should carry you off then as my property. Besides, if"
110 "you were not the most uncalculating girl in the world you would know"
110 "that we could not go on like this for ever.”"
110 ""
110 "“I wish we could. That it would always be summer and autumn, and you"
110 "always courting me, and always thinking as much of me as you have done"
110 "through the past summer-time!”"
110 ""
110 "“I always shall.”"
110 ""
110 "“O, I know you will!” she cried, with a sudden fervour of faith in him."
110 "“Angel, I will fix the day when I will become yours for always!”"
110 ""
110 "Thus at last it was arranged between them, during that dark walk home,"
110 "amid the myriads of liquid voices on the right and left."
110 ""
110 "When they reached the dairy Mr and Mrs Crick were promptly told—with"
110 "injunctions of secrecy; for each of the lovers was desirous that the"
110 "marriage should be kept as private as possible. The dairyman, though he"
110 "had thought of dismissing her soon, now made a great concern about"
110 "losing her. What should he do about his skimming? Who would make the"
110 "ornamental butter-pats for the Anglebury and Sandbourne ladies? Mrs"
110 "Crick congratulated Tess on the shilly-shallying having at last come to"
110 "an end, and said that directly she set eyes on Tess she divined that"
110 "she was to be the chosen one of somebody who was no common outdoor man;"
110 "Tess had looked so superior as she walked across the barton on that"
110 "afternoon of her arrival; that she was of a good family she could have"
110 "sworn. In point of fact Mrs Crick did remember thinking that Tess was"
110 "graceful and good-looking as she approached; but the superiority might"
110 "have been a growth of the imagination aided by subsequent knowledge."
110 ""
110 "Tess was now carried along upon the wings of the hours, without the"
110 "sense of a will. The word had been given; the number of the day written"
110 "down. Her naturally bright intelligence had begun to admit the"
110 "fatalistic convictions common to field-folk and those who associate"
110 "more extensively with natural phenomena than with their"
110 "fellow-creatures; and she accordingly drifted into that passive"
110 "responsiveness to all things her lover suggested, characteristic of the"
110 "frame of mind."
110 ""
110 "But she wrote anew to her mother, ostensibly to notify the wedding-day;"
110 "really to again implore her advice. It was a gentleman who had chosen"
110 "her, which perhaps her mother had not sufficiently considered. A"
110 "post-nuptial explanation, which might be accepted with a light heart by"
110 "a rougher man, might not be received with the same feeling by him. But"
110 "this communication brought no reply from Mrs Durbeyfield."
110 ""
110 "Despite Angel Clare’s plausible representation to himself and to Tess"
110 "of the practical need for their immediate marriage, there was in truth"
110 "an element of precipitancy in the step, as became apparent at a later"
110 "date. He loved her dearly, though perhaps rather ideally and fancifully"
110 "than with the impassioned thoroughness of her feeling for him. He had"
110 "entertained no notion, when doomed as he had thought to an"
110 "unintellectual bucolic life, that such charms as he beheld in this"
110 "idyllic creature would be found behind the scenes. Unsophistication was"
110 "a thing to talk of; but he had not known how it really struck one until"
110 "he came here. Yet he was very far from seeing his future track clearly,"
110 "and it might be a year or two before he would be able to consider"
110 "himself fairly started in life. The secret lay in the tinge of"
110 "recklessness imparted to his career and character by the sense that he"
110 "had been made to miss his true destiny through the prejudices of his"
110 "family."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t you think ’twould have been better for us to wait till you were"
110 "quite settled in your midland farm?” she once asked timidly. (A midland"
110 "farm was the idea just then.)"
110 ""
110 "“To tell the truth, my Tess, I don’t like you to be left anywhere away"
110 "from my protection and sympathy.”"
110 ""
110 "The reason was a good one, so far as it went. His influence over her"
110 "had been so marked that she had caught his manner and habits, his"
110 "speech and phrases, his likings and his aversions. And to leave her in"
110 "farmland would be to let her slip back again out of accord with him. He"
110 "wished to have her under his charge for another reason. His parents had"
110 "naturally desired to see her once at least before he carried her off to"
110 "a distant settlement, English or colonial; and as no opinion of theirs"
110 "was to be allowed to change his intention, he judged that a couple of"
110 "months’ life with him in lodgings whilst seeking for an advantageous"
110 "opening would be of some social assistance to her at what she might"
110 "feel to be a trying ordeal—her presentation to his mother at the"
110 "Vicarage."
110 ""
110 "Next, he wished to see a little of the working of a flour-mill, having"
110 "an idea that he might combine the use of one with corn-growing. The"
110 "proprietor of a large old water-mill at Wellbridge—once the mill of an"
110 "Abbey—had offered him the inspection of his time-honoured mode of"
110 "procedure, and a hand in the operations for a few days, whenever he"
110 "should choose to come. Clare paid a visit to the place, some few miles"
110 "distant, one day at this time, to inquire particulars, and returned to"
110 "Talbothays in the evening. She found him determined to spend a short"
110 "time at the Wellbridge flour-mills. And what had determined him? Less"
110 "the opportunity of an insight into grinding and bolting than the casual"
110 "fact that lodgings were to be obtained in that very farmhouse which,"
110 "before its mutilation, had been the mansion of a branch of the"
110 "d’Urberville family. This was always how Clare settled practical"
110 "questions; by a sentiment which had nothing to do with them. They"
110 "decided to go immediately after the wedding, and remain for a"
110 "fortnight, instead of journeying to towns and inns."
110 ""
110 "“Then we will start off to examine some farms on the other side of"
110 "London that I have heard of,” he said, “and by March or April we will"
110 "pay a visit to my father and mother.”"
110 ""
110 "Questions of procedure such as these arose and passed, and the day, the"
110 "incredible day, on which she was to become his, loomed large in the"
110 "near future. The thirty-first of December, New Year’s Eve, was the"
110 "date. His wife, she said to herself. Could it ever be? Their two selves"
110 "together, nothing to divide them, every incident shared by them; why"
110 "not? And yet why?"
110 ""
110 "One Sunday morning Izz Huett returned from church, and spoke privately"
110 "to Tess."
110 ""
110 "“You was not called home this morning.”"
110 ""
110 "“What?”"
110 ""
110 "“It should ha’ been the first time of asking to-day,” she answered,"
110 "looking quietly at Tess. “You meant to be married New Year’s Eve,"
110 "deary?”"
110 ""
110 "The other returned a quick affirmative."
110 ""
110 "“And there must be three times of asking. And now there be only two"
110 "Sundays left between.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess felt her cheek paling; Izz was right; of course there must be"

110 ""
110 "A natural incident relieved her anxiety. Izz mentioned the omission of"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“No, I have not forgot ’em,” says Clare."
110 ""
110 "As soon as he caught Tess alone he assured her:"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t let them tease you about the banns. A licence will be quieter"

110 "you wished to.”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "But everything went smoothly. She wondered whether he would like her to"

110 ""

110 "eyes."
110 ""

110 ""
110 "She did return upstairs, and put on the gown. Alone, she stood for a"

110 ""
110 "    That never would become that wife"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXXIII>"
110 ""
110 "Angel felt that he would like to spend a day with her before the"
110 "wedding, somewhere away from the dairy, as a last jaunt in her company"
110 "while there were yet mere lover and mistress; a romantic day, in"
110 "circumstances that would never be repeated; with that other and greater"
110 "day beaming close ahead of them. During the preceding week, therefore,"
110 "he suggested making a few purchases in the nearest town, and they"
110 "started together."
110 ""
110 "Clare’s life at the dairy had been that of a recluse in respect the"
110 "world of his own class. For months he had never gone near a town, and,"
110 "requiring no vehicle, had never kept one, hiring the dairyman’s cob or"
110 "gig if he rode or drove. They went in the gig that day."
110 ""
110 "And then for the first time in their lives they shopped as partners in"
110 "one concern. It was Christmas Eve, with its loads a holly and"
110 "mistletoe, and the town was very full of strangers who had come in from"
110 "all parts of the country on account of the day. Tess paid the penalty"
110 "of walking about with happiness superadded to beauty on her countenance"
110 "by being much stared at as she moved amid them on his arm."
110 ""
110 "In the evening they returned to the inn at which they had put up, and"
110 "Tess waited in the entry while Angel went to see the horse and gig"
110 "brought to the door. The general sitting-room was full of guests, who"
110 "were continually going in and out. As the door opened and shut each"
110 "time for the passage of these, the light within the parlour fell full"
110 "upon Tess’s face. Two men came out and passed by her among the rest."
110 "One of them had stared her up and down in surprise, and she fancied he"
110 "was a Trantridge man, though that village lay so many miles off that"
110 "Trantridge folk were rarities here."
110 ""
110 "“A comely maid that,” said the other."
110 ""
110 "“True, comely enough. But unless I make a great mistake—” And he"
110 "negatived the remainder of the definition forthwith."
110 ""
110 "Clare had just returned from the stable-yard, and, confronting the man"
110 "on the threshold, heard the words, and saw the shrinking of Tess. The"
110 "insult to her stung him to the quick, and before he had considered"
110 "anything at all he struck the man on the chin with the full force of"
110 "his fist, sending him staggering backwards into the passage."
110 ""
110 "The man recovered himself, and seemed inclined to come on, and Clare,"
110 "stepping outside the door, put himself in a posture of defence. But his"
110 "opponent began to think better of the matter. He looked anew at Tess as"
110 "he passed her, and said to Clare—"
110 ""
110 "“I beg pardon, sir; ’twas a complete mistake. I thought she was another"
110 "woman, forty miles from here.”"
110 ""
110 "Clare, feeling then that he had been too hasty, and that he was,"
110 "moreover, to blame for leaving her standing in an inn-passage, did what"
110 "he usually did in such cases, gave the man five shillings to plaster"
110 "the blow; and thus they parted, bidding each other a pacific good"
110 "night. As soon as Clare had taken the reins from the ostler, and the"
110 "young couple had driven off, the two men went in the other direction."
110 ""
110 "“And was it a mistake?” said the second one."
110 ""
110 "“Not a bit of it. But I didn’t want to hurt the gentleman’s"
110 "feelings—not I.”"
110 ""
110 "In the meantime the lovers were driving onward."
110 ""
110 "“Could we put off our wedding till a little later?” Tess asked in a dry"
110 "dull voice. “I mean if we wished?”"
110 ""
110 "“No, my love. Calm yourself. Do you mean that the fellow may have time"
110 "to summon me for assault?” he asked good-humouredly."
110 ""
110 "“No—I only meant—if it should have to be put off.”"
110 ""
110 "What she meant was not very clear, and he directed her to dismiss such"
110 "fancies from her mind, which she obediently did as well as she could."
110 "But she was grave, very grave, all the way home; till she thought, “We"
110 "shall go away, a very long distance, hundreds of miles from these"
110 "parts, and such as this can never happen again, and no ghost of the"
110 "past reach there.”"
110 ""
110 "They parted tenderly that night on the landing, and Clare ascended to"
110 "his attic. Tess sat up getting on with some little requisites, lest the"
110 "few remaining days should not afford sufficient time. While she sat she"
110 "heard a noise in Angel’s room overhead, a sound of thumping and"
110 "struggling. Everybody else in the house was asleep, and in her anxiety"
110 "lest Clare should be ill she ran up and knocked at his door, and asked"
110 "him what was the matter."
110 ""
110 "“Oh, nothing, dear,” he said from within. “I am so sorry I disturbed"
110 "you! But the reason is rather an amusing one: I fell asleep and dreamt"
110 "that I was fighting that fellow again who insulted you, and the noise"
110 "you heard was my pummelling away with my fists at my portmanteau, which"
110 "I pulled out to-day for packing. I am occasionally liable to these"
110 "freaks in my sleep. Go to bed and think of it no more.”"
110 ""
110 "This was the last drachm required to turn the scale of her indecision."
110 "Declare the past to him by word of mouth she could not; but there was"
110 "another way. She sat down and wrote on the four pages of a note-sheet a"
110 "succinct narrative of those events of three or four years ago, put it"
110 "into an envelope, and directed it to Clare. Then, lest the flesh should"
110 "again be weak, she crept upstairs without any shoes and slipped the"
110 "note under his door."
110 ""
110 "Her night was a broken one, as it well might be, and she listened for"
110 "the first faint noise overhead. It came, as usual; he descended, as"
110 "usual. She descended. He met her at the bottom of the stairs and kissed"
110 "her. Surely it was as warmly as ever!"
110 ""
110 "He looked a little disturbed and worn, she thought. But he said not a"
110 "word to her about her revelation, even when they were alone. Could he"
110 "have had it? Unless he began the subject she felt that she could say"
110 "nothing. So the day passed, and it was evident that whatever he thought"
110 "he meant to keep to himself. Yet he was frank and affectionate as"
110 "before. Could it be that her doubts were childish? that he forgave her;"
110 "that he loved her for what she was, just as she was, and smiled at her"
110 "disquiet as at a foolish nightmare? Had he really received her note?"
110 "She glanced into his room, and could see nothing of it. It might be"
110 "that he forgave her. But even if he had not received it she had a"
110 "sudden enthusiastic trust that he surely would forgive her."
110 ""
110 "Every morning and night he was the same, and thus New Year’s Eve"
110 "broke—the wedding day."
110 ""
110 "The lovers did not rise at milking-time, having through the whole of"
110 "this last week of their sojourn at the dairy been accorded something of"
110 "the position of guests, Tess being honoured with a room of her own."
110 "When they arrived downstairs at breakfast-time they were surprised to"
110 "see what effects had been produced in the large kitchen for their glory"
110 "since they had last beheld it. At some unnatural hour of the morning"
110 "the dairyman had caused the yawning chimney-corner to be whitened, and"
110 "the brick hearth reddened, and a blazing yellow damask blower to be"
110 "hung across the arch in place of the old grimy blue cotton one with a"
110 "black sprig pattern which had formerly done duty there. This renovated"
110 "aspect of what was the focus indeed of the room on a full winter"

110 ""
110 "“I was determined to do summat in honour o’t”, said the dairyman. “And"
110 "as you wouldn’t hear of my gieing a rattling good randy wi’ fiddles and"
110 "bass-viols complete, as we should ha’ done in old times, this was all I"
110 "could think o’ as a noiseless thing.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess’s friends lived so far off that none could conveniently have been"
110 "present at the ceremony, even had any been asked; but as a fact nobody"

110 "while his father and mother had written a rather sad letter, deploring"
110 "his precipitancy in rushing into marriage, but making the best of the"
110 "matter by saying that, though a dairywoman was the last daughter-in-law"
110 "they could have expected, their son had arrived at an age which he"
110 "might be supposed to be the best judge."
110 ""
110 "This coolness in his relations distressed Clare less than it would have"
110 "done had he been without the grand card with which he meant to surprise"
110 "them ere long. To produce Tess, fresh from the dairy, as a d’Urberville"

110 "concealed her lineage till such time as, familiarized with worldly ways"
110 "by a few months’ travel and reading with him, he could take her on a"
110 "visit to his parents and impart the knowledge while triumphantly"

110 "lover’s dream, if no more. Perhaps Tess’s lineage had more value for"
110 "himself than for anybody in the world beside."
110 ""
110 "Her perception that Angel’s bearing towards her still remained in no"
110 "whit altered by her own communication rendered Tess guiltily doubtful"

110 "finished, and hastened upstairs. It had occurred to her to look once"

110 "well as beneath the door."
110 ""
110 "With a feeling of faintness she withdrew the letter. There it"
110 "was—sealed up, just as it had left her hands. The mountain had not yet"
110 "been removed. She could not let him read it now, the house being in"
110 "full bustle of preparation; and descending to her own room she"
110 "destroyed the letter there."
110 ""
110 "She was so pale when he saw her again that he felt quite anxious. The"

110 ""
110 "“I am so anxious to talk to you—I want to confess all my faults and"

110 ""
110 "“No, no—we can’t have faults talked of—you must be deemed perfect"

110 ""
110 "The hurry of dressing and starting left no time for more than this."
110 "Those words of his seemed to reassure her on further reflection. She"
110 "was whirled onward through the next couple of critical hours by the"
110 "mastering tide of her devotion to him, which closed up further"

110 "eclipsed all sinister contingencies by its brightness."
110 ""
110 "The church was a long way off, and they were obliged to drive,"
110 "particularly as it was winter. A closed carriage was ordered from a"
110 "roadside inn, a vehicle which had been kept there ever since the old"
110 "days of post-chaise travelling. It had stout wheel-spokes and heavy"
110 "felloes, a great curved bed, immense straps and springs, and a pole"
110 "like a battering-ram. The postilion was a venerable “boy” of sixty—a"
110 "martyr to rheumatic gout, the result of excessive exposure in youth,"
110 "counter-acted by strong liquors—who had stood at inn-doors doing"
110 "nothing for the whole five-and-twenty years that had elapsed since he"
110 "had no longer been required to ride professionally, as if expecting the"
110 "old times to come back again. He had a permanent running wound on the"
110 "outside of his right leg, originated by the constant bruisings of"
110 "aristocratic carriage-poles during the many years that he had been in"
110 "regular employ at the King’s Arms, Casterbridge."
110 ""
110 "Inside this cumbrous and creaking structure, and behind this decayed"
110 "conductor, the _partie carrée_ took their seats—the bride and"
110 "bridegroom and Mr and Mrs Crick. Angel would have liked one at least of"
110 "his brothers to be present as groomsman, but their silence after his"
110 "gentle hint to that effect by letter had signified that they did not"
110 "care to come. They disapproved of the marriage, and could not be"
110 "expected to countenance it. Perhaps it was as well that they could not"
110 "be present. They were not worldly young fellows, but fraternizing with"
110 "dairy-folk would have struck unpleasantly upon their biased niceness,"
110 "apart from their views of the match."
110 ""
110 "Upheld by the momentum of the time, Tess knew nothing of this, did not"
110 "see anything, did not know the road they were taking to the church. She"
110 "knew that Angel was close to her; all the rest was a luminous mist. She"
110 "was a sort of celestial person, who owed her being to poetry—one of"
110 "those classical divinities Clare was accustomed to talk to her about"
110 "when they took their walks together."
110 ""
110 "The marriage being by licence there were only a dozen or so of people"
110 "in the church; had there been a thousand they would have produced no"
110 "more effect upon her. They were at stellar distances from her present"
110 "world. In the ecstatic solemnity with which she swore her faith to him"
110 "the ordinary sensibilities of sex seemed a flippancy. At a pause in the"
110 "service, while they were kneeling together, she unconsciously inclined"
110 "herself towards him, so that her shoulder touched his arm; she had been"
110 "frightened by a passing thought, and the movement had been automatic,"
110 "to assure herself that he was really there, and to fortify her belief"
110 "that his fidelity would be proof against all things."
110 ""
110 "Clare knew that she loved him—every curve of her form showed that—but"
110 "he did not know at that time the full depth of her devotion, its"
110 "single-mindedness, its meekness; what long-suffering it guaranteed,"
110 "what honesty, what endurance, what good faith."
110 ""
110 "As they came out of church the ringers swung the bells off their rests,"
110 "and a modest peal of three notes broke forth—that limited amount of"
110 "expression having been deemed sufficient by the church builders for the"
110 "joys of such a small parish. Passing by the tower with her husband on"
110 "the path to the gate she could feel the vibrant air humming round them"
110 "from the louvred belfry in the circle of sound, and it matched the"
110 "highly-charged mental atmosphere in which she was living."
110 ""
110 "This condition of mind, wherein she felt glorified by an irradiation"
110 "not her own, like the angel whom St John saw in the sun, lasted till"
110 "the sound of the church bells had died away, and the emotions of the"
110 "wedding-service had calmed down. Her eyes could dwell upon details more"
110 "clearly now, and Mr and Mrs Crick having directed their own gig to be"
110 "sent for them, to leave the carriage to the young couple, she observed"
110 "the build and character of that conveyance for the first time. Sitting"
110 "in silence she regarded it long."
110 ""
110 "“I fancy you seem oppressed, Tessy,” said Clare."
110 ""
110 "“Yes,” she answered, putting her hand to her brow. “I tremble at many"
110 "things. It is all so serious, Angel. Among other things I seem to have"
110 "seen this carriage before, to be very well acquainted with it. It is"
110 "very odd—I must have seen it in a dream.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh—you have heard the legend of the d’Urberville Coach—that well-known"
110 "superstition of this county about your family when they were very"
110 "popular here; and this lumbering old thing reminds you of it.”"
110 ""
110 "“I have never heard of it to my knowledge,” said she. “What is the"
110 "legend—may I know it?”"
110 ""
110 "“Well—I would rather not tell it in detail just now. A certain"
110 "d’Urberville of the sixteenth or seventeenth century committed a"
110 "dreadful crime in his family coach; and since that time members of the"
110 "family see or hear the old coach whenever—— But I’ll tell you another"
110 "day—it is rather gloomy. Evidently some dim knowledge of it has been"
110 "brought back to your mind by the sight of this venerable caravan.”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t remember hearing it before,” she murmured. “Is it when we are"
110 "going to die, Angel, that members of my family see it, or is it when we"
110 "have committed a crime?”"
110 ""
110 "“Now, Tess!”"
110 ""
110 "He silenced her by a kiss."
110 ""
110 "By the time they reached home she was contrite and spiritless. She was"
110 "Mrs Angel Clare, indeed, but had she any moral right to the name? Was"
110 "she not more truly Mrs Alexander d’Urberville? Could intensity of love"
110 "justify what might be considered in upright souls as culpable"
110 "reticence? She knew not what was expected of women in such cases; and"
110 "she had no counsellor."
110 ""
110 "However, when she found herself alone in her room for a few minutes—the"
110 "last day this on which she was ever to enter it—she knelt down and"
110 "prayed. She tried to pray to God, but it was her husband who really had"
110 "her supplication. Her idolatry of this man was such that she herself"
110 "almost feared it to be ill-omened. She was conscious of the notion"
110 "expressed by Friar Laurence: “These violent delights have violent"
110 "ends.” It might be too desperate for human conditions—too rank, to"
110 "wild, too deadly."
110 ""
110 "“O my love, why do I love you so!” she whispered there alone; “for she"
110 "you love is not my real self, but one in my image; the one I might have"
110 "been!”"
110 ""
110 "Afternoon came, and with it the hour for departure. They had decided to"
110 "fulfil the plan of going for a few days to the lodgings in the old"
110 "farmhouse near Wellbridge Mill, at which he meant to reside during his"
110 "investigation of flour processes. At two o’clock there was nothing left"
110 "to do but to start. All the servantry of the dairy were standing in the"
110 "red-brick entry to see them go out, the dairyman and his wife following"
110 "to the door. Tess saw her three chamber-mates in a row against the"
110 "wall, pensively inclining their heads. She had much questioned if they"
110 "would appear at the parting moment; but there they were, stoical and"
110 "staunch to the last. She knew why the delicate Retty looked so fragile,"
110 "and Izz so tragically sorrowful, and Marian so blank; and she forgot"
110 "her own dogging shadow for a moment in contemplating theirs."
110 ""
110 "She impulsively whispered to him—"
110 ""

110 "time?”"
110 ""
110 "Clare had not the least objection to such a farewell formality—which"
110 "was all that it was to him—and as he passed them he kissed them in"
110 "succession where they stood, saying “Goodbye” to each as he did so."

110 "awakening feelings they were trying to subdue."
110 ""

110 "valley of rocks."
110 ""
110 "“Oh?” said Mrs Crick. “An afternoon crow!”"
110 ""
110 "Two men were standing by the yard gate, holding it open."
110 ""

110 "could be heard by the group at the door-wicket."
110 ""
110 "The cock crew again—straight towards Clare."
110 ""
110 "“Well!” said the dairyman."
110 ""

110 "drive on. Goodbye, goodbye!”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 "<CHAPTER XXXIV>"
110 ""
110 "They drove by the level road along the valley to a distance of a few"
110 "miles, and, reaching Wellbridge, turned away from the village to the"
110 "left, and over the great Elizabethan bridge which gives the place half"
110 "its name. Immediately behind it stood the house wherein they had"
110 "engaged lodgings, whose exterior features are so well known to all"
110 "travellers through the Froom Valley; once portion of a fine manorial"
110 "residence, and the property and seat of a d’Urberville, but since its"
110 "partial demolition a farmhouse."
110 ""
110 "“Welcome to one of your ancestral mansions!” said Clare as he handed"
110 "her down. But he regretted the pleasantry; it was too near a satire."
110 ""
110 "On entering they found that, though they had only engaged a couple of"
110 "rooms, the farmer had taken advantage of their proposed presence during"

110 "woman from a neighbouring cottage to minister to their few wants. The"
110 "absoluteness of possession pleased them, and they realized it as the"
110 "first moment of their experience under their own exclusive roof-tree."
110 ""
110 "But he found that the mouldy old habitation somewhat depressed his"
110 "bride. When the carriage was gone they ascended the stairs to wash"
110 "their hands, the charwoman showing the way. On the landing Tess stopped"
110 "and started."
110 ""
110 "“What’s the matter?” said he."
110 ""

110 "me.”"
110 ""
110 "He looked up, and perceived two life-size portraits on panels built"
110 "into the masonry. As all visitors to the mansion are aware, these"
110 "paintings represent women of middle age, of a date some two hundred"
110 "years ago, whose lineaments once seen can never be forgotten. The long"
110 "pointed features, narrow eye, and smirk of the one, so suggestive of"
110 "merciless treachery; the bill-hook nose, large teeth, and bold eye of"
110 "the other suggesting arrogance to the point of ferocity, haunt the"
110 "beholder afterwards in his dreams."
110 ""
110 "“Whose portraits are those?” asked Clare of the charwoman."
110 ""

110 "being builded into the wall they can’t be moved away.”"
110 ""
110 "The unpleasantness of the matter was that, in addition to their effect"

110 "exaggerated forms. He said nothing of this, however, and, regretting"
110 "that he had gone out of his way to choose the house for their bridal"

110 "touched hers under the water."
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "The sun was so low on that short last afternoon of the year that it"

110 ""

110 "crime!”"
110 ""
110 "They sat on over the tea-table waiting for their luggage, which the"

110 ""

110 ""

110 "“You did not expect it? Ah—you thought too highly of me. Now listen."
110 "Put your head there, because I want you to forgive me, and not to be"
110 "indignant with me for not telling you before, as perhaps I ought to"
110 "have done.”"
110 ""
110 "How strange it was! He seemed to be her double. She did not speak, and"
110 "Clare went on—"
110 ""
110 "“I did not mention it because I was afraid of endangering my chance of"
110 "you, darling, the great prize of my life—my Fellowship I call you. My"
110 "brother’s Fellowship was won at his college, mine at Talbothays Dairy."
110 "Well, I would not risk it. I was going to tell you a month ago—at the"
110 "time you agreed to be mine, but I could not; I thought it might"
110 "frighten you away from me. I put it off; then I thought I would tell"
110 "you yesterday, to give you a chance at least of escaping me. But I did"
110 "not. And I did not this morning, when you proposed our confessing our"
110 "faults on the landing—the sinner that I was! But I must, now I see you"
110 "sitting there so solemnly. I wonder if you will forgive me?”"
110 ""
110 "“O yes! I am sure that—”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, I hope so. But wait a minute. You don’t know. To begin at the"
110 "beginning. Though I imagine my poor father fears that I am one of the"
110 "eternally lost for my doctrines, I am of course, a believer in good"
110 "morals, Tess, as much as you. I used to wish to be a teacher of men,"
110 "and it was a great disappointment to me when I found I could not enter"
110 "the Church. I admired spotlessness, even though I could lay no claim to"
110 "it, and hated impurity, as I hope I do now. Whatever one may think of"
110 "plenary inspiration, one must heartily subscribe to these words of"
110 "Paul: ‘Be thou an example—in word, in conversation, in charity, in"
110 "spirit, in faith, in purity.’ It is the only safeguard for us poor"
110 "human beings. ‘_Integer vitae_,’ says a Roman poet, who is strange"
110 "company for St Paul—"
110 ""
110 "    The man of upright life, from frailties free,"
110 "    Stands not in need of Moorish spear or bow."
110 ""
110 "“Well, a certain place is paved with good intentions, and having felt"
110 "all that so strongly, you will see what a terrible remorse it bred in"
110 "me when, in the midst of my fine aims for other people, I myself fell.”"
110 ""
110 "He then told her of that time of his life to which allusion has been"
110 "made when, tossed about by doubts and difficulties in London, like a"
110 "cork on the waves, he plunged into eight-and-forty hours’ dissipation"
110 "with a stranger."
110 ""
110 "“Happily I awoke almost immediately to a sense of my folly,” he"
110 "continued. “I would have no more to say to her, and I came home. I have"
110 "never repeated the offence. But I felt I should like to treat you with"
110 "perfect frankness and honour, and I could not do so without telling"
110 "this. Do you forgive me?”"
110 ""
110 "She pressed his hand tightly for an answer."
110 ""
110 "“Then we will dismiss it at once and for ever!—too painful as it is for"
110 "the occasion—and talk of something lighter.”"
110 ""
110 "“O, Angel—I am almost glad—because now _you_ can forgive _me_! I have"
110 "not made my confession. I have a confession, too—remember, I said so.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah, to be sure! Now then for it, wicked little one.”"
110 ""
110 "“Perhaps, although you smile, it is as serious as yours, or more so.”"
110 ""
110 "“It can hardly be more serious, dearest.”"
110 ""
110 "“It cannot—O no, it cannot!” She jumped up joyfully at the hope. “No,"
110 "it cannot be more serious, certainly,” she cried, “because ’tis just"
110 "the same! I will tell you now.”"
110 ""
110 "She sat down again."
110 ""
110 "Their hands were still joined. The ashes under the grate were lit by"
110 "the fire vertically, like a torrid waste. Imagination might have beheld"
110 "a Last Day luridness in this red-coaled glow, which fell on his face"

110 "The Woman Pays"

110 "<CHAPTER XXXV>"
110 ""
110 "Her narrative ended; even its re-assertions and secondary explanations"
110 "were done. Tess’s voice throughout had hardly risen higher than its"
110 "opening tone; there had been no exculpatory phrase of any kind, and she"
110 "had not wept."
110 ""
110 "But the complexion even of external things seemed to suffer"
110 "transmutation as her announcement progressed. The fire in the grate"
110 "looked impish—demoniacally funny, as if it did not care in the least"
110 "about her strait. The fender grinned idly, as if it too did not care."
110 "The light from the water-bottle was merely engaged in a chromatic"
110 "problem. All material objects around announced their irresponsibility"
110 "with terrible iteration. And yet nothing had changed since the moments"
110 "when he had been kissing her; or rather, nothing in the substance of"
110 "things. But the essence of things had changed."
110 ""
110 "When she ceased, the auricular impressions from their previous"
110 "endearments seemed to hustle away into the corner of their brains,"
110 "repeating themselves as echoes from a time of supremely purblind"
110 "foolishness."
110 ""
110 "Clare performed the irrelevant act of stirring the fire; the"
110 "intelligence had not even yet got to the bottom of him. After stirring"
110 "the embers he rose to his feet; all the force of her disclosure had"
110 "imparted itself now. His face had withered. In the strenuousness of his"
110 "concentration he treadled fitfully on the floor. He could not, by any"
110 "contrivance, think closely enough; that was the meaning of his vague"
110 "movement. When he spoke it was in the most inadequate, commonplace"
110 "voice of the many varied tones she had heard from him."
110 ""
110 "“Tess!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, dearest.”"
110 ""
110 "“Am I to believe this? From your manner I am to take it as true. O you"
110 "cannot be out of your mind! You ought to be! Yet you are not... My"
110 "wife, my Tess—nothing in you warrants such a supposition as that?”"
110 ""
110 "“I am not out of my mind,” she said."
110 ""
110 "“And yet—” He looked vacantly at her, to resume with dazed senses: “Why"
110 "didn’t you tell me before? Ah, yes, you would have told me, in a"
110 "way—but I hindered you, I remember!”"
110 ""
110 "These and other of his words were nothing but the perfunctory babble of"
110 "the surface while the depths remained paralyzed. He turned away, and"
110 "bent over a chair. Tess followed him to the middle of the room, where"
110 "he was, and stood there staring at him with eyes that did not weep."
110 "Presently she slid down upon her knees beside his foot, and from this"
110 "position she crouched in a heap."
110 ""
110 "“In the name of our love, forgive me!” she whispered with a dry mouth."
110 "“I have forgiven you for the same!”"
110 ""
110 "And, as he did not answer, she said again—"
110 ""
110 "“Forgive me as you are forgiven! _I_ forgive _you_, Angel.”"
110 ""
110 "“You—yes, you do.”"
110 ""
110 "“But you do not forgive me?”"
110 ""
110 "“O Tess, forgiveness does not apply to the case! You were one person;"
110 "now you are another. My God—how can forgiveness meet such a"
110 "grotesque—prestidigitation as that!”"
110 ""
110 "He paused, contemplating this definition; then suddenly broke into"
110 "horrible laughter—as unnatural and ghastly as a laugh in hell."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t—don’t! It kills me quite, that!” she shrieked. “O have mercy"
110 "upon me—have mercy!”"
110 ""
110 "He did not answer; and, sickly white, she jumped up."
110 ""
110 "“Angel, Angel! what do you mean by that laugh?” she cried out. “Do you"
110 "know what this is to me?”"
110 ""
110 "He shook his head."
110 ""
110 "“I have been hoping, longing, praying, to make you happy! I have"
110 "thought what joy it will be to do it, what an unworthy wife I shall be"
110 "if I do not! That’s what I have felt, Angel!”"
110 ""
110 "“I know that.”"
110 ""
110 "“I thought, Angel, that you loved me—me, my very self! If it is I you"
110 "do love, O how can it be that you look and speak so? It frightens me!"
110 "Having begun to love you, I love you for ever—in all changes, in all"
110 "disgraces, because you are yourself. I ask no more. Then how can you, O"
110 "my own husband, stop loving me?”"
110 ""
110 "“I repeat, the woman I have been loving is not you.”"
110 ""
110 "“But who?”"
110 ""
110 "“Another woman in your shape.”"
110 ""
110 "She perceived in his words the realization of her own apprehensive"
110 "foreboding in former times. He looked upon her as a species of"
110 "imposter; a guilty woman in the guise of an innocent one. Terror was"
110 "upon her white face as she saw it; her cheek was flaccid, and her mouth"
110 "had almost the aspect of a round little hole. The horrible sense of his"
110 "view of her so deadened her that she staggered, and he stepped forward,"
110 "thinking she was going to fall."
110 ""
110 "“Sit down, sit down,” he said gently. “You are ill; and it is natural"
110 "that you should be.”"
110 ""
110 "She did sit down, without knowing where she was, that strained look"
110 "still upon her face, and her eyes such as to make his flesh creep."
110 ""
110 "“I don’t belong to you any more, then; do I, Angel?” she asked"

110 ""
110 "The image raised caused her to take pity upon herself as one who was"
110 "ill-used. Her eyes filled as she regarded her position further; she"
110 "turned round and burst into a flood of self-sympathetic tears."
110 ""
110 "Clare was relieved at this change, for the effect on her of what had"
110 "happened was beginning to be a trouble to him only less than the woe of"
110 "the disclosure itself. He waited patiently, apathetically, till the"
110 "violence of her grief had worn itself out, and her rush of weeping had"
110 "lessened to a catching gasp at intervals."
110 ""
110 "“Angel,” she said suddenly, in her natural tones, the insane, dry voice"
110 "of terror having left her now. “Angel, am I too wicked for you and me"
110 "to live together?”"
110 ""
110 "“I have not been able to think what we can do.”"
110 ""
110 "“I shan’t ask you to let me live with you, Angel, because I have no"
110 "right to! I shall not write to mother and sisters to say we be married,"
110 "as I said I would do; and I shan’t finish the good hussif I cut out and"
110 "meant to make while we were in lodgings.”"
110 ""
110 "“Shan’t you?”"
110 ""
110 "“No, I shan’t do anything, unless you order me to; and if you go away"
110 "from me I shall not follow ’ee; and if you never speak to me any more I"

110 ""
110 "“And if I order you to do anything?”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 "self-preservation.”"
110 ""
110 "These were the first words of antagonism. To fling elaborate sarcasms"
110 "at Tess, however, was much like flinging them at a dog or cat. The"
110 "charms of their subtlety passed by her unappreciated, and she only"

110 "tear so large that it magnified the pores of the skin over which it"

110 "action was necessary; yet what?"
110 ""
110 "“Tess,” he said, as gently as he could speak, “I cannot stay—in this"
110 "room—just now. I will walk out a little way.”"
110 ""
110 "He quietly left the room, and the two glasses of wine that he had"
110 "poured out for their supper—one for her, one for him—remained on the"
110 "table untasted. This was what their _Agape_ had come to. At tea, two or"
110 "three hours earlier, they had, in the freakishness of affection, drunk"
110 "from one cup."
110 ""
110 "The closing of the door behind him, gently as it had been pulled to,"
110 "roused Tess from her stupor. He was gone; she could not stay. Hastily"
110 "flinging her cloak around her she opened the door and followed, putting"
110 "out the candles as if she were never coming back. The rain was over and"
110 "the night was now clear."
110 ""
110 "She was soon close at his heels, for Clare walked slowly and without"
110 "purpose. His form beside her light gray figure looked black, sinister,"
110 "and forbidding, and she felt as sarcasm the touch of the jewels of"
110 "which she had been momentarily so proud. Clare turned at hearing her"
110 "footsteps, but his recognition of her presence seemed to make no"
110 "difference to him, and he went on over the five yawning arches of the"
110 "great bridge in front of the house."
110 ""
110 "The cow and horse tracks in the road were full of water, the rain"
110 "having been enough to charge them, but not enough to wash them away."
110 "Across these minute pools the reflected stars flitted in a quick"
110 "transit as she passed; she would not have known they were shining"
110 "overhead if she had not seen them there—the vastest things of the"
110 "universe imaged in objects so mean."
110 ""
110 "The place to which they had travelled to-day was in the same valley as"
110 "Talbothays, but some miles lower down the river; and the surroundings"
110 "being open, she kept easily in sight of him. Away from the house the"
110 "road wound through the meads, and along these she followed Clare"
110 "without any attempt to come up with him or to attract him, but with"
110 "dumb and vacant fidelity."
110 ""
110 "At last, however, her listless walk brought her up alongside him, and"
110 "still he said nothing. The cruelty of fooled honesty is often great"
110 "after enlightenment, and it was mighty in Clare now. The outdoor air"
110 "had apparently taken away from him all tendency to act on impulse; she"
110 "knew that he saw her without irradiation—in all her bareness; that Time"
110 "was chanting his satiric psalm at her then—"
110 ""
110 "    Behold, when thy face is made bare, he that loved thee shall hate;"
110 "    Thy face shall be no more fair at the fall of thy fate. For thy"
110 "    life shall fall as a leaf and be shed as the rain; And the veil of"
110 "    thine head shall be grief, and the crown shall be pain."

110 "He was still intently thinking, and her companionship had now"
110 "insufficient power to break or divert the strain of thought. What a"

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“O Angel—my mother says that it sometimes happens so!—she knows several"
110 "cases where they were worse than I, and the husband has not minded it"

110 ""
110 "“Don’t, Tess; don’t argue. Different societies, different manners. You"
110 "almost make me say you are an unapprehending peasant woman, who have"

110 "know what you say.”"
110 ""
110 "“I am only a peasant by position, not by nature!”"
110 ""
110 "She spoke with an impulse to anger, but it went as it came."
110 ""

110 "pedigree would have done better if he had held his tongue. I cannot"

110 ""

110 "large landowners, and so were Dairyman Billett’s. And the Debbyhouses,"

110 ""
110 "“So much the worse for the county.”"
110 ""
110 "She took these reproaches in their bulk simply, not in their"
110 "particulars; he did not love her as he had loved her hitherto, and to"
110 "all else she was indifferent."
110 ""
110 "They wandered on again in silence. It was said afterwards that a"
110 "cottager of Wellbridge, who went out late that night for a doctor, met"
110 "two lovers in the pastures, walking very slowly, without converse, one"
110 "behind the other, as in a funeral procession, and the glimpse that he"

110 "sad. Returning later, he passed them again in the same field,"
110 "progressing just as slowly, and as regardless of the hour and of the"

110 "bear in mind the curious incident, which, however, he recalled a long"
110 "while after."
110 ""
110 "During the interval of the cottager’s going and coming, she had said to"
110 "her husband—"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“I will,” said she dutifully."
110 ""
110 "They had rambled round by a road which led to the well-known ruins of"
110 "the Cistercian abbey behind the mill, the latter having, in centuries"
110 "past, been attached to the monastic establishment. The mill still"
110 "worked on, food being a perennial necessity; the abbey had perished,"
110 "creeds being transient. One continually sees the ministration of the"
110 "temporary outlasting the ministration of the eternal. Their walk having"
110 "been circuitous, they were still not far from the house, and in obeying"

110 "undress. In removing the light towards the bedstead its rays fell upon"

110 "that mysterious parcel which it had been so difficult to pack and"
110 "bring; whose contents he would not explain to her, saying that time"
110 "would soon show her the purpose thereof. In his zest and his gaiety he"
110 "had hung it there. How foolish and inopportune that mistletoe looked"
110 "now."
110 ""
110 "Having nothing more to fear, having scarce anything to hope, for that"
110 "he would relent there seemed no promise whatever, she lay down dully."
110 "When sorrow ceases to be speculative, sleep sees her opportunity. Among"
110 "so many happier moods which forbid repose this was a mood which"
110 "welcomed it, and in a few minutes the lonely Tess forgot existence,"
110 "surrounded by the aromatic stillness of the chamber that had once,"
110 "possibly, been the bride-chamber of her own ancestry."
110 ""
110 "Later on that night Clare also retraced his steps to the house."
110 "Entering softly to the sitting-room he obtained a light, and with the"
110 "manner of one who had considered his course he spread his rugs upon the"
110 "old horse-hair sofa which stood there, and roughly shaped it to a"
110 "sleeping-couch. Before lying down he crept shoeless upstairs, and"
110 "listened at the door of her apartment. Her measured breathing told that"
110 "she was sleeping profoundly."
110 ""
110 "“Thank God!” murmured Clare; and yet he was conscious of a pang of"
110 "bitterness at the thought—approximately true, though not wholly so—that"
110 "having shifted the burden of her life to his shoulders, she was now"
110 "reposing without care."
110 ""
110 "He turned away to descend; then, irresolute, faced round to her door"
110 "again. In the act he caught sight of one of the d’Urberville dames,"
110 "whose portrait was immediately over the entrance to Tess’s bedchamber."
110 "In the candlelight the painting was more than unpleasant. Sinister"
110 "design lurked in the woman’s features, a concentrated purpose of"
110 "revenge on the other sex—so it seemed to him then. The Caroline bodice"
110 "of the portrait was low—precisely as Tess’s had been when he tucked it"
110 "in to show the necklace; and again he experienced the distressing"
110 "sensation of a resemblance between them."
110 ""
110 "The check was sufficient. He resumed his retreat and descended."
110 ""
110 "His air remained calm and cold, his small compressed mouth indexing his"
110 "powers of self-control; his face wearing still that terrible sterile"
110 "expression which had spread thereon since her disclosure. It was the"
110 "face of a man who was no longer passion’s slave, yet who found no"
110 "advantage in his enfranchisement. He was simply regarding the harrowing"
110 "contingencies of human experience, the unexpectedness of things."
110 "Nothing so pure, so sweet, so virginal as Tess had seemed possible all"
110 "the long while that he had adored her, up to an hour ago; but"
110 ""
110 "    The little less, and what worlds away!"
110 ""
110 "He argued erroneously when he said to himself that her heart was not"
110 "indexed in the honest freshness of her face; but Tess had no advocate"
110 "to set him right. Could it be possible, he continued, that eyes which"
110 "as they gazed never expressed any divergence from what the tongue was"
110 "telling, were yet ever seeing another world behind her ostensible one,"
110 "discordant and contrasting?"
110 ""
110 "He reclined on his couch in the sitting-room, and extinguished the"
110 "light. The night came in, and took up its place there, unconcerned and"
110 "indifferent; the night which had already swallowed up his happiness,"
110 "and was now digesting it listlessly; and was ready to swallow up the"
110 "happiness of a thousand other people with as little disturbance or"
110 "change of mien."

110 "<CHAPTER XXXVI>"
110 ""
110 "Clare arose in the light of a dawn that was ashy and furtive, as though"
110 "associated with crime. The fireplace confronted him with its extinct"
110 "embers; the spread supper-table, whereon stood the two full glasses of"
110 "untasted wine, now flat and filmy; her vacated seat and his own; the"
110 "other articles of furniture, with their eternal look of not being able"
110 "to help it, their intolerable inquiry what was to be done? From above"
110 "there was no sound; but in a few minutes there came a knock at the"
110 "door. He remembered that it would be the neighbouring cottager’s wife,"
110 "who was to minister to their wants while they remained here."
110 ""
110 "The presence of a third person in the house would be extremely awkward"
110 "just now, and, being already dressed, he opened the window and informed"
110 "her that they could manage to shift for themselves that morning. She"
110 "had a milk-can in her hand, which he told her to leave at the door."
110 "When the dame had gone away he searched in the back quarters of the"
110 "house for fuel, and speedily lit a fire. There was plenty of eggs,"
110 "butter, bread, and so on in the larder, and Clare soon had breakfast"
110 "laid, his experiences at the dairy having rendered him facile in"
110 "domestic preparations. The smoke of the kindled wood rose from the"
110 "chimney without like a lotus-headed column; local people who were"
110 "passing by saw it, and thought of the newly-married couple, and envied"
110 "their happiness."
110 ""
110 "Angel cast a final glance round, and then going to the foot of the"
110 "stairs, called in a conventional voice—"
110 ""
110 "“Breakfast is ready!”"
110 ""
110 "He opened the front door, and took a few steps in the morning air."
110 "When, after a short space, he came back she was already in the"
110 "sitting-room mechanically readjusting the breakfast things. As she was"
110 "fully attired, and the interval since his calling her had been but two"
110 "or three minutes, she must have been dressed or nearly so before he"
110 "went to summon her. Her hair was twisted up in a large round mass at"
110 "the back of her head, and she had put on one of the new frocks—a pale"
110 "blue woollen garment with neck-frillings of white. Her hands and face"
110 "appeared to be cold, and she had possibly been sitting dressed in the"
110 "bedroom a long time without any fire. The marked civility of Clare’s"
110 "tone in calling her seemed to have inspired her, for the moment, with a"
110 "new glimmer of hope. But it soon died when she looked at him."
110 ""
110 "The pair were, in truth, but the ashes of their former fires. To the"
110 "hot sorrow of the previous night had succeeded heaviness; it seemed as"
110 "if nothing could kindle either of them to fervour of sensation any"
110 "more."
110 ""
110 "He spoke gently to her, and she replied with a like"
110 "undemonstrativeness. At last she came up to him, looking in his"
110 "sharply-defined face as one who had no consciousness that her own"
110 "formed a visible object also."
110 ""

110 "pull upon it would cause real illness, dull her characteristic eyes,"
110 "and make her mouth thin."
110 ""
110 "She looked absolutely pure. Nature, in her fantastic trickery, had set"
110 "such a seal of maidenhood upon Tess’s countenance that he gazed at her"
110 "with a stupefied air."
110 ""
110 "“Tess! Say it is not true! No, it is not true!”"
110 ""
110 "“It is true.”"
110 ""
110 "“Every word?”"
110 ""
110 "“Every word.”"
110 ""
110 "He looked at her imploringly, as if he would willingly have taken a lie"
110 "from her lips, knowing it to be one, and have made of it, by some sort"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Is he living?” Angel then asked."
110 ""
110 "“The baby died.”"
110 ""
110 "“But the man?”"
110 ""
110 "“He is alive.”"
110 ""
110 "A last despair passed over Clare’s face."
110 ""
110 "“Is he in England?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes.”"
110 ""
110 "He took a few vague steps."
110 ""
110 "“My position—is this,” he said abruptly. “I thought—any man would have"
110 "thought—that by giving up all ambition to win a wife with social"
110 "standing, with fortune, with knowledge of the world, I should secure"

110 ""
110 "Tess felt his position so entirely that the remainder had not been"

110 "all round."
110 ""
110 "“Angel—I should not have let it go on to marriage with you if I had not"

110 "hoped you would never—”"
110 ""
110 "Her voice grew husky."
110 ""
110 "“A last way?”"
110 ""
110 "“I mean, to get rid of me. You _can_ get rid of me.”"
110 ""
110 "“How?”"
110 ""
110 "“By divorcing me.”"
110 ""
110 "“Good heavens—how can you be so simple! How can I divorce you?”"

110 "grounds for that.”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Indeed I cannot.”"
110 ""
110 "A quick shame mixed with the misery upon his listener’s face."
110 ""
