110 "could cast me off if you were determined, and didn’t love me"

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“What were you thinking of doing?” he inquired."
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“When?”"
110 ""
110 "She writhed under this inquisitorial manner of his. “Last night,” she"

110 ""
110 "“Where?”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Ssh!”"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "He knew this to be true enough. Since the desperation of the night her"
110 "activities had dropped to zero, and there was no further rashness to be"
110 "feared."
110 ""
110 "Tess tried to busy herself again over the breakfast-table with more or"
110 "less success, and they sat down both on the same side, so that their"
110 "glances did not meet. There was at first something awkward in hearing"
110 "each other eat and drink, but this could not be escaped; moreover, the"

110 "and telling her the hour at which he might be expected to dinner, went"

110 "that business, which had been his only practical reason for coming"
110 "here."
110 ""
110 "When he was gone Tess stood at the window, and presently saw his form"
110 "crossing the great stone bridge which conducted to the mill premises."
110 "He sank behind it, crossed the railway beyond, and disappeared. Then,"
110 "without a sigh, she turned her attention to the room, and began"
110 "clearing the table and setting it in order."
110 ""
110 "The charwoman soon came. Her presence was at first a strain upon Tess,"
110 "but afterwards an alleviation. At half-past twelve she left her"
110 "assistant alone in the kitchen, and, returning to the sitting-room,"
110 "waited for the reappearance of Angel’s form behind the bridge."
110 ""
110 "About one he showed himself. Her face flushed, although he was a"
110 "quarter of a mile off. She ran to the kitchen to get the dinner served"
110 "by the time he should enter. He went first to the room where they had"
110 "washed their hands together the day before, and as he entered the"
110 "sitting-room the dish-covers rose from the dishes as if by his own"
110 "motion."
110 ""
110 "“How punctual!” he said."
110 ""
110 "“Yes. I saw you coming over the bridge,” said she."
110 ""
110 "The meal was passed in commonplace talk of what he had been doing"
110 "during the morning at the Abbey Mill, of the methods of bolting and the"
110 "old-fashioned machinery, which he feared would not enlighten him"
110 "greatly on modern improved methods, some of it seeming to have been in"
110 "use ever since the days it ground for the monks in the adjoining"
110 "conventual buildings—now a heap of ruins. He left the house again in"
110 "the course of an hour, coming home at dusk, and occupying himself"
110 "through the evening with his papers. She feared she was in the way and,"
110 "when the old woman was gone, retired to the kitchen, where she made"
110 "herself busy as well as she could for more than an hour."
110 ""
110 "Clare’s shape appeared at the door. “You must not work like this,” he"
110 "said. “You are not my servant; you are my wife.”"
110 ""
110 "She raised her eyes, and brightened somewhat. “I may think myself"
110 "that—indeed?” she murmured, in piteous raillery. “You mean in name!"
110 "Well, I don’t want to be anything more.”"
110 ""
110 "“You _may_ think so, Tess! You are. What do you mean?”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t know,” she said hastily, with tears in her accents. “I thought"
110 "I—because I am not respectable, I mean. I told you I thought I was not"
110 "respectable enough long ago—and on that account I didn’t want to marry"
110 "you, only—only you urged me!”"
110 ""
110 "She broke into sobs, and turned her back to him. It would almost have"
110 "won round any man but Angel Clare. Within the remote depths of his"
110 "constitution, so gentle and affectionate as he was in general, there"
110 "lay hidden a hard logical deposit, like a vein of metal in a soft loam,"
110 "which turned the edge of everything that attempted to traverse it. It"
110 "had blocked his acceptance of the Church; it blocked his acceptance of"
110 "Tess. Moreover, his affection itself was less fire than radiance, and,"
110 "with regard to the other sex, when he ceased to believe he ceased to"
110 "follow: contrasting in this with many impressionable natures, who"
110 "remain sensuously infatuated with what they intellectually despise. He"
110 "waited till her sobbing ceased."
110 ""

110 "in an ebullition of bitterness against womankind in general. “It isn’t"
110 "a question of respectability, but one of principle!”"
110 ""
110 "He spoke such things as these and more of a kindred sort to her, being"
110 "still swayed by the antipathetic wave which warps direct souls with"
110 "such persistence when once their vision finds itself mocked by"
110 "appearances. There was, it is true, underneath, a back current of"
110 "sympathy through which a woman of the world might have conquered him."
110 "But Tess did not think of this; she took everything as her deserts, and"
110 "hardly opened her mouth. The firmness of her devotion to him was indeed"
110 "almost pitiful; quick-tempered as she naturally was, nothing that he"
110 "could say made her unseemly; she sought not her own; was not provoked;"
110 "thought no evil of his treatment of her. She might just now have been"
110 "Apostolic Charity herself returned to a self-seeking modern world."
110 ""
110 "This evening, night, and morning were passed precisely as the preceding"
110 "ones had been passed. On one, and only one, occasion did she—the"
110 "formerly free and independent Tess—venture to make any advances. It was"
110 "on the third occasion of his starting after a meal to go out to the"
110 "flour-mill. As he was leaving the table he said “Goodbye,” and she"
110 "replied in the same words, at the same time inclining her mouth in the"
110 "way of his. He did not avail himself of the invitation, saying, as he"
110 "turned hastily aside—"
110 ""
110 "“I shall be home punctually.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess shrank into herself as if she had been struck. Often enough had he"
110 "tried to reach those lips against her consent—often had he said gaily"
110 "that her mouth and breath tasted of the butter and eggs and milk and"
110 "honey on which she mainly lived, that he drew sustenance from them, and"
110 "other follies of that sort. But he did not care for them now. He"
110 "observed her sudden shrinking, and said gently—"
110 ""
110 "“You know, I have to think of a course. It was imperative that we"
110 "should stay together a little while, to avoid the scandal to you that"
110 "would have resulted from our immediate parting. But you must see it is"
110 "only for form’s sake.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes,” said Tess absently."
110 ""
110 "He went out, and on his way to the mill stood still, and wished for a"
110 "moment that he had responded yet more kindly, and kissed her once at"
110 "least."
110 ""
110 "Thus they lived through this despairing day or two; in the same house,"
110 "truly; but more widely apart than before they were lovers. It was"
110 "evident to her that he was, as he had said, living with paralyzed"
110 "activities in his endeavour to think of a plan of procedure. She was"
110 "awe-stricken to discover such determination under such apparent"
110 "flexibility. His consistency was, indeed, too cruel. She no longer"
110 "expected forgiveness now. More than once she thought of going away from"
110 "him during his absence at the mill; but she feared that this, instead"
110 "of benefiting him, might be the means of hampering and humiliating him"
110 "yet more if it should become known."
110 ""
110 "Meanwhile Clare was meditating, verily. His thought had been"
110 "unsuspended; he was becoming ill with thinking; eaten out with"
110 "thinking, withered by thinking; scourged out of all his former"
110 "pulsating, flexuous domesticity. He walked about saying to himself,"
110 "“What’s to be done—what’s to be done?” and by chance she overheard him."
110 "It caused her to break the reserve about their future which had"
110 "hitherto prevailed."
110 ""
110 "“I suppose—you are not going to live with me—long, are you, Angel?” she"
110 "asked, the sunk corners of her mouth betraying how purely mechanical"
110 "were the means by which she retained that expression of chastened calm"
110 "upon her face."
110 ""
110 "“I cannot” he said, “without despising myself, and what is worse,"
110 "perhaps, despising you. I mean, of course, cannot live with you in the"
110 "ordinary sense. At present, whatever I feel, I do not despise you. And,"
110 "let me speak plainly, or you may not see all my difficulties. How can"
110 "we live together while that man lives?—he being your husband in nature,"
110 "and not I. If he were dead it might be different... Besides, that’s not"
110 "all the difficulty; it lies in another consideration—one bearing upon"
110 "the future of other people than ourselves. Think of years to come, and"
110 "children being born to us, and this past matter getting known—for it"
110 "must get known. There is not an uttermost part of the earth but"
110 "somebody comes from it or goes to it from elsewhere. Well, think of"
110 "wretches of our flesh and blood growing up under a taunt which they"
110 "will gradually get to feel the full force of with their expanding"
110 "years. What an awakening for them! What a prospect! Can you honestly"
110 "say ‘Remain’ after contemplating this contingency? Don’t you think we"
110 "had better endure the ills we have than fly to others?”"
110 ""
110 "Her eyelids, weighted with trouble, continued drooping as before."
110 ""
110 "“I cannot say ‘Remain,’” she answered, “I cannot; I had not thought so"
110 "far.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess’s feminine hope—shall we confess it?—had been so obstinately"
110 "recuperative as to revive in her surreptitious visions of a domiciliary"
110 "intimacy continued long enough to break down his coldness even against"
110 "his judgement. Though unsophisticated in the usual sense, she was not"
110 "incomplete; and it would have denoted deficiency of womanhood if she"
110 "had not instinctively known what an argument lies in propinquity."
110 "Nothing else would serve her, she knew, if this failed. It was wrong to"
110 "hope in what was of the nature of strategy, she said to herself: yet"
110 "that sort of hope she could not extinguish. His last representation had"
110 "now been made, and it was, as she said, a new view. She had truly never"
110 "thought so far as that, and his lucid picture of possible offspring who"
110 "would scorn her was one that brought deadly convictions to an honest"
110 "heart which was humanitarian to its centre. Sheer experience had"
110 "already taught her that in some circumstances there was one thing"
110 "better than to lead a good life, and that was to be saved from leading"
110 "any life whatever. Like all who have been previsioned by suffering, she"
110 "could, in the words of M. Sully-Prudhomme, hear a penal sentence in the"
110 "fiat, “You shall be born,” particularly if addressed to potential issue"
110 "of hers."
110 ""
110 "Yet such is the vulpine slyness of Dame Nature, that, till now, Tess"
110 "had been hoodwinked by her love for Clare into forgetting it might"
110 "result in vitalizations that would inflict upon others what she had"
110 "bewailed as misfortune to herself."
110 ""
110 "She therefore could not withstand his argument. But with the"
110 "self-combating proclivity of the supersensitive, an answer thereto"
110 "arose in Clare’s own mind, and he almost feared it. It was based on her"
110 "exceptional physical nature; and she might have used it promisingly."
110 "She might have added besides: “On an Australian upland or Texan plain,"
110 "who is to know or care about my misfortunes, or to reproach me or you?”"
110 "Yet, like the majority of women, she accepted the momentary presentment"
110 "as if it were the inevitable. And she may have been right. The"
110 "intuitive heart of woman knoweth not only its own bitterness, but its"
110 "husband’s, and even if these assumed reproaches were not likely to be"
110 "addressed to him or to his by strangers, they might have reached his"
110 "ears from his own fastidious brain."
110 ""
110 "It was the third day of the estrangement. Some might risk the odd"
110 "paradox that with more animalism he would have been the nobler man. We"
110 "do not say it. Yet Clare’s love was doubtless ethereal to a fault,"
110 "imaginative to impracticability. With these natures, corporal presence"
110 "is something less appealing than corporal absence; the latter creating"
110 "an ideal presence that conveniently drops the defects of the real. She"
110 "found that her personality did not plead her cause so forcibly as she"
110 "had anticipated. The figurative phrase was true: she was another woman"
110 "than the one who had excited his desire."
110 ""
110 "“I have thought over what you say,” she remarked to him, moving her"
110 "forefinger over the tablecloth, her other hand, which bore the ring"
110 "that mocked them both, supporting her forehead. “It is quite true, all"
110 "of it; it must be. You must go away from me.”"
110 ""
110 "“But what can you do?”"
110 ""
110 "“I can go home.”"
110 ""
110 "Clare had not thought of that."
110 ""
110 "“Are you sure?” he inquired."
110 ""
110 "“Quite sure. We ought to part, and we may as well get it past and done."
110 "You once said that I was apt to win men against their better judgement;"
110 "and if I am constantly before your eyes I may cause you to change your"
110 "plans in opposition to your reason and wish; and afterwards your"
110 "repentance and my sorrow will be terrible.”"
110 ""
110 "“And you would like to go home?” he asked."
110 ""
110 "“I want to leave you, and go home.”"
110 ""
110 "“Then it shall be so.”"
110 ""
110 "Though she did not look up at him, she started. There was a difference"
110 "between the proposition and the covenant, which she had felt only too"
110 "quickly."
110 ""
110 "“I feared it would come to this,” she murmured, her countenance meekly"
110 "fixed. “I don’t complain, Angel, I—I think it best. What you said has"
110 "quite convinced me. Yes, though nobody else should reproach me if we"
110 "should stay together, yet somewhen, years hence, you might get angry"
110 "with me for any ordinary matter, and knowing what you do of my bygones,"
110 "you yourself might be tempted to say words, and they might be"
110 "overheard, perhaps by my own children. O, what only hurts me now would"
110 "torture and kill me then! I will go—to-morrow.”"
110 ""
110 "“And I shall not stay here. Though I didn’t like to initiate it, I have"
110 "seen that it was advisable we should part—at least for a while, till I"
110 "can better see the shape that things have taken, and can write to you.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess stole a glance at her husband. He was pale, even tremulous; but,"
110 "as before, she was appalled by the determination revealed in the depths"
110 "of this gentle being she had married—the will to subdue the grosser to"
110 "the subtler emotion, the substance to the conception, the flesh to the"
110 "spirit. Propensities, tendencies, habits, were as dead leaves upon the"
110 "tyrannous wind of his imaginative ascendency."
110 ""
110 "He may have observed her look, for he explained—"
110 ""
110 "“I think of people more kindly when I am away from them”; adding"
110 "cynically, “God knows; perhaps we will shake down together some day,"
110 "for weariness; thousands have done it!”"
110 ""
110 "That day he began to pack up, and she went upstairs and began to pack"
110 "also. Both knew that it was in their two minds that they might part the"
110 "next morning for ever, despite the gloss of assuaging conjectures"
110 "thrown over their proceeding because they were of the sort to whom any"
110 "parting which has an air of finality is a torture. He knew, and she"
110 "knew, that, though the fascination which each had exercised over the"
110 "other—on her part independently of accomplishments—would probably in"
110 "the first days of their separation be even more potent than ever, time"
110 "must attenuate that effect; the practical arguments against accepting"
110 "her as a housemate might pronounce themselves more strongly in the"
110 "boreal light of a remoter view. Moreover, when two people are once"
110 "parted—have abandoned a common domicile and a common environment—new"
110 "growths insensibly bud upward to fill each vacated place; unforeseen"
110 "accidents hinder intentions, and old plans are forgotten."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXXVII>"
110 ""
110 "Midnight came and passed silently, for there was nothing to announce it"
110 "in the Valley of the Froom."
110 ""
110 "Not long after one o’clock there was a slight creak in the darkened"
110 "farmhouse once the mansion of the d’Urbervilles. Tess, who used the"
110 "upper chamber, heard it and awoke. It had come from the corner step of"
110 "the staircase, which, as usual, was loosely nailed. She saw the door of"
110 "her bedroom open, and the figure of her husband crossed the stream of"
110 "moonlight with a curiously careful tread. He was in his shirt and"
110 "trousers only, and her first flush of joy died when she perceived that"
110 "his eyes were fixed in an unnatural stare on vacancy. When he reached"
110 "the middle of the room he stood still and murmured in tones of"
110 "indescribable sadness—"
110 ""
110 "“Dead! dead! dead!”"
110 ""
110 "Under the influence of any strongly-disturbing force, Clare would"
110 "occasionally walk in his sleep, and even perform strange feats, such as"
110 "he had done on the night of their return from market just before their"
110 "marriage, when he re-enacted in his bedroom his combat with the man who"
110 "had insulted her. Tess saw that continued mental distress had wrought"
110 "him into that somnambulistic state now."
110 ""
110 "Her loyal confidence in him lay so deep down in her heart, that, awake"
110 "or asleep, he inspired her with no sort of personal fear. If he had"
110 "entered with a pistol in his hand he would scarcely have disturbed her"
110 "trust in his protectiveness."
110 ""
110 "Clare came close, and bent over her. “Dead, dead, dead!” he murmured."
110 ""
110 "After fixedly regarding her for some moments with the same gaze of"
110 "unmeasurable woe, he bent lower, enclosed her in his arms, and rolled"
110 "her in the sheet as in a shroud. Then lifting her from the bed with as"
110 "much respect as one would show to a dead body, he carried her across"
110 "the room, murmuring—"
110 ""
110 "“My poor, poor Tess—my dearest, darling Tess! So sweet, so good, so"
110 "true!”"
110 ""
110 "The words of endearment, withheld so severely in his waking hours, were"
110 "inexpressibly sweet to her forlorn and hungry heart. If it had been to"
110 "save her weary life she would not, by moving or struggling, have put an"
110 "end to the position she found herself in. Thus she lay in absolute"
110 "stillness, scarcely venturing to breathe, and, wondering what he was"
110 "going to do with her, suffered herself to be borne out upon the"
110 "landing."
110 ""
110 "“My wife—dead, dead!” he said."
110 ""
110 "He paused in his labours for a moment to lean with her against the"
110 "banister. Was he going to throw her down? Self-solicitude was near"
110 "extinction in her, and in the knowledge that he had planned to depart"
110 "on the morrow, possibly for always, she lay in his arms in this"
110 "precarious position with a sense rather of luxury than of terror. If"
110 "they could only fall together, and both be dashed to pieces, how fit,"
110 "how desirable."
110 ""
110 "However, he did not let her fall, but took advantage of the support of"
110 "the handrail to imprint a kiss upon her lips—lips in the day-time"
110 "scorned. Then he clasped her with a renewed firmness of hold, and"
110 "descended the staircase. The creak of the loose stair did not awaken"
110 "him, and they reached the ground-floor safely. Freeing one of his hands"
110 "from his grasp of her for a moment, he slid back the door-bar and"
110 "passed out, slightly striking his stockinged toe against the edge of"
110 "the door. But this he seemed not to mind, and, having room for"
110 "extension in the open air, he lifted her against his shoulder, so that"
110 "he could carry her with ease, the absence of clothes taking much from"
110 "his burden. Thus he bore her off the premises in the direction of the"
110 "river a few yards distant."
110 ""
110 "His ultimate intention, if he had any, she had not yet divined; and she"
110 "found herself conjecturing on the matter as a third person might have"
110 "done. So easefully had she delivered her whole being up to him that it"
110 "pleased her to think he was regarding her as his absolute possession,"
110 "to dispose of as he should choose. It was consoling, under the hovering"
110 "terror of to-morrow’s separation, to feel that he really recognized her"
110 "now as his wife Tess, and did not cast her off, even if in that"
110 "recognition he went so far as to arrogate to himself the right of"
110 "harming her."
110 ""
110 "Ah! now she knew what he was dreaming of—that Sunday morning when he"
110 "had borne her along through the water with the other dairymaids, who"
110 "had loved him nearly as much as she, if that were possible, which Tess"
110 "could hardly admit. Clare did not cross the bridge with her, but"
110 "proceeding several paces on the same side towards the adjoining mill,"
110 "at length stood still on the brink of the river."
110 ""
110 "Its waters, in creeping down these miles of meadowland, frequently"
110 "divided, serpentining in purposeless curves, looping themselves around"
110 "little islands that had no name, returning and re-embodying themselves"
110 "as a broad main stream further on. Opposite the spot to which he had"
110 "brought her was such a general confluence, and the river was"
110 "proportionately voluminous and deep. Across it was a narrow"
110 "foot-bridge; but now the autumn flood had washed the handrail away,"
110 "leaving the bare plank only, which, lying a few inches above the"
110 "speeding current, formed a giddy pathway for even steady heads; and"
110 "Tess had noticed from the window of the house in the day-time young men"
110 "walking across upon it as a feat in balancing. Her husband had possibly"
110 "observed the same performance; anyhow, he now mounted the plank, and,"
110 "sliding one foot forward, advanced along it."
110 ""
110 "Was he going to drown her? Probably he was. The spot was lonely, the"
110 "river deep and wide enough to make such a purpose easy of"
110 "accomplishment. He might drown her if he would; it would be better than"
110 "parting to-morrow to lead severed lives."
110 ""
110 "The swift stream raced and gyrated under them, tossing, distorting, and"
110 "splitting the moon’s reflected face. Spots of froth travelled past, and"
110 "intercepted weeds waved behind the piles. If they could both fall"
110 "together into the current now, their arms would be so tightly clasped"
110 "together that they could not be saved; they would go out of the world"
110 "almost painlessly, and there would be no more reproach to her, or to"
110 "him for marrying her. His last half-hour with her would have been a"
110 "loving one, while if they lived till he awoke, his day-time aversion"
110 "would return, and this hour would remain to be contemplated only as a"
110 "transient dream."
110 ""
110 "The impulse stirred in her, yet she dared not indulge it, to make a"
110 "movement that would have precipitated them both into the gulf. How she"
110 "valued her own life had been proved; but his—she had no right to tamper"
110 "with it. He reached the other side with her in safety."
110 ""
110 "Here they were within a plantation which formed the Abbey grounds, and"
110 "taking a new hold of her he went onward a few steps till they reached"
110 "the ruined choir of the Abbey-church. Against the north wall was the"
110 "empty stone coffin of an abbot, in which every tourist with a turn for"
110 "grim humour was accustomed to stretch himself. In this Clare carefully"
110 "laid Tess. Having kissed her lips a second time he breathed deeply, as"
110 "if a greatly desired end were attained. Clare then lay down on the"
110 "ground alongside, when he immediately fell into the deep dead slumber"
110 "of exhaustion, and remained motionless as a log. The spurt of mental"
110 "excitement which had produced the effort was now over."
110 ""
110 "Tess sat up in the coffin. The night, though dry and mild for the"
110 "season, was more than sufficiently cold to make it dangerous for him to"
110 "remain here long, in his half-clothed state. If he were left to himself"
110 "he would in all probability stay there till the morning, and be chilled"
110 "to certain death. She had heard of such deaths after sleep-walking. But"
110 "how could she dare to awaken him, and let him know what he had been"
110 "doing, when it would mortify him to discover his folly in respect of"
110 "her? Tess, however, stepping out of her stone confine, shook him"
110 "slightly, but was unable to arouse him without being violent. It was"
110 "indispensable to do something, for she was beginning to shiver, the"
110 "sheet being but a poor protection. Her excitement had in a measure kept"
110 "her warm during the few minutes’ adventure; but that beatific interval"
110 "was over."
110 ""
110 "It suddenly occurred to her to try persuasion; and accordingly she"
110 "whispered in his ear, with as much firmness and decision as she could"
110 "summon—"
110 ""
110 "“Let us walk on, darling,” at the same time taking him suggestively by"
110 "the arm. To her relief, he unresistingly acquiesced; her words had"
110 "apparently thrown him back into his dream, which thenceforward seemed"
110 "to enter on a new phase, wherein he fancied she had risen as a spirit,"
110 "and was leading him to Heaven. Thus she conducted him by the arm to the"
110 "stone bridge in front of their residence, crossing which they stood at"
110 "the manor-house door. Tess’s feet were quite bare, and the stones hurt"
110 "her, and chilled her to the bone; but Clare was in his woollen"
110 "stockings and appeared to feel no discomfort."
110 ""
110 "There was no further difficulty. She induced him to lie down on his own"
110 "sofa bed, and covered him up warmly, lighting a temporary fire of wood,"
110 "to dry any dampness out of him. The noise of these attentions she"
110 "thought might awaken him, and secretly wished that they might. But the"
110 "exhaustion of his mind and body was such that he remained undisturbed."
110 ""
110 "As soon as they met the next morning Tess divined that Angel knew"
110 "little or nothing of how far she had been concerned in the night’s"
110 "excursion, though, as regarded himself, he may have been aware that he"
110 "had not lain still. In truth, he had awakened that morning from a sleep"
110 "deep as annihilation; and during those first few moments in which the"
110 "brain, like a Samson shaking himself, is trying its strength, he had"
110 "some dim notion of an unusual nocturnal proceeding. But the realities"
110 "of his situation soon displaced conjecture on the other subject."
110 ""
110 "He waited in expectancy to discern some mental pointing; he knew that"
110 "if any intention of his, concluded over-night, did not vanish in the"
110 "light of morning, it stood on a basis approximating to one of pure"
110 "reason, even if initiated by impulse of feeling; that it was so far,"
110 "therefore, to be trusted. He thus beheld in the pale morning light the"
110 "resolve to separate from her; not as a hot and indignant instinct, but"
110 "denuded of the passionateness which had made it scorch and burn;"
110 "standing in its bones; nothing but a skeleton, but none the less there."
110 "Clare no longer hesitated."
110 ""
110 "At breakfast, and while they were packing the few remaining articles,"
110 "he showed his weariness from the night’s effort so unmistakeably that"
110 "Tess was on the point of revealing all that had happened; but the"
110 "reflection that it would anger him, grieve him, stultify him, to know"
110 "that he had instinctively manifested a fondness for her of which his"
110 "common-sense did not approve, that his inclination had compromised his"
110 "dignity when reason slept, again deterred her. It was too much like"
110 "laughing at a man when sober for his erratic deeds during intoxication."
110 ""
110 "It just crossed her mind, too, that he might have a faint recollection"
110 "of his tender vagary, and was disinclined to allude to it from a"
110 "conviction that she would take amatory advantage of the opportunity it"
110 "gave her of appealing to him anew not to go."
110 ""
110 "He had ordered by letter a vehicle from the nearest town, and soon"
110 "after breakfast it arrived. She saw in it the beginning of the end—the"
110 "temporary end, at least, for the revelation of his tenderness by the"
110 "incident of the night raised dreams of a possible future with him. The"
110 "luggage was put on the top, and the man drove them off, the miller and"
110 "the old waiting-woman expressing some surprise at their precipitate"
110 "departure, which Clare attributed to his discovery that the mill-work"
110 "was not of the modern kind which he wished to investigate, a statement"
110 "that was true so far as it went. Beyond this there was nothing in the"
110 "manner of their leaving to suggest a _fiasco_, or that they were not"
110 "going together to visit friends."
110 ""
110 "Their route lay near the dairy from which they had started with such"
110 "solemn joy in each other a few days back, and as Clare wished to wind"
110 "up his business with Mr Crick, Tess could hardly avoid paying Mrs Crick"
110 "a call at the same time, unless she would excite suspicion of their"
110 "unhappy state."
110 ""
110 "To make the call as unobtrusive as possible, they left the carriage by"
110 "the wicket leading down from the high road to the dairy-house, and"
110 "descended the track on foot, side by side. The withy-bed had been cut,"
110 "and they could see over the stumps the spot to which Clare had followed"
110 "her when he pressed her to be his wife; to the left the enclosure in"
110 "which she had been fascinated by his harp; and far away behind the"
110 "cow-stalls the mead which had been the scene of their first embrace."
110 "The gold of the summer picture was now gray, the colours mean, the rich"
110 "soil mud, and the river cold."
110 ""
110 "Over the barton-gate the dairyman saw them, and came forward, throwing"
110 "into his face the kind of jocularity deemed appropriate in Talbothays"
110 "and its vicinity on the re-appearance of the newly-married. Then Mrs"
110 "Crick emerged from the house, and several others of their old"
110 "acquaintance, though Marian and Retty did not seem to be there."
110 ""
110 "Tess valiantly bore their sly attacks and friendly humours, which"
110 "affected her far otherwise than they supposed. In the tacit agreement"
110 "of husband and wife to keep their estrangement a secret they behaved as"
110 "would have been ordinary. And then, although she would rather there had"
110 "been no word spoken on the subject, Tess had to hear in detail the"
110 "story of Marian and Retty. The later had gone home to her father’s, and"
110 "Marian had left to look for employment elsewhere. They feared she would"
110 "come to no good."
110 ""
110 "To dissipate the sadness of this recital Tess went and bade all her"
110 "favourite cows goodbye, touching each of them with her hand, and as she"
110 "and Clare stood side by side at leaving, as if united body and soul,"
110 "there would have been something peculiarly sorry in their aspect to one"
110 "who should have seen it truly; two limbs of one life, as they outwardly"
110 "were, his arm touching hers, her skirts touching him, facing one way,"
110 "as against all the dairy facing the other, speaking in their adieux as"
110 "“we”, and yet sundered like the poles. Perhaps something unusually"
110 "stiff and embarrassed in their attitude, some awkwardness in acting up"
110 "to their profession of unity, different from the natural shyness of"
110 "young couples, may have been apparent, for when they were gone Mrs"
110 "Crick said to her husband—"
110 ""
110 "“How onnatural the brightness of her eyes did seem, and how they stood"
110 "like waxen images and talked as if they were in a dream! Didn’t it"
110 "strike ’ee that ’twas so? Tess had always sommat strange in her, and"
110 "she’s not now quite like the proud young bride of a well-be-doing man.”"
110 ""
110 "They re-entered the vehicle, and were driven along the roads towards"
110 "Weatherbury and Stagfoot Lane, till they reached the Lane inn, where"
110 "Clare dismissed the fly and man. They rested here a while, and entering"
110 "the Vale were next driven onward towards her home by a stranger who did"
110 "not know their relations. At a midway point, when Nuttlebury had been"
110 "passed, and where there were cross-roads, Clare stopped the conveyance"
110 "and said to Tess that if she meant to return to her mother’s house it"
110 "was here that he would leave her. As they could not talk with freedom"
110 "in the driver’s presence he asked her to accompany him for a few steps"
110 "on foot along one of the branch roads; she assented, and directing the"
110 "man to wait a few minutes they strolled away."
110 ""
110 "“Now, let us understand each other,” he said gently. “There is no anger"
110 "between us, though there is that which I cannot endure at present. I"
110 "will try to bring myself to endure it. I will let you know where I go"
110 "to as soon as I know myself. And if I can bring myself to bear it—if it"
110 "is desirable, possible—I will come to you. But until I come to you it"
110 "will be better that you should not try to come to me.”"
110 ""
110 "The severity of the decree seemed deadly to Tess; she saw his view of"
110 "her clearly enough; he could regard her in no other light than that of"
110 "one who had practised gross deceit upon him. Yet could a woman who had"
110 "done even what she had done deserve all this? But she could contest the"
110 "point with him no further. She simply repeated after him his own words."
110 ""
110 "“Until you come to me I must not try to come to you?”"
110 ""
110 "“Just so.”"
110 ""
110 "“May I write to you?”"
110 ""
110 "“O yes—if you are ill, or want anything at all. I hope that will not be"
110 "the case; so that it may happen that I write first to you.”"
110 ""
110 "“I agree to the conditions, Angel; because you know best what my"
110 "punishment ought to be; only—only—don’t make it more than I can bear!”"
110 ""
110 "That was all she said on the matter. If Tess had been artful, had she"
110 "made a scene, fainted, wept hysterically, in that lonely lane,"
110 "notwithstanding the fury of fastidiousness with which he was possessed,"
110 "he would probably not have withstood her. But her mood of"
110 "long-suffering made his way easy for him, and she herself was his best"
110 "advocate. Pride, too, entered into her submission—which perhaps was a"
110 "symptom of that reckless acquiescence in chance too apparent in the"
110 "whole d’Urberville family—and the many effective chords which she could"
110 "have stirred by an appeal were left untouched."
110 ""
110 "The remainder of their discourse was on practical matters only. He now"
110 "handed her a packet containing a fairly good sum of money, which he had"
110 "obtained from his bankers for the purpose. The brilliants, the interest"
110 "in which seemed to be Tess’s for her life only (if he understood the"
110 "wording of the will), he advised her to let him send to a bank for"
110 "safety; and to this she readily agreed."
110 ""
110 "These things arranged, he walked with Tess back to the carriage, and"
110 "handed her in. The coachman was paid and told where to drive her."
110 "Taking next his own bag and umbrella—the sole articles he had brought"
110 "with him hitherwards—he bade her goodbye; and they parted there and"
110 "then."
110 ""
110 "The fly moved creepingly up a hill, and Clare watched it go with an"
110 "unpremeditated hope that Tess would look out of the window for one"
110 "moment. But that she never thought of doing, would not have ventured to"
110 "do, lying in a half-dead faint inside. Thus he beheld her recede, and"
110 "in the anguish of his heart quoted a line from a poet, with peculiar"
110 "emendations of his own—"
110 ""
110 "    God’s _not_ in his heaven: All’s _wrong_ with the world!"
110 ""
110 "When Tess had passed over the crest of the hill he turned to go his own"
110 "way, and hardly knew that he loved her still."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XXXVIII>"
110 ""
110 "As she drove on through Blackmoor Vale, and the landscape of her youth"
110 "began to open around her, Tess aroused herself from her stupor. Her"
110 "first thought was how would she be able to face her parents?"
110 ""
110 "She reached a turnpike-gate which stood upon the highway to the"
110 "village. It was thrown open by a stranger, not by the old man who had"
110 "kept it for many years, and to whom she had been known; he had probably"
110 "left on New Year’s Day, the date when such changes were made. Having"
110 "received no intelligence lately from her home, she asked the"
110 "turnpike-keeper for news."
110 ""
110 "“Oh—nothing, miss,” he answered. “Marlott is Marlott still. Folks have"
110 "died and that. John Durbeyfield, too, hev had a daughter married this"
110 "week to a gentleman-farmer; not from John’s own house, you know; they"
110 "was married elsewhere; the gentleman being of that high standing that"
110 "John’s own folk was not considered well-be-doing enough to have any"
110 "part in it, the bridegroom seeming not to know how’t have been"
110 "discovered that John is a old and ancient nobleman himself by blood,"
110 "with family skillentons in their own vaults to this day, but done out"
110 "of his property in the time o’ the Romans. However, Sir John, as we"
110 "call ’n now, kept up the wedding-day as well as he could, and stood"
110 "treat to everybody in the parish; and John’s wife sung songs at The"
110 "Pure Drop till past eleven o’clock.”"
110 ""
110 "Hearing this, Tess felt so sick at heart that she could not decide to"
110 "go home publicly in the fly with her luggage and belongings. She asked"
110 "the turnpike-keeper if she might deposit her things at his house for a"
110 "while, and, on his offering no objection, she dismissed her carriage,"
110 "and went on to the village alone by a back lane."
110 ""
110 "At sight of her father’s chimney she asked herself how she could"
110 "possibly enter the house? Inside that cottage her relations were calmly"
110 "supposing her far away on a wedding-tour with a comparatively rich man,"
110 "who was to conduct her to bouncing prosperity; while here she was,"
110 "friendless, creeping up to the old door quite by herself, with no"
110 "better place to go to in the world."
110 ""
110 "She did not reach the house unobserved. Just by the garden-hedge she"
110 "was met by a girl who knew her—one of the two or three with whom she"
110 "had been intimate at school. After making a few inquiries as to how"
110 "Tess came there, her friend, unheeding her tragic look, interrupted"
110 "with—"
110 ""
110 "“But where’s thy gentleman, Tess?”"
110 ""
110 "Tess hastily explained that he had been called away on business, and,"
110 "leaving her interlocutor, clambered over the garden-hedge, and thus"
110 "made her way to the house."
110 ""
110 "As she went up the garden-path she heard her mother singing by the back"
110 "door, coming in sight of which she perceived Mrs Durbeyfield on the"
110 "doorstep in the act of wringing a sheet. Having performed this without"
110 "observing Tess, she went indoors, and her daughter followed her."
110 ""
110 "The washing-tub stood in the same old place on the same old"
110 "quarter-hogshead, and her mother, having thrown the sheet aside, was"
110 "about to plunge her arms in anew."
110 ""
110 "“Why—Tess!—my chil’—I thought you was married!—married really and truly"
110 "this time—we sent the cider—”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, mother; so I am.”"
110 ""
110 "“Going to be?”"
110 ""
110 "“No—I am married.”"
110 ""
110 "“Married! Then where’s thy husband?”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh, he’s gone away for a time.”"
110 ""
110 "“Gone away! When was you married, then? The day you said?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, Tuesday, mother.”"
110 ""
110 "“And now ’tis on’y Saturday, and he gone away?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, he’s gone.”"
110 ""
110 "“What’s the meaning o’ that? ’Nation seize such husbands as you seem to"
110 "get, say I!”"
110 ""
110 "“Mother!” Tess went across to Joan Durbeyfield, laid her face upon the"
110 "matron’s bosom, and burst into sobs. “I don’t know how to tell ’ee,"
110 "mother! You said to me, and wrote to me, that I was not to tell him."
110 "But I did tell him—I couldn’t help it—and he went away!”"
110 ""
110 "“O you little fool—you little fool!” burst out Mrs Durbeyfield,"
110 "splashing Tess and herself in her agitation. “My good God! that ever I"
110 "should ha’ lived to say it, but I say it again, you little fool!”"
110 ""
110 "Tess was convulsed with weeping, the tension of so many days having"
110 "relaxed at last."
110 ""
110 "“I know it—I know—I know!” she gasped through her sobs. “But, O my"
110 "mother, I could not help it! He was so good—and I felt the wickedness"
110 "of trying to blind him as to what had happened! If—if—it were to be"
110 "done again—I should do the same. I could not—I dared not—so sin—against"
110 "him!”"
110 ""
110 "“But you sinned enough to marry him first!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, yes; that’s where my misery do lie! But I thought he could get"
110 "rid o’ me by law if he were determined not to overlook it. And O, if"
110 "you knew—if you could only half know how I loved him—how anxious I was"
110 "to have him—and how wrung I was between caring so much for him and my"
110 "wish to be fair to him!”"
110 ""
110 "Tess was so shaken that she could get no further, and sank, a helpless"
110 "thing, into a chair."
110 ""
110 "“Well, well; what’s done can’t be undone! I’m sure I don’t know why"
110 "children o’ my bringing forth should all be bigger simpletons than"
110 "other people’s—not to know better than to blab such a thing as that,"
110 "when he couldn’t ha’ found it out till too late!” Here Mrs Durbeyfield"
110 "began shedding tears on her own account as a mother to be pitied. “What"
110 "your father will say I don’t know,” she continued; “for he’s been"
110 "talking about the wedding up at Rolliver’s and The Pure Drop every day"
110 "since, and about his family getting back to their rightful position"
110 "through you—poor silly man!—and now you’ve made this mess of it! The"
110 "Lord-a-Lord!”"
110 ""
110 "As if to bring matters to a focus, Tess’s father was heard approaching"
110 "at that moment. He did not, however, enter immediately, and Mrs"
110 "Durbeyfield said that she would break the bad news to him herself, Tess"
110 "keeping out of sight for the present. After her first burst of"
110 "disappointment Joan began to take the mishap as she had taken Tess’s"
110 "original trouble, as she would have taken a wet holiday or failure in"
110 "the potato-crop; as a thing which had come upon them irrespective of"
110 "desert or folly; a chance external impingement to be borne with; not a"
110 "lesson."
110 ""
110 "Tess retreated upstairs and beheld casually that the beds had been"
110 "shifted, and new arrangements made. Her old bed had been adapted for"
110 "two younger children. There was no place here for her now."
110 ""
110 "The room below being unceiled she could hear most of what went on"
110 "there. Presently her father entered, apparently carrying in a live hen."
110 "He was a foot-haggler now, having been obliged to sell his second"
110 "horse, and he travelled with his basket on his arm. The hen had been"
110 "carried about this morning as it was often carried, to show people that"
110 "he was in his work, though it had lain, with its legs tied, under the"
110 "table at Rolliver’s for more than an hour."
110 ""
110 "“We’ve just had up a story about—” Durbeyfield began, and thereupon"
110 "related in detail to his wife a discussion which had arisen at the inn"
110 "about the clergy, originated by the fact of his daughter having married"
110 "into a clerical family. “They was formerly styled ‘sir’, like my own"
110 "ancestry,” he said, “though nowadays their true style, strictly"
110 "speaking, is ‘clerk’ only.” As Tess had wished that no great publicity"
110 "should be given to the event, he had mentioned no particulars. He hoped"
110 "she would remove that prohibition soon. He proposed that the couple"
110 "should take Tess’s own name, d’Urberville, as uncorrupted. It was"
110 "better than her husbands’s. He asked if any letter had come from her"
110 "that day."
110 ""
110 "Then Mrs Durbeyfield informed him that no letter had come, but Tess"
110 "unfortunately had come herself."
110 ""
110 "When at length the collapse was explained to him, a sullen"
110 "mortification, not usual with Durbeyfield, overpowered the influence of"
110 "the cheering glass. Yet the intrinsic quality of the event moved his"
110 "touchy sensitiveness less than its conjectured effect upon the minds of"
110 "others."
110 ""
110 "“To think, now, that this was to be the end o’t!” said Sir John. “And I"
110 "with a family vault under that there church of Kingsbere as big as"
110 "Squire Jollard’s ale-cellar, and my folk lying there in sixes and"
110 "sevens, as genuine county bones and marrow as any recorded in history."
110 "And now to be sure what they fellers at Rolliver’s and The Pure Drop"
110 "will say to me! How they’ll squint and glane, and say, ‘This is yer"
110 "mighty match is it; this is yer getting back to the true level of yer"
110 "forefathers in King Norman’s time!’ I feel this is too much, Joan; I"
110 "shall put an end to myself, title and all—I can bear it no longer!..."
110 "But she can make him keep her if he’s married her?”"
110 ""
110 "“Why, yes. But she won’t think o’ doing that.”"
110 ""
110 "“D’ye think he really have married her?—or is it like the first—”"
110 ""
110 "Poor Tess, who had heard as far as this, could not bear to hear more."
110 "The perception that her word could be doubted even here, in her own"
110 "parental house, set her mind against the spot as nothing else could"
110 "have done. How unexpected were the attacks of destiny! And if her"
110 "father doubted her a little, would not neighbours and acquaintance"
110 "doubt her much? O, she could not live long at home!"
110 ""
110 "A few days, accordingly, were all that she allowed herself here, at the"
110 "end of which time she received a short note from Clare, informing her"
110 "that he had gone to the North of England to look at a farm. In her"
110 "craving for the lustre of her true position as his wife, and to hide"
110 "from her parents the vast extent of the division between them, she made"
110 "use of this letter as her reason for again departing, leaving them"
110 "under the impression that she was setting out to join him. Still"
110 "further to screen her husband from any imputation of unkindness to her,"
110 "she took twenty-five of the fifty pounds Clare had given her, and"
110 "handed the sum over to her mother, as if the wife of a man like Angel"
110 "Clare could well afford it, saying that it was a slight return for the"
110 "trouble and humiliation she had brought upon them in years past. With"
110 "this assertion of her dignity she bade them farewell; and after that"
110 "there were lively doings in the Durbeyfield household for some time on"
110 "the strength of Tess’s bounty, her mother saying, and, indeed,"
110 "believing, that the rupture which had arisen between the young husband"
110 "and wife had adjusted itself under their strong feeling that they could"
110 "not live apart from each other."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "XXXIX"
110 ""
110 "It was three weeks after the marriage that Clare found himself"
110 "descending the hill which led to the well-known parsonage of his"
110 "father. With his downward course the tower of the church rose into the"
110 "evening sky in a manner of inquiry as to why he had come; and no living"
110 "person in the twilighted town seemed to notice him, still less to"
110 "expect him. He was arriving like a ghost, and the sound of his own"
110 "footsteps was almost an encumbrance to be got rid of."
110 ""
110 "The picture of life had changed for him. Before this time he had known"
110 "it but speculatively; now he thought he knew it as a practical man;"
110 "though perhaps he did not, even yet. Nevertheless humanity stood before"
110 "him no longer in the pensive sweetness of Italian art, but in the"
110 "staring and ghastly attitudes of a Wiertz Museum, and with the leer of"
110 "a study by Van Beers."
110 ""
110 "His conduct during these first weeks had been desultory beyond"
110 "description. After mechanically attempting to pursue his agricultural"
110 "plans as though nothing unusual had happened, in the manner recommended"
110 "by the great and wise men of all ages, he concluded that very few of"
110 "those great and wise men had ever gone so far outside themselves as to"
110 "test the feasibility of their counsel. “This is the chief thing: be not"
110 "perturbed,” said the Pagan moralist. That was just Clare’s own opinion."
110 "But he was perturbed. “Let not your heart be troubled, neither let it"
110 "be afraid,” said the Nazarene. Clare chimed in cordially; but his heart"
110 "was troubled all the same. How he would have liked to confront those"
110 "two great thinkers, and earnestly appeal to them as fellow-man to"
110 "fellow-men, and ask them to tell him their method!"
110 ""
110 "His mood transmuted itself into a dogged indifference till at length he"
110 "fancied he was looking on his own existence with the passive interest"
110 "of an outsider."
110 ""
110 "He was embittered by the conviction that all this desolation had been"
110 "brought about by the accident of her being a d’Urberville. When he"
110 "found that Tess came of that exhausted ancient line, and was not of the"
110 "new tribes from below, as he had fondly dreamed, why had he not"
110 "stoically abandoned her in fidelity to his principles? This was what he"
110 "had got by apostasy, and his punishment was deserved."
110 ""
110 "Then he became weary and anxious, and his anxiety increased. He"
110 "wondered if he had treated her unfairly. He ate without knowing that he"
110 "ate, and drank without tasting. As the hours dropped past, as the"
110 "motive of each act in the long series of bygone days presented itself"
110 "to his view, he perceived how intimately the notion of having Tess as a"
110 "dear possession was mixed up with all his schemes and words and ways."
110 ""
110 "In going hither and thither he observed in the outskirts of a small"
110 "town a red-and-blue placard setting forth the great advantages of the"
110 "Empire of Brazil as a field for the emigrating agriculturist. Land was"
110 "offered there on exceptionally advantageous terms. Brazil somewhat"
110 "attracted him as a new idea. Tess could eventually join him there, and"
110 "perhaps in that country of contrasting scenes and notions and habits"
110 "the conventions would not be so operative which made life with her seem"
110 "impracticable to him here. In brief he was strongly inclined to try"
110 "Brazil, especially as the season for going thither was just at hand."
110 ""
110 "With this view he was returning to Emminster to disclose his plan to"
110 "his parents, and to make the best explanation he could make of arriving"
110 "without Tess, short of revealing what had actually separated them. As"
110 "he reached the door the new moon shone upon his face, just as the old"
110 "one had done in the small hours of that morning when he had carried his"
110 "wife in his arms across the river to the graveyard of the monks; but"
110 "his face was thinner now."
110 ""
110 "Clare had given his parents no warning of his visit, and his arrival"
110 "stirred the atmosphere of the Vicarage as the dive of the kingfisher"
110 "stirs a quiet pool. His father and mother were both in the"
110 "drawing-room, but neither of his brothers was now at home. Angel"
110 "entered, and closed the door quietly behind him."
110 ""
110 "“But—where’s your wife, dear Angel?” cried his mother. “How you"
110 "surprise us!”"
110 ""
110 "“She is at her mother’s—temporarily. I have come home rather in a hurry"
110 "because I’ve decided to go to Brazil.”"
110 ""
110 "“Brazil! Why they are all Roman Catholics there surely!”"
110 ""
110 "“Are they? I hadn’t thought of that.”"
110 ""
110 "But even the novelty and painfulness of his going to a Papistical land"
110 "could not displace for long Mr and Mrs Clare’s natural interest in"
110 "their son’s marriage."
110 ""
110 "“We had your brief note three weeks ago announcing that it had taken"
110 "place,” said Mrs Clare, “and your father sent your godmother’s gift to"

110 "present, especially as you preferred to marry her from the dairy, and"

110 "and given us no pleasure. Your bothers felt that very strongly. Now it"

110 "have not brought her. It seems strange. What has happened?”"
110 ""
110 "He replied that it had been thought best by them that she should go to"
110 "her parents’ home for the present, whilst he came there."
110 ""
110 "“I don’t mind telling you, dear mother,” he said, “that I always meant"
110 "to keep her away from this house till I should feel she could come with"

110 "go it will be unadvisable for me to take her on this my first journey."
110 "She will remain at her mother’s till I come back.”"
110 ""
110 "“And I shall not see her before you start?”"
110 ""
110 "He was afraid they would not. His original plan had been, as he had"
110 "said, to refrain from bringing her there for some little while—not to"
110 "wound their prejudices—feelings—in any way; and for other reasons he"
110 "had adhered to it. He would have to visit home in the course of a year,"

110 "before he started a second time—with her."
110 ""
110 "A hastily prepared supper was brought in, and Clare made further"
110 "exposition of his plans. His mother’s disappointment at not seeing the"

110 ""
110 "“Pure and virtuous, of course, she is.”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "His father was less inquisitive; but when the time came for the chapter"
110 "from the Bible which was always read before evening prayers, the Vicar"
110 "observed to Mrs Clare—"
110 ""
110 "“I think, since Angel has come, that it will be more appropriate to"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "A lump rose in Clare’s throat. The portable lectern was taken out from"

110 "aforesaid chapter—"
110 ""
110 "“Who can find a virtuous woman? for her price is far above rubies. She"
110 "riseth while it is yet night, and giveth meat to her household. She"
110 "girdeth her loins with strength and strengtheneth her arms. She"
110 "perceiveth that her merchandise is good; her candle goeth not out by"

110 ""

110 ""

110 "fine lady; but one who used her hands and her head and her heart for"

110 "husband also, and he praiseth her. Many daughters have done virtuously,"
110 "but she excelleth them all.’ Well, I wish I could have seen her, Angel."
110 "Since she is pure and chaste, she would have been refined enough for"
110 "me.”"
110 ""
110 "Clare could bear this no longer. His eyes were full of tears, which"
110 "seemed like drops of molten lead. He bade a quick good night to these"
110 "sincere and simple souls whom he loved so well; who knew neither the"
110 "world, the flesh, nor the devil in their own hearts, only as something"
110 "vague and external to themselves. He went to his own chamber."
110 ""
110 "His mother followed him, and tapped at his door. Clare opened it to"
110 "discover her standing without, with anxious eyes."
110 ""
110 "“Angel,” she asked, “is there something wrong that you go away so soon?"
110 "I am quite sure you are not yourself.”"
110 ""
110 "“I am not, quite, mother,” said he."
110 ""
110 "“About her? Now, my son, I know it is that—I know it is about her! Have"
110 "you quarrelled in these three weeks?”"
110 ""
110 "“We have not exactly quarrelled,” he said. “But we have had a"
110 "difference—”"
110 ""
110 "“Angel—is she a young woman whose history will bear investigation?”"
110 ""
110 "With a mother’s instinct Mrs Clare had put her finger on the kind of"
110 "trouble that would cause such a disquiet as seemed to agitate her son."
110 ""
110 "“She is spotless!” he replied; and felt that if it had sent him to"
110 "eternal hell there and then he would have told that lie."
110 ""
110 "“Then never mind the rest. After all, there are few purer things in"
110 "nature then an unsullied country maid. Any crudeness of manner which"
110 "may offend your more educated sense at first, will, I am sure,"
110 "disappear under the influence or your companionship and tuition.”"
110 ""
110 "Such terrible sarcasm of blind magnanimity brought home to Clare the"
110 "secondary perception that he had utterly wrecked his career by this"
110 "marriage, which had not been among his early thoughts after the"
110 "disclosure. True, on his own account he cared very little about his"
110 "career; but he had wished to make it at least a respectable one on"
110 "account of his parents and brothers. And now as he looked into the"
110 "candle its flame dumbly expressed to him that it was made to shine on"
110 "sensible people, and that it abhorred lighting the face of a dupe and a"
110 "failure."
110 ""
110 "When his agitation had cooled he would be at moments incensed with his"
110 "poor wife for causing a situation in which he was obliged to practise"
110 "deception on his parents. He almost talked to her in his anger, as if"
110 "she had been in the room. And then her cooing voice, plaintive in"
110 "expostulation, disturbed the darkness, the velvet touch of her lips"
110 "passed over his brow, and he could distinguish in the air the warmth of"
110 "her breath."
110 ""
110 "This night the woman of his belittling deprecations was thinking how"
110 "great and good her husband was. But over them both there hung a deeper"
110 "shade than the shade which Angel Clare perceived, namely, the shade of"
110 "his own limitations. With all his attempted independence of judgement"
110 "this advanced and well-meaning young man, a sample product of the last"
110 "five-and-twenty years, was yet the slave to custom and conventionality"
110 "when surprised back into his early teachings. No prophet had told him,"
110 "and he was not prophet enough to tell himself, that essentially this"
110 "young wife of his was as deserving of the praise of King Lemuel as any"
110 "other woman endowed with the same dislike of evil, her moral value"
110 "having to be reckoned not by achievement but by tendency. Moreover, the"
110 "figure near at hand suffers on such occasion, because it shows up its"
110 "sorriness without shade; while vague figures afar off are honoured, in"
110 "that their distance makes artistic virtues of their stains. In"
110 "considering what Tess was not, he overlooked what she was, and forgot"
110 "that the defective can be more than the entire."

110 "<CHAPTER XL>"
110 ""
110 "At breakfast Brazil was the topic, and all endeavoured to take a"
110 "hopeful view of Clare’s proposed experiment with that country’s soil,"
110 "notwithstanding the discouraging reports of some farm-labourers who had"
110 "emigrated thither and returned home within the twelve months. After"
110 "breakfast Clare went into the little town to wind up such trifling"
110 "matters as he was concerned with there, and to get from the local bank"
110 "all the money he possessed. On his way back he encountered Miss Mercy"
110 "Chant by the church, from whose walls she seemed to be a sort of"
110 "emanation. She was carrying an armful of Bibles for her class, and such"
110 "was her view of life that events which produced heartache in others"
110 "wrought beatific smiles upon her—an enviable result, although, in the"
110 "opinion of Angel, it was obtained by a curiously unnatural sacrifice of"
110 "humanity to mysticism."
110 ""
110 "She had learnt that he was about to leave England, and observed what an"
110 "excellent and promising scheme it seemed to be."
110 ""
110 "“Yes; it is a likely scheme enough in a commercial sense, no doubt,” he"
110 "replied. “But, my dear Mercy, it snaps the continuity of existence."
110 "Perhaps a cloister would be preferable.”"
110 ""
110 "“A cloister! O, Angel Clare!”"
110 ""
110 "“Well?”"
110 ""
110 "“Why, you wicked man, a cloister implies a monk, and a monk Roman"
110 "Catholicism.”"
110 ""
110 "“And Roman Catholicism sin, and sin damnation. Thou art in a parlous"
110 "state, Angel Clare.”"
110 ""
110 "“_I_ glory in my Protestantism!” she said severely."
110 ""
110 "Then Clare, thrown by sheer misery into one of the demoniacal moods in"
110 "which a man does despite to his true principles, called her close to"
110 "him, and fiendishly whispered in her ear the most heterodox ideas he"
110 "could think of. His momentary laughter at the horror which appeared on"
110 "her fair face ceased when it merged in pain and anxiety for his"
110 "welfare."
110 ""
110 "“Dear Mercy,” he said, “you must forgive me. I think I am going crazy!”"
110 ""
110 "She thought that he was; and thus the interview ended, and Clare"
110 "re-entered the Vicarage. With the local banker he deposited the jewels"
110 "till happier days should arise. He also paid into the bank thirty"
110 "pounds—to be sent to Tess in a few months, as she might require; and"
110 "wrote to her at her parents’ home in Blackmoor Vale to inform her of"
110 "what he had done. This amount, with the sum he had already placed in"
110 "her hands—about fifty pounds—he hoped would be amply sufficient for her"
110 "wants just at present, particularly as in an emergency she had been"
110 "directed to apply to his father."
110 ""
110 "He deemed it best not to put his parents into communication with her by"
110 "informing them of her address; and, being unaware of what had really"
110 "happened to estrange the two, neither his father nor his mother"
110 "suggested that he should do so. During the day he left the parsonage,"
110 "for what he had to complete he wished to get done quickly."
110 ""
110 "As the last duty before leaving this part of England it was necessary"
110 "for him to call at the Wellbridge farmhouse, in which he had spent with"
110 "Tess the first three days of their marriage, the trifle of rent having"
110 "to be paid, the key given up of the rooms they had occupied, and two or"
110 "three small articles fetched away that they had left behind. It was"
110 "under this roof that the deepest shadow ever thrown upon his life had"
110 "stretched its gloom over him. Yet when he had unlocked the door of the"
110 "sitting-room and looked into it, the memory which returned first upon"
110 "him was that of their happy arrival on a similar afternoon, the first"
110 "fresh sense of sharing a habitation conjointly, the first meal"
110 "together, the chatting by the fire with joined hands."
110 ""
110 "The farmer and his wife were in the field at the moment of his visit,"
110 "and Clare was in the rooms alone for some time. Inwardly swollen with a"
110 "renewal of sentiment that he had not quite reckoned with, he went"
110 "upstairs to her chamber, which had never been his. The bed was smooth"
110 "as she had made it with her own hands on the morning of leaving. The"
110 "mistletoe hung under the tester just as he had placed it. Having been"
110 "there three or four weeks it was turning colour, and the leaves and"
110 "berries were wrinkled. Angel took it down and crushed it into the"
110 "grate. Standing there, he for the first time doubted whether his course"
110 "in this conjecture had been a wise, much less a generous, one. But had"
110 "he not been cruelly blinded? In the incoherent multitude of his"
110 "emotions he knelt down at the bedside wet-eyed. “O Tess! If you had"
110 "only told me sooner, I would have forgiven you!” he mourned."
110 ""
110 "Hearing a footstep below, he rose and went to the top of the stairs. At"
110 "the bottom of the flight he saw a woman standing, and on her turning up"
110 "her face recognized the pale, dark-eyed Izz Huett."
110 ""
110 "“Mr Clare,” she said, “I’ve called to see you and Mrs Clare, and to"
110 "inquire if ye be well. I thought you might be back here again.”"
110 ""
110 "This was a girl whose secret he had guessed, but who had not yet"
110 "guessed his; an honest girl who loved him—one who would have made as"
110 "good, or nearly as good, a practical farmer’s wife as Tess."
110 ""
110 "“I am here alone,” he said; “we are not living here now.” Explaining"
110 "why he had come, he asked, “Which way are you going home, Izz?”"
110 ""
110 "“I have no home at Talbothays Dairy now, sir,” she said."
110 ""
110 "“Why is that?”"
110 ""
110 "Izz looked down."
110 ""

110 "pointed in a contrary direction, the direction in which he was"
110 "journeying."
110 ""
110 "“Well—are you going there now? I can take you if you wish for a lift.”"
110 ""
110 "Her olive complexion grew richer in hue."
110 ""
110 "“Thank ’ee, Mr Clare,” she said."
110 ""
110 "He soon found the farmer, and settled the account for his rent and the"
110 "few other items which had to be considered by reason of the sudden"

110 "Izz jumped up beside him."

110 "By-and-by they reached a branch road to a village."
110 ""
110 "“I must get down. I live out there,” said Izz abruptly, never having"
110 "spoken since her avowal."
110 ""
110 "Clare slowed the horse. He was incensed against his fate, bitterly"
110 "disposed towards social ordinances; for they had cooped him up in a"
110 "corner, out of which there was no legitimate pathway. Why not be"
110 "revenged on society by shaping his future domesticities loosely,"
110 "instead of kissing the pedagogic rod of convention in this ensnaring"
110 "manner?"
110 ""
110 "“I am going to Brazil alone, Izz,” said he. “I have separated from my"
110 "wife for personal, not voyaging, reasons. I may never live with her"

110 "of her?”"
110 ""
110 "“You truly wish me to go?”"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“You will? You know what it means, Izz?”"
110 ""

110 "there—that’s good enough for me.”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "They reached the corner of the lane which they had passed half an hour"
110 "earlier, and she hopped down."
110 ""

110 ""

110 "He felt how richly he deserved the reproach that the wounded cry"

110 ""

110 "had to bear!”"
110 ""
110 "She was a really generous girl, and allowed no further bitterness to"
110 "mar their adieux."
110 ""
