110 ""
110 "“Now, Izz,” he said, while she stood beside him there, forcing himself"
110 "to the mentor’s part he was far from feeling; “I want you to tell"
110 "Marian when you see her that she is to be a good woman, and not to give"
110 "way to folly. Promise that, and tell Retty that there are more worthy"
110 "men than I in the world, that for my sake she is to act wisely and"
110 "well—remember the words—wisely and well—for my sake. I send this"
110 "message to them as a dying man to the dying; for I shall never see them"
110 "again. And you, Izzy, you have saved me by your honest words about my"
110 "wife from an incredible impulse towards folly and treachery. Women may"
110 "be bad, but they are not so bad as men in these things! On that one"
110 "account I can never forget you. Be always the good and sincere girl you"
110 "have hitherto been; and think of me as a worthless lover, but a"
110 "faithful friend. Promise.”"
110 ""
110 "She gave the promise."
110 ""
110 "“Heaven bless and keep you, sir. Goodbye!”"
110 ""
110 "He drove on; but no sooner had Izz turned into the lane, and Clare was"
110 "out of sight, than she flung herself down on the bank in a fit of"
110 "racking anguish; and it was with a strained unnatural face that she"
110 "entered her mother’s cottage late that night. Nobody ever was told how"
110 "Izz spent the dark hours that intervened between Angel Clare’s parting"
110 "from her and her arrival home."
110 ""
110 "Clare, too, after bidding the girl farewell, was wrought to aching"
110 "thoughts and quivering lips. But his sorrow was not for Izz. That"
110 "evening he was within a feather-weight’s turn of abandoning his road to"
110 "the nearest station, and driving across that elevated dorsal line of"
110 "South Wessex which divided him from his Tess’s home. It was neither a"
110 "contempt for her nature, nor the probable state of her heart, which"
110 "deterred him."
110 ""
110 "No; it was a sense that, despite her love, as corroborated by Izz’s"
110 "admission, the facts had not changed. If he was right at first, he was"
110 "right now. And the momentum of the course on which he had embarked"
110 "tended to keep him going in it, unless diverted by a stronger, more"
110 "sustained force than had played upon him this afternoon. He could soon"
110 "come back to her. He took the train that night for London, and five"
110 "days after shook hands in farewell of his brothers at the port of"
110 "embarkation."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XLI>"
110 ""
110 "From the foregoing events of the winter-time let us press on to an"
110 "October day, more than eight months subsequent to the parting of Clare"
110 "and Tess. We discover the latter in changed conditions; instead of a"
110 "bride with boxes and trunks which others bore, we see her a lonely"
110 "woman with a basket and a bundle in her own porterage, as at an earlier"
110 "time when she was no bride; instead of the ample means that were"
110 "projected by her husband for her comfort through this probationary"
110 "period, she can produce only a flattened purse."
110 ""
110 "After again leaving Marlott, her home, she had got through the spring"
110 "and summer without any great stress upon her physical powers, the time"
110 "being mainly spent in rendering light irregular service at dairy-work"
110 "near Port-Bredy to the west of the Blackmoor Valley, equally remote"
110 "from her native place and from Talbothays. She preferred this to living"
110 "on his allowance. Mentally she remained in utter stagnation, a"
110 "condition which the mechanical occupation rather fostered than checked."
110 "Her consciousness was at that other dairy, at that other season, in the"
110 "presence of the tender lover who had confronted her there—he who, the"
110 "moment she had grasped him to keep for her own, had disappeared like a"
110 "shape in a vision."
110 ""
110 "The dairy-work lasted only till the milk began to lessen, for she had"
110 "not met with a second regular engagement as at Talbothays, but had done"
110 "duty as a supernumerary only. However, as harvest was now beginning,"
110 "she had simply to remove from the pasture to the stubble to find plenty"
110 "of further occupation, and this continued till harvest was done."
110 ""
110 "Of the five-and-twenty pounds which had remained to her of Clare’s"
110 "allowance, after deducting the other half of the fifty as a"
110 "contribution to her parents for the trouble and expense to which she"
110 "had put them, she had as yet spent but little. But there now followed"
110 "an unfortunate interval of wet weather, during which she was obliged to"
110 "fall back upon her sovereigns."
110 ""
110 "She could not bear to let them go. Angel had put them into her hand,"
110 "had obtained them bright and new from his bank for her; his touch had"
110 "consecrated them to souvenirs of himself—they appeared to have had as"
110 "yet no other history than such as was created by his and her own"
110 "experiences—and to disperse them was like giving away relics. But she"
110 "had to do it, and one by one they left her hands."
110 ""
110 "She had been compelled to send her mother her address from time to"
110 "time, but she concealed her circumstances. When her money had almost"
110 "gone a letter from her mother reached her. Joan stated that they were"
110 "in dreadful difficulty; the autumn rains had gone through the thatch of"
110 "the house, which required entire renewal; but this could not be done"
110 "because the previous thatching had never been paid for. New rafters and"
110 "a new ceiling upstairs also were required, which, with the previous"
110 "bill, would amount to a sum of twenty pounds. As her husband was a man"
110 "of means, and had doubtless returned by this time, could she not send"
110 "them the money?"
110 ""
110 "Tess had thirty pounds coming to her almost immediately from Angel’s"
110 "bankers, and, the case being so deplorable, as soon as the sum was"
110 "received she sent the twenty as requested. Part of the remainder she"
110 "was obliged to expend in winter clothing, leaving only a nominal sum"
110 "for the whole inclement season at hand. When the last pound had gone, a"
110 "remark of Angel’s that whenever she required further resources she was"
110 "to apply to his father, remained to be considered."
110 ""
110 "But the more Tess thought of the step, the more reluctant was she to"
110 "take it. The same delicacy, pride, false shame, whatever it may be"
110 "called, on Clare’s account, which had led her to hide from her own"
110 "parents the prolongation of the estrangement, hindered her owning to"
110 "his that she was in want after the fair allowance he had left her. They"
110 "probably despised her already; how much more they would despise her in"
110 "the character of a mendicant! The consequence was that by no effort"
110 "could the parson’s daughter-in-law bring herself to let him know her"
110 "state."
110 ""
110 "Her reluctance to communicate with her husband’s parents might, she"
110 "thought, lessen with the lapse of time; but with her own the reverse"
110 "obtained. On her leaving their house after the short visit subsequent"
110 "to her marriage they were under the impression that she was ultimately"
110 "going to join her husband; and from that time to the present she had"
110 "done nothing to disturb their belief that she was awaiting his return"
110 "in comfort, hoping against hope that his journey to Brazil would result"
110 "in a short stay only, after which he would come to fetch her, or that"
110 "he would write for her to join him; in any case that they would soon"
110 "present a united front to their families and the world. This hope she"
110 "still fostered. To let her parents know that she was a deserted wife,"
110 "dependent, now that she had relieved their necessities, on her own"
110 "hands for a living, after the _éclat_ of a marriage which was to"
110 "nullify the collapse of the first attempt, would be too much indeed."
110 ""
110 "The set of brilliants returned to her mind. Where Clare had deposited"
110 "them she did not know, and it mattered little, if it were true that she"
110 "could only use and not sell them. Even were they absolutely hers it"
110 "would be passing mean to enrich herself by a legal title to them which"
110 "was not essentially hers at all."
110 ""
110 "Meanwhile her husband’s days had been by no means free from trial. At"
110 "this moment he was lying ill of fever in the clay lands near Curitiba"
110 "in Brazil, having been drenched with thunder-storms and persecuted by"
110 "other hardships, in common with all the English farmers and"
110 "farm-labourers who, just at this time, were deluded into going thither"
110 "by the promises of the Brazilian Government, and by the baseless"
110 "assumption that those frames which, ploughing and sowing on English"
110 "uplands, had resisted all the weathers to whose moods they had been"
110 "born, could resist equally well all the weathers by which they were"
110 "surprised on Brazilian plains."
110 ""
110 "To return. Thus it happened that when the last of Tess’s sovereigns had"
110 "been spent she was unprovided with others to take their place, while on"
110 "account of the season she found it increasingly difficult to get"
110 "employment. Not being aware of the rarity of intelligence, energy,"
110 "health, and willingness in any sphere of life, she refrained from"
110 "seeking an indoor occupation; fearing towns, large houses, people of"
110 "means and social sophistication, and of manners other than rural. From"
110 "that direction of gentility Black Care had come. Society might be"
110 "better than she supposed from her slight experience of it. But she had"
110 "no proof of this, and her instinct in the circumstances was to avoid"
110 "its purlieus."
110 ""
110 "The small dairies to the west, beyond Port-Bredy, in which she had"
110 "served as supernumerary milkmaid during the spring and summer required"
110 "no further aid. Room would probably have been made for her at"
110 "Talbothays, if only out of sheer compassion; but comfortable as her"
110 "life had been there, she could not go back. The anti-climax would be"
110 "too intolerable; and her return might bring reproach upon her idolized"
110 "husband. She could not have borne their pity, and their whispered"
110 "remarks to one another upon her strange situation; though she would"
110 "almost have faced a knowledge of her circumstances by every individual"
110 "there, so long as her story had remained isolated in the mind of each."
110 "It was the interchange of ideas about her that made her sensitiveness"
110 "wince. Tess could not account for this distinction; she simply knew"
110 "that she felt it."
110 ""
110 "She was now on her way to an upland farm in the centre of the county,"
110 "to which she had been recommended by a wandering letter which had"
110 "reached her from Marian. Marian had somehow heard that Tess was"
110 "separated from her husband—probably through Izz Huett—and the"
110 "good-natured and now tippling girl, deeming Tess in trouble, had"
110 "hastened to notify to her former friend that she herself had gone to"
110 "this upland spot after leaving the dairy, and would like to see her"
110 "there, where there was room for other hands, if it was really true that"
110 "she worked again as of old."
110 ""
110 "With the shortening of the days all hope of obtaining her husband’s"
110 "forgiveness began to leave her; and there was something of the habitude"
110 "of the wild animal in the unreflecting instinct with which she rambled"
110 "on—disconnecting herself by littles from her eventful past at every"
110 "step, obliterating her identity, giving no thought to accidents or"
110 "contingencies which might make a quick discovery of her whereabouts by"
110 "others of importance to her own happiness, if not to theirs."
110 ""
110 "Among the difficulties of her lonely position not the least was the"
110 "attention she excited by her appearance, a certain bearing of"
110 "distinction, which she had caught from Clare, being superadded to her"
110 "natural attractiveness. Whilst the clothes lasted which had been"
110 "prepared for her marriage, these casual glances of interest caused her"
110 "no inconvenience, but as soon as she was compelled to don the wrapper"
110 "of a fieldwoman, rude words were addressed to her more than once; but"
110 "nothing occurred to cause her bodily fear till a particular November"
110 "afternoon."
110 ""
110 "She had preferred the country west of the River Brit to the upland farm"
110 "for which she was now bound, because, for one thing, it was nearer to"
110 "the home of her husband’s father; and to hover about that region"
110 "unrecognized, with the notion that she might decide to call at the"
110 "Vicarage some day, gave her pleasure. But having once decided to try"
110 "the higher and drier levels, she pressed back eastward, marching afoot"
110 "towards the village of Chalk-Newton, where she meant to pass the night."
110 ""
110 "The lane was long and unvaried, and, owing to the rapid shortening of"
110 "the days, dusk came upon her before she was aware. She had reached the"
110 "top of a hill down which the lane stretched its serpentine length in"
110 "glimpses, when she heard footsteps behind her back, and in a few"
110 "moments she was overtaken by a man. He stepped up alongside Tess and"
110 "said—"
110 ""
110 "“Good night, my pretty maid”: to which she civilly replied."
110 ""
110 "The light still remaining in the sky lit up her face, though the"
110 "landscape was nearly dark. The man turned and stared hard at her."
110 ""
110 "“Why, surely, it is the young wench who was at Trantridge awhile—young"
110 "Squire d’Urberville’s friend? I was there at that time, though I don’t"
110 "live there now.”"
110 ""
110 "She recognized in him the well-to-do boor whom Angel had knocked down"
110 "at the inn for addressing her coarsely. A spasm of anguish shot through"
110 "her, and she returned him no answer."
110 ""
110 "“Be honest enough to own it, and that what I said in the town was true,"
110 "though your fancy-man was so up about it—hey, my sly one? You ought to"
110 "beg my pardon for that blow of his, considering.”"
110 ""
110 "Still no answer came from Tess. There seemed only one escape for her"
110 "hunted soul. She suddenly took to her heels with the speed of the wind,"
110 "and, without looking behind her, ran along the road till she came to a"
110 "gate which opened directly into a plantation. Into this she plunged,"
110 "and did not pause till she was deep enough in its shade to be safe"
110 "against any possibility of discovery."
110 ""
110 "Under foot the leaves were dry, and the foliage of some holly bushes"
110 "which grew among the deciduous trees was dense enough to keep off"
110 "draughts. She scraped together the dead leaves till she had formed them"
110 "into a large heap, making a sort of nest in the middle. Into this Tess"
110 "crept."
110 ""
110 "Such sleep as she got was naturally fitful; she fancied she heard"
110 "strange noises, but persuaded herself that they were caused by the"
110 "breeze. She thought of her husband in some vague warm clime on the"
110 "other side of the globe, while she was here in the cold. Was there"
110 "another such a wretched being as she in the world? Tess asked herself;"
110 "and, thinking of her wasted life, said, “All is vanity.” She repeated"
110 "the words mechanically, till she reflected that this was a most"
110 "inadequate thought for modern days. Solomon had thought as far as that"
110 "more than two thousand years ago; she herself, though not in the van of"
110 "thinkers, had got much further. If all were only vanity, who would mind"
110 "it? All was, alas, worse than vanity—injustice, punishment, exaction,"
110 "death. The wife of Angel Clare put her hand to her brow, and felt its"
110 "curve, and the edges of her eye-sockets perceptible under the soft"
110 "skin, and thought as she did so that a time would come when that bone"
110 "would be bare. “I wish it were now,” she said."
110 ""
110 "In the midst of these whimsical fancies she heard a new strange sound"
110 "among the leaves. It might be the wind; yet there was scarcely any"
110 "wind. Sometimes it was a palpitation, sometimes a flutter; sometimes it"
110 "was a sort of gasp or gurgle. Soon she was certain that the noises came"
110 "from wild creatures of some kind, the more so when, originating in the"
110 "boughs overhead, they were followed by the fall of a heavy body upon"
110 "the ground. Had she been ensconced here under other and more pleasant"
110 "conditions she would have become alarmed; but, outside humanity, she"
110 "had at present no fear."
110 ""
110 "Day at length broke in the sky. When it had been day aloft for some"
110 "little while it became day in the wood."
110 ""
110 "Directly the assuring and prosaic light of the world’s active hours had"
110 "grown strong, she crept from under her hillock of leaves, and looked"
110 "around boldly. Then she perceived what had been going on to disturb"
110 "her. The plantation wherein she had taken shelter ran down at this spot"
110 "into a peak, which ended it hitherward, outside the hedge being arable"
110 "ground. Under the trees several pheasants lay about, their rich plumage"
110 "dabbled with blood; some were dead, some feebly twitching a wing, some"
110 "staring up at the sky, some pulsating quickly, some contorted, some"
110 "stretched out—all of them writhing in agony, except the fortunate ones"
110 "whose tortures had ended during the night by the inability of nature to"
110 "bear more."
110 ""
110 "Tess guessed at once the meaning of this. The birds had been driven"
110 "down into this corner the day before by some shooting-party; and while"
110 "those that had dropped dead under the shot, or had died before"
110 "nightfall, had been searched for and carried off, many badly wounded"
110 "birds had escaped and hidden themselves away, or risen among the thick"
110 "boughs, where they had maintained their position till they grew weaker"
110 "with loss of blood in the night-time, when they had fallen one by one"
110 "as she had heard them."
110 ""
110 "She had occasionally caught glimpses of these men in girlhood, looking"
110 "over hedges, or peeping through bushes, and pointing their guns,"
110 "strangely accoutred, a bloodthirsty light in their eyes. She had been"
110 "told that, rough and brutal as they seemed just then, they were not"
110 "like this all the year round, but were, in fact, quite civil persons"
110 "save during certain weeks of autumn and winter, when, like the"
110 "inhabitants of the Malay Peninsula, they ran amuck, and made it their"
110 "purpose to destroy life—in this case harmless feathered creatures,"
110 "brought into being by artificial means solely to gratify these"
110 "propensities—at once so unmannerly and so unchivalrous towards their"
110 "weaker fellows in Nature’s teeming family."
110 ""
110 "With the impulse of a soul who could feel for kindred sufferers as much"
110 "as for herself, Tess’s first thought was to put the still living birds"
110 "out of their torture, and to this end with her own hands she broke the"
110 "necks of as many as she could find, leaving them to lie where she had"
110 "found them till the game-keepers should come—as they probably would"
110 "come—to look for them a second time."
110 ""
110 "“Poor darlings—to suppose myself the most miserable being on earth in"
110 "the sight o’ such misery as yours!” she exclaimed, her tears running"
110 "down as she killed the birds tenderly. “And not a twinge of bodily pain"
110 "about me! I be not mangled, and I be not bleeding, and I have two hands"
110 "to feed and clothe me.” She was ashamed of herself for her gloom of the"
110 "night, based on nothing more tangible than a sense of condemnation"
110 "under an arbitrary law of society which had no foundation in Nature."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XLII>"
110 ""
110 "It was now broad day, and she started again, emerging cautiously upon"
110 "the highway. But there was no need for caution; not a soul was at hand,"
110 "and Tess went onward with fortitude, her recollection of the birds’"
110 "silent endurance of their night of agony impressing upon her the"
110 "relativity of sorrows and the tolerable nature of her own, if she could"
110 "once rise high enough to despise opinion. But that she could not do so"
110 "long as it was held by Clare."
110 ""
110 "She reached Chalk-Newton, and breakfasted at an inn, where several"
110 "young men were troublesomely complimentary to her good looks. Somehow"
110 "she felt hopeful, for was it not possible that her husband also might"
110 "say these same things to her even yet? She was bound to take care of"
110 "herself on the chance of it, and keep off these casual lovers. To this"
110 "end Tess resolved to run no further risks from her appearance. As soon"
110 "as she got out of the village she entered a thicket and took from her"
110 "basket one of the oldest field-gowns, which she had never put on even"
110 "at the dairy—never since she had worked among the stubble at Marlott."
110 "She also, by a felicitous thought, took a handkerchief from her bundle"
110 "and tied it round her face under her bonnet, covering her chin and half"
110 "her cheeks and temples, as if she were suffering from toothache. Then"
110 "with her little scissors, by the aid of a pocket looking-glass, she"
110 "mercilessly nipped her eyebrows off, and thus insured against"
110 "aggressive admiration, she went on her uneven way."
110 ""
110 "“What a mommet of a maid!” said the next man who met her to a"
110 "companion."
110 ""
110 "Tears came into her eyes for very pity of herself as she heard him."
110 ""
110 "“But I don’t care!” she said. “O no—I don’t care! I’ll always be ugly"
110 "now, because Angel is not here, and I have nobody to take care of me."
110 "My husband that was is gone away, and never will love me any more; but"
110 "I love him just the same, and hate all other men, and like to make ’em"
110 "think scornfully of me!”"
110 ""
110 "Thus Tess walks on; a figure which is part of the landscape; a"
110 "fieldwoman pure and simple, in winter guise; a gray serge cape, a red"
110 "woollen cravat, a stuff skirt covered by a whitey-brown rough wrapper,"
110 "and buff-leather gloves. Every thread of that old attire has become"
110 "faded and thin under the stroke of raindrops, the burn of sunbeams, and"
110 "the stress of winds. There is no sign of young passion in her now—"
110 ""
110 "    The maiden’s mouth is cold"
110 "    . . . . ."
110 "    Fold over simple fold"
110 "    Binding her head."
110 ""
110 "Inside this exterior, over which the eye might have roved as over a"
110 "thing scarcely percipient, almost inorganic, there was the record of a"
110 "pulsing life which had learnt too well, for its years, of the dust and"
110 "ashes of things, of the cruelty of lust and the fragility of love."
110 ""
110 "Next day the weather was bad, but she trudged on, the honesty,"
110 "directness, and impartiality of elemental enmity disconcerting her but"
110 "little. Her object being a winter’s occupation and a winter’s home,"
110 "there was no time to lose. Her experience of short hirings had been"
110 "such that she was determined to accept no more."
110 ""
110 "Thus she went forward from farm to farm in the direction of the place"
110 "whence Marian had written to her, which she determined to make use of"
110 "as a last shift only, its rumoured stringencies being the reverse of"
110 "tempting. First she inquired for the lighter kinds of employment, and,"
110 "as acceptance in any variety of these grew hopeless, applied next for"
110 "the less light, till, beginning with the dairy and poultry tendance"
110 "that she liked best, she ended with the heavy and course pursuits which"
110 "she liked least—work on arable land: work of such roughness, indeed, as"
110 "she would never have deliberately voluteered for."
110 ""
110 "Towards the second evening she reached the irregular chalk table-land"
110 "or plateau, bosomed with semi-globular tumuli—as if Cybele the"
110 "Many-breasted were supinely extended there—which stretched between the"
110 "valley of her birth and the valley of her love."
110 ""
110 "Here the air was dry and cold, and the long cart-roads were blown white"
110 "and dusty within a few hours after rain. There were few trees, or none,"
110 "those that would have grown in the hedges being mercilessly plashed"
110 "down with the quickset by the tenant-farmers, the natural enemies of"
110 "tree, bush, and brake. In the middle distance ahead of her she could"
110 "see the summits of Bulbarrow and of Nettlecombe Tout, and they seemed"
110 "friendly. They had a low and unassuming aspect from this upland, though"
110 "as approached on the other side from Blackmoor in her childhood they"
110 "were as lofty bastions against the sky. Southerly, at many miles’"
110 "distance, and over the hills and ridges coastward, she could discern a"
110 "surface like polished steel: it was the English Channel at a point far"
110 "out towards France."
110 ""
110 "Before her, in a slight depression, were the remains of a village. She"
110 "had, in fact, reached Flintcomb-Ash, the place of Marian’s sojourn."
110 "There seemed to be no help for it; hither she was doomed to come. The"
110 "stubborn soil around her showed plainly enough that the kind of labour"
110 "in demand here was of the roughest kind; but it was time to rest from"
110 "searching, and she resolved to stay, particularly as it began to rain."
110 "At the entrance to the village was a cottage whose gable jutted into"
110 "the road, and before applying for a lodging she stood under its"
110 "shelter, and watched the evening close in."
110 ""
110 "“Who would think I was Mrs Angel Clare!” she said."
110 ""
110 "The wall felt warm to her back and shoulders, and she found that"
110 "immediately within the gable was the cottage fireplace, the heat of"
110 "which came through the bricks. She warmed her hands upon them, and also"
110 "put her cheek—red and moist with the drizzle—against their comforting"
110 "surface. The wall seemed to be the only friend she had. She had so"
110 "little wish to leave it that she could have stayed there all night."
110 ""
110 "Tess could hear the occupants of the cottage—gathered together after"
110 "their day’s labour—talking to each other within, and the rattle of"
110 "their supper-plates was also audible. But in the village-street she had"
110 "seen no soul as yet. The solitude was at last broken by the approach of"
110 "one feminine figure, who, though the evening was cold, wore the print"
110 "gown and the tilt-bonnet of summer time. Tess instinctively thought it"
110 "might be Marian, and when she came near enough to be distinguishable in"
110 "the gloom, surely enough it was she. Marian was even stouter and redder"
110 "in the face than formerly, and decidedly shabbier in attire. At any"
110 "previous period of her existence Tess would hardly have cared to renew"
110 "the acquaintance in such conditions; but her loneliness was excessive,"
110 "and she responded readily to Marian’s greeting."
110 ""
110 "Marian was quite respectful in her inquiries, but seemed much moved by"
110 "the fact that Tess should still continue in no better condition than at"
110 "first; though she had dimly heard of the separation."
110 ""
110 "“Tess—Mrs Clare—the dear wife of dear he! And is it really so bad as"
110 "this, my child? Why is your cwomely face tied up in such a way? Anybody"
110 "been beating ’ee? Not _he_?”"
110 ""
110 "“No, no, no! I merely did it not to be clipsed or colled, Marian.”"
110 ""
110 "She pulled off in disgust a bandage which could suggest such wild"
110 "thoughts."
110 ""
110 "“And you’ve got no collar on” (Tess had been accustomed to wear a"
110 "little white collar at the dairy)."
110 ""
110 "“I know it, Marian.”"
110 ""
110 "“You’ve lost it travelling.”"
110 ""
110 "“I’ve not lost it. The truth is, I don’t care anything about my looks;"
110 "and so I didn’t put it on.”"
110 ""
110 "“And you don’t wear your wedding-ring?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, I do; but not in public. I wear it round my neck on a ribbon. I"
110 "don’t wish people to think who I am by marriage, or that I am married"
110 "at all; it would be so awkward while I lead my present life.”"
110 ""
110 "Marian paused."
110 ""
110 "“But you _be_ a gentleman’s wife; and it seems hardly fair that you"
110 "should live like this!”"
110 ""
110 "“O yes it is, quite fair; though I am very unhappy.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, well. _He_ married you—and you can be unhappy!”"
110 ""
110 "“Wives are unhappy sometimes; from no fault of their husbands—from"
110 "their own.”"
110 ""
110 "“You’ve no faults, deary; that I’m sure of. And he’s none. So it must"
110 "be something outside ye both.”"
110 ""
110 "“Marian, dear Marian, will you do me a good turn without asking"
110 "questions? My husband has gone abroad, and somehow I have overrun my"
110 "allowance, so that I have to fall back upon my old work for a time. Do"
110 "not call me Mrs Clare, but Tess, as before. Do they want a hand here?”"
110 ""
110 "“O yes; they’ll take one always, because few care to come. ’Tis a"
110 "starve-acre place. Corn and swedes are all they grow. Though I be here"
110 "myself, I feel ’tis a pity for such as you to come.”"
110 ""
110 "“But you used to be as good a dairywoman as I.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes; but I’ve got out o’ that since I took to drink. Lord, that’s the"
110 "only comfort I’ve got now! If you engage, you’ll be set swede-hacking."
110 "That’s what I be doing; but you won’t like it.”"
110 ""
110 "“O—anything! Will you speak for me?”"
110 ""
110 "“You will do better by speaking for yourself.”"
110 ""
110 "“Very well. Now, Marian, remember—nothing about _him_ if I get the"
110 "place. I don’t wish to bring his name down to the dirt.”"
110 ""
110 "Marian, who was really a trustworthy girl though of coarser grain than"
110 "Tess, promised anything she asked."
110 ""
110 "“This is pay-night,” she said, “and if you were to come with me you"
110 "would know at once. I be real sorry that you are not happy; but ’tis"
110 "because he’s away, I know. You couldn’t be unhappy if he were here,"
110 "even if he gie’d ye no money—even if he used you like a drudge.”"
110 ""
110 "“That’s true; I could not!”"
110 ""
110 "They walked on together and soon reached the farmhouse, which was"
110 "almost sublime in its dreariness. There was not a tree within sight;"
110 "there was not, at this season, a green pasture—nothing but fallow and"
110 "turnips everywhere, in large fields divided by hedges plashed to"
110 "unrelieved levels."
110 ""
110 "Tess waited outside the door of the farmhouse till the group of"
110 "workfolk had received their wages, and then Marian introduced her. The"
110 "farmer himself, it appeared, was not at home, but his wife, who"
110 "represented him this evening, made no objection to hiring Tess, on her"
110 "agreeing to remain till Old Lady-Day. Female field-labour was seldom"
110 "offered now, and its cheapness made it profitable for tasks which women"
110 "could perform as readily as men."
110 ""
110 "Having signed the agreement, there was nothing more for Tess to do at"
110 "present than to get a lodging, and she found one in the house at whose"
110 "gable-wall she had warmed herself. It was a poor subsistence that she"
110 "had ensured, but it would afford a shelter for the winter at any rate."
110 ""
110 "That night she wrote to inform her parents of her new address, in case"
110 "a letter should arrive at Marlott from her husband. But she did not"
110 "tell them of the sorriness of her situation: it might have brought"
110 "reproach upon him."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XLIII>"
110 ""
110 "There was no exaggeration in Marian’s definition of Flintcomb-Ash farm"
110 "as a starve-acre place. The single fat thing on the soil was Marian"
110 "herself; and she was an importation. Of the three classes of village,"
110 "the village cared for by its lord, the village cared for by itself, and"
110 "the village uncared for either by itself or by its lord (in other"
110 "words, the village of a resident squire’s tenantry, the village of free"
110 "or copy-holders, and the absentee-owner’s village, farmed with the"
110 "land) this place, Flintcomb-Ash, was the third."
110 ""
110 "But Tess set to work. Patience, that blending of moral courage with"
110 "physical timidity, was now no longer a minor feature in Mrs Angel"
110 "Clare; and it sustained her."
110 ""
110 "The swede-field in which she and her companion were set hacking was a"
110 "stretch of a hundred odd acres in one patch, on the highest ground of"
110 "the farm, rising above stony lanchets or lynchets—the outcrop of"
110 "siliceous veins in the chalk formation, composed of myriads of loose"
110 "white flints in bulbous, cusped, and phallic shapes. The upper half of"
110 "each turnip had been eaten off by the live-stock, and it was the"
110 "business of the two women to grub up the lower or earthy half of the"
110 "root with a hooked fork called a hacker, that it might be eaten also."
110 "Every leaf of the vegetable having already been consumed, the whole"
110 "field was in colour a desolate drab; it was a complexion without"
110 "features, as if a face, from chin to brow, should be only an expanse of"
110 "skin. The sky wore, in another colour, the same likeness; a white"
110 "vacuity of countenance with the lineaments gone. So these two upper and"
110 "nether visages confronted each other all day long, the white face"
110 "looking down on the brown face, and the brown face looking up at the"
110 "white face, without anything standing between them but the two girls"
110 "crawling over the surface of the former like flies."
110 ""
110 "Nobody came near them, and their movements showed a mechanical"
110 "regularity; their forms standing enshrouded in Hessian"
110 "“wroppers”—sleeved brown pinafores, tied behind to the bottom, to keep"
110 "their gowns from blowing about—scant skirts revealing boots that"
110 "reached high up the ankles, and yellow sheepskin gloves with gauntlets."
110 "The pensive character which the curtained hood lent to their bent heads"
110 "would have reminded the observer of some early Italian conception of"
110 "the two Marys."
110 ""
110 "They worked on hour after hour, unconscious of the forlorn aspect they"
110 "bore in the landscape, not thinking of the justice or injustice of"
110 "their lot. Even in such a position as theirs it was possible to exist"
110 "in a dream. In the afternoon the rain came on again, and Marian said"
110 "that they need not work any more. But if they did not work they would"
110 "not be paid; so they worked on. It was so high a situation, this field,"
110 "that the rain had no occasion to fall, but raced along horizontally"
110 "upon the yelling wind, sticking into them like glass splinters till"
110 "they were wet through. Tess had not known till now what was really"
110 "meant by that. There are degrees of dampness, and a very little is"
110 "called being wet through in common talk. But to stand working slowly in"
110 "a field, and feel the creep of rain-water, first in legs and shoulders,"
110 "then on hips and head, then at back, front, and sides, and yet to work"
110 "on till the leaden light diminishes and marks that the sun is down,"
110 "demands a distinct modicum of stoicism, even of valour."
110 ""
110 "Yet they did not feel the wetness so much as might be supposed. They"
110 "were both young, and they were talking of the time when they lived and"
110 "loved together at Talbothays Dairy, that happy green tract of land"
110 "where summer had been liberal in her gifts; in substance to all,"
110 "emotionally to these. Tess would fain not have conversed with Marian of"
110 "the man who was legally, if not actually, her husband; but the"
110 "irresistible fascination of the subject betrayed her into reciprocating"
110 "Marian’s remarks. And thus, as has been said, though the damp curtains"
110 "of their bonnets flapped smartly into their faces, and their wrappers"
110 "clung about them to wearisomeness, they lived all this afternoon in"
110 "memories of green, sunny, romantic Talbothays."
110 ""
110 "“You can see a gleam of a hill within a few miles o’ Froom Valley from"
110 "here when ’tis fine,” said Marian."
110 ""
110 "“Ah! Can you?” said Tess, awake to the new value of this locality."
110 ""
110 "So the two forces were at work here as everywhere, the inherent will to"
110 "enjoy, and the circumstantial will against enjoyment. Marian’s will had"
110 "a method of assisting itself by taking from her pocket as the afternoon"
110 "wore on a pint bottle corked with white rag, from which she invited"
110 "Tess to drink. Tess’s unassisted power of dreaming, however, being"
110 "enough for her sublimation at present, she declined except the merest"
110 "sip, and then Marian took a pull from the spirits."
110 ""
110 "“I’ve got used to it,” she said, “and can’t leave it off now. ’Tis my"
110 "only comfort—You see I lost him: you didn’t; and you can do without it"
110 "perhaps.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess thought her loss as great as Marian’s, but upheld by the dignity"
110 "of being Angel’s wife, in the letter at least, she accepted Marian’s"
110 "differentiation."
110 ""
110 "Amid this scene Tess slaved in the morning frosts and in the afternoon"
110 "rains. When it was not swede-grubbing it was swede-trimming, in which"
110 "process they sliced off the earth and the fibres with a bill-hook"
110 "before storing the roots for future use. At this occupation they could"
110 "shelter themselves by a thatched hurdle if it rained; but if it was"
110 "frosty even their thick leather gloves could not prevent the frozen"
110 "masses they handled from biting their fingers. Still Tess hoped. She"
110 "had a conviction that sooner or later the magnanimity which she"
110 "persisted in reckoning as a chief ingredient of Clare’s character would"
110 "lead him to rejoin her."
110 ""
110 "Marian, primed to a humorous mood, would discover the queer-shaped"
110 "flints aforesaid, and shriek with laughter, Tess remaining severely"
110 "obtuse. They often looked across the country to where the Var or Froom"
110 "was known to stretch, even though they might not be able to see it;"
110 "and, fixing their eyes on the cloaking gray mist, imagined the old"
110 "times they had spent out there."
110 ""
110 "“Ah,” said Marian, “how I should like another or two of our old set to"
110 "come here! Then we could bring up Talbothays every day here afield, and"
110 "talk of he, and of what nice times we had there, and o’ the old things"
110 "we used to know, and make it all come back a’most, in seeming!”"
110 "Marian’s eyes softened, and her voice grew vague as the visions"
110 "returned. “I’ll write to Izz Huett,” she said. “She’s biding at home"

110 ""
110 "Tess had nothing to say against the proposal, and the next she heard of"

110 ""
110 "There had not been such a winter for years. It came on in stealthy and"
110 "measured glides, like the moves of a chess-player. One morning the few"
110 "lonely trees and the thorns of the hedgerows appeared as if they had"
110 "put off a vegetable for an animal integument. Every twig was covered"
110 "with a white nap as of fur grown from the rind during the night, giving"

110 "had ever been observed till brought out into visibility by the"
110 "crystallizing atmosphere, hanging like loops of white worsted from"
110 "salient points of the out-houses, posts, and gates."
110 ""
110 "After this season of congealed dampness came a spell of dry frost, when"
110 "strange birds from behind the North Pole began to arrive silently on"
110 "the upland of Flintcomb-Ash; gaunt spectral creatures with tragical"
110 "eyes—eyes which had witnessed scenes of cataclysmal horror in"

110 "which had beheld the crash of icebergs and the slide of snow-hills by"

110 "expression of feature that such scenes had engendered. These nameless"
110 "birds came quite near to Tess and Marian, but of all they had seen"
110 "which humanity would never see, they brought no account. The"
110 "traveller’s ambition to tell was not theirs, and, with dumb"
110 "impassivity, they dismissed experiences which they did not value for"
110 "the immediate incidents of this homely upland—the trivial movements of"
110 "the two girls in disturbing the clods with their hackers so as to"
110 "uncover something or other that these visitants relished as food."
110 ""
110 "Then one day a peculiar quality invaded the air of this open country."
110 "There came a moisture which was not of rain, and a cold which was not"
110 "of frost. It chilled the eyeballs of the twain, made their brows ache,"
110 "penetrated to their skeletons, affecting the surface of the body less"
110 "than its core. They knew that it meant snow, and in the night the snow"
110 "came. Tess, who continued to live at the cottage with the warm gable"
110 "that cheered any lonely pedestrian who paused beside it, awoke in the"
110 "night, and heard above the thatch noises which seemed to signify that"
110 "the roof had turned itself into a gymnasium of all the winds. When she"
110 "lit her lamp to get up in the morning she found that the snow had blown"
110 "through a chink in the casement, forming a white cone of the finest"
110 "powder against the inside, and had also come down the chimney, so that"
110 "it lay sole-deep upon the floor, on which her shoes left tracks when"
110 "she moved about. Without, the storm drove so fast as to create a"
110 "snow-mist in the kitchen; but as yet it was too dark out-of-doors to"
110 "see anything."
110 ""
110 "Tess knew that it was impossible to go on with the swedes; and by the"
110 "time she had finished breakfast beside the solitary little lamp, Marian"
110 "arrived to tell her that they were to join the rest of the women at"

110 ""
110 "“Ha-ha! the cunning northern birds knew this was coming,” said Marian."

110 ""
110 "“You mustn’t talk about him to me, Marian,” said Tess severely."
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "They reached the wheat-barn and entered it. One end of the long"
110 "structure was full of corn; the middle was where the reed-drawing was"

110 ""

110 "In addition to Tess, Marian, and Izz, there were two women from a"

110 ""
110 "Putting on their gloves, all set to work in a row in front of the"

110 ""
110 "Then Izz Huett broke down. She had walked more than a dozen miles the"
110 "previous evening, had gone to bed at midnight, and had risen again at"

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "Tess, in her start at the words, narrowly missed cutting off a finger"

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Pooh—then he didn’t mean it! ’Twas just a man’s jest!”"

110 ""

110 "The dim light in the barn grew dimmer, and they could see to work no"

110 "<CHAPTER XLIV>"

110 "By the disclosure in the barn her thoughts were led anew in the"
110 "direction which they had taken more than once of late—to the distant"
110 "Emminster Vicarage. It was through her husband’s parents that she had"
110 "been charged to send a letter to Clare if she desired; and to write to"
110 "them direct if in difficulty. But that sense of her having morally no"
110 "claim upon him had always led Tess to suspend her impulse to send these"
110 "notes; and to the family at the Vicarage, therefore, as to her own"
110 "parents since her marriage, she was virtually non-existent. This"
110 "self-effacement in both directions had been quite in consonance with"
110 "her independent character of desiring nothing by way of favour or pity"
110 "to which she was not entitled on a fair consideration of her deserts."
110 "She had set herself to stand or fall by her qualities, and to waive"
110 "such merely technical claims upon a strange family as had been"
110 "established for her by the flimsy fact of a member of that family, in a"
110 "season of impulse, writing his name in a church-book beside hers."
110 ""
110 "But now that she was stung to a fever by Izz’s tale, there was a limit"
110 "to her powers of renunciation. Why had her husband not written to her?"
110 "He had distinctly implied that he would at least let her know of the"
110 "locality to which he had journeyed; but he had not sent a line to"
110 "notify his address. Was he really indifferent? But was he ill? Was it"
110 "for her to make some advance? Surely she might summon the courage of"
110 "solicitude, call at the Vicarage for intelligence, and express her"
110 "grief at his silence. If Angel’s father were the good man she had heard"
110 "him represented to be, he would be able to enter into her heart-starved"
110 "situation. Her social hardships she could conceal."
110 ""
110 "To leave the farm on a week-day was not in her power; Sunday was the"
110 "only possible opportunity. Flintcomb-Ash being in the middle of the"
110 "cretaceous tableland over which no railway had climbed as yet, it would"
110 "be necessary to walk. And the distance being fifteen miles each way she"
110 "would have to allow herself a long day for the undertaking by rising"
110 "early."
110 ""
110 "A fortnight later, when the snow had gone, and had been followed by a"
110 "hard black frost, she took advantage of the state of the roads to try"
110 "the experiment. At four o’clock that Sunday morning she came downstairs"
110 "and stepped out into the starlight. The weather was still favourable,"
110 "the ground ringing under her feet like an anvil."
110 ""
110 "Marian and Izz were much interested in her excursion, knowing that the"
110 "journey concerned her husband. Their lodgings were in a cottage a"
110 "little further along the lane, but they came and assisted Tess in her"
110 "departure, and argued that she should dress up in her very prettiest"
110 "guise to captivate the hearts of her parents-in-law; though she,"
110 "knowing of the austere and Calvinistic tenets of old Mr Clare, was"
110 "indifferent, and even doubtful. A year had now elapsed since her sad"
110 "marriage, but she had preserved sufficient draperies from the wreck of"
110 "her then full wardrobe to clothe her very charmingly as a simple"
110 "country girl with no pretensions to recent fashion; a soft gray woollen"
110 "gown, with white crape quilling against the pink skin of her face and"
110 "neck, and a black velvet jacket and hat."
110 ""
110 "“’Tis a thousand pities your husband can’t see ’ee now—you do look a"
110 "real beauty!” said Izz Huett, regarding Tess as she stood on the"
110 "threshold between the steely starlight without and the yellow"
110 "candlelight within. Izz spoke with a magnanimous abandonment of herself"
110 "to the situation; she could not be—no woman with a heart bigger than a"
110 "hazel-nut could be—antagonistic to Tess in her presence, the influence"
110 "which she exercised over those of her own sex being of a warmth and"
110 "strength quite unusual, curiously overpowering the less worthy feminine"
110 "feelings of spite and rivalry."
110 ""
110 "With a final tug and touch here, and a slight brush there, they let her"
110 "go; and she was absorbed into the pearly air of the fore-dawn. They"
110 "heard her footsteps tap along the hard road as she stepped out to her"
110 "full pace. Even Izz hoped she would win, and, though without any"
110 "particular respect for her own virtue, felt glad that she had been"
110 "prevented wronging her friend when momentarily tempted by Clare."
110 ""
110 "It was a year ago, all but a day, that Clare had married Tess, and only"
110 "a few days less than a year that he had been absent from her. Still, to"
110 "start on a brisk walk, and on such an errand as hers, on a dry clear"
110 "wintry morning, through the rarefied air of these chalky hogs’-backs,"
110 "was not depressing; and there is no doubt that her dream at starting"
110 "was to win the heart of her mother-in-law, tell her whole history to"
110 "that lady, enlist her on her side, and so gain back the truant."
110 ""
110 "In time she reached the edge of the vast escarpment below which"
110 "stretched the loamy Vale of Blackmoor, now lying misty and still in the"
110 "dawn. Instead of the colourless air of the uplands, the atmosphere down"
110 "there was a deep blue. Instead of the great enclosures of a hundred"
110 "acres in which she was now accustomed to toil, there were little fields"
110 "below her of less than half-a-dozen acres, so numerous that they looked"
110 "from this height like the meshes of a net. Here the landscape was"
110 "whitey-brown; down there, as in Froom Valley, it was always green. Yet"
110 "it was in that vale that her sorrow had taken shape, and she did not"
110 "love it as formerly. Beauty to her, as to all who have felt, lay not in"
110 "the thing, but in what the thing symbolized."
110 ""
110 "Keeping the Vale on her right, she steered steadily westward; passing"
110 "above the Hintocks, crossing at right-angles the high-road from"
110 "Sherton-Abbas to Casterbridge, and skirting Dogbury Hill and High-Stoy,"
110 "with the dell between them called “The Devil’s Kitchen”. Still"
110 "following the elevated way she reached Cross-in-Hand, where the stone"
110 "pillar stands desolate and silent, to mark the site of a miracle, or"
110 "murder, or both. Three miles further she cut across the straight and"
110 "deserted Roman road called Long-Ash Lane; leaving which as soon as she"
110 "reached it she dipped down a hill by a transverse lane into the small"
110 "town or village of Evershead, being now about halfway over the"
110 "distance. She made a halt here, and breakfasted a second time, heartily"
110 "enough—not at the Sow-and-Acorn, for she avoided inns, but at a cottage"
110 "by the church."
110 ""
110 "The second half of her journey was through a more gentle country, by"
110 "way of Benvill Lane. But as the mileage lessened between her and the"
110 "spot of her pilgrimage, so did Tess’s confidence decrease, and her"
110 "enterprise loom out more formidably. She saw her purpose in such"
110 "staring lines, and the landscape so faintly, that she was sometimes in"
110 "danger of losing her way. However, about noon she paused by a gate on"
110 "the edge of the basin in which Emminster and its Vicarage lay."
110 ""
110 "The square tower, beneath which she knew that at that moment the Vicar"
110 "and his congregation were gathered, had a severe look in her eyes. She"
110 "wished that she had somehow contrived to come on a week-day. Such a"
110 "good man might be prejudiced against a woman who had chosen Sunday,"
110 "never realizing the necessities of her case. But it was incumbent upon"
110 "her to go on now. She took off the thick boots in which she had walked"
110 "thus far, put on her pretty thin ones of patent leather, and, stuffing"
110 "the former into the hedge by the gatepost where she might readily find"
110 "them again, descended the hill; the freshness of colour she had derived"
110 "from the keen air thinning away in spite of her as she drew near the"
110 "parsonage."
110 ""
110 "Tess hoped for some accident that might favour her, but nothing"
110 "favoured her. The shrubs on the Vicarage lawn rustled uncomfortably in"
110 "the frosty breeze; she could not feel by any stretch of imagination,"
110 "dressed to her highest as she was, that the house was the residence of"
110 "near relations; and yet nothing essential, in nature or emotion,"
110 "divided her from them: in pains, pleasures, thoughts, birth, death, and"
110 "after-death, they were the same."
110 ""
110 "She nerved herself by an effort, entered the swing-gate, and rang the"
110 "door-bell. The thing was done; there could be no retreat. No; the thing"
110 "was not done. Nobody answered to her ringing. The effort had to be"
110 "risen to and made again. She rang a second time, and the agitation of"
110 "the act, coupled with her weariness after the fifteen miles’ walk, led"
110 "her to support herself while she waited by resting her hand on her hip"
110 "and her elbow against the wall of the porch. The wind was so nipping"
110 "that the ivy-leaves had become wizened and gray, each tapping"
110 "incessantly upon its neighbour with a disquieting stir of her nerves. A"
110 "piece of blood-stained paper, caught up from some meat-buyer’s"
110 "dust-heap, beat up and down the road without the gate; too flimsy to"
110 "rest, too heavy to fly away; and a few straws kept it company."
110 ""
110 "The second peal had been louder, and still nobody came. Then she walked"
110 "out of the porch, opened the gate, and passed through. And though she"
110 "looked dubiously at the house-front as if inclined to return, it was"
110 "with a breath of relief that she closed the gate. A feeling haunted her"
110 "that she might have been recognized (though how she could not tell),"
110 "and orders been given not to admit her."
110 ""
110 "Tess went as far as the corner. She had done all she could do; but"
110 "determined not to escape present trepidation at the expense of future"
110 "distress, she walked back again quite past the house, looking up at all"
110 "the windows."
110 ""
110 "Ah—the explanation was that they were all at church, every one. She"
110 "remembered her husband saying that his father always insisted upon the"
110 "household, servants included, going to morning-service, and, as a"
110 "consequence, eating cold food when they came home. It was, therefore,"
110 "only necessary to wait till the service was over. She would not make"
110 "herself conspicuous by waiting on the spot, and she started to get past"
110 "the church into the lane. But as she reached the churchyard-gate the"
110 "people began pouring out, and Tess found herself in the midst of them."
110 ""
110 "The Emminster congregation looked at her as only a congregation of"
110 "small country-townsfolk walking home at its leisure can look at a woman"
110 "out of the common whom it perceives to be a stranger. She quickened her"
110 "pace, and ascended the road by which she had come, to find a retreat"
110 "between its hedges till the Vicar’s family should have lunched, and it"
110 "might be convenient for them to receive her. She soon distanced the"
110 "churchgoers, except two youngish men, who, linked arm-in-arm, were"
110 "beating up behind her at a quick step."
110 ""
110 "As they drew nearer she could hear their voices engaged in earnest"
110 "discourse, and, with the natural quickness of a woman in her situation,"
110 "did not fail to recognize in those noises the quality of her husband’s"
110 "tones. The pedestrians were his two brothers. Forgetting all her plans,"
110 "Tess’s one dread was lest they should overtake her now, in her"
110 "disorganized condition, before she was prepared to confront them; for"
110 "though she felt that they could not identify her, she instinctively"
110 "dreaded their scrutiny. The more briskly they walked, the more briskly"
110 "walked she. They were plainly bent upon taking a short quick stroll"
110 "before going indoors to lunch or dinner, to restore warmth to limbs"
110 "chilled with sitting through a long service."
110 ""
110 "Only one person had preceded Tess up the hill—a ladylike young woman,"
110 "somewhat interesting, though, perhaps, a trifle _guindée_ and prudish."
110 "Tess had nearly overtaken her when the speed of her brothers-in-law"
110 "brought them so nearly behind her back that she could hear every word"
110 "of their conversation. They said nothing, however, which particularly"
110 "interested her till, observing the young lady still further in front,"
110 "one of them remarked, “There is Mercy Chant. Let us overtake her.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess knew the name. It was the woman who had been destined for Angel’s"
110 "life-companion by his and her parents, and whom he probably would have"
110 "married but for her intrusive self. She would have known as much"
110 "without previous information if she had waited a moment, for one of the"
110 "brothers proceeded to say: “Ah! poor Angel, poor Angel! I never see"
110 "that nice girl without more and more regretting his precipitancy in"
110 "throwing himself away upon a dairymaid, or whatever she may be. It is a"
110 "queer business, apparently. Whether she has joined him yet or not I"
110 "don’t know; but she had not done so some months ago when I heard from"
110 "him.”"
110 ""
110 "“I can’t say. He never tells me anything nowadays. His ill-considered"
110 "marriage seems to have completed that estrangement from me which was"
110 "begun by his extraordinary opinions.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess beat up the long hill still faster; but she could not outwalk them"
110 "without exciting notice. At last they outsped her altogether, and"
110 "passed her by. The young lady still further ahead heard their footsteps"
110 "and turned. Then there was a greeting and a shaking of hands, and the"
110 "three went on together."
110 ""
110 "They soon reached the summit of the hill, and, evidently intending this"
110 "point to be the limit of their promenade, slackened pace and turned all"
110 "three aside to the gate whereat Tess had paused an hour before that"
110 "time to reconnoitre the town before descending into it. During their"
110 "discourse one of the clerical brothers probed the hedge carefully with"
110 "his umbrella, and dragged something to light."
110 ""
110 "“Here’s a pair of old boots,” he said. “Thrown away, I suppose, by some"
110 "tramp or other.”"
110 ""
110 "“Some imposter who wished to come into the town barefoot, perhaps, and"
110 "so excite our sympathies,” said Miss Chant. “Yes, it must have been,"
110 "for they are excellent walking-boots—by no means worn out. What a"
110 "wicked thing to do! I’ll carry them home for some poor person.”"
110 ""
110 "Cuthbert Clare, who had been the one to find them, picked them up for"
110 "her with the crook of his stick; and Tess’s boots were appropriated."
110 ""
110 "She, who had heard this, walked past under the screen of her woollen"
110 "veil till, presently looking back, she perceived that the church party"
110 "had left the gate with her boots and retreated down the hill."
110 ""
110 "Thereupon our heroine resumed her walk. Tears, blinding tears, were"
110 "running down her face. She knew that it was all sentiment, all baseless"
110 "impressibility, which had caused her to read the scene as her own"
110 "condemnation; nevertheless she could not get over it; she could not"
110 "contravene in her own defenceless person all those untoward omens. It"
110 "was impossible to think of returning to the Vicarage. Angel’s wife felt"
110 "almost as if she had been hounded up that hill like a scorned thing by"
110 "those—to her—superfine clerics. Innocently as the slight had been"
110 "inflicted, it was somewhat unfortunate that she had encountered the"
110 "sons and not the father, who, despite his narrowness, was far less"
110 "starched and ironed than they, and had to the full the gift of charity."
110 "As she again thought of her dusty boots she almost pitied those"
110 "habiliments for the quizzing to which they had been subjected, and felt"
110 "how hopeless life was for their owner."
110 ""
110 "“Ah!” she said, still sighing in pity of herself, “_they_ didn’t know"
110 "that I wore those over the roughest part of the road to save these"
110 "pretty ones _he_ bought for me—no—they did not know it! And they didn’t"
110 "think that _he_ chose the colour o’ my pretty frock—no—how could they?"
110 "If they had known perhaps they would not have cared, for they don’t"
110 "care much for him, poor thing!”"
110 ""
110 "Then she grieved for the beloved man whose conventional standard of"
110 "judgement had caused her all these latter sorrows; and she went her way"
110 "without knowing that the greatest misfortune of her life was this"
110 "feminine loss of courage at the last and critical moment through her"
110 "estimating her father-in-law by his sons. Her present condition was"
110 "precisely one which would have enlisted the sympathies of old Mr and"
110 "Mrs Clare. Their hearts went out of them at a bound towards extreme"
110 "cases, when the subtle mental troubles of the less desperate among"
110 "mankind failed to win their interest or regard. In jumping at Publicans"
110 "and Sinners they would forget that a word might be said for the worries"
110 "of Scribes and Pharisees; and this defect or limitation might have"
110 "recommended their own daughter-in-law to them at this moment as a"
110 "fairly choice sort of lost person for their love."
110 ""
110 "Thereupon she began to plod back along the road by which she had come"
110 "not altogether full of hope, but full of a conviction that a crisis in"
110 "her life was approaching. No crisis, apparently, had supervened; and"
110 "there was nothing left for her to do but to continue upon that"
110 "starve-acre farm till she could again summon courage to face the"
110 "Vicarage. She did, indeed, take sufficient interest in herself to throw"
110 "up her veil on this return journey, as if to let the world see that she"
110 "could at least exhibit a face such as Mercy Chant could not show. But"
110 "it was done with a sorry shake of the head. “It is nothing—it is"
110 "nothing!” she said. “Nobody loves it; nobody sees it. Who cares about"
110 "the looks of a castaway like me!”"
110 ""
110 "Her journey back was rather a meander than a march. It had no"
110 "sprightliness, no purpose; only a tendency. Along the tedious length of"
110 "Benvill Lane she began to grow tired, and she leant upon gates and"
110 "paused by milestones."
110 ""
110 "She did not enter any house till, at the seventh or eighth mile, she"
110 "descended the steep long hill below which lay the village or townlet of"
110 "Evershead, where in the morning she had breakfasted with such"
110 "contrasting expectations. The cottage by the church, in which she again"
110 "sat down, was almost the first at that end of the village, and while"
110 "the woman fetched her some milk from the pantry, Tess, looking down the"
110 "street, perceived that the place seemed quite deserted."
110 ""
110 "“The people are gone to afternoon service, I suppose?” she said."
110 ""
110 "“No, my dear,” said the old woman. “’Tis too soon for that; the bells"
110 "hain’t strook out yet. They be all gone to hear the preaching in yonder"
110 "barn. A ranter preaches there between the services—an excellent, fiery,"
110 "Christian man, they say. But, Lord, I don’t go to hear’n! What comes in"
110 "the regular way over the pulpit is hot enough for I.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess soon went onward into the village, her footsteps echoing against"
110 "the houses as though it were a place of the dead. Nearing the central"
110 "part, her echoes were intruded on by other sounds; and seeing the barn"
110 "not far off the road, she guessed these to be the utterances of the"
110 "preacher."
110 ""
110 "His voice became so distinct in the still clear air that she could soon"
110 "catch his sentences, though she was on the closed side of the barn. The"
110 "sermon, as might be expected, was of the extremest antinomian type; on"
110 "justification by faith, as expounded in the theology of St Paul. This"
110 "fixed idea of the rhapsodist was delivered with animated enthusiasm, in"
110 "a manner entirely declamatory, for he had plainly no skill as a"
110 "dialectician. Although Tess had not heard the beginning of the address,"
110 "she learnt what the text had been from its constant iteration—"
110 ""
110 "    “O foolish Galatians, who hath bewitched you, that ye should not"
110 "    obey the truth, before whose eyes Jesus Christ hath been evidently"
110 "    set forth, crucified among you?”"
110 ""
110 "Tess was all the more interested, as she stood listening behind, in"
110 "finding that the preacher’s doctrine was a vehement form of the view of"
110 "Angel’s father, and her interest intensified when the speaker began to"
110 "detail his own spiritual experiences of how he had come by those views."
110 "He had, he said, been the greatest of sinners. He had scoffed; he had"
110 "wantonly associated with the reckless and the lewd. But a day of"
110 "awakening had come, and, in a human sense, it had been brought about"
110 "mainly by the influence of a certain clergyman, whom he had at first"
110 "grossly insulted; but whose parting words had sunk into his heart, and"
110 "had remained there, till by the grace of Heaven they had worked this"
110 "change in him, and made him what they saw him."
110 ""
110 "But more startling to Tess than the doctrine had been the voice, which,"
110 "impossible as it seemed, was precisely that of Alec d’Urberville. Her"
110 "face fixed in painful suspense, she came round to the front of the"
110 "barn, and passed before it. The low winter sun beamed directly upon the"
110 "great double-doored entrance on this side; one of the doors being open,"
110 "so that the rays stretched far in over the threshing-floor to the"
110 "preacher and his audience, all snugly sheltered from the northern"
110 "breeze. The listeners were entirely villagers, among them being the man"
110 "whom she had seen carrying the red paint-pot on a former memorable"
110 "occasion. But her attention was given to the central figure, who stood"
110 "upon some sacks of corn, facing the people and the door. The three"
110 "o’clock sun shone full upon him, and the strange enervating conviction"
110 "that her seducer confronted her, which had been gaining ground in Tess"
110 "ever since she had heard his words distinctly, was at last established"
110 "as a fact indeed."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "End of Phase the Fifth"

110 "Phase the Sixth:"
110 ""
110 "The Convert"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XLV>"
110 ""
110 "Till this moment she had never seen or heard from d’Urberville since"
110 "her departure from Trantridge."
110 ""
110 "The rencounter came at a heavy moment, one of all moments calculated to"
110 "permit its impact with the least emotional shock. But such was"
110 "unreasoning memory that, though he stood there openly and palpably a"
110 "converted man, who was sorrowing for his past irregularities, a fear"
110 "overcame her, paralyzing her movement so that she neither retreated nor"
110 "advanced."
110 ""
110 "To think of what emanated from that countenance when she saw it last,"
110 "and to behold it now!... There was the same handsome unpleasantness of"
110 "mien, but now he wore neatly trimmed, old-fashioned whiskers, the sable"
110 "moustache having disappeared; and his dress was half-clerical, a"
110 "modification which had changed his expression sufficiently to abstract"
110 "the dandyism from his features, and to hinder for a second her belief"
110 "in his identity."
110 ""
110 "To Tess’s sense there was, just at first, a ghastly _bizarrerie_, a"
110 "grim incongruity, in the march of these solemn words of Scripture out"
110 "of such a mouth. This too familiar intonation, less than four years"
110 "earlier, had brought to her ears expressions of such divergent purpose"
110 "that her heart became quite sick at the irony of the contrast."
110 ""
110 "It was less a reform than a transfiguration. The former curves of"
110 "sensuousness were now modulated to lines of devotional passion. The"
110 "lip-shapes that had meant seductiveness were now made to express"
110 "supplication; the glow on the cheek that yesterday could be translated"
110 "as riotousness was evangelized to-day into the splendour of pious"
110 "rhetoric; animalism had become fanaticism; Paganism, Paulinism; the"
110 "bold rolling eye that had flashed upon her form in the old time with"
110 "such mastery now beamed with the rude energy of a theolatry that was"
110 "almost ferocious. Those black angularities which his face had used to"
110 "put on when his wishes were thwarted now did duty in picturing the"
110 "incorrigible backslider who would insist upon turning again to his"
110 "wallowing in the mire."
110 ""
110 "The lineaments, as such, seemed to complain. They had been diverted"
110 "from their hereditary connotation to signify impressions for which"
110 "Nature did not intend them. Strange that their very elevation was a"
110 "misapplication, that to raise seemed to falsify."
110 ""
110 "Yet could it be so? She would admit the ungenerous sentiment no longer."
110 "D’Urberville was not the first wicked man who had turned away from his"
110 "wickedness to save his soul alive, and why should she deem it unnatural"
110 "in him? It was but the usage of thought which had been jarred in her at"
110 "hearing good new words in bad old notes. The greater the sinner, the"
110 "greater the saint; it was not necessary to dive far into Christian"
110 "history to discover that."
110 ""
110 "Such impressions as these moved her vaguely, and without strict"
110 "definiteness. As soon as the nerveless pause of her surprise would"
110 "allow her to stir, her impulse was to pass on out of his sight. He had"
110 "obviously not discerned her yet in her position against the sun."
110 ""
110 "But the moment that she moved again he recognized her. The effect upon"
110 "her old lover was electric, far stronger than the effect of his"
110 "presence upon her. His fire, the tumultuous ring of his eloquence,"
110 "seemed to go out of him. His lip struggled and trembled under the words"
110 "that lay upon it; but deliver them it could not as long as she faced"
110 "him. His eyes, after their first glance upon her face, hung confusedly"
110 "in every other direction but hers, but came back in a desperate leap"
110 "every few seconds. This paralysis lasted, however, but a short time;"
110 "for Tess’s energies returned with the atrophy of his, and she walked as"
110 "fast as she was able past the barn and onward."
110 ""
110 "As soon as she could reflect, it appalled her, this change in their"
110 "relative platforms. He who had wrought her undoing was now on the side"
110 "of the Spirit, while she remained unregenerate. And, as in the legend,"
110 "it had resulted that her Cyprian image had suddenly appeared upon his"
110 "altar, whereby the fire of the priest had been well nigh extinguished."
110 ""
110 "She went on without turning her head. Her back seemed to be endowed"
110 "with a sensitiveness to ocular beams—even her clothing—so alive was she"
110 "to a fancied gaze which might be resting upon her from the outside of"
110 "that barn. All the way along to this point her heart had been heavy"
110 "with an inactive sorrow; now there was a change in the quality of its"
110 "trouble. That hunger for affection too long withheld was for the time"
110 "displaced by an almost physical sense of an implacable past which still"
110 "engirdled her. It intensified her consciousness of error to a practical"
110 "despair; the break of continuity between her earlier and present"
110 "existence, which she had hoped for, had not, after all, taken place."
110 "Bygones would never be complete bygones till she was a bygone herself."
110 ""
110 "Thus absorbed, she recrossed the northern part of Long-Ash Lane at"
110 "right angles, and presently saw before her the road ascending whitely"
110 "to the upland along whose margin the remainder of her journey lay. Its"
110 "dry pale surface stretched severely onward, unbroken by a single"
110 "figure, vehicle, or mark, save some occasional brown horse-droppings"
110 "which dotted its cold aridity here and there. While slowly breasting"
110 "this ascent Tess became conscious of footsteps behind her, and turning"
110 "she saw approaching that well-known form—so strangely accoutred as the"
110 "Methodist—the one personage in all the world she wished not to"
110 "encounter alone on this side of the grave."
110 ""
110 "There was not much time, however, for thought or elusion, and she"
110 "yielded as calmly as she could to the necessity of letting him overtake"
110 "her. She saw that he was excited, less by the speed of his walk than by"
110 "the feelings within him."
110 ""
110 "“Tess!” he said."
110 ""
110 "She slackened speed without looking round."
110 ""
110 "“Tess!” he repeated. “It is I—Alec d’Urberville.”"
110 ""
110 "She then looked back at him, and he came up."
110 ""
110 "“I see it is,” she answered coldly."
110 ""
110 "“Well—is that all? Yet I deserve no more! Of course,” he added, with a"
110 "slight laugh, “there is something of the ridiculous to your eyes in"
110 "seeing me like this. But—I must put up with that.... I heard you had"
110 "gone away; nobody knew where. Tess, you wonder why I have followed"
110 "you?”"
110 ""
110 "“I do, rather; and I would that you had not, with all my heart!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes—you may well say it,” he returned grimly, as they moved onward"
110 "together, she with unwilling tread. “But don’t mistake me; I beg this"
110 "because you may have been led to do so in noticing—if you did notice"
110 "it—how your sudden appearance unnerved me down there. It was but a"

110 "me a humbug for saying it—and immediately afterwards I felt that of all"
110 "persons in the world whom it was my duty and desire to save from the"

110 "wronged was that person. I have come with that sole purpose in"
110 "view—nothing more.”"
110 ""
110 "There was the smallest vein of scorn in her words of rejoinder: “Have"
110 "you saved yourself? Charity begins at home, they say.”"
110 ""
110 "“_I_ have done nothing!” said he indifferently. “Heaven, as I have been"
110 "telling my hearers, has done all. No amount of contempt that you can"
110 "pour upon me, Tess, will equal what I have poured upon myself—the old"
110 "Adam of my former years! Well, it is a strange story; believe it or"
110 "not; but I can tell you the means by which my conversion was brought"
110 "about, and I hope you will be interested enough at least to listen."
110 "Have you ever heard the name of the parson of Emminster—you must have"
110 "done do?—old Mr Clare; one of the most earnest of his school; one of"
110 "the few intense men left in the Church; not so intense as the extreme"
110 "wing of Christian believers with which I have thrown in my lot, but"
110 "quite an exception among the Established clergy, the younger of whom"
110 "are gradually attenuating the true doctrines by their sophistries, till"
110 "they are but the shadow of what they were. I only differ from him on"
110 "the question of Church and State—the interpretation of the text, ‘Come"
110 "out from among them and be ye separate, saith the Lord’—that’s all. He"
110 "is one who, I firmly believe, has been the humble means of saving more"
110 "souls in this country than any other man you can name. You have heard"
110 "of him?”"
110 ""
110 "“I have,” she said."
110 ""
110 "“He came to Trantridge two or three years ago to preach on behalf of"
110 "some missionary society; and I, wretched fellow that I was, insulted"
110 "him when, in his disinterestedness, he tried to reason with me and show"
110 "me the way. He did not resent my conduct, he simply said that some day"
110 "I should receive the first-fruits of the Spirit—that those who came to"
110 "scoff sometimes remained to pray. There was a strange magic in his"
110 "words. They sank into my mind. But the loss of my mother hit me most;"
110 "and by degrees I was brought to see daylight. Since then my one desire"
110 "has been to hand on the true view to others, and that is what I was"
110 "trying to do to-day; though it is only lately that I have preached"
110 "hereabout. The first months of my ministry have been spent in the North"
110 "of England among strangers, where I preferred to make my earliest"
110 "clumsy attempts, so as to acquire courage before undergoing that"
110 "severest of all tests of one’s sincerity, addressing those who have"
110 "known one, and have been one’s companions in the days of darkness. If"
110 "you could only know, Tess, the pleasure of having a good slap at"
110 "yourself, I am sure—”"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t go on with it!” she cried passionately, as she turned away from"
110 "him to a stile by the wayside, on which she bent herself. “I can’t"
110 "believe in such sudden things! I feel indignant with you for talking to"
110 "me like this, when you know—when you know what harm you’ve done me!"
110 "You, and those like you, take your fill of pleasure on earth by making"
110 "the life of such as me bitter and black with sorrow; and then it is a"
110 "fine thing, when you have had enough of that, to think of securing your"
110 "pleasure in heaven by becoming converted! Out upon such—I don’t believe"
110 "in you—I hate it!”"
110 ""
110 "“Tess,” he insisted; “don’t speak so! It came to me like a jolly new"
110 "idea! And you don’t believe me? What don’t you believe?”"
110 ""
110 "“Your conversion. Your scheme of religion.”"
110 ""
110 "“Why?”"
110 ""
110 "She dropped her voice. “Because a better man than you does not believe"
110 "in such.”"
110 ""
110 "“What a woman’s reason! Who is this better man?”"
110 ""
110 "“I cannot tell you.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well,” he declared, a resentment beneath his words seeming ready to"

110 "truly; but newcomers see furthest sometimes.”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes,” she replied sadly. “But I cannot believe in your conversion to a"
110 "new spirit. Such flashes as you feel, Alec, I fear don’t last!”"
110 ""
110 "Thus speaking she turned from the stile over which she had been"
110 "leaning, and faced him; whereupon his eyes, falling casually upon the"
110 "familiar countenance and form, remained contemplating her. The inferior"

110 ""
110 "“Don’t look at me like that!” he said abruptly."
110 ""

110 ""

110 "good looks, why don’t you keep it down?”"
110 ""
110 "She pulled down the veil, saying hastily, “It was mostly to keep off"
110 "the wind.”"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Ssh!” said Tess."
110 ""

110 "reminds me of the old times that I would forget!”"
110 ""
110 "After this their conversation dwindled to a casual remark now and then"

110 "with her, and not liking to send him back by positive mandate."
110 "Frequently when they came to a gate or stile they found painted thereon"

110 "her that the man was employed by himself and others who were working"
