110 "be left untried which might move the hearts of a wicked generation."
110 ""
110 "At length the road touched the spot called “Cross-in-Hand.” Of all"
110 "spots on the bleached and desolate upland this was the most forlorn. It"
110 "was so far removed from the charm which is sought in landscape by"
110 "artists and view-lovers as to reach a new kind of beauty, a negative"
110 "beauty of tragic tone. The place took its name from a stone pillar"
110 "which stood there, a strange rude monolith, from a stratum unknown in"
110 "any local quarry, on which was roughly carved a human hand. Differing"
110 "accounts were given of its history and purport. Some authorities stated"
110 "that a devotional cross had once formed the complete erection thereon,"
110 "of which the present relic was but the stump; others that the stone as"
110 "it stood was entire, and that it had been fixed there to mark a"
110 "boundary or place of meeting. Anyhow, whatever the origin of the relic,"
110 "there was and is something sinister, or solemn, according to mood, in"
110 "the scene amid which it stands; something tending to impress the most"
110 "phlegmatic passer-by."
110 ""
110 "“I think I must leave you now,” he remarked, as they drew near to this"
110 "spot. “I have to preach at Abbot’s-Cernel at six this evening, and my"
110 "way lies across to the right from here. And you upset me somewhat too,"
110 "Tessy—I cannot, will not, say why. I must go away and get strength...."
110 "How is it that you speak so fluently now? Who has taught you such good"
110 "English?”"
110 ""
110 "“I have learnt things in my troubles,” she said evasively."
110 ""
110 "“What troubles have you had?”"
110 ""
110 "She told him of the first one—the only one that related to him."
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville was struck mute. “I knew nothing of this till now!” he"
110 "next murmured. “Why didn’t you write to me when you felt your trouble"
110 "coming on?”"
110 ""
110 "She did not reply; and he broke the silence by adding: “Well—you will"
110 "see me again.”"
110 ""
110 "“No,” she answered. “Do not again come near me!”"
110 ""
110 "“I will think. But before we part come here.” He stepped up to the"

110 ""
110 "“Good God—how can you ask what is so unnecessary! All that is furthest"
110 "from my thought!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes—but swear it.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess, half frightened, gave way to his importunity; placed her hand"
110 "upon the stone and swore."
110 ""
110 "“I am sorry you are not a believer,” he continued; “that some"
110 "unbeliever should have got hold of you and unsettled your mind. But no"

110 "knows what may not happen? I’m off. Goodbye!”"
110 ""
110 "He turned to a hunting-gate in the hedge and, without letting his eyes"
110 "again rest upon her, leapt over and struck out across the down in the"
110 "direction of Abbot’s-Cernel. As he walked his pace showed perturbation,"
110 "and by-and-by, as if instigated by a former thought, he drew from his"
110 "pocket a small book, between the leaves of which was folded a letter,"
110 "worn and soiled, as from much re-reading. D’Urberville opened the"
110 "letter. It was dated several months before this time, and was signed by"
110 "Parson Clare."
110 ""
110 "The letter began by expressing the writer’s unfeigned joy at"
110 "d’Urberville’s conversion, and thanked him for his kindness in"
110 "communicating with the parson on the subject. It expressed Mr Clare’s"
110 "warm assurance of forgiveness for d’Urberville’s former conduct and his"
110 "interest in the young man’s plans for the future. He, Mr Clare, would"
110 "much have liked to see d’Urberville in the Church to whose ministry he"
110 "had devoted so many years of his own life, and would have helped him to"
110 "enter a theological college to that end; but since his correspondent"
110 "had possibly not cared to do this on account of the delay it would have"
110 "entailed, he was not the man to insist upon its paramount importance."
110 "Every man must work as he could best work, and in the method towards"
110 "which he felt impelled by the Spirit."
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville read and re-read this letter, and seemed to quiz himself"
110 "cynically. He also read some passages from memoranda as he walked till"
110 "his face assumed a calm, and apparently the image of Tess no longer"
110 "troubled his mind."
110 ""
110 "She meanwhile had kept along the edge of the hill by which lay her"
110 "nearest way home. Within the distance of a mile she met a solitary"
110 "shepherd."
110 ""
110 "“What is the meaning of that old stone I have passed?” she asked of"
110 "him. “Was it ever a Holy Cross?”"
110 ""
110 "“Cross—no; ’twer not a cross! ’Tis a thing of ill-omen, Miss. It was"
110 "put up in wuld times by the relations of a malefactor who was tortured"
110 "there by nailing his hand to a post and afterwards hung. The bones lie"
110 "underneath. They say he sold his soul to the devil, and that he walks"
110 "at times.”"
110 ""
110 "She felt the _petite mort_ at this unexpectedly gruesome information,"
110 "and left the solitary man behind her. It was dusk when she drew near to"
110 "Flintcomb-Ash, and in the lane at the entrance to the hamlet she"
110 "approached a girl and her lover without their observing her. They were"
110 "talking no secrets, and the clear unconcerned voice of the young woman,"
110 "in response to the warmer accents of the man, spread into the chilly"
110 "air as the one soothing thing within the dusky horizon, full of a"
110 "stagnant obscurity upon which nothing else intruded. For a moment the"
110 "voices cheered the heart of Tess, till she reasoned that this interview"
110 "had its origin, on one side or the other, in the same attraction which"
110 "had been the prelude to her own tribulation. When she came close, the"
110 "girl turned serenely and recognized her, the young man walking off in"
110 "embarrassment. The woman was Izz Huett, whose interest in Tess’s"
110 "excursion immediately superseded her own proceedings. Tess did not"
110 "explain very clearly its results, and Izz, who was a girl of tact,"
110 "began to speak of her own little affair, a phase of which Tess had just"
110 "witnessed."
110 ""
110 "“He is Amby Seedling, the chap who used to sometimes come and help at"
110 "Talbothays,” she explained indifferently. “He actually inquired and"
110 "found out that I had come here, and has followed me. He says he’s been"
110 "in love wi’ me these two years. But I’ve hardly answered him.”"
110 ""

110 "<CHAPTER XLVI>"
110 ""
110 "Several days had passed since her futile journey, and Tess was afield."
110 "The dry winter wind still blew, but a screen of thatched hurdles"
110 "erected in the eye of the blast kept its force away from her. On the"
110 "sheltered side was a turnip-slicing machine, whose bright blue hue of"
110 "new paint seemed almost vocal in the otherwise subdued scene. Opposite"
110 "its front was a long mound or “grave”, in which the roots had been"
110 "preserved since early winter. Tess was standing at the uncovered end,"
110 "chopping off with a bill-hook the fibres and earth from each root, and"
110 "throwing it after the operation into the slicer. A man was turning the"
110 "handle of the machine, and from its trough came the newly-cut swedes,"
110 "the fresh smell of whose yellow chips was accompanied by the sounds of"
110 "the snuffling wind, the smart swish of the slicing-blades, and the"
110 "choppings of the hook in Tess’s leather-gloved hand."
110 ""
110 "The wide acreage of blank agricultural brownness, apparent where the"
110 "swedes had been pulled, was beginning to be striped in wales of darker"
110 "brown, gradually broadening to ribands. Along the edge of each of these"
110 "something crept upon ten legs, moving without haste and without rest up"
110 "and down the whole length of the field; it was two horses and a man,"
110 "the plough going between them, turning up the cleared ground for a"
110 "spring sowing."
110 ""
110 "For hours nothing relieved the joyless monotony of things. Then, far"
110 "beyond the ploughing-teams, a black speck was seen. It had come from"
110 "the corner of a fence, where there was a gap, and its tendency was up"
110 "the incline, towards the swede-cutters. From the proportions of a mere"
110 "point it advanced to the shape of a ninepin, and was soon perceived to"
110 "be a man in black, arriving from the direction of Flintcomb-Ash. The"
110 "man at the slicer, having nothing else to do with his eyes, continually"
110 "observed the comer, but Tess, who was occupied, did not perceive him"
110 "till her companion directed her attention to his approach."
110 ""
110 "It was not her hard taskmaster, Farmer Groby; it was one in a"
110 "semi-clerical costume, who now represented what had once been the"
110 "free-and-easy Alec d’Urberville. Not being hot at his preaching there"
110 "was less enthusiasm about him now, and the presence of the grinder"
110 "seemed to embarrass him. A pale distress was already on Tess’s face,"
110 "and she pulled her curtained hood further over it."
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville came up and said quietly—"
110 ""
110 "“I want to speak to you, Tess.”"
110 ""
110 "“You have refused my last request, not to come near me!” said she."
110 ""
110 "“Yes, but I have a good reason.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, tell it.”"
110 ""
110 "“It is more serious than you may think.”"
110 ""
110 "He glanced round to see if he were overheard. They were at some"
110 "distance from the man who turned the slicer, and the movement of the"
110 "machine, too, sufficiently prevented Alec’s words reaching other ears."
110 "D’Urberville placed himself so as to screen Tess from the labourer,"
110 "turning his back to the latter."
110 ""
110 "“It is this,” he continued, with capricious compunction. “In thinking"
110 "of your soul and mine when we last met, I neglected to inquire as to"
110 "your worldly condition. You were well dressed, and I did not think of"
110 "it. But I see now that it is hard—harder than it used to be when I—knew"
110 "you—harder than you deserve. Perhaps a good deal of it is owning to"
110 "me!”"
110 ""
110 "She did not answer, and he watched her inquiringly, as, with bent head,"
110 "her face completely screened by the hood, she resumed her trimming of"
110 "the swedes. By going on with her work she felt better able to keep him"
110 "outside her emotions."
110 ""
110 "“Tess,” he added, with a sigh of discontent,—“yours was the very worst"
110 "case I ever was concerned in! I had no idea of what had resulted till"

110 "blame was mine—the whole unconventional business of our time at"
110 "Trantridge. You, too, the real blood of which I am but the base"
110 "imitation, what a blind young thing you were as to possibilities! I say"
110 "in all earnestness that it is a shame for parents to bring up their"
110 "girls in such dangerous ignorance of the gins and nets that the wicked"
110 "may set for them, whether their motive be a good one or the result of"
110 "simple indifference.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess still did no more than listen, throwing down one globular root and"
110 "taking up another with automatic regularity, the pensive contour of the"
110 "mere fieldwoman alone marking her."
110 ""
110 "“But it is not that I came to say,” d’Urberville went on. “My"
110 "circumstances are these. I have lost my mother since you were at"
110 "Trantridge, and the place is my own. But I intend to sell it, and"
110 "devote myself to missionary work in Africa. A devil of a poor hand I"
110 "shall make at the trade, no doubt. However, what I want to ask you is,"

110 "can make for the trick played you: that is, will you be my wife, and go"
110 "with me?... I have already obtained this precious document. It was my"
110 "old mother’s dying wish.”"
110 ""
110 "He drew a piece of parchment from his pocket, with a slight fumbling of"
110 "embarrassment."
110 ""
110 "“What is it?” said she."
110 ""
110 "“A marriage licence.”"
110 ""
110 "“O no, sir—no!” she said quickly, starting back."

110 ""
110 "And as he asked the question a disappointment which was not entirely"

110 "“Indeed, Mister! And what mid Methodist pa’sons have to do with she?”"
110 ""
110 "“Who is the fellow?” asked d’Urberville, turning to Tess."
110 ""
110 "She went close up to him."
110 ""
110 "“Go—I do beg you!” she said."
110 ""
110 "“What! And leave you to that tyrant? I can see in his face what a churl"
110 "he is.”"
110 ""
110 "“He won’t hurt me. _He’s_ not in love with me. I can leave at"
110 "Lady-Day.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, I have no right but to obey, I suppose. But—well, goodbye!”"
110 ""
110 "Her defender, whom she dreaded more than her assailant, having"

110 "took with the greatest coolness, that sort of attack being independent"
110 "of sex. To have as a master this man of stone, who would have cuffed"

110 "She silently walked back towards the summit of the field that was the"
110 "scene of her labour, so absorbed in the interview which had just taken"
110 "place that she was hardly aware that the nose of Groby’s horse almost"
110 "touched her shoulders."
110 ""
110 "“If so be you make an agreement to work for me till Lady-Day, I’ll see"

110 "thing, and then ’tis another. But I’ll put up with it no longer!”"
110 ""
110 "Knowing very well that he did not harass the other women of the farm as"
110 "he harassed her out of spite for the flooring he had once received, she"

110 "been free to accept the offer just made her of being the monied Alec’s"

110 "married him now! He is so unpleasant to me.”"
110 ""
110 "That very night she began an appealing letter to Clare, concealing from"
110 "him her hardships, and assuring him of her undying affection. Any one"
110 "who had been in a position to read between the lines would have seen"
110 "that at the back of her great love was some monstrous fear—almost a"
110 "desperation—as to some secret contingencies which were not disclosed."
110 "But again she did not finish her effusion; he had asked Izz to go with"
110 "him, and perhaps he did not care for her at all. She put the letter in"
110 "her box, and wondered if it would ever reach Angel’s hands."
110 ""
110 "After this her daily tasks were gone through heavily enough, and"
110 "brought on the day which was of great import to agriculturists—the day"
110 "of the Candlemas Fair. It was at this fair that new engagements were"
110 "entered into for the twelve months following the ensuing Lady-Day, and"
110 "those of the farming population who thought of changing their places"
110 "duly attended at the county-town where the fair was held. Nearly all"
110 "the labourers on Flintcomb-Ash farm intended flight, and early in the"
110 "morning there was a general exodus in the direction of the town, which"
110 "lay at a distance of from ten to a dozen miles over hilly country."
110 "Though Tess also meant to leave at the quarter-day, she was one of the"
110 "few who did not go to the fair, having a vaguely-shaped hope that"
110 "something would happen to render another outdoor engagement"
110 "unnecessary."
110 ""
110 "It was a peaceful February day, of wonderful softness for the time, and"
110 "one would almost have thought that winter was over. She had hardly"
110 "finished her dinner when d’Urberville’s figure darkened the window of"
110 "the cottage wherein she was a lodger, which she had all to herself"
110 "to-day."
110 ""
110 "Tess jumped up, but her visitor had knocked at the door, and she could"
110 "hardly in reason run away. D’Urberville’s knock, his walk up to the"
110 "door, had some indescribable quality of difference from his air when"
110 "she last saw him. They seemed to be acts of which the doer was ashamed."
110 "She thought that she would not open the door; but, as there was no"
110 "sense in that either, she arose, and having lifted the latch stepped"
110 "back quickly. He came in, saw her, and flung himself down into a chair"
110 "before speaking."
110 ""
110 "“Tess—I couldn’t help it!” he began desperately, as he wiped his heated"
110 "face, which had also a superimposed flush of excitement. “I felt that I"

110 "harm to a bad man; yet so it is. If you would only pray for me, Tess!”"
110 ""
110 "The suppressed discontent of his manner was almost pitiable, and yet"
110 "Tess did not pity him."
110 ""
110 "“How can I pray for you,” she said, “when I am forbidden to believe"
110 "that the great Power who moves the world would alter His plans on my"
110 "account?”"
110 ""
110 "“You really think that?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes. I have been cured of the presumption of thinking otherwise.”"
110 ""
110 "“Cured? By whom?”"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Ah—your husband—your husband! How strange it seems! I remember you"

110 "religion—perhaps owing to me.”"
110 ""
110 "“But I have. Though I don’t believe in anything supernatural.”"
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville looked at her with misgiving."
110 ""
110 "“Then do you think that the line I take is all wrong?”"
110 ""
110 "“A good deal of it.”"
110 ""
110 "“H’m—and yet I’ve felt so sure about it,” he said uneasily."
110 ""

110 "dear husband.... But I don’t believe—”"
110 ""
110 "Here she gave her negations."
110 ""
110 "“The fact is,” said d’Urberville drily, “whatever your dear husband"
110 "believed you accept, and whatever he rejected you reject, without the"

110 "women. Your mind is enslaved to his.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah, because he knew everything!” said she, with a triumphant"

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Say that again,” asked d’Urberville, who had listened with the"
110 "greatest attention."
110 ""
110 "She repeated the argument, and d’Urberville thoughtfully murmured the"
110 "words after her."
110 ""
110 "“Anything else?” he presently asked."
110 ""
110 "“He said at another time something like this”; and she gave another,"
110 "which might possibly have been paralleled in many a work of the"
110 "pedigree ranging from the _Dictionnaire Philosophique_ to Huxley’s"

110 ""
110 "“Ah—ha! How do you remember them?”"
110 ""

110 "quite understand that one; but I know it is right.”"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "He fell into thought."
110 ""

110 ""

110 ""

110 "“How?”"
110 ""
110 "“Why,” he said aridly; “I have come all the way here to see you to-day!"
110 "But I started from home to go to Casterbridge Fair, where I have"

110 "Here’s the announcement.”"
110 ""
110 "He drew from his breast-pocket a poster whereon was printed the day,"

110 ""
110 "“But how can you get there?” said Tess, looking at the clock."
110 ""

110 ""

110 "intentionally. But you have been the means—the innocent means—of my"

110 "of the world, are again entangled therein and overcome’—whose latter"

110 "“Tess, my girl, I was on the way to, at least, social salvation till I"

110 "“And why then have you tempted me? I was firm as a man could be till I"

110 ""
110 "“I couldn’t help your seeing me again!” said Tess, recoiling."
110 ""
110 "“I know it—I repeat that I do not blame you. But the fact remains. When"
110 "I saw you ill-used on the farm that day I was nearly mad to think that"

110 "who has it seems to neglect you utterly!”"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t speak against him—he is absent!” she cried in much excitement."
110 "“Treat him honourably—he has never wronged you! O leave his wife before"
110 "any scandal spreads that may do harm to his honest name!”"
110 ""
110 "“I will—I will,” he said, like a man awakening from a luring dream. “I"
110 "have broken my engagement to preach to those poor drunken boobies at"

110 "month ago I should have been horrified at such a possibility. I’ll go"

110 "Tessy—one! Only for old friendship—”"
110 ""
110 "“I am without defence. Alec! A good man’s honour is in my"
110 "keeping—think—be ashamed!”"
110 ""
110 "“Pooh! Well, yes—yes!”"
110 ""
110 "He clenched his lips, mortified with himself for his weakness. His eyes"
110 "were equally barren of worldly and religious faith. The corpses of"
110 "those old fitful passions which had lain inanimate amid the lines of"
110 "his face ever since his reformation seemed to wake and come together as"
110 "in a resurrection. He went out indeterminately."
110 ""
110 "Though d’Urberville had declared that this breach of his engagement"
110 "to-day was the simple backsliding of a believer, Tess’s words, as"
110 "echoed from Angel Clare, had made a deep impression upon him, and"
110 "continued to do so after he had left her. He moved on in silence, as if"
110 "his energies were benumbed by the hitherto undreamt-of possibility that"
110 "his position was untenable. Reason had had nothing to do with his"
110 "whimsical conversion, which was perhaps the mere freak of a careless"
110 "man in search of a new sensation, and temporarily impressed by his"
110 "mother’s death."
110 ""
110 "The drops of logic Tess had let fall into the sea of his enthusiasm"
110 "served to chill its effervescence to stagnation. He said to himself, as"
110 "he pondered again and again over the crystallized phrases that she had"
110 "handed on to him, “That clever fellow little thought that, by telling"
110 "her those things, he might be paving my way back to her!”"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER XLVII>"
110 ""
110 "It is the threshing of the last wheat-rick at Flintcomb-Ash farm. The"
110 "dawn of the March morning is singularly inexpressive, and there is"
110 "nothing to show where the eastern horizon lies. Against the twilight"
110 "rises the trapezoidal top of the stack, which has stood forlornly here"
110 "through the washing and bleaching of the wintry weather."
110 ""
110 "When Izz Huett and Tess arrived at the scene of operations only a"
110 "rustling denoted that others had preceded them; to which, as the light"
110 "increased, there were presently added the silhouettes of two men on the"
110 "summit. They were busily “unhaling” the rick, that is, stripping off"
110 "the thatch before beginning to throw down the sheaves; and while this"
110 "was in progress Izz and Tess, with the other women-workers, in their"
110 "whitey-brown pinners, stood waiting and shivering, Farmer Groby having"
110 "insisted upon their being on the spot thus early to get the job over if"
110 "possible by the end of the day. Close under the eaves of the stack, and"
110 "as yet barely visible, was the red tyrant that the women had come to"
110 "serve—a timber-framed construction, with straps and wheels"
110 "appertaining—the threshing-machine which, whilst it was going, kept up"
110 "a despotic demand upon the endurance of their muscles and nerves."
110 ""
110 "A little way off there was another indistinct figure; this one black,"
110 "with a sustained hiss that spoke of strength very much in reserve. The"
110 "long chimney running up beside an ash-tree, and the warmth which"
110 "radiated from the spot, explained without the necessity of much"
110 "daylight that here was the engine which was to act as the _primum"
110 "mobile_ of this little world. By the engine stood a dark, motionless"
110 "being, a sooty and grimy embodiment of tallness, in a sort of trance,"
110 "with a heap of coals by his side: it was the engine-man. The isolation"
110 "of his manner and colour lent him the appearance of a creature from"
110 "Tophet, who had strayed into the pellucid smokelessness of this region"
110 "of yellow grain and pale soil, with which he had nothing in common, to"
110 "amaze and to discompose its aborigines."
110 ""
110 "What he looked he felt. He was in the agricultural world, but not of"
110 "it. He served fire and smoke; these denizens of the fields served"
110 "vegetation, weather, frost, and sun. He travelled with his engine from"
110 "farm to farm, from county to county, for as yet the steam"
110 "threshing-machine was itinerant in this part of Wessex. He spoke in a"
110 "strange northern accent; his thoughts being turned inwards upon"
110 "himself, his eye on his iron charge, hardly perceiving the scenes"
110 "around him, and caring for them not at all: holding only strictly"
110 "necessary intercourse with the natives, as if some ancient doom"
110 "compelled him to wander here against his will in the service of his"
110 "Plutonic master. The long strap which ran from the driving-wheel of his"
110 "engine to the red thresher under the rick was the sole tie-line between"
110 "agriculture and him."
110 ""
110 "While they uncovered the sheaves he stood apathetic beside his portable"
110 "repository of force, round whose hot blackness the morning air"
110 "quivered. He had nothing to do with preparatory labour. His fire was"
110 "waiting incandescent, his steam was at high pressure, in a few seconds"
110 "he could make the long strap move at an invisible velocity. Beyond its"
110 "extent the environment might be corn, straw, or chaos; it was all the"
110 "same to him. If any of the autochthonous idlers asked him what he"
110 "called himself, he replied shortly, “an engineer.”"
110 ""
110 "The rick was unhaled by full daylight; the men then took their places,"
110 "the women mounted, and the work began. Farmer Groby—or, as they called"
110 "him, “he”—had arrived ere this, and by his orders Tess was placed on"
110 "the platform of the machine, close to the man who fed it, her business"
110 "being to untie every sheaf of corn handed on to her by Izz Huett, who"
110 "stood next, but on the rick; so that the feeder could seize it and"
110 "spread it over the revolving drum, which whisked out every grain in one"
110 "moment."
110 ""
110 "They were soon in full progress, after a preparatory hitch or two,"
110 "which rejoiced the hearts of those who hated machinery. The work sped"
110 "on till breakfast time, when the thresher was stopped for half an hour;"
110 "and on starting again after the meal the whole supplementary strength"
110 "of the farm was thrown into the labour of constructing the straw-rick,"
110 "which began to grow beside the stack of corn. A hasty lunch was eaten"
110 "as they stood, without leaving their positions, and then another couple"
110 "of hours brought them near to dinner-time; the inexorable wheel"
110 "continuing to spin, and the penetrating hum of the thresher to thrill"
110 "to the very marrow all who were near the revolving wire-cage."
110 ""
110 "The old men on the rising straw-rick talked of the past days when they"
110 "had been accustomed to thresh with flails on the oaken barn-floor; when"
110 "everything, even to winnowing, was effected by hand-labour, which, to"
110 "their thinking, though slow, produced better results. Those, too, on"
110 "the corn-rick talked a little; but the perspiring ones at the machine,"
110 "including Tess, could not lighten their duties by the exchange of many"
110 "words. It was the ceaselessness of the work which tried her so"
110 "severely, and began to make her wish that she had never come to"
110 "Flintcomb-Ash. The women on the corn-rick—Marian, who was one of them,"
110 "in particular—could stop to drink ale or cold tea from the flagon now"
110 "and then, or to exchange a few gossiping remarks while they wiped their"
110 "faces or cleared the fragments of straw and husk from their clothing;"
110 "but for Tess there was no respite; for, as the drum never stopped, the"
110 "man who fed it could not stop, and she, who had to supply the man with"
110 "untied sheaves, could not stop either, unless Marian changed places"
110 "with her, which she sometimes did for half an hour in spite of Groby’s"
110 "objections that she was too slow-handed for a feeder."
110 ""
110 "For some probably economical reason it was usually a woman who was"
110 "chosen for this particular duty, and Groby gave as his motive in"
110 "selecting Tess that she was one of those who best combined strength"
110 "with quickness in untying, and both with staying power, and this may"
110 "have been true. The hum of the thresher, which prevented speech,"
110 "increased to a raving whenever the supply of corn fell short of the"
110 "regular quantity. As Tess and the man who fed could never turn their"
110 "heads she did not know that just before the dinner-hour a person had"
110 "come silently into the field by the gate, and had been standing under a"
110 "second rick watching the scene and Tess in particular. He was dressed"
110 "in a tweed suit of fashionable pattern, and he twirled a gay"
110 "walking-cane."
110 ""
110 "“Who is that?” said Izz Huett to Marian. She had at first addressed the"
110 "inquiry to Tess, but the latter could not hear it."
110 ""
110 "“Somebody’s fancy-man, I s’pose,” said Marian laconically."
110 ""
110 "“I’ll lay a guinea he’s after Tess.”"
110 ""
110 "“O no. ’Tis a ranter pa’son who’s been sniffing after her lately; not a"
110 "dandy like this.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well—this is the same man.”"
110 ""
110 "“The same man as the preacher? But he’s quite different!”"
110 ""
110 "“He hev left off his black coat and white neckercher, and hev cut off"
110 "his whiskers; but he’s the same man for all that.”"
110 ""
110 "“D’ye really think so? Then I’ll tell her,” said Marian."
110 ""
110 "“Don’t. She’ll see him soon enough, good-now.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, I don’t think it at all right for him to join his preaching to"
110 "courting a married woman, even though her husband mid be abroad, and"
110 "she, in a sense, a widow.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh—he can do her no harm,” said Izz drily. “Her mind can no more be"
110 "heaved from that one place where it do bide than a stooded waggon from"
110 "the hole he’s in. Lord love ’ee, neither court-paying, nor preaching,"
110 "nor the seven thunders themselves, can wean a woman when ’twould be"
110 "better for her that she should be weaned.”"
110 ""
110 "Dinner-time came, and the whirling ceased; whereupon Tess left her"
110 "post, her knees trembling so wretchedly with the shaking of the machine"
110 "that she could scarcely walk."
110 ""
110 "“You ought to het a quart o’ drink into ’ee, as I’ve done,” said"
110 "Marian. “You wouldn’t look so white then. Why, souls above us, your"
110 "face is as if you’d been hagrode!”"
110 ""
110 "It occurred to the good-natured Marian that, as Tess was so tired, her"
110 "discovery of her visitor’s presence might have the bad effect of taking"
110 "away her appetite; and Marian was thinking of inducing Tess to descend"
110 "by a ladder on the further side of the stack when the gentleman came"
110 "forward and looked up."
110 ""
110 "Tess uttered a short little “Oh!” And a moment after she said, quickly,"
110 "“I shall eat my dinner here—right on the rick.”"
110 ""
110 "Sometimes, when they were so far from their cottages, they all did"
110 "this; but as there was rather a keen wind going to-day, Marian and the"
110 "rest descended, and sat under the straw-stack."
110 ""
110 "The newcomer was, indeed, Alec d’Urberville, the late Evangelist,"
110 "despite his changed attire and aspect. It was obvious at a glance that"
110 "the original _Weltlust_ had come back; that he had restored himself, as"
110 "nearly as a man could do who had grown three or four years older, to"
110 "the old jaunty, slapdash guise under which Tess had first known her"
110 "admirer, and cousin so-called. Having decided to remain where she was,"
110 "Tess sat down among the bundles, out of sight of the ground, and began"
110 "her meal; till, by-and-by, she heard footsteps on the ladder, and"
110 "immediately after Alec appeared upon the stack—now an oblong and level"
110 "platform of sheaves. He strode across them, and sat down opposite of"
110 "her without a word."
110 ""
110 "Tess continued to eat her modest dinner, a slice of thick pancake which"
110 "she had brought with her. The other workfolk were by this time all"
110 "gathered under the rick, where the loose straw formed a comfortable"
110 "retreat."
110 ""
110 "“I am here again, as you see,” said d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "“Why do you trouble me so!” she cried, reproach flashing from her very"
110 "finger-ends."
110 ""
110 "“_I_ trouble _you_? I think I may ask, why do you trouble me?”"
110 ""
110 "“Sure, I don’t trouble you any-when!”"
110 ""
110 "“You say you don’t? But you do! You haunt me. Those very eyes that you"
110 "turned upon me with such a bitter flash a moment ago, they come to me"
110 "just as you showed them then, in the night and in the day! Tess, ever"
110 "since you told me of that child of ours, it is just as if my feelings,"
110 "which have been flowing in a strong puritanical stream, had suddenly"
110 "found a way open in the direction of you, and had all at once gushed"
110 "through. The religious channel is left dry forthwith; and it is you who"
110 "have done it!”"
110 ""
110 "She gazed in silence."
110 ""
110 "“What—you have given up your preaching entirely?” she asked. She had"
110 "gathered from Angel sufficient of the incredulity of modern thought to"
110 "despise flash enthusiasm; but, as a woman, she was somewhat appalled."
110 ""
110 "In affected severity d’Urberville continued—"
110 ""
110 "“Entirely. I have broken every engagement since that afternoon I was to"
110 "address the drunkards at Casterbridge Fair. The deuce only knows what I"
110 "am thought of by the brethren. Ah-ha! The brethren! No doubt they pray"
110 "for me—weep for me; for they are kind people in their way. But what do"
110 "I care? How could I go on with the thing when I had lost my faith in"
110 "it?—it would have been hypocrisy of the basest kind! Among them I"
110 "should have stood like Hymenaeus and Alexander, who were delivered over"
110 "to Satan that they might learn not to blaspheme. What a grand revenge"
110 "you have taken! I saw you innocent, and I deceived you. Four years"
110 "after, you find me a Christian enthusiast; you then work upon me,"
110 "perhaps to my complete perdition! But Tess, my coz, as I used to call"
110 "you, this is only my way of talking, and you must not look so horribly"
110 "concerned. Of course you have done nothing except retain your pretty"
110 "face and shapely figure. I saw it on the rick before you saw me—that"
110 "tight pinafore-thing sets it off, and that wing-bonnet—you field-girls"
110 "should never wear those bonnets if you wish to keep out of danger.” He"
110 "regarded her silently for a few moments, and with a short cynical laugh"
110 "resumed: “I believe that if the bachelor-apostle, whose deputy I"
110 "thought I was, had been tempted by such a pretty face, he would have"
110 "let go the plough for her sake as I do!”"
110 ""
110 "Tess attempted to expostulate, but at this juncture all her fluency"
110 "failed her, and without heeding he added:"
110 ""
110 "“Well, this paradise that you supply is perhaps as good as any other,"
110 "after all. But to speak seriously, Tess.” D’Urberville rose and came"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“O no! I’m a different sort of fellow from that! If there’s nobody to"

110 "She could not get her morsels of food down her throat; her lips were"

110 ""

110 ""

110 ""
110 "One of her leather gloves, which she had taken off to eat her"

110 ""

110 "<CHAPTER XLVIII>"
110 ""
110 "In the afternoon the farmer made it known that the rick was to be"
110 "finished that night, since there was a moon by which they could see to"
110 "work, and the man with the engine was engaged for another farm on the"
110 "morrow. Hence the twanging and humming and rustling proceeded with even"
110 "less intermission than usual."
110 ""
110 "It was not till “nammet”-time, about three o’clock, that Tess raised"
110 "her eyes and gave a momentary glance round. She felt but little"
110 "surprise at seeing that Alec d’Urberville had come back, and was"
110 "standing under the hedge by the gate. He had seen her lift her eyes,"
110 "and waved his hand urbanely to her, while he blew her a kiss. It meant"
110 "that their quarrel was over. Tess looked down again, and carefully"
110 "abstained from gazing in that direction."
110 ""
110 "Thus the afternoon dragged on. The wheat-rick shrank lower, and the"
110 "straw-rick grew higher, and the corn-sacks were carted away. At six"
110 "o’clock the wheat-rick was about shoulder-high from the ground. But the"
110 "unthreshed sheaves remaining untouched seemed countless still,"
110 "notwithstanding the enormous numbers that had been gulped down by the"
110 "insatiable swallower, fed by the man and Tess, through whose two young"
110 "hands the greater part of them had passed. And the immense stack of"
110 "straw where in the morning there had been nothing, appeared as the"
110 "_faeces_ of the same buzzing red glutton. From the west sky a wrathful"
110 "shine—all that wild March could afford in the way of sunset—had burst"
110 "forth after the cloudy day, flooding the tired and sticky faces of the"
110 "threshers, and dyeing them with a coppery light, as also the flapping"
110 "garments of the women, which clung to them like dull flames."
110 ""
110 "A panting ache ran through the rick. The man who fed was weary, and"
110 "Tess could see that the red nape of his neck was encrusted with dirt"
110 "and husks. She still stood at her post, her flushed and perspiring face"
110 "coated with the corndust, and her white bonnet embrowned by it. She was"
110 "the only woman whose place was upon the machine so as to be shaken"
110 "bodily by its spinning, and the decrease of the stack now separated her"
110 "from Marian and Izz, and prevented their changing duties with her as"
110 "they had done. The incessant quivering, in which every fibre of her"
110 "frame participated, had thrown her into a stupefied reverie in which"
110 "her arms worked on independently of her consciousness. She hardly knew"
110 "where she was, and did not hear Izz Huett tell her from below that her"
110 "hair was tumbling down."
110 ""
110 "By degrees the freshest among them began to grow cadaverous and"
110 "saucer-eyed. Whenever Tess lifted her head she beheld always the great"
110 "upgrown straw-stack, with the men in shirt-sleeves upon it, against the"
110 "gray north sky; in front of it the long red elevator like a Jacob’s"
110 "ladder, on which a perpetual stream of threshed straw ascended, a"
110 "yellow river running uphill, and spouting out on the top of the rick."
110 ""
110 "She knew that Alec d’Urberville was still on the scene, observing her"
110 "from some point or other, though she could not say where. There was an"
110 "excuse for his remaining, for when the threshed rick drew near its"
110 "final sheaves a little ratting was always done, and men unconnected"
110 "with the threshing sometimes dropped in for that performance—sporting"
110 "characters of all descriptions, gents with terriers and facetious"
110 "pipes, roughs with sticks and stones."
110 ""
110 "But there was another hour’s work before the layer of live rats at the"
110 "base of the stack would be reached; and as the evening light in the"
110 "direction of the Giant’s Hill by Abbot’s-Cernel dissolved away, the"

110 "two Marian had felt uneasy about Tess, whom she could not get near"
110 "enough to speak to, the other women having kept up their strength by"
110 "drinking ale, and Tess having done without it through traditionary"
110 "dread, owing to its results at her home in childhood. But Tess still"
110 "kept going: if she could not fill her part she would have to leave; and"
110 "this contingency, which she would have regarded with equanimity and"
110 "even with relief a month or two earlier, had become a terror since"
110 "d’Urberville had begun to hover round her."
110 ""
110 "The sheaf-pitchers and feeders had now worked the rick so low that"
110 "people on the ground could talk to them. To Tess’s surprise Farmer"
110 "Groby came up on the machine to her, and said that if she desired to"
110 "join her friend he did not wish her to keep on any longer, and would"

110 "she knew, and also that this concession had been granted in obedience"
110 "to the request of that friend, or enemy. She shook her head and toiled"
110 "on."
110 ""
110 "The time for the rat-catching arrived at last, and the hunt began. The"
110 "creatures had crept downwards with the subsidence of the rick till they"
110 "were all together at the bottom, and being now uncovered from their"
110 "last refuge, they ran across the open ground in all directions, a loud"

110 "the women had guarded against by various schemes of skirt-tucking and"

110 "dogs, masculine shouts, feminine screams, oaths, stampings, and"
110 "confusion as of Pandemonium, Tess untied her last sheaf; the drum"

110 "ground."
110 ""
110 "Her lover, who had only looked on at the rat-catching, was promptly at"

110 ""

110 "    My own Husband,"

110 "    Let me call you so—I must—even if it makes you angry to think of"
