110 "<CHAPTER XLIX>"
110 ""
110 "The appeal duly found its way to the breakfast-table of the quiet"
110 "Vicarage to the westward, in that valley where the air is so soft and"
110 "the soil so rich that the effort of growth requires but superficial aid"
110 "by comparison with the tillage at Flintcomb-Ash, and where to Tess the"
110 "human world seemed so different (though it was much the same). It was"
110 "purely for security that she had been requested by Angel to send her"
110 "communications through his father, whom he kept pretty well informed of"
110 "his changing addresses in the country he had gone to exploit for"
110 "himself with a heavy heart."
110 ""
110 "“Now,” said old Mr Clare to his wife, when he had read the envelope,"
110 "“if Angel proposes leaving Rio for a visit home at the end of next"
110 "month, as he told us that he hoped to do, I think this may hasten his"
110 "plans; for I believe it to be from his wife.” He breathed deeply at the"
110 "thought of her; and the letter was redirected to be promptly sent on to"
110 "Angel."
110 ""
110 "“Dear fellow, I hope he will get home safely,” murmured Mrs Clare. “To"
110 "my dying day I shall feel that he has been ill-used. You should have"
110 "sent him to Cambridge in spite of his want of faith and given him the"
110 "same chance as the other boys had. He would have grown out of it under"
110 "proper influence, and perhaps would have taken Orders after all. Church"
110 "or no Church, it would have been fairer to him.”"
110 ""
110 "This was the only wail with which Mrs Clare ever disturbed her"
110 "husband’s peace in respect to their sons. And she did not vent this"
110 "often; for she was as considerate as she was devout, and knew that his"
110 "mind too was troubled by doubts as to his justice in this matter. Only"
110 "too often had she heard him lying awake at night, stifling sighs for"
110 "Angel with prayers. But the uncompromising Evangelical did not even now"
110 "hold that he would have been justified in giving his son, an"
110 "unbeliever, the same academic advantages that he had given to the two"
110 "others, when it was possible, if not probable, that those very"
110 "advantages might have been used to decry the doctrines which he had"
110 "made it his life’s mission and desire to propagate, and the mission of"
110 "his ordained sons likewise. To put with one hand a pedestal under the"
110 "feet of the two faithful ones, and with the other to exalt the"
110 "unfaithful by the same artificial means, he deemed to be alike"
110 "inconsistent with his convictions, his position, and his hopes."
110 "Nevertheless, he loved his misnamed Angel, and in secret mourned over"
110 "this treatment of him as Abraham might have mourned over the doomed"
110 "Isaac while they went up the hill together. His silent self-generated"
110 "regrets were far bitterer than the reproaches which his wife rendered"
110 "audible."
110 ""
110 "They blamed themselves for this unlucky marriage. If Angel had never"
110 "been destined for a farmer he would never have been thrown with"
110 "agricultural girls. They did not distinctly know what had separated him"
110 "and his wife, nor the date on which the separation had taken place. At"
110 "first they had supposed it must be something of the nature of a serious"
110 "aversion. But in his later letters he occasionally alluded to the"
110 "intention of coming home to fetch her; from which expressions they"
110 "hoped the division might not owe its origin to anything so hopelessly"
110 "permanent as that. He had told them that she was with her relatives,"
110 "and in their doubts they had decided not to intrude into a situation"
110 "which they knew no way of bettering."
110 ""
110 "The eyes for which Tess’s letter was intended were gazing at this time"
110 "on a limitless expanse of country from the back of a mule which was"
110 "bearing him from the interior of the South-American Continent towards"
110 "the coast. His experiences of this strange land had been sad. The"
110 "severe illness from which he had suffered shortly after his arrival had"
110 "never wholly left him, and he had by degrees almost decided to"
110 "relinquish his hope of farming here, though, as long as the bare"
110 "possibility existed of his remaining, he kept this change of view a"
110 "secret from his parents."
110 ""
110 "The crowds of agricultural labourers who had come out to the country in"
110 "his wake, dazzled by representations of easy independence, had"
110 "suffered, died, and wasted away. He would see mothers from English"
110 "farms trudging along with their infants in their arms, when the child"
110 "would be stricken with fever and would die; the mother would pause to"
110 "dig a hole in the loose earth with her bare hands, would bury the babe"
110 "therein with the same natural grave-tools, shed one tear, and again"
110 "trudge on."
110 ""
110 "Angel’s original intention had not been emigration to Brazil but a"
110 "northern or eastern farm in his own country. He had come to this place"
110 "in a fit of desperation, the Brazil movement among the English"
110 "agriculturists having by chance coincided with his desire to escape"
110 "from his past existence."
110 ""
110 "During this time of absence he had mentally aged a dozen years. What"
110 "arrested him now as of value in life was less its beauty than its"
110 "pathos. Having long discredited the old systems of mysticism, he now"
110 "began to discredit the old appraisements of morality. He thought they"
110 "wanted readjusting. Who was the moral man? Still more pertinently, who"
110 "was the moral woman? The beauty or ugliness of a character lay not only"
110 "in its achievements, but in its aims and impulses; its true history"
110 "lay, not among things done, but among things willed."
110 ""
110 "How, then, about Tess?"
110 ""
110 "Viewing her in these lights, a regret for his hasty judgement began to"
110 "oppress him. Did he reject her eternally, or did he not? He could no"
110 "longer say that he would always reject her, and not to say that was in"
110 "spirit to accept her now."
110 ""
110 "This growing fondness for her memory coincided in point of time with"
110 "her residence at Flintcomb-Ash, but it was before she had felt herself"
110 "at liberty to trouble him with a word about her circumstances or her"
110 "feelings. He was greatly perplexed; and in his perplexity as to her"
110 "motives in withholding intelligence, he did not inquire. Thus her"
110 "silence of docility was misinterpreted. How much it really said if he"
110 "had understood!—that she adhered with literal exactness to orders which"
110 "he had given and forgotten; that despite her natural fearlessness she"
110 "asserted no rights, admitted his judgement to be in every respect the"
110 "true one, and bent her head dumbly thereto."
110 ""
110 "In the before-mentioned journey by mules through the interior of the"
110 "country, another man rode beside him. Angel’s companion was also an"
110 "Englishman, bent on the same errand, though he came from another part"
110 "of the island. They were both in a state of mental depression, and they"
110 "spoke of home affairs. Confidence begat confidence. With that curious"
110 "tendency evinced by men, more especially when in distant lands, to"
110 "entrust to strangers details of their lives which they would on no"
110 "account mention to friends, Angel admitted to this man as they rode"
110 "along the sorrowful facts of his marriage."
110 ""
110 "The stranger had sojourned in many more lands and among many more"
110 "peoples than Angel; to his cosmopolitan mind such deviations from the"
110 "social norm, so immense to domesticity, were no more than are the"
110 "irregularities of vale and mountain-chain to the whole terrestrial"
110 "curve. He viewed the matter in quite a different light from Angel;"
110 "thought that what Tess had been was of no importance beside what she"
110 "would be, and plainly told Clare that he was wrong in coming away from"
110 "her."
110 ""
110 "The next day they were drenched in a thunder-storm. Angel’s companion"
110 "was struck down with fever, and died by the week’s end. Clare waited a"
110 "few hours to bury him, and then went on his way."
110 ""
110 "The cursory remarks of the large-minded stranger, of whom he knew"
110 "absolutely nothing beyond a commonplace name, were sublimed by his"
110 "death, and influenced Clare more than all the reasoned ethics of the"
110 "philosophers. His own parochialism made him ashamed by its contrast."
110 "His inconsistencies rushed upon him in a flood. He had persistently"
110 "elevated Hellenic Paganism at the expense of Christianity; yet in that"
110 "civilization an illegal surrender was not certain disesteem. Surely"
110 "then he might have regarded that abhorrence of the un-intact state,"
110 "which he had inherited with the creed of mysticism, as at least open to"
110 "correction when the result was due to treachery. A remorse struck into"
110 "him. The words of Izz Huett, never quite stilled in his memory, came"
110 "back to him. He had asked Izz if she loved him, and she had replied in"
110 "the affirmative. Did she love him more than Tess did? No, she had"
110 "replied; Tess would lay down her life for him, and she herself could do"
110 "no more."
110 ""
110 "He thought of Tess as she had appeared on the day of the wedding. How"
110 "her eyes had lingered upon him; how she had hung upon his words as if"
110 "they were a god’s! And during the terrible evening over the hearth,"
110 "when her simple soul uncovered itself to his, how pitiful her face had"
110 "looked by the rays of the fire, in her inability to realize that his"
110 "love and protection could possibly be withdrawn."
110 ""
110 "Thus from being her critic he grew to be her advocate. Cynical things"
110 "he had uttered to himself about her; but no man can be always a cynic"
110 "and live; and he withdrew them. The mistake of expressing them had"
110 "arisen from his allowing himself to be influenced by general principles"
110 "to the disregard of the particular instance."
110 ""
110 "But the reasoning is somewhat musty; lovers and husbands have gone over"
110 "the ground before to-day. Clare had been harsh towards her; there is no"
110 "doubt of it. Men are too often harsh with women they love or have"
110 "loved; women with men. And yet these harshnesses are tenderness itself"
110 "when compared with the universal harshness out of which they grow; the"
110 "harshness of the position towards the temperament, of the means towards"
110 "the aims, of to-day towards yesterday, of hereafter towards to-day."
110 ""
110 "The historic interest of her family—that masterful line of"
110 "d’Urbervilles—whom he had despised as a spent force, touched his"
110 "sentiments now. Why had he not known the difference between the"
110 "political value and the imaginative value of these things? In the"
110 "latter aspect her d’Urberville descent was a fact of great dimensions;"
110 "worthless to economics, it was a most useful ingredient to the dreamer,"
110 "to the moralizer on declines and falls. It was a fact that would soon"
110 "be forgotten—that bit of distinction in poor Tess’s blood and name, and"
110 "oblivion would fall upon her hereditary link with the marble monuments"
110 "and leaded skeletons at Kingsbere. So does Time ruthlessly destroy his"
110 "own romances. In recalling her face again and again, he thought now"
110 "that he could see therein a flash of the dignity which must have graced"
110 "her grand-dames; and the vision sent that _aura_ through his veins"
110 "which he had formerly felt, and which left behind it a sense of"
110 "sickness."
110 ""
110 "Despite her not inviolate past, what still abode in such a woman as"
110 "Tess outvalued the freshness of her fellows. Was not the gleaning of"
110 "the grapes of Ephraim better than the vintage of Abi-ezer?"
110 ""
110 "So spoke love renascent, preparing the way for Tess’s devoted"
110 "outpouring, which was then just being forwarded to him by his father;"
110 "though owing to his distance inland it was to be a long time in"
110 "reaching him."
110 ""
110 "Meanwhile the writer’s expectation that Angel would come in response to"
110 "the entreaty was alternately great and small. What lessened it was that"
110 "the facts of her life which had led to the parting had not"
110 "changed—could never change; and that, if her presence had not"
110 "attenuated them, her absence could not. Nevertheless she addressed her"
110 "mind to the tender question of what she could do to please him best if"
110 "he should arrive. Sighs were expended on the wish that she had taken"
110 "more notice of the tunes he played on his harp, that she had inquired"
110 "more curiously of him which were his favourite ballads among those the"
110 "country-girls sang. She indirectly inquired of Amby Seedling, who had"
110 "followed Izz from Talbothays, and by chance Amby remembered that,"
110 "amongst the snatches of melody in which they had indulged at the"
110 "dairyman’s, to induce the cows to let down their milk, Clare had seemed"
110 "to like “Cupid’s Gardens”, “I have parks, I have hounds”, and “The"
110 "break o’ the day”; and had seemed not to care for “The Tailor’s"
110 "Breeches” and “Such a beauty I did grow”, excellent ditties as they"
110 "were."
110 ""
110 "To perfect the ballads was now her whimsical desire. She practised them"
110 "privately at odd moments, especially “The break o’ the day”:"
110 ""
110 "    Arise, arise, arise!"
110 "    And pick your love a posy,"
110 "    All o’ the sweetest flowers"
110 "    That in the garden grow."
110 "    The turtle doves and sma’ birds"
110 "    In every bough a-building,"
110 "    So early in the May-time"
110 "    At the break o’ the day!"
110 ""
110 "It would have melted the heart of a stone to hear her singing these"
110 "ditties whenever she worked apart from the rest of the girls in this"
110 "cold dry time; the tears running down her cheeks all the while at the"
110 "thought that perhaps he would not, after all, come to hear her, and the"
110 "simple silly words of the songs resounding in painful mockery of the"
110 "aching heart of the singer."
110 ""
110 "Tess was so wrapt up in this fanciful dream that she seemed not to know"
110 "how the season was advancing; that the days had lengthened, that"
110 "Lady-Day was at hand, and would soon be followed by Old Lady-Day, the"
110 "end of her term here."
110 ""
110 "But before the quarter-day had quite come, something happened which"
110 "made Tess think of far different matters. She was at her lodging as"
110 "usual one evening, sitting in the downstairs room with the rest of the"
110 "family, when somebody knocked at the door and inquired for Tess."
110 "Through the doorway she saw against the declining light a figure with"
110 "the height of a woman and the breadth of a child, a tall, thin, girlish"
110 "creature whom she did not recognize in the twilight till the girl said"
110 "“Tess!”"
110 ""
110 "“What—is it ’Liza-Lu?” asked Tess, in startled accents. Her sister,"
110 "whom a little over a year ago she had left at home as a child, had"
110 "sprung up by a sudden shoot to a form of this presentation, of which as"
110 "yet Lu seemed herself scarce able to understand the meaning. Her thin"
110 "legs, visible below her once-long frock, now short by her growing, and"
110 "her uncomfortable hands and arms revealed her youth and inexperience."
110 ""
110 "“Yes, I have been traipsing about all day, Tess,” said Lu, with"
110 "unemotional gravity, “a-trying to find ’ee; and I’m very tired.”"
110 ""
110 "“What is the matter at home?”"
110 ""
110 "“Mother is took very bad, and the doctor says she’s dying, and as"
110 "father is not very well neither, and says ’tis wrong for a man of such"
110 "a high family as his to slave and drave at common labouring work, we"
110 "don’t know what to do.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess stood in reverie a long time before she thought of asking ’Liza-Lu"
110 "to come in and sit down. When she had done so, and ’Liza-Lu was having"
110 "some tea, she came to a decision. It was imperative that she should go"
110 "home. Her agreement did not end till Old Lady-Day, the sixth of April,"
110 "but as the interval thereto was not a long one she resolved to run the"
110 "risk of starting at once."
110 ""
110 "To go that night would be a gain of twelve-hours; but her sister was"
110 "too tired to undertake such a distance till the morrow. Tess ran down"
110 "to where Marian and Izz lived, informed them of what had happened, and"
110 "begged them to make the best of her case to the farmer. Returning, she"
110 "got Lu a supper, and after that, having tucked the younger into her own"
110 "bed, packed up as many of her belongings as would go into a withy"
110 "basket, and started, directing Lu to follow her next morning."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER L>"
110 ""
110 "She plunged into the chilly equinoctial darkness as the clock struck"
110 "ten, for her fifteen miles’ walk under the steely stars. In lonely"
110 "districts night is a protection rather than a danger to a noiseless"
110 "pedestrian, and knowing this, Tess pursued the nearest course along"
110 "by-lanes that she would almost have feared in the day-time; but"
110 "marauders were wanting now, and spectral fears were driven out of her"
110 "mind by thoughts of her mother. Thus she proceeded mile after mile,"
110 "ascending and descending till she came to Bulbarrow, and about midnight"
110 "looked from that height into the abyss of chaotic shade which was all"
110 "that revealed itself of the vale on whose further side she was born."
110 "Having already traversed about five miles on the upland, she had now"
110 "some ten or eleven in the lowland before her journey would be finished."
110 "The winding road downwards became just visible to her under the wan"
110 "starlight as she followed it, and soon she paced a soil so contrasting"
110 "with that above it that the difference was perceptible to the tread and"
110 "to the smell. It was the heavy clay land of Blackmoor Vale, and a part"
110 "of the Vale to which turnpike-roads had never penetrated. Superstitions"
110 "linger longest on these heavy soils. Having once been forest, at this"
110 "shadowy time it seemed to assert something of its old character, the"
110 "far and the near being blended, and every tree and tall hedge making"
110 "the most of its presence. The harts that had been hunted here, the"
110 "witches that had been pricked and ducked, the green-spangled fairies"
110 "that “whickered” at you as you passed;—the place teemed with beliefs in"
110 "them still, and they formed an impish multitude now."
110 ""
110 "At Nuttlebury she passed the village inn, whose sign creaked in"
110 "response to the greeting of her footsteps, which not a human soul heard"
110 "but herself. Under the thatched roofs her mind’s eye beheld relaxed"
110 "tendons and flaccid muscles, spread out in the darkness beneath"
110 "coverlets made of little purple patchwork squares, and undergoing a"
110 "bracing process at the hands of sleep for renewed labour on the morrow,"
110 "as soon as a hint of pink nebulosity appeared on Hambledon Hill."
110 ""
110 "At three she turned the last corner of the maze of lanes she had"
110 "threaded, and entered Marlott, passing the field in which as a"
110 "club-girl she had first seen Angel Clare, when he had not danced with"
110 "her; the sense of disappointment remained with her yet. In the"
110 "direction of her mother’s house she saw a light. It came from the"
110 "bedroom window, and a branch waved in front of it and made it wink at"
110 "her. As soon as she could discern the outline of the house—newly"
110 "thatched with her money—it had all its old effect upon Tess’s"
110 "imagination. Part of her body and life it ever seemed to be; the slope"
110 "of its dormers, the finish of its gables, the broken courses of brick"
110 "which topped the chimney, all had something in common with her personal"
110 "character. A stupefaction had come into these features, to her regard;"
110 "it meant the illness of her mother."
110 ""
110 "She opened the door so softly as to disturb nobody; the lower room was"
110 "vacant, but the neighbour who was sitting up with her mother came to"
110 "the top of the stairs, and whispered that Mrs Durbeyfield was no"
110 "better, though she was sleeping just then. Tess prepared herself a"
110 "breakfast, and then took her place as nurse in her mother’s chamber."
110 ""
110 "In the morning, when she contemplated the children, they had all a"
110 "curiously elongated look; although she had been away little more than a"
110 "year, their growth was astounding; and the necessity of applying"
110 "herself heart and soul to their needs took her out of her own cares."
110 ""
110 "Her father’s ill-health was the same indefinite kind, and he sat in his"
110 "chair as usual. But the day after her arrival he was unusually bright."
110 "He had a rational scheme for living, and Tess asked him what it was."
110 ""
110 "“I’m thinking of sending round to all the old antiqueerians in this"
110 "part of England,” he said, “asking them to subscribe to a fund to"
110 "maintain me. I’m sure they’d see it as a romantical, artistical, and"
110 "proper thing to do. They spend lots o’ money in keeping up old ruins,"
110 "and finding the bones o’ things, and such like; and living remains must"
110 "be more interesting to ’em still, if they only knowed of me. Would that"
110 "somebody would go round and tell ’em what there is living among ’em,"
110 "and they thinking nothing of him! If Pa’son Tringham, who discovered"
110 "me, had lived, he’d ha’ done it, I’m sure.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess postponed her arguments on this high project till she had grappled"
110 "with pressing matters in hand, which seemed little improved by her"
110 "remittances. When indoor necessities had been eased, she turned her"
110 "attention to external things. It was now the season for planting and"
110 "sowing; many gardens and allotments of the villagers had already"
110 "received their spring tillage; but the garden and the allotment of the"
110 "Durbeyfields were behindhand. She found, to her dismay, that this was"
110 "owing to their having eaten all the seed potatoes,—that last lapse of"
110 "the improvident. At the earliest moment she obtained what others she"
110 "could procure, and in a few days her father was well enough to see to"
110 "the garden, under Tess’s persuasive efforts: while she herself"
110 "undertook the allotment-plot which they rented in a field a couple of"
110 "hundred yards out of the village."
110 ""
110 "She liked doing it after the confinement of the sick chamber, where she"
110 "was not now required by reason of her mother’s improvement. Violent"
110 "motion relieved thought. The plot of ground was in a high, dry, open"
110 "enclosure, where there were forty or fifty such pieces, and where"
110 "labour was at its briskest when the hired labour of the day had ended."
110 "Digging began usually at six o’clock and extended indefinitely into the"
110 "dusk or moonlight. Just now heaps of dead weeds and refuse were burning"
110 "on many of the plots, the dry weather favouring their combustion."
110 ""
110 "One fine day Tess and ’Liza-Lu worked on here with their neighbours"
110 "till the last rays of the sun smote flat upon the white pegs that"
110 "divided the plots. As soon as twilight succeeded to sunset the flare of"
110 "the couch-grass and cabbage-stalk fires began to light up the"
110 "allotments fitfully, their outlines appearing and disappearing under"
110 "the dense smoke as wafted by the wind. When a fire glowed, banks of"
110 "smoke, blown level along the ground, would themselves become"
110 "illuminated to an opaque lustre, screening the workpeople from one"
110 "another; and the meaning of the “pillar of a cloud”, which was a wall"
110 "by day and a light by night, could be understood."
110 ""
110 "As evening thickened, some of the gardening men and women gave over for"
110 "the night, but the greater number remained to get their planting done,"
110 "Tess being among them, though she sent her sister home. It was on one"
110 "of the couch-burning plots that she laboured with her fork, its four"
110 "shining prongs resounding against the stones and dry clods in little"
110 "clicks. Sometimes she was completely involved in the smoke of her fire;"
110 "then it would leave her figure free, irradiated by the brassy glare"
110 "from the heap. She was oddly dressed to-night, and presented a somewhat"
110 "staring aspect, her attire being a gown bleached by many washings, with"
110 "a short black jacket over it, the effect of the whole being that of a"
110 "wedding and funeral guest in one. The women further back wore white"
110 "aprons, which, with their pale faces, were all that could be seen of"
110 "them in the gloom, except when at moments they caught a flash from the"
110 "flames."
110 ""
110 "Westward, the wiry boughs of the bare thorn hedge which formed the"
110 "boundary of the field rose against the pale opalescence of the lower"
110 "sky. Above, Jupiter hung like a full-blown jonquil, so bright as almost"
110 "to throw a shade. A few small nondescript stars were appearing"
110 "elsewhere. In the distance a dog barked, and wheels occasionally"
110 "rattled along the dry road."
110 ""
110 "Still the prongs continued to click assiduously, for it was not late;"
110 "and though the air was fresh and keen there was a whisper of spring in"
110 "it that cheered the workers on. Something in the place, the hours, the"
110 "crackling fires, the fantastic mysteries of light and shade, made"
110 "others as well as Tess enjoy being there. Nightfall, which in the frost"
110 "of winter comes as a fiend and in the warmth of summer as a lover, came"
110 "as a tranquillizer on this March day."
110 ""
110 "Nobody looked at his or her companions. The eyes of all were on the"
110 "soil as its turned surface was revealed by the fires. Hence as Tess"
110 "stirred the clods and sang her foolish little songs with scarce now a"
110 "hope that Clare would ever hear them, she did not for a long time"
110 "notice the person who worked nearest to her—a man in a long smockfrock"
110 "who, she found, was forking the same plot as herself, and whom she"
110 "supposed her father had sent there to advance the work. She became more"
110 "conscious of him when the direction of his digging brought him closer."
110 "Sometimes the smoke divided them; then it swerved, and the two were"
110 "visible to each other but divided from all the rest."
110 ""
110 "Tess did not speak to her fellow-worker, nor did he speak to her. Nor"
110 "did she think of him further than to recollect that he had not been"
110 "there when it was broad daylight, and that she did not know him as any"
110 "one of the Marlott labourers, which was no wonder, her absences having"
110 "been so long and frequent of late years. By-and-by he dug so close to"
110 "her that the fire-beams were reflected as distinctly from the steel"
110 "prongs of his fork as from her own. On going up to the fire to throw a"
110 "pitch of dead weeds upon it, she found that he did the same on the"
110 "other side. The fire flared up, and she beheld the face of"
110 "d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "The unexpectedness of his presence, the grotesqueness of his appearance"
110 "in a gathered smockfrock, such as was now worn only by the most"
110 "old-fashioned of the labourers, had a ghastly comicality that chilled"
110 "her as to its bearing. D’Urberville emitted a low, long laugh."
110 ""
110 "“If I were inclined to joke, I should say, How much this seems like"
110 "Paradise!” he remarked whimsically, looking at her with an inclined"
110 "head."
110 ""
110 "“What do you say?” she weakly asked."
110 ""
110 "“A jester might say this is just like Paradise. You are Eve, and I am"
110 "the old Other One come to tempt you in the disguise of an inferior"
110 "animal. I used to be quite up in that scene of Milton’s when I was"
110 "theological. Some of it goes—"
110 ""
110 "    ‘Empress, the way is ready, and not long,"
110 "    Beyond a row of myrtles...."
110 "    ... If thou accept"
110 "    My conduct, I can bring thee thither soon.’"
110 "    ‘Lead then,’ said Eve."
110 ""
110 "“And so on. My dear Tess, I am only putting this to you as a thing that"
110 "you might have supposed or said quite untruly, because you think so"
110 "badly of me.”"
110 ""
110 "“I never said you were Satan, or thought it. I don’t think of you in"
110 "that way at all. My thoughts of you are quite cold, except when you"
110 "affront me. What, did you come digging here entirely because of me?”"
110 ""
110 "“Entirely. To see you; nothing more. The smockfrock, which I saw"
110 "hanging for sale as I came along, was an afterthought, that I mightn’t"
110 "be noticed. I come to protest against your working like this.”"
110 ""
110 "“But I like doing it—it is for my father.”"
110 ""
110 "“Your engagement at the other place is ended?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes.”"
110 ""
110 "“Where are you going to next? To join your dear husband?”"
110 ""
110 "She could not bear the humiliating reminder."
110 ""
110 "“O—I don’t know!” she said bitterly. “I have no husband!”"
110 ""
110 "“It is quite true—in the sense you mean. But you have a friend, and I"
110 "have determined that you shall be comfortable in spite of yourself."
110 "When you get down to your house you will see what I have sent there for"
110 "you.”"
110 ""
110 "“O, Alec, I wish you wouldn’t give me anything at all! I cannot take it"
110 "from you! I don’t like—it is not right!”"
110 ""
110 "“It _is_ right!” he cried lightly. “I am not going to see a woman whom"
110 "I feel so tenderly for as I do for you in trouble without trying to"
110 "help her.”"
110 ""
110 "“But I am very well off! I am only in trouble about—about—not about"
110 "living at all!”"
110 ""
110 "She turned, and desperately resumed her digging, tears dripping upon"
110 "the fork-handle and upon the clods."
110 ""
110 "“About the children—your brothers and sisters,” he resumed. “I’ve been"
110 "thinking of them.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess’s heart quivered—he was touching her in a weak place. He had"
110 "divined her chief anxiety. Since returning home her soul had gone out"
110 "to those children with an affection that was passionate."
110 ""
110 "“If your mother does not recover, somebody ought to do something for"
110 "them; since your father will not be able to do much, I suppose?”"
110 ""
110 "“He can with my assistance. He must!”"
110 ""
110 "“And with mine.”"
110 ""
110 "“No, sir!”"
110 ""
110 "“How damned foolish this is!” burst out d’Urberville. “Why, he thinks"
110 "we are the same family; and will be quite satisfied!”"
110 ""
110 "“He don’t. I’ve undeceived him.”"
110 ""
110 "“The more fool you!”"
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville in anger retreated from her to the hedge, where he pulled"
110 "off the long smockfrock which had disguised him; and rolling it up and"
110 "pushing it into the couch-fire, went away."
110 ""
110 "Tess could not get on with her digging after this; she felt restless;"
110 "she wondered if he had gone back to her father’s house; and taking the"
110 "fork in her hand proceeded homewards."
110 ""
110 "Some twenty yards from the house she was met by one of her sisters."
110 ""
110 "“O, Tessy—what do you think! ’Liza-Lu is a-crying, and there’s a lot of"
110 "folk in the house, and mother is a good deal better, but they think"
110 "father is dead!”"
110 ""
110 "The child realized the grandeur of the news; but not as yet its"
110 "sadness, and stood looking at Tess with round-eyed importance till,"
110 "beholding the effect produced upon her, she said—"
110 ""
110 "“What, Tess, shan’t we talk to father never no more?”"
110 ""
110 "“But father was only a little bit ill!” exclaimed Tess distractedly."
110 ""
110 "’Liza-Lu came up."
110 ""
110 "“He dropped down just now, and the doctor who was there for mother said"
110 "there was no chance for him, because his heart was growed in.”"
110 ""
110 "Yes; the Durbeyfield couple had changed places; the dying one was out"
110 "of danger, and the indisposed one was dead. The news meant even more"
110 "than it sounded. Her father’s life had a value apart from his personal"
110 "achievements, or perhaps it would not have had much. It was the last of"
110 "the three lives for whose duration the house and premises were held"
110 "under a lease; and it had long been coveted by the tenant-farmer for"
110 "his regular labourers, who were stinted in cottage accommodation."
110 "Moreover, “liviers” were disapproved of in villages almost as much as"
110 "little freeholders, because of their independence of manner, and when a"
110 "lease determined it was never renewed."
110 ""
110 "Thus the Durbeyfields, once d’Urbervilles, saw descending upon them the"
110 "destiny which, no doubt, when they were among the Olympians of the"
110 "county, they had caused to descend many a time, and severely enough,"
110 "upon the heads of such landless ones as they themselves were now. So do"
110 "flux and reflux—the rhythm of change—alternate and persist in"
110 "everything under the sky."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER LI>"
110 ""
110 "At length it was the eve of Old Lady-Day, and the agricultural world"
110 "was in a fever of mobility such as only occurs at that particular date"
110 "of the year. It is a day of fulfilment; agreements for outdoor service"
110 "during the ensuing year, entered into at Candlemas, are to be now"
110 "carried out. The labourers—or “work-folk”, as they used to call"
110 "themselves immemorially till the other word was introduced from"
110 "without—who wish to remain no longer in old places are removing to the"
110 "new farms."
110 ""
110 "These annual migrations from farm to farm were on the increase here."
110 "When Tess’s mother was a child the majority of the field-folk about"
110 "Marlott had remained all their lives on one farm, which had been the"
110 "home also of their fathers and grandfathers; but latterly the desire"
110 "for yearly removal had risen to a high pitch. With the younger families"
110 "it was a pleasant excitement which might possibly be an advantage. The"
110 "Egypt of one family was the Land of Promise to the family who saw it"
110 "from a distance, till by residence there it became in turn their Egypt"
110 "also; and so they changed and changed."
110 ""
110 "However, all the mutations so increasingly discernible in village life"
110 "did not originate entirely in the agricultural unrest. A depopulation"
110 "was also going on. The village had formerly contained, side by side"
110 "with the argicultural labourers, an interesting and better-informed"
110 "class, ranking distinctly above the former—the class to which Tess’s"
110 "father and mother had belonged—and including the carpenter, the smith,"
110 "the shoemaker, the huckster, together with nondescript workers other"
110 "than farm-labourers; a set of people who owed a certain stability of"
110 "aim and conduct to the fact of their being lifeholders like Tess’s"
110 "father, or copyholders, or occasionally, small freeholders. But as the"
110 "long holdings fell in, they were seldom again let to similar tenants,"
110 "and were mostly pulled down, if not absolutely required by the farmer"
110 "for his hands. Cottagers who were not directly employed on the land"
110 "were looked upon with disfavour, and the banishment of some starved the"
110 "trade of others, who were thus obliged to follow. These families, who"
110 "had formed the backbone of the village life in the past, who were the"
110 "depositaries of the village traditions, had to seek refuge in the large"
110 "centres; the process, humorously designated by statisticians as “the"
110 "tendency of the rural population towards the large towns”, being really"
110 "the tendency of water to flow uphill when forced by machinery."
110 ""
110 "The cottage accommodation at Marlott having been in this manner"
110 "considerably curtailed by demolitions, every house which remained"
110 "standing was required by the agriculturist for his work-people. Ever"
110 "since the occurrence of the event which had cast such a shadow over"
110 "Tess’s life, the Durbeyfield family (whose descent was not credited)"
110 "had been tacitly looked on as one which would have to go when their"
110 "lease ended, if only in the interests of morality. It was, indeed,"
110 "quite true that the household had not been shining examples either of"
110 "temperance, soberness, or chastity. The father, and even the mother,"
110 "had got drunk at times, the younger children seldom had gone to church,"
110 "and the eldest daughter had made queer unions. By some means the"
110 "village had to be kept pure. So on this, the first Lady-Day on which"
110 "the Durbeyfields were expellable, the house, being roomy, was required"
110 "for a carter with a large family; and Widow Joan, her daughters Tess"
110 "and ’Liza-Lu, the boy Abraham, and the younger children had to go"
110 "elsewhere."
110 ""
110 "On the evening preceding their removal it was getting dark betimes by"
110 "reason of a drizzling rain which blurred the sky. As it was the last"
110 "night they would spend in the village which had been their home and"
110 "birthplace, Mrs Durbeyfield, ’Liza-Lu, and Abraham had gone out to bid"
110 "some friends goodbye, and Tess was keeping house till they should"
110 "return."
110 ""
110 "She was kneeling in the window-bench, her face close to the casement,"
110 "where an outer pane of rain-water was sliding down the inner pane of"
110 "glass. Her eyes rested on the web of a spider, probably starved long"
110 "ago, which had been mistakenly placed in a corner where no flies ever"
110 "came, and shivered in the slight draught through the casement. Tess was"
110 "reflecting on the position of the household, in which she perceived her"
110 "own evil influence. Had she not come home, her mother and the children"
110 "might probably have been allowed to stay on as weekly tenants. But she"
110 "had been observed almost immediately on her return by some people of"
110 "scrupulous character and great influence: they had seen her idling in"
110 "the churchyard, restoring as well as she could with a little trowel a"
110 "baby’s obliterated grave. By this means they had found that she was"
110 "living here again; her mother was scolded for “harbouring” her; sharp"
110 "retorts had ensued from Joan, who had independently offered to leave at"
110 "once; she had been taken at her word; and here was the result."
110 ""
110 "“I ought never to have come home,” said Tess to herself, bitterly."
110 ""
110 "She was so intent upon these thoughts that she hardly at first took"
110 "note of a man in a white mackintosh whom she saw riding down the"
110 "street. Possibly it was owing to her face being near to the pane that"
110 "he saw her so quickly, and directed his horse so close to the"
110 "cottage-front that his hoofs were almost upon the narrow border for"
110 "plants growing under the wall. It was not till he touched the window"
110 "with his riding-crop that she observed him. The rain had nearly ceased,"
110 "and she opened the casement in obedience to his gesture."
110 ""
110 "“Didn’t you see me?” asked d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "“I was not attending,” she said. “I heard you, I believe, though I"
110 "fancied it was a carriage and horses. I was in a sort of dream.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah! you heard the d’Urberville Coach, perhaps. You know the legend, I"
110 "suppose?”"
110 ""
110 "“No. My—somebody was going to tell it me once, but didn’t.”"
110 ""
110 "“If you are a genuine d’Urberville I ought not to tell you either, I"
110 "suppose. As for me, I’m a sham one, so it doesn’t matter. It is rather"
110 "dismal. It is that this sound of a non-existent coach can only be heard"
110 "by one of d’Urberville blood, and it is held to be of ill-omen to the"
110 "one who hears it. It has to do with a murder, committed by one of the"
110 "family, centuries ago.”"
110 ""
110 "“Now you have begun it, finish it.”"
110 ""
110 "“Very well. One of the family is said to have abducted some beautiful"
110 "woman, who tried to escape from the coach in which he was carrying her"
110 "off, and in the struggle he killed her—or she killed him—I forget"
110 "which. Such is one version of the tale... I see that your tubs and"
110 "buckets are packed. Going away, aren’t you?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, to-morrow—Old Lady Day.”"
110 ""
110 "“I heard you were, but could hardly believe it; it seems so sudden. Why"
110 "is it?”"
110 ""
110 "“Father’s was the last life on the property, and when that dropped we"
110 "had no further right to stay. Though we might, perhaps, have stayed as"
110 "weekly tenants—if it had not been for me.”"
110 ""
110 "“What about you?”"
110 ""
110 "“I am not a—proper woman.”"
110 ""
110 "D’Urberville’s face flushed."
110 ""
110 "“What a blasted shame! Miserable snobs! May their dirty souls be burnt"
110 "to cinders!” he exclaimed in tones of ironic resentment. “That’s why"
110 "you are going, is it? Turned out?”"
110 ""
110 "“We are not turned out exactly; but as they said we should have to go"
110 "soon, it was best to go now everybody was moving, because there are"
110 "better chances.”"
110 ""
110 "“Where are you going to?”"
110 ""
110 "“Kingsbere. We have taken rooms there. Mother is so foolish about"
110 "father’s people that she will go there.”"
110 ""
110 "“But your mother’s family are not fit for lodgings, and in a little"
110 "hole of a town like that. Now why not come to my garden-house at"
110 "Trantridge? There are hardly any poultry now, since my mother’s death;"
110 "but there’s the house, as you know it, and the garden. It can be"
110 "whitewashed in a day, and your mother can live there quite comfortably;"
110 "and I will put the children to a good school. Really I ought to do"
110 "something for you!”"
110 ""
110 "“But we have already taken the rooms at Kingsbere!” she declared. “And"
110 "we can wait there—”"
110 ""
110 "“Wait—what for? For that nice husband, no doubt. Now look here, Tess, I"
110 "know what men are, and, bearing in mind the _grounds_ of your"
110 "separation, I am quite positive he will never make it up with you. Now,"
110 "though I have been your enemy, I am your friend, even if you won’t"
110 "believe it. Come to this cottage of mine. We’ll get up a regular colony"
110 "of fowls, and your mother can attend to them excellently; and the"
110 "children can go to school.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess breathed more and more quickly, and at length she said—"
110 ""
110 "“How do I know that you would do all this? Your views may change—and"
110 "then—we should be—my mother would be—homeless again.”"
110 ""
110 "“O no—no. I would guarantee you against such as that in writing, if"
110 "necessary. Think it over.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess shook her head. But d’Urberville persisted; she had seldom seen"
110 "him so determined; he would not take a negative."
110 ""
110 "“Please just tell your mother,” he said, in emphatic tones. “It is her"
110 "business to judge—not yours. I shall get the house swept out and"
110 "whitened to-morrow morning, and fires lit; and it will be dry by the"
110 "evening, so that you can come straight there. Now mind, I shall expect"
110 "you.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess again shook her head, her throat swelling with complicated"
110 "emotion. She could not look up at d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "“I owe you something for the past, you know,” he resumed. “And you"
110 "cured me, too, of that craze; so I am glad—”"
110 ""
110 "“I would rather you had kept the craze, so that you had kept the"
110 "practice which went with it!”"
110 ""
110 "“I am glad of this opportunity of repaying you a little. To-morrow I"
110 "shall expect to hear your mother’s goods unloading... Give me your hand"
110 "on it now—dear, beautiful Tess!”"
110 ""
110 "With the last sentence he had dropped his voice to a murmur, and put"
110 "his hand in at the half-open casement. With stormy eyes she pulled the"
110 "stay-bar quickly, and, in doing so, caught his arm between the casement"
110 "and the stone mullion."
110 ""
110 "“Damnation—you are very cruel!” he said, snatching out his arm. “No,"
110 "no!—I know you didn’t do it on purpose. Well, I shall expect you, or"
110 "your mother and children at least.”"
110 ""
110 "“I shall not come—I have plenty of money!” she cried."
110 ""
110 "“Where?”"
110 ""
110 "“At my father-in-law’s, if I ask for it.”"
110 ""
110 "“_If_ you ask for it. But you won’t, Tess; I know you; you’ll never ask"
110 "for it—you’ll starve first!”"
110 ""
110 "With these words he rode off. Just at the corner of the street he met"
110 "the man with the paint-pot, who asked him if he had deserted the"
110 "brethren."
110 ""
110 "“You go to the devil!” said d’Urberville."
110 ""
110 "Tess remained where she was a long while, till a sudden rebellious"
110 "sense of injustice caused the region of her eyes to swell with the rush"
110 "of hot tears thither. Her husband, Angel Clare himself, had, like"
110 "others, dealt out hard measure to her; surely he had! She had never"
110 "before admitted such a thought; but he had surely! Never in her"
110 "life—she could swear it from the bottom of her soul—had she ever"
110 "intended to do wrong; yet these hard judgements had come. Whatever her"
110 "sins, they were not sins of intention, but of inadvertence, and why"
110 "should she have been punished so persistently?"
110 ""
110 "She passionately seized the first piece of paper that came to hand, and"
110 "scribbled the following lines:"
110 ""
110 "    O why have you treated me so monstrously, Angel! I do not deserve"
110 "    it. I have thought it all over carefully, and I can never, never"
110 "    forgive you! You know that I did not intend to wrong you—why have"
110 "    you so wronged me? You are cruel, cruel indeed! I will try to"
110 "    forget you. It is all injustice I have received at your hands!"
110 ""
110 "            T."
110 ""
110 "She watched till the postman passed by, ran out to him with her"
110 "epistle, and then again took her listless place inside the"
110 "window-panes."
110 ""
110 "It was just as well to write like that as to write tenderly. How could"
110 "he give way to entreaty? The facts had not changed: there was no new"
110 "event to alter his opinion."
110 ""
110 "It grew darker, the fire-light shining over the room. The two biggest"
110 "of the younger children had gone out with their mother; the four"
110 "smallest, their ages ranging from three-and-a-half years to eleven, all"
110 "in black frocks, were gathered round the hearth babbling their own"
110 "little subjects. Tess at length joined them, without lighting a candle."
110 ""
110 "“This is the last night that we shall sleep here, dears, in the house"
110 "where we were born,” she said quickly. “We ought to think of it,"
110 "oughtn’t we?”"
110 ""
110 "They all became silent; with the impressibility of their age they were"
110 "ready to burst into tears at the picture of finality she had conjured"
110 "up, though all the day hitherto they had been rejoicing in the idea of"
110 "a new place. Tess changed the subject."
110 ""
110 "“Sing to me, dears,” she said."
110 ""
110 "“What shall we sing?”"
110 ""
110 "“Anything you know; I don’t mind.”"
110 ""
110 "There was a momentary pause; it was broken, first, in one little"
110 "tentative note; then a second voice strengthened it, and a third and a"
110 "fourth chimed in unison, with words they had learnt at the"
110 "Sunday-school—"
110 ""
110 "    Here we suffer grief and pain,"
110 "    Here we meet to part again;"
110 "    In Heaven we part no more."
110 ""
110 "The four sang on with the phlegmatic passivity of persons who had long"
110 "ago settled the question, and there being no mistake about it, felt"
110 "that further thought was not required. With features strained hard to"
110 "enunciate the syllables they continued to regard the centre of the"
110 "flickering fire, the notes of the youngest straying over into the"
110 "pauses of the rest."
110 ""
110 "Tess turned from them, and went to the window again. Darkness had now"
110 "fallen without, but she put her face to the pane as though to peer into"
110 "the gloom. It was really to hide her tears. If she could only believe"
110 "what the children were singing; if she were only sure, how different"
110 "all would now be; how confidently she would leave them to Providence"
110 "and their future kingdom! But, in default of that, it behoved her to do"
110 "something; to be their Providence; for to Tess, as to not a few"
110 "millions of others, there was ghastly satire in the poet’s lines—"
110 ""
110 "    Not in utter nakedness"
110 "    But trailing clouds of glory do we come."
110 ""
110 "To her and her like, birth itself was an ordeal of degrading personal"
110 "compulsion, whose gratuitousness nothing in the result seemed to"
110 "justify, and at best could only palliate."
110 ""
110 "In the shades of the wet road she soon discerned her mother with tall"
110 "’Liza-Lu and Abraham. Mrs Durbeyfield’s pattens clicked up to the door,"
110 "and Tess opened it."
110 ""
110 "“I see the tracks of a horse outside the window,” said Joan. “Hev"
110 "somebody called?”"
110 ""
110 "“No,” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "The children by the fire looked gravely at her, and one murmured—"
110 ""
110 "“Why, Tess, the gentleman a-horseback!”"
110 ""
110 "“He didn’t call,” said Tess. “He spoke to me in passing.”"
110 ""
110 "“Who was the gentleman?” asked the mother. “Your husband?”"
110 ""
110 "“No. He’ll never, never come,” answered Tess in stony hopelessness."
110 ""
110 "“Then who was it?”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh, you needn’t ask. You’ve seen him before, and so have I.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah! What did he say?” said Joan curiously."
110 ""
110 "“I will tell you when we are settled in our lodging at Kingsbere"
110 "to-morrow—every word.”"
110 ""
110 "It was not her husband, she had said. Yet a consciousness that in a"
110 "physical sense this man alone was her husband seemed to weigh on her"
110 "more and more."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER LII>"
110 ""
110 "During the small hours of the next morning, while it was still dark,"
110 "dwellers near the highways were conscious of a disturbance of their"
110 "night’s rest by rumbling noises, intermittently continuing till"
110 "daylight—noises as certain to recur in this particular first week of"
110 "the month as the voice of the cuckoo in the third week of the same."
110 "They were the preliminaries of the general removal, the passing of the"
110 "empty waggons and teams to fetch the goods of the migrating families;"
110 "for it was always by the vehicle of the farmer who required his"
110 "services that the hired man was conveyed to his destination. That this"
110 "might be accomplished within the day was the explanation of the"
110 "reverberation occurring so soon after midnight, the aim of the carters"
110 "being to reach the door of the outgoing households by six o’clock, when"
110 "the loading of their movables at once began."
110 ""
110 "But to Tess and her mother’s household no such anxious farmer sent his"
110 "team. They were only women; they were not regular labourers; they were"
110 "not particularly required anywhere; hence they had to hire a waggon at"
110 "their own expense, and got nothing sent gratuitously."
110 ""
110 "It was a relief to Tess, when she looked out of the window that"
110 "morning, to find that though the weather was windy and louring, it did"
110 "not rain, and that the waggon had come. A wet Lady-Day was a spectre"
110 "which removing families never forgot; damp furniture, damp bedding,"
110 "damp clothing accompanied it, and left a train of ills."
110 ""
110 "Her mother, ’Liza-Lu, and Abraham were also awake, but the younger"
110 "children were let sleep on. The four breakfasted by the thin light, and"
110 "the “house-ridding” was taken in hand."
110 ""
110 "It proceeded with some cheerfulness, a friendly neighbour or two"
110 "assisting. When the large articles of furniture had been packed in"
110 "position, a circular nest was made of the beds and bedding, in which"
110 "Joan Durbeyfield and the young children were to sit through the"
110 "journey. After loading there was a long delay before the horses were"
110 "brought, these having been unharnessed during the ridding; but at"
110 "length, about two o’clock, the whole was under way, the cooking-pot"
110 "swinging from the axle of the waggon, Mrs Durbeyfield and family at the"
110 "top, the matron having in her lap, to prevent injury to its works, the"
110 "head of the clock, which, at any exceptional lurch of the waggon,"
110 "struck one, or one-and-a-half, in hurt tones. Tess and the next eldest"
110 "girl walked alongside till they were out of the village."
110 ""
110 "They had called on a few neighbours that morning and the previous"
110 "evening, and some came to see them off, all wishing them well, though,"
110 "in their secret hearts, hardly expecting welfare possible to such a"
110 "family, harmless as the Durbeyfields were to all except themselves."
110 "Soon the equipage began to ascend to higher ground, and the wind grew"
110 "keener with the change of level and soil."
110 ""
110 "The day being the sixth of April, the Durbeyfield waggon met many other"
110 "waggons with families on the summit of the load, which was built on a"
110 "wellnigh unvarying principle, as peculiar, probably, to the rural"
110 "labourer as the hexagon to the bee. The groundwork of the arrangement"
110 "was the family dresser, which, with its shining handles, and"
110 "finger-marks, and domestic evidences thick upon it, stood importantly"
110 "in front, over the tails of the shaft-horses, in its erect and natural"
110 "position, like some Ark of the Covenant that they were bound to carry"
110 "reverently."
110 ""
110 "Some of the households were lively, some mournful; some were stopping"
110 "at the doors of wayside inns; where, in due time, the Durbeyfield"
110 "menagerie also drew up to bait horses and refresh the travellers."
110 ""
110 "During the halt Tess’s eyes fell upon a three-pint blue mug, which was"
110 "ascending and descending through the air to and from the feminine"
110 "section of a household, sitting on the summit of a load that had also"
110 "drawn up at a little distance from the same inn. She followed one of"
110 "the mug’s journeys upward, and perceived it to be clasped by hands"
110 "whose owner she well knew. Tess went towards the waggon."
110 ""
110 "“Marian and Izz!” she cried to the girls, for it was they, sitting with"
110 "the moving family at whose house they had lodged. “Are you"
110 "house-ridding to-day, like everybody else?”"
110 ""
110 "They were, they said. It had been too rough a life for them at"
110 "Flintcomb-Ash, and they had come away, almost without notice, leaving"
110 "Groby to prosecute them if he chose. They told Tess their destination,"
110 "and Tess told them hers."
110 ""
110 "Marian leant over the load, and lowered her voice. “Do you know that"
110 "the gentleman who follows ’ee—you’ll guess who I mean—came to ask for"
110 "’ee at Flintcomb after you had gone? We didn’t tell’n where you was,"
110 "knowing you wouldn’t wish to see him.”"
110 ""
110 "“Ah—but I did see him!” Tess murmured. “He found me.”"
110 ""
110 "“And do he know where you be going?”"
110 ""
110 "“I think so.”"
110 ""
110 "“Husband come back?”"
110 ""
110 "“No.”"
110 ""
110 "She bade her acquaintance goodbye—for the respective carters had now"
110 "come out from the inn—and the two waggons resumed their journey in"
110 "opposite directions; the vehicle whereon sat Marian, Izz, and the"
110 "ploughman’s family with whom they had thrown in their lot, being"
110 "brightly painted, and drawn by three powerful horses with shining brass"
110 "ornaments on their harness; while the waggon on which Mrs Durbeyfield"
110 "and her family rode was a creaking erection that would scarcely bear"
110 "the weight of the superincumbent load; one which had known no paint"
110 "since it was made, and drawn by two horses only. The contrast well"
110 "marked the difference between being fetched by a thriving farmer and"
110 "conveying oneself whither no hirer waited one’s coming."
110 ""
110 "The distance was great—too great for a day’s journey—and it was with"
110 "the utmost difficulty that the horses performed it. Though they had"
110 "started so early, it was quite late in the afternoon when they turned"
110 "the flank of an eminence which formed part of the upland called"
110 "Greenhill. While the horses stood to stale and breathe themselves Tess"
110 "looked around. Under the hill, and just ahead of them, was the"
110 "half-dead townlet of their pilgrimage, Kingsbere, where lay those"
110 "ancestors of whom her father had spoken and sung to painfulness:"
110 "Kingsbere, the spot of all spots in the world which could be considered"
110 "the d’Urbervilles’ home, since they had resided there for full five"
110 "hundred years."
110 ""
110 "A man could be seen advancing from the outskirts towards them, and when"
110 "he beheld the nature of their waggon-load he quickened his steps."
110 ""
110 "“You be the woman they call Mrs Durbeyfield, I reckon?” he said to"
110 "Tess’s mother, who had descended to walk the remainder of the way."
110 ""
110 "She nodded. “Though widow of the late Sir John d’Urberville, poor"
110 "nobleman, if I cared for my rights; and returning to the domain of his"
110 "forefathers.”"
110 ""
110 "“Oh? Well, I know nothing about that; but if you be Mrs Durbeyfield, I"
110 "am sent to tell ’ee that the rooms you wanted be let. We didn’t know"
110 "that you was coming till we got your letter this morning—when ’twas too"
110 "late. But no doubt you can get other lodgings somewhere.”"
110 ""
110 "The man had noticed the face of Tess, which had become ash-pale at his"
110 "intelligence. Her mother looked hopelessly at fault. “What shall we do"
110 "now, Tess?” she said bitterly. “Here’s a welcome to your ancestors’"
110 "lands! However, let’s try further.”"
110 ""
110 "They moved on into the town, and tried with all their might, Tess"
110 "remaining with the waggon to take care of the children whilst her"
110 "mother and ’Liza-Lu made inquiries. At the last return of Joan to the"
110 "vehicle, an hour later, when her search for accommodation had still"
110 "been fruitless, the driver of the waggon said the goods must be"
110 "unloaded, as the horses were half-dead, and he was bound to return part"
110 "of the way at least that night."
110 ""
110 "“Very well—unload it here,” said Joan recklessly. “I’ll get shelter"
110 "somewhere.”"
110 ""
110 "The waggon had drawn up under the churchyard wall, in a spot screened"
110 "from view, and the driver, nothing loth, soon hauled down the poor heap"
110 "of household goods. This done, she paid him, reducing herself to almost"
110 "her last shilling thereby, and he moved off and left them, only too"
110 "glad to get out of further dealings with such a family. It was a dry"
110 "night, and he guessed that they would come to no harm."
110 ""
110 "Tess gazed desperately at the pile of furniture. The cold sunlight of"
110 "this spring evening peered invidiously upon the crocks and kettles,"
110 "upon the bunches of dried herbs shivering in the breeze, upon the brass"
110 "handles of the dresser, upon the wicker-cradle they had all been rocked"
110 "in, and upon the well-rubbed clock-case, all of which gave out the"
110 "reproachful gleam of indoor articles abandoned to the vicissitudes of a"
110 "roofless exposure for which they were never made. Round about were"
110 "deparked hills and slopes—now cut up into little paddocks—and the green"
110 "foundations that showed where the d’Urberville mansion once had stood;"
110 "also an outlying stretch of Egdon Heath that had always belonged to the"
110 "estate. Hard by, the aisle of the church called the d’Urberville Aisle"
110 "looked on imperturbably."
110 ""
110 "“Isn’t your family vault your own freehold?” said Tess’s mother, as she"
110 "returned from a reconnoitre of the church and graveyard. “Why, of"
110 "course ’tis, and that’s where we will camp, girls, till the place of"
110 "your ancestors finds us a roof! Now, Tess and ’Liza and Abraham, you"
110 "help me. We’ll make a nest for these children, and then we’ll have"
110 "another look round.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess listlessly lent a hand, and in a quarter of an hour the old"
110 "four-post bedstead was dissociated from the heap of goods, and erected"
110 "under the south wall of the church, the part of the building known as"
110 "the d’Urberville Aisle, beneath which the huge vaults lay. Over the"
110 "tester of the bedstead was a beautiful traceried window, of many"
110 "lights, its date being the fifteenth century. It was called the"
110 "d’Urberville Window, and in the upper part could be discerned heraldic"
110 "emblems like those on Durbeyfield’s old seal and spoon."
110 ""
110 "Joan drew the curtains round the bed so as to make an excellent tent of"
110 "it, and put the smaller children inside. “If it comes to the worst we"
110 "can sleep there too, for one night,” she said. “But let us try further"
110 "on, and get something for the dears to eat! O, Tess, what’s the use of"
110 "your playing at marrying gentlemen, if it leaves us like this!”"
110 ""
110 "Accompanied by ’Liza-Lu and the boy, she again ascended the little lane"
110 "which secluded the church from the townlet. As soon as they got into"
110 "the street they beheld a man on horseback gazing up and down. “Ah—I’m"
110 "looking for you!” he said, riding up to them. “This is indeed a family"
110 "gathering on the historic spot!”"
110 ""
110 "It was Alec d’Urberville. “Where is Tess?” he asked."
110 ""
110 "Personally Joan had no liking for Alec. She cursorily signified the"
110 "direction of the church, and went on, d’Urberville saying that he would"
110 "see them again, in case they should be still unsuccessful in their"
110 "search for shelter, of which he had just heard. When they had gone,"
110 "d’Urberville rode to the inn, and shortly after came out on foot."
110 ""
110 "In the interim Tess, left with the children inside the bedstead,"
110 "remained talking with them awhile, till, seeing that no more could be"
110 "done to make them comfortable just then, she walked about the"
110 "churchyard, now beginning to be embrowned by the shades of nightfall."
110 "The door of the church was unfastened, and she entered it for the first"
110 "time in her life."
110 ""
110 "Within the window under which the bedstead stood were the tombs of the"
110 "family, covering in their dates several centuries. They were canopied,"
110 "altar-shaped, and plain; their carvings being defaced and broken; their"
110 "brasses torn from the matrices, the rivet-holes remaining like"
110 "martin-holes in a sandcliff. Of all the reminders that she had ever"
110 "received that her people were socially extinct, there was none so"
110 "forcible as this spoliation."
110 ""
110 "She drew near to a dark stone on which was inscribed:"
110 ""
110 "    OSTIUM SEPULCHRI ANTIQUAE FAMILIAE D’URBERVILLE"
110 ""
110 "Tess did not read Church-Latin like a Cardinal, but she knew that this"
110 "was the door of her ancestral sepulchre, and that the tall knights of"
110 "whom her father had chanted in his cups lay inside."
110 ""
110 "She musingly turned to withdraw, passing near an altar-tomb, the oldest"
110 "of them all, on which was a recumbent figure. In the dusk she had not"
110 "noticed it before, and would hardly have noticed it now but for an odd"
110 "fancy that the effigy moved. As soon as she drew close to it she"
110 "discovered all in a moment that the figure was a living person; and the"
110 "shock to her sense of not having been alone was so violent that she was"
110 "quite overcome, and sank down nigh to fainting, not, however, till she"
110 "had recognized Alec d’Urberville in the form."
110 ""
110 "He leapt off the slab and supported her."
110 ""
110 "“I saw you come in,” he said smiling, “and got up there not to"
110 "interrupt your meditations. A family gathering, is it not, with these"
110 "old fellows under us here? Listen.”"
110 ""
110 "He stamped with his heel heavily on the floor; whereupon there arose a"
110 "hollow echo from below."
110 ""
110 "“That shook them a bit, I’ll warrant!” he continued. “And you thought I"
110 "was the mere stone reproduction of one of them. But no. The old order"
110 "changeth. The little finger of the sham d’Urberville can do more for"
110 "you than the whole dynasty of the real underneath.... Now command me."
110 "What shall I do?”"
110 ""
110 "“Go away!” she murmured."
110 ""
110 "“I will—I’ll look for your mother,” said he blandly. But in passing her"
110 "he whispered: “Mind this; you’ll be civil yet!”"
110 ""
110 "When he was gone she bent down upon the entrance to the vaults, and"
110 "said—"
110 ""
110 "“Why am I on the wrong side of this door!”"
110 ""
110 "In the meantime Marian and Izz Huett had journeyed onward with the"
110 "chattels of the ploughman in the direction of their land of Canaan—the"
110 "Egypt of some other family who had left it only that morning. But the"
110 "girls did not for a long time think of where they were going. Their"
110 "talk was of Angel Clare and Tess, and Tess’s persistent lover, whose"
110 "connection with her previous history they had partly heard and partly"
110 "guessed ere this."
110 ""
110 "“’Tisn’t as though she had never known him afore,” said Marian. “His"
110 "having won her once makes all the difference in the world. ’Twould be a"
110 "thousand pities if he were to tole her away again. Mr Clare can never"
110 "be anything to us, Izz; and why should we grudge him to her, and not"
110 "try to mend this quarrel? If he could on’y know what straits she’s put"
110 "to, and what’s hovering round, he might come to take care of his own.”"
110 ""
110 "“Could we let him know?”"
110 ""
110 "They thought of this all the way to their destination; but the bustle"
110 "of re-establishment in their new place took up all their attention"
110 "then. But when they were settled, a month later, they heard of Clare’s"
110 "approaching return, though they had learnt nothing more of Tess. Upon"
110 "that, agitated anew by their attachment to him, yet honourably disposed"
110 "to her, Marian uncorked the penny ink-bottle they shared, and a few"
110 "lines were concocted between the two girls."
110 ""
110 "    Honour’d Sir,"
110 ""
110 "    Look to your Wife if you do love her as much as she do love you."
110 "    For she is sore put to by an Enemy in the shape of a Friend. Sir,"
110 "    there is one near her who ought to be Away. A woman should not be"
110 "    try’d beyond her Strength, and continual dropping will wear away a"
110 "    Stone—ay, more—a Diamond."
110 ""
110 "    From Two Well-Wishers"
110 ""
110 "This was addressed to Angel Clare at the only place they had ever heard"
110 "him to be connected with, Emminster Vicarage; after which they"
110 "continued in a mood of emotional exaltation at their own generosity,"
110 "which made them sing in hysterical snatches and weep at the same time."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "End of Phase the Sixth"

110 "<CHAPTER LIII>"
110 ""
110 "It was evening at Emminster Vicarage. The two customary candles were"
110 "burning under their green shades in the Vicar’s study, but he had not"
110 "been sitting there. Occasionally he came in, stirred the small fire"
110 "which sufficed for the increasing mildness of the spring, and went out"
110 "again; sometimes pausing at the front door, going on to the"
110 "drawing-room, then returning again to the front door."
110 ""
110 "It faced westward, and though gloom prevailed inside, there was still"
110 "light enough without to see with distinctness. Mrs Clare, who had been"
110 "sitting in the drawing-room, followed him hither."
110 ""
110 "“Plenty of time yet,” said the Vicar. “He doesn’t reach Chalk-Newton"
110 "till six, even if the train should be punctual, and ten miles of"
110 "country-road, five of them in Crimmercrock Lane, are not jogged over in"
110 "a hurry by our old horse.”"
110 ""
110 "“But he has done it in an hour with us, my dear.”"
110 ""
110 "“Years ago.”"
110 ""
110 "Thus they passed the minutes, each well knowing that this was only"
110 "waste of breath, the one essential being simply to wait."
110 ""
110 "At length there was a slight noise in the lane, and the old pony-chaise"
110 "appeared indeed outside the railings. They saw alight therefrom a form"
110 "which they affected to recognize, but would actually have passed by in"
110 "the street without identifying had he not got out of their carriage at"
110 "the particular moment when a particular person was due."
110 ""
110 "Mrs Clare rushed through the dark passage to the door, and her husband"
110 "came more slowly after her."
110 ""
110 "The new arrival, who was just about to enter, saw their anxious faces"
110 "in the doorway and the gleam of the west in their spectacles because"
110 "they confronted the last rays of day; but they could only see his shape"
110 "against the light."
110 ""
110 "“O, my boy, my boy—home again at last!” cried Mrs Clare, who cared no"
110 "more at that moment for the stains of heterodoxy which had caused all"
110 "this separation than for the dust upon his clothes. What woman, indeed,"
110 "among the most faithful adherents of the truth, believes the promises"
110 "and threats of the Word in the sense in which she believes in her own"
110 "children, or would not throw her theology to the wind if weighed"
110 "against their happiness? As soon as they reached the room where the"
110 "candles were lighted she looked at his face."
110 ""
110 "“O, it is not Angel—not my son—the Angel who went away!” she cried in"
110 "all the irony of sorrow, as she turned herself aside."
110 ""
110 "His father, too, was shocked to see him, so reduced was that figure"
110 "from its former contours by worry and the bad season that Clare had"
110 "experienced, in the climate to which he had so rashly hurried in his"
110 "first aversion to the mockery of events at home. You could see the"
110 "skeleton behind the man, and almost the ghost behind the skeleton. He"
110 "matched Crivelli’s dead _Christus_. His sunken eye-pits were of morbid"
110 "hue, and the light in his eyes had waned. The angular hollows and lines"
110 "of his aged ancestors had succeeded to their reign in his face twenty"
110 "years before their time."
110 ""
110 "“I was ill over there, you know,” he said. “I am all right now.”"
110 ""
110 "As if, however, to falsify this assertion, his legs seemed to give way,"
110 "and he suddenly sat down to save himself from falling. It was only a"
110 "slight attack of faintness, resulting from the tedious day’s journey,"
110 "and the excitement of arrival."
110 ""
110 "“Has any letter come for me lately?” he asked. “I received the last you"
110 "sent on by the merest chance, and after considerable delay through"
110 "being inland; or I might have come sooner.”"
110 ""
110 "“It was from your wife, we supposed?”"
110 ""
110 "“It was.”"
110 ""
110 "Only one other had recently come. They had not sent it on to him,"
110 "knowing he would start for home so soon."
110 ""
110 "He hastily opened the letter produced, and was much disturbed to read"
110 "in Tess’s handwriting the sentiments expressed in her last hurried"
110 "scrawl to him."
110 ""
110 "    O why have you treated me so monstrously, Angel! I do not deserve"
110 "    it. I have thought it all over carefully, and I can never, never"
110 "    forgive you! You know that I did not intend to wrong you—why have"
110 "    you so wronged me? You are cruel, cruel indeed! I will try to"
110 "    forget you. It is all injustice I have received at your hands!"
110 ""
110 "            T."
110 ""
110 "“It is quite true!” said Angel, throwing down the letter. “Perhaps she"
110 "will never be reconciled to me!”"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t, Angel, be so anxious about a mere child of the soil!” said his"
110 "mother."
110 ""
110 "“Child of the soil! Well, we all are children of the soil. I wish she"
110 "were so in the sense you mean; but let me now explain to you what I"
110 "have never explained before, that her father is a descendant in the"
110 "male line of one of the oldest Norman houses, like a good many others"
110 "who lead obscure agricultural lives in our villages, and are dubbed"
110 "‘sons of the soil.’”"
110 ""
110 "He soon retired to bed; and the next morning, feeling exceedingly"
110 "unwell, he remained in his room pondering. The circumstances amid which"
110 "he had left Tess were such that though, while on the south of the"
110 "Equator and just in receipt of her loving epistle, it had seemed the"
110 "easiest thing in the world to rush back into her arms the moment he"
110 "chose to forgive her, now that he had arrived it was not so easy as it"
110 "had seemed. She was passionate, and her present letter, showing that"
110 "her estimate of him had changed under his delay—too justly changed, he"
110 "sadly owned,—made him ask himself if it would be wise to confront her"
110 "unannounced in the presence of her parents. Supposing that her love had"
110 "indeed turned to dislike during the last weeks of separation, a sudden"
110 "meeting might lead to bitter words."
110 ""
110 "Clare therefore thought it would be best to prepare Tess and her family"
110 "by sending a line to Marlott announcing his return, and his hope that"
110 "she was still living with them there, as he had arranged for her to do"
110 "when he left England. He despatched the inquiry that very day, and"
110 "before the week was out there came a short reply from Mrs Durbeyfield"
110 "which did not remove his embarrassment, for it bore no address, though"
110 "to his surprise it was not written from Marlott."
110 ""
110 "    Sir,"
110 ""
110 "    J write these few lines to say that my Daughter is away from me at"
110 "    present, and J am not sure when she will return, but J will let you"
110 "    know as Soon as she do. J do not feel at liberty to tell you Where"
110 "    she is temperly biding. J should say that me and my Family have"
110 "    left Marlott for some Time.—"
110 ""
110 "    Yours,"
110 ""
110 "    J. Durbeyfield"
110 ""
110 "It was such a relief to Clare to learn that Tess was at least"
110 "apparently well that her mother’s stiff reticence as to her whereabouts"
110 "did not long distress him. They were all angry with him, evidently. He"
110 "would wait till Mrs Durbeyfield could inform him of Tess’s return,"
110 "which her letter implied to be soon. He deserved no more. His had been"
110 "a love “which alters when it alteration finds”. He had undergone some"
110 "strange experiences in his absence; he had seen the virtual Faustina in"
110 "the literal Cornelia, a spiritual Lucretia in a corporeal Phryne; he"
110 "had thought of the woman taken and set in the midst as one deserving to"
110 "be stoned, and of the wife of Uriah being made a queen; and he had"
110 "asked himself why he had not judged Tess constructively rather than"
110 "biographically, by the will rather than by the deed?"
110 ""
110 "A day or two passed while he waited at his father’s house for the"
110 "promised second note from Joan Durbeyfield, and indirectly to recover a"
110 "little more strength. The strength showed signs of coming back, but"
110 "there was no sign of Joan’s letter. Then he hunted up the old letter"
110 "sent on to him in Brazil, which Tess had written from Flintcomb-Ash,"
110 "and re-read it. The sentences touched him now as much as when he had"
110 "first perused them."
110 ""
110 "...I must cry to you in my trouble—I have no one else!... I think I"
110 "must die if you do not come soon, or tell me to come to you... please,"
110 "please, not to be just—only a little kind to me.... If you would come,"
110 "I could die in your arms! I would be well content to do that if so be"
110 "you had forgiven me!... if you will send me one little line, and say,"
110 "_I am coming soon_, I will bide on, Angel—O, so cheerfully!... think"
110 "how it do hurt my heart not to see you ever—ever! Ah, if I could only"
110 "make your dear heart ache one little minute of each day as mine does"
110 "every day and all day long, it might lead you to show pity to your poor"
110 "lonely one.... I would be content, ay, glad, to live with you as your"
110 "servant, if I may not as your wife; so that I could only be near you,"
110 "and get glimpses of you, and think of you as mine.... I long for only"
110 "one thing in heaven or earth or under the earth, to meet you, my own"
110 "dear! Come to me—come to me, and save me from what threatens me!"
110 ""
110 ""
110 "Clare determined that he would no longer believe in her more recent and"
110 "severer regard of him, but would go and find her immediately. He asked"
110 "his father if she had applied for any money during his absence. His"
110 "father returned a negative, and then for the first time it occurred to"
110 "Angel that her pride had stood in her way, and that she had suffered"
110 "privation. From his remarks his parents now gathered the real reason of"
110 "the separation; and their Christianity was such that, reprobates being"
110 "their especial care, the tenderness towards Tess which her blood, her"
110 "simplicity, even her poverty, had not engendered, was instantly excited"
110 "by her sin."
110 ""
110 "Whilst he was hastily packing together a few articles for his journey"
110 "he glanced over a poor plain missive also lately come to hand—the one"
110 "from Marian and Izz Huett, beginning—"
110 ""
110 "“Honour’d Sir, Look to your Wife if you do love her as much as she do"
110 "love you,” and signed, “From Two Well-Wishers.”"
