110 "<CHAPTER LIV>"
110 ""
110 "In a quarter of an hour Clare was leaving the house, whence his mother"
110 "watched his thin figure as it disappeared into the street. He had"
110 "declined to borrow his father’s old mare, well knowing of its necessity"
110 "to the household. He went to the inn, where he hired a trap, and could"
110 "hardly wait during the harnessing. In a very few minutes after, he was"
110 "driving up the hill out of the town which, three or four months earlier"
110 "in the year, Tess had descended with such hopes and ascended with such"
110 "shattered purposes."
110 ""
110 "Benvill Lane soon stretched before him, its hedges and trees purple"
110 "with buds; but he was looking at other things, and only recalled"
110 "himself to the scene sufficiently to enable him to keep the way. In"
110 "something less than an hour-and-a-half he had skirted the south of the"
110 "King’s Hintock estates and ascended to the untoward solitude of"
110 "Cross-in-Hand, the unholy stone whereon Tess had been compelled by Alec"
110 "d’Urberville, in his whim of reformation, to swear the strange oath"
110 "that she would never wilfully tempt him again. The pale and blasted"
110 "nettle-stems of the preceding year even now lingered nakedly in the"
110 "banks, young green nettles of the present spring growing from their"
110 "roots."
110 ""
110 "Thence he went along the verge of the upland overhanging the other"
110 "Hintocks, and, turning to the right, plunged into the bracing"
110 "calcareous region of Flintcomb-Ash, the address from which she had"
110 "written to him in one of the letters, and which he supposed to be the"
110 "place of sojourn referred to by her mother. Here, of course, he did not"
110 "find her; and what added to his depression was the discovery that no"
110 "“Mrs Clare” had ever been heard of by the cottagers or by the farmer"
110 "himself, though Tess was remembered well enough by her Christian name."
110 "His name she had obviously never used during their separation, and her"
110 "dignified sense of their total severance was shown not much less by"
110 "this abstention than by the hardships she had chosen to undergo (of"
110 "which he now learnt for the first time) rather than apply to his father"
110 "for more funds."
110 ""
110 "From this place they told him Tess Durbeyfield had gone, without due"
110 "notice, to the home of her parents on the other side of Blackmoor, and"
110 "it therefore became necessary to find Mrs Durbeyfield. She had told him"
110 "she was not now at Marlott, but had been curiously reticent as to her"
110 "actual address, and the only course was to go to Marlott and inquire"
110 "for it. The farmer who had been so churlish with Tess was quite"
110 "smooth-tongued to Clare, and lent him a horse and man to drive him"
110 "towards Marlott, the gig he had arrived in being sent back to"
110 "Emminster; for the limit of a day’s journey with that horse was"
110 "reached."
110 ""
110 "Clare would not accept the loan of the farmer’s vehicle for a further"
110 "distance than to the outskirts of the Vale, and, sending it back with"
110 "the man who had driven him, he put up at an inn, and next day entered"
110 "on foot the region wherein was the spot of his dear Tess’s birth. It"
110 "was as yet too early in the year for much colour to appear in the"
110 "gardens and foliage; the so-called spring was but winter overlaid with"
110 "a thin coat of greenness, and it was of a parcel with his expectations."
110 ""
110 "The house in which Tess had passed the years of her childhood was now"
110 "inhabited by another family who had never known her. The new residents"
110 "were in the garden, taking as much interest in their own doings as if"
110 "the homestead had never passed its primal time in conjunction with the"
110 "histories of others, beside which the histories of these were but as a"
110 "tale told by an idiot. They walked about the garden paths with thoughts"
110 "of their own concerns entirely uppermost, bringing their actions at"
110 "every moment in jarring collision with the dim ghosts behind them,"
110 "talking as though the time when Tess lived there were not one whit"
110 "intenser in story than now. Even the spring birds sang over their heads"
110 "as if they thought there was nobody missing in particular."
110 ""
110 "On inquiry of these precious innocents, to whom even the name of their"
110 "predecessors was a failing memory, Clare learned that John Durbeyfield"
110 "was dead; that his widow and children had left Marlott, declaring that"
110 "they were going to live at Kingsbere, but instead of doing so had gone"
110 "on to another place they mentioned. By this time Clare abhorred the"
110 "house for ceasing to contain Tess, and hastened away from its hated"
110 "presence without once looking back."
110 ""
110 "His way was by the field in which he had first beheld her at the dance."
110 "It was as bad as the house—even worse. He passed on through the"
110 "churchyard, where, amongst the new headstones, he saw one of a somewhat"
110 "superior design to the rest. The inscription ran thus:"
110 ""
110 "    In memory of John Durbeyfield, rightly d’Urberville, of the once"
110 "    powerful family of that Name, and Direct Descendant through an"
110 "    illustrious Line from Sir Pagan d’Urberville, one of the Knights of"
110 "    the Conqueror. Died March 10th, 18—"
110 ""
110 "    How are the Mighty Fallen."
110 ""
110 "Some man, apparently the sexton, had observed Clare standing there, and"
110 "drew nigh. “Ah, sir, now that’s a man who didn’t want to lie here, but"
110 "wished to be carried to Kingsbere, where his ancestors be.”"
110 ""
110 "“And why didn’t they respect his wish?”"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“Ah, who put it up?”"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "The distance was too long for a walk, but Clare felt such a strong"

110 ""

110 ""
110 "“I admit it. Where is she staying?”"
110 ""

110 "<CHAPTER LV>"
110 ""
110 "At eleven o’clock that night, having secured a bed at one of the hotels"
110 "and telegraphed his address to his father immediately on his arrival,"
110 "he walked out into the streets of Sandbourne. It was too late to call"
110 "on or inquire for any one, and he reluctantly postponed his purpose"
110 "till the morning. But he could not retire to rest just yet."
110 ""
110 "This fashionable watering-place, with its eastern and its western"
110 "stations, its piers, its groves of pines, its promenades, and its"
110 "covered gardens, was, to Angel Clare, like a fairy place suddenly"
110 "created by the stroke of a wand, and allowed to get a little dusty. An"
110 "outlying eastern tract of the enormous Egdon Waste was close at hand,"
110 "yet on the very verge of that tawny piece of antiquity such a"
110 "glittering novelty as this pleasure city had chosen to spring up."
110 "Within the space of a mile from its outskirts every irregularity of the"
110 "soil was prehistoric, every channel an undisturbed British trackway;"
110 "not a sod having been turned there since the days of the Cæsars. Yet"
110 "the exotic had grown here, suddenly as the prophet’s gourd; and had"
110 "drawn hither Tess."
110 ""
110 "By the midnight lamps he went up and down the winding way of this new"
110 "world in an old one, and could discern between the trees and against"
110 "the stars the lofty roofs, chimneys, gazebos, and towers of the"
110 "numerous fanciful residences of which the place was composed. It was a"
110 "city of detached mansions; a Mediterranean lounging-place on the"
110 "English Channel; and as seen now by night it seemed even more imposing"
110 "than it was."
110 ""
110 "The sea was near at hand, but not intrusive; it murmured, and he"
110 "thought it was the pines; the pines murmured in precisely the same"
110 "tones, and he thought they were the sea."
110 ""
110 "Where could Tess possibly be, a cottage-girl, his young wife, amidst"
110 "all this wealth and fashion? The more he pondered, the more was he"
110 "puzzled. Were there any cows to milk here? There certainly were no"
110 "fields to till. She was most probably engaged to do something in one of"
110 "these large houses; and he sauntered along, looking at the"
110 "chamber-windows and their lights going out one by one, and wondered"
110 "which of them might be hers."
110 ""
110 "Conjecture was useless, and just after twelve o’clock he entered and"
110 "went to bed. Before putting out his light he re-read Tess’s impassioned"
110 "letter. Sleep, however, he could not—so near her, yet so far from"
110 "her—and he continually lifted the window-blind and regarded the backs"
110 "of the opposite houses, and wondered behind which of the sashes she"
110 "reposed at that moment."
110 ""
110 "He might almost as well have sat up all night. In the morning he arose"
110 "at seven, and shortly after went out, taking the direction of the chief"
110 "post-office. At the door he met an intelligent postman coming out with"
110 "letters for the morning delivery."
110 ""
110 "“Do you know the address of a Mrs Clare?” asked Angel. The postman"
110 "shook his head."
110 ""
110 "Then, remembering that she would have been likely to continue the use"
110 "of her maiden name, Clare said—"
110 ""
110 "“Of a Miss Durbeyfield?”"
110 ""
110 "“Durbeyfield?”"
110 ""
110 "This also was strange to the postman addressed."
110 ""
110 "“There’s visitors coming and going every day, as you know, sir,” he"
110 "said; “and without the name of the house ’tis impossible to find ’em.”"
110 ""
110 "One of his comrades hastening out at that moment, the name was repeated"
110 "to him."
110 ""
110 "“I know no name of Durbeyfield; but there is the name of d’Urberville"
110 "at The Herons,” said the second."
110 ""
110 "“That’s it!” cried Clare, pleased to think that she had reverted to the"
110 "real pronunciation. “What place is The Herons?”"
110 ""
110 "“A stylish lodging-house. ’Tis all lodging-houses here, bless ’ee.”"
110 ""
110 "Clare received directions how to find the house, and hastened thither,"
110 "arriving with the milkman. The Herons, though an ordinary villa, stood"
110 "in its own grounds, and was certainly the last place in which one would"
110 "have expected to find lodgings, so private was its appearance. If poor"
110 "Tess was a servant here, as he feared, she would go to the back-door to"
110 "that milkman, and he was inclined to go thither also. However, in his"
110 "doubts he turned to the front, and rang."
110 ""
110 "The hour being early, the landlady herself opened the door. Clare"
110 "inquired for Teresa d’Urberville or Durbeyfield."
110 ""
110 "“Mrs d’Urberville?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes.”"
110 ""
110 "Tess, then, passed as a married woman, and he felt glad, even though"
110 "she had not adopted his name."
110 ""
110 "“Will you kindly tell her that a relative is anxious to see her?”"
110 ""
110 "“It is rather early. What name shall I give, sir?”"
110 ""
110 "“Angel.”"
110 ""
110 "“Mr Angel?”"
110 ""
110 "“No; Angel. It is my Christian name. She’ll understand.”"
110 ""
110 "“I’ll see if she is awake.”"
110 ""
110 "He was shown into the front room—the dining-room—and looked out through"
110 "the spring curtains at the little lawn, and the rhododendrons and other"
110 "shrubs upon it. Obviously her position was by no means so bad as he had"
110 "feared, and it crossed his mind that she must somehow have claimed and"
110 "sold the jewels to attain it. He did not blame her for one moment. Soon"
110 "his sharpened ear detected footsteps upon the stairs, at which his"
110 "heart thumped so painfully that he could hardly stand firm. “Dear me!"
110 "what will she think of me, so altered as I am!” he said to himself; and"
110 "the door opened."
110 ""
110 "Tess appeared on the threshold—not at all as he had expected to see"
110 "her—bewilderingly otherwise, indeed. Her great natural beauty was, if"
110 "not heightened, rendered more obvious by her attire. She was loosely"
110 "wrapped in a cashmere dressing-gown of gray-white, embroidered in"
110 "half-mourning tints, and she wore slippers of the same hue. Her neck"
110 "rose out of a frill of down, and her well-remembered cable of"
110 "dark-brown hair was partially coiled up in a mass at the back of her"
110 "head and partly hanging on her shoulder—the evident result of haste."
110 ""
110 "He had held out his arms, but they had fallen again to his side; for"
110 "she had not come forward, remaining still in the opening of the"
110 "doorway. Mere yellow skeleton that he was now, he felt the contrast"
110 "between them, and thought his appearance distasteful to her."
110 ""
110 "“Tess!” he said huskily, “can you forgive me for going away? Can’t"
110 "you—come to me? How do you get to be—like this?”"
110 ""
110 "“It is too late,” said she, her voice sounding hard through the room,"
110 "her eyes shining unnaturally."
110 ""
110 "“I did not think rightly of you—I did not see you as you were!” he"
110 "continued to plead. “I have learnt to since, dearest Tessy mine!”"
110 ""
110 "“Too late, too late!” she said, waving her hand in the impatience of a"
110 "person whose tortures cause every instant to seem an hour. “Don’t come"
110 "close to me, Angel! No—you must not. Keep away.”"
110 ""
110 "“But don’t you love me, my dear wife, because I have been so pulled"
110 "down by illness? You are not so fickle—I am come on purpose for you—my"
110 "mother and father will welcome you now!”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes—O, yes, yes! But I say, I say it is too late.”"
110 ""
110 "She seemed to feel like a fugitive in a dream, who tries to move away,"
110 "but cannot. “Don’t you know all—don’t you know it? Yet how do you come"
110 "here if you do not know?”"
110 ""
110 "“I inquired here and there, and I found the way.”"
110 ""
110 "“I waited and waited for you,” she went on, her tones suddenly resuming"
110 "their old fluty pathos. “But you did not come! And I wrote to you, and"
110 "you did not come! He kept on saying you would never come any more, and"
110 "that I was a foolish woman. He was very kind to me, and to mother, and"
110 "to all of us after father’s death. He—”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t understand.”"
110 ""
110 "“He has won me back to him.”"
110 ""
110 "Clare looked at her keenly, then, gathering her meaning, flagged like"
110 "one plague-stricken, and his glance sank; it fell on her hands, which,"
110 "once rosy, were now white and more delicate."
110 ""
110 "She continued—"
110 ""
110 "“He is upstairs. I hate him now, because he told me a lie—that you"
110 "would not come again; and you _have_ come! These clothes are what he’s"
110 "put upon me: I didn’t care what he did wi’ me! But—will you go away,"
110 "Angel, please, and never come any more?”"
110 ""
110 "They stood fixed, their baffled hearts looking out of their eyes with a"
110 "joylessness pitiful to see. Both seemed to implore something to shelter"
110 "them from reality."
110 ""
110 "“Ah—it is my fault!” said Clare."
110 ""
110 "But he could not get on. Speech was as inexpressive as silence. But he"
110 "had a vague consciousness of one thing, though it was not clear to him"
110 "till later; that his original Tess had spiritually ceased to recognize"
110 "the body before him as hers—allowing it to drift, like a corpse upon"
110 "the current, in a direction dissociated from its living will."
110 ""
110 "A few instants passed, and he found that Tess was gone. His face grew"
110 "colder and more shrunken as he stood concentrated on the moment, and a"
110 "minute or two after, he found himself in the street, walking along he"
110 "did not know whither."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER LVI>"
110 ""
110 "Mrs Brooks, the lady who was the householder at The Herons and owner of"
110 "all the handsome furniture, was not a person of an unusually curious"
110 "turn of mind. She was too deeply materialized, poor woman, by her long"
110 "and enforced bondage to that arithmetical demon Profit-and-Loss, to"
110 "retain much curiousity for its own sake, and apart from possible"
110 "lodgers’ pockets. Nevertheless, the visit of Angel Clare to her"
110 "well-paying tenants, Mr and Mrs d’Urberville, as she deemed them, was"
110 "sufficiently exceptional in point of time and manner to reinvigorate"
110 "the feminine proclivity which had been stifled down as useless save in"
110 "its bearings to the letting trade."
110 ""
110 "Tess had spoken to her husband from the doorway, without entering the"
110 "dining-room, and Mrs Brooks, who stood within the partly-closed door of"
110 "her own sitting-room at the back of the passage, could hear fragments"
110 "of the conversation—if conversation it could be called—between those"
110 "two wretched souls. She heard Tess re-ascend the stairs to the first"
110 "floor, and the departure of Clare, and the closing of the front door"
110 "behind him. Then the door of the room above was shut, and Mrs Brooks"
110 "knew that Tess had re-entered her apartment. As the young lady was not"
110 "fully dressed, Mrs Brooks knew that she would not emerge again for some"
110 "time."
110 ""
110 "She accordingly ascended the stairs softly, and stood at the door of"
110 "the front room—a drawing-room, connected with the room immediately"
110 "behind it (which was a bedroom) by folding-doors in the common manner."
110 "This first floor, containing Mrs Brooks’s best apartments, had been"
110 "taken by the week by the d’Urbervilles. The back room was now in"
110 "silence; but from the drawing-room there came sounds."
110 ""
110 "All that she could at first distinguish of them was one syllable,"
110 "continually repeated in a low note of moaning, as if it came from a"
110 "soul bound to some Ixionian wheel—"
110 ""
110 "“O—O—O!”"
110 ""
110 "Then a silence, then a heavy sigh, and again—"
110 ""
110 "“O—O—O!”"
110 ""
110 "The landlady looked through the keyhole. Only a small space of the room"
110 "inside was visible, but within that space came a corner of the"
110 "breakfast table, which was already spread for the meal, and also a"
110 "chair beside. Over the seat of the chair Tess’s face was bowed, her"
110 "posture being a kneeling one in front of it; her hands were clasped"
110 "over her head, the skirts of her dressing-gown and the embroidery of"
110 "her night-gown flowed upon the floor behind her, and her stockingless"
110 "feet, from which the slippers had fallen, protruded upon the carpet. It"
110 "was from her lips that came the murmur of unspeakable despair."
110 ""
110 "Then a man’s voice from the adjoining bedroom—"
110 ""
110 "“What’s the matter?”"
110 ""
110 "She did not answer, but went on, in a tone which was a soliloquy rather"
110 "than an exclamation, and a dirge rather than a soliloquy. Mrs Brooks"
110 "could only catch a portion:"
110 ""
110 "“And then my dear, dear husband came home to me ... and I did not know"
110 "it!... And you had used your cruel persuasion upon me ... you did not"
110 "stop using it—no—you did not stop! My little sisters and brothers and"
110 "my mother’s needs—they were the things you moved me by ... and you said"
110 "my husband would never come back—never; and you taunted me, and said"
110 "what a simpleton I was to expect him!... And at last I believed you and"
110 "gave way!... And then he came back! Now he is gone. Gone a second time,"
110 "and I have lost him now for ever ... and he will not love me the"
110 "littlest bit ever any more—only hate me!... O yes, I have lost him"
110 "now—again because of—you!” In writhing, with her head on the chair, she"
110 "turned her face towards the door, and Mrs Brooks could see the pain"
110 "upon it, and that her lips were bleeding from the clench of her teeth"
110 "upon them, and that the long lashes of her closed eyes stuck in wet"
110 "tags to her cheeks. She continued: “And he is dying—he looks as if he"
110 "is dying!... And my sin will kill him and not kill me!... O, you have"
110 "torn my life all to pieces ... made me be what I prayed you in pity not"
110 "to make me be again!... My own true husband will never, never—O God—I"
110 "can’t bear this!—I cannot!”"
110 ""
110 "There were more and sharper words from the man; then a sudden rustle;"
110 "she had sprung to her feet. Mrs Brooks, thinking that the speaker was"
110 "coming to rush out of the door, hastily retreated down the stairs."
110 ""
110 "She need not have done so, however, for the door of the sitting-room"
110 "was not opened. But Mrs Brooks felt it unsafe to watch on the landing"
110 "again, and entered her own parlour below."
110 ""
110 "She could hear nothing through the floor, although she listened"
110 "intently, and thereupon went to the kitchen to finish her interrupted"
110 "breakfast. Coming up presently to the front room on the ground floor"
110 "she took up some sewing, waiting for her lodgers to ring that she might"
110 "take away the breakfast, which she meant to do herself, to discover"
110 "what was the matter if possible. Overhead, as she sat, she could now"
110 "hear the floorboards slightly creak, as if some one were walking about,"
110 "and presently the movement was explained by the rustle of garments"
110 "against the banisters, the opening and the closing of the front door,"
110 "and the form of Tess passing to the gate on her way into the street."
110 "She was fully dressed now in the walking costume of a well-to-do young"
110 "lady in which she had arrived, with the sole addition that over her hat"
110 "and black feathers a veil was drawn."
110 ""
110 "Mrs Brooks had not been able to catch any word of farewell, temporary"
110 "or otherwise, between her tenants at the door above. They might have"
110 "quarrelled, or Mr d’Urberville might still be asleep, for he was not an"
110 "early riser."
110 ""
110 "She went into the back room, which was more especially her own"
110 "apartment, and continued her sewing there. The lady lodger did not"
110 "return, nor did the gentleman ring his bell. Mrs Brooks pondered on the"
110 "delay, and on what probable relation the visitor who had called so"
110 "early bore to the couple upstairs. In reflecting she leant back in her"
110 "chair."
110 ""
110 "As she did so her eyes glanced casually over the ceiling till they were"
110 "arrested by a spot in the middle of its white surface which she had"
110 "never noticed there before. It was about the size of a wafer when she"
110 "first observed it, but it speedily grew as large as the palm of her"
110 "hand, and then she could perceive that it was red. The oblong white"
110 "ceiling, with this scarlet blot in the midst, had the appearance of a"
110 "gigantic ace of hearts."
110 ""
110 "Mrs Brooks had strange qualms of misgiving. She got upon the table, and"
110 "touched the spot in the ceiling with her fingers. It was damp, and she"
110 "fancied that it was a blood stain."
110 ""
110 "Descending from the table, she left the parlour, and went upstairs,"
110 "intending to enter the room overhead, which was the bedchamber at the"
110 "back of the drawing-room. But, nerveless woman as she had now become,"
110 "she could not bring herself to attempt the handle. She listened. The"
110 "dead silence within was broken only by a regular beat."
110 ""
110 "Drip, drip, drip."
110 ""
110 "Mrs Brooks hastened downstairs, opened the front door, and ran into the"
110 "street. A man she knew, one of the workmen employed at an adjoining"
110 "villa, was passing by, and she begged him to come in and go upstairs"
110 "with her; she feared something had happened to one of her lodgers. The"
110 "workman assented, and followed her to the landing."
110 ""
110 "She opened the door of the drawing-room, and stood back for him to pass"
110 "in, entering herself behind him. The room was empty; the breakfast—a"
110 "substantial repast of coffee, eggs, and a cold ham—lay spread upon the"
110 "table untouched, as when she had taken it up, excepting that the"
110 "carving-knife was missing. She asked the man to go through the"
110 "folding-doors into the adjoining room."
110 ""
110 "He opened the doors, entered a step or two, and came back almost"
110 "instantly with a rigid face. “My good God, the gentleman in bed is"
110 "dead! I think he has been hurt with a knife—a lot of blood has run down"
110 "upon the floor!”"
110 ""
110 "The alarm was soon given, and the house which had lately been so quiet"
110 "resounded with the tramp of many footsteps, a surgeon among the rest."
110 "The wound was small, but the point of the blade had touched the heart"
110 "of the victim, who lay on his back, pale, fixed, dead, as if he had"
110 "scarcely moved after the infliction of the blow. In a quarter of an"
110 "hour the news that a gentleman who was a temporary visitor to the town"
110 "had been stabbed in his bed, spread through every street and villa of"
110 "the popular watering-place."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER LVII>"
110 ""
110 "Meanwhile Angel Clare had walked automatically along the way by which"
110 "he had come, and, entering his hotel, sat down over the breakfast,"
110 "staring at nothingness. He went on eating and drinking unconsciously"
110 "till on a sudden he demanded his bill; having paid which, he took his"
110 "dressing-bag in his hand, the only luggage he had brought with him, and"
110 "went out."
110 ""
110 "At the moment of his departure a telegram was handed to him—a few words"
110 "from his mother, stating that they were glad to know his address, and"
110 "informing him that his brother Cuthbert had proposed to and been"
110 "accepted by Mercy Chant."
110 ""
110 "Clare crumpled up the paper and followed the route to the station;"
110 "reaching it, he found that there would be no train leaving for an hour"
110 "and more. He sat down to wait, and having waited a quarter of an hour"
110 "felt that he could wait there no longer. Broken in heart and numbed, he"
110 "had nothing to hurry for; but he wished to get out of a town which had"
110 "been the scene of such an experience, and turned to walk to the first"
110 "station onward, and let the train pick him up there."
110 ""
110 "The highway that he followed was open, and at a little distance dipped"
110 "into a valley, across which it could be seen running from edge to edge."
110 "He had traversed the greater part of this depression, and was climbing"
110 "the western acclivity when, pausing for breath, he unconsciously looked"
110 "back. Why he did so he could not say, but something seemed to impel him"
110 "to the act. The tape-like surface of the road diminished in his rear as"
110 "far as he could see, and as he gazed a moving spot intruded on the"
110 "white vacuity of its perspective."
110 ""
110 "It was a human figure running. Clare waited, with a dim sense that"
110 "somebody was trying to overtake him."
110 ""
110 "The form descending the incline was a woman’s, yet so entirely was his"
110 "mind blinded to the idea of his wife’s following him that even when she"
110 "came nearer he did not recognize her under the totally changed attire"
110 "in which he now beheld her. It was not till she was quite close that he"
110 "could believe her to be Tess."
110 ""
110 "“I saw you—turn away from the station—just before I got there—and I"
110 "have been following you all this way!”"
110 ""
110 "She was so pale, so breathless, so quivering in every muscle, that he"
110 "did not ask her a single question, but seizing her hand, and pulling it"
110 "within his arm, he led her along. To avoid meeting any possible"
110 "wayfarers he left the high road and took a footpath under some"
110 "fir-trees. When they were deep among the moaning boughs he stopped and"
110 "looked at her inquiringly."
110 ""
110 "“Angel,” she said, as if waiting for this, “do you know what I have"
110 "been running after you for? To tell you that I have killed him!” A"
110 "pitiful white smile lit her face as she spoke."
110 ""
110 "“What!” said he, thinking from the strangeness of her manner that she"
110 "was in some delirium."
110 ""
110 "“I have done it—I don’t know how,” she continued. “Still, I owed it to"
110 "you, and to myself, Angel. I feared long ago, when I struck him on the"
110 "mouth with my glove, that I might do it some day for the trap he set"
110 "for me in my simple youth, and his wrong to you through me. He has come"
110 "between us and ruined us, and now he can never do it any more. I never"
110 "loved him at all, Angel, as I loved you. You know it, don’t you? You"
110 "believe it? You didn’t come back to me, and I was obliged to go back to"
110 "him. Why did you go away—why did you—when I loved you so? I can’t think"
110 "why you did it. But I don’t blame you; only, Angel, will you forgive me"
110 "my sin against you, now I have killed him? I thought as I ran along"
110 "that you would be sure to forgive me now I have done that. It came to"
110 "me as a shining light that I should get you back that way. I could not"
110 "bear the loss of you any longer—you don’t know how entirely I was"
110 "unable to bear your not loving me! Say you do now, dear, dear husband;"
110 "say you do, now I have killed him!”"
110 ""
110 "“I do love you, Tess—O, I do—it is all come back!” he said, tightening"
110 "his arms round her with fervid pressure. “But how do you mean—you have"
110 "killed him?”"
110 ""
110 "“I mean that I have,” she murmured in a reverie."
110 ""
110 "“What, bodily? Is he dead?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes. He heard me crying about you, and he bitterly taunted me; and"
110 "called you by a foul name; and then I did it. My heart could not bear"
110 "it. He had nagged me about you before. And then I dressed myself and"
110 "came away to find you.”"
110 ""
110 "By degrees he was inclined to believe that she had faintly attempted,"
110 "at least, what she said she had done; and his horror at her impulse was"
110 "mixed with amazement at the strength of her affection for himself, and"
110 "at the strangeness of its quality, which had apparently extinguished"
110 "her moral sense altogether. Unable to realize the gravity of her"
110 "conduct, she seemed at last content; and he looked at her as she lay"
110 "upon his shoulder, weeping with happiness, and wondered what obscure"
110 "strain in the d’Urberville blood had led to this aberration—if it were"
110 "an aberration. There momentarily flashed through his mind that the"
110 "family tradition of the coach and murder might have arisen because the"
110 "d’Urbervilles had been known to do these things. As well as his"
110 "confused and excited ideas could reason, he supposed that in the moment"
110 "of mad grief of which she spoke, her mind had lost its balance, and"
110 "plunged her into this abyss."
110 ""
110 "It was very terrible if true; if a temporary hallucination, sad. But,"
110 "anyhow, here was this deserted wife of his, this passionately-fond"
110 "woman, clinging to him without a suspicion that he would be anything to"
110 "her but a protector. He saw that for him to be otherwise was not, in"
110 "her mind, within the region of the possible. Tenderness was absolutely"
110 "dominant in Clare at last. He kissed her endlessly with his white lips,"
110 "and held her hand, and said—"
110 ""
110 "“I will not desert you! I will protect you by every means in my power,"
110 "dearest love, whatever you may have done or not have done!”"
110 ""
110 "They then walked on under the trees, Tess turning her head every now"
110 "and then to look at him. Worn and unhandsome as he had become, it was"
110 "plain that she did not discern the least fault in his appearance. To"
110 "her he was, as of old, all that was perfection, personally and"
110 "mentally. He was still her Antinous, her Apollo even; his sickly face"
110 "was beautiful as the morning to her affectionate regard on this day no"
110 "less than when she first beheld him; for was it not the face of the one"
110 "man on earth who had loved her purely, and who had believed in her as"
110 "pure!"
110 ""
110 "With an instinct as to possibilities, he did not now, as he had"
110 "intended, make for the first station beyond the town, but plunged still"
110 "farther under the firs, which here abounded for miles. Each clasping"
110 "the other round the waist they promenaded over the dry bed of"
110 "fir-needles, thrown into a vague intoxicating atmosphere at the"
110 "consciousness of being together at last, with no living soul between"
110 "them; ignoring that there was a corpse. Thus they proceeded for several"
110 "miles till Tess, arousing herself, looked about her, and said, timidly—"
110 ""
110 "“Are we going anywhere in particular?”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t know, dearest. Why?”"
110 ""
110 "“I don’t know.”"
110 ""
110 "“Well, we might walk a few miles further, and when it is evening find"
110 "lodgings somewhere or other—in a lonely cottage, perhaps. Can you walk"
110 "well, Tessy?”"
110 ""
110 "“O yes! I could walk for ever and ever with your arm round me!”"
110 ""
110 "Upon the whole it seemed a good thing to do. Thereupon they quickened"
110 "their pace, avoiding high roads, and following obscure paths tending"
110 "more or less northward. But there was an unpractical vagueness in their"
110 "movements throughout the day; neither one of them seemed to consider"
110 "any question of effectual escape, disguise, or long concealment. Their"
110 "every idea was temporary and unforefending, like the plans of two"
110 "children."
110 ""
110 "At mid-day they drew near to a roadside inn, and Tess would have"
110 "entered it with him to get something to eat, but he persuaded her to"
110 "remain among the trees and bushes of this half-woodland, half-moorland"
110 "part of the country till he should come back. Her clothes were of"
110 "recent fashion; even the ivory-handled parasol that she carried was of"
110 "a shape unknown in the retired spot to which they had now wandered; and"
110 "the cut of such articles would have attracted attention in the settle"
110 "of a tavern. He soon returned, with food enough for half-a-dozen people"
110 "and two bottles of wine—enough to last them for a day or more, should"
110 "any emergency arise."
110 ""
110 "They sat down upon some dead boughs and shared their meal. Between one"
110 "and two o’clock they packed up the remainder and went on again."
110 ""
110 "“I feel strong enough to walk any distance,” said she."
110 ""
110 "“I think we may as well steer in a general way towards the interior of"
110 "the country, where we can hide for a time, and are less likely to be"
110 "looked for than anywhere near the coast,” Clare remarked. “Later on,"
110 "when they have forgotten us, we can make for some port.”"
110 ""
110 "She made no reply to this beyond that of grasping him more tightly, and"
110 "straight inland they went. Though the season was an English May, the"
110 "weather was serenely bright, and during the afternoon it was quite"
110 "warm. Through the latter miles of their walk their footpath had taken"
110 "them into the depths of the New Forest, and towards evening, turning"
110 "the corner of a lane, they perceived behind a brook and bridge a large"
110 "board on which was painted in white letters, “This desirable Mansion to"
110 "be Let Furnished”; particulars following, with directions to apply to"
110 "some London agents. Passing through the gate they could see the house,"
110 "an old brick building of regular design and large accommodation."
110 ""
110 "“I know it,” said Clare. “It is Bramshurst Court. You can see that it"
110 "is shut up, and grass is growing on the drive.”"
110 ""
110 "“Some of the windows are open,” said Tess."
110 ""
110 "“Just to air the rooms, I suppose.”"
110 ""
110 "“All these rooms empty, and we without a roof to our heads!”"
110 ""
110 "“You are getting tired, my Tess!” he said. “We’ll stop soon.” And"
110 "kissing her sad mouth, he again led her onwards."
110 ""
110 "He was growing weary likewise, for they had wandered a dozen or fifteen"
110 "miles, and it became necessary to consider what they should do for"
110 "rest. They looked from afar at isolated cottages and little inns, and"
110 "were inclined to approach one of the latter, when their hearts failed"
110 "them, and they sheered off. At length their gait dragged, and they"
110 "stood still."
110 ""
110 "“Could we sleep under the trees?” she asked."
110 ""
110 "He thought the season insufficiently advanced."
110 ""
110 "“I have been thinking of that empty mansion we passed,” he said. “Let"
110 "us go back towards it again.”"
110 ""
110 "They retraced their steps, but it was half an hour before they stood"
110 "without the entrance-gate as earlier. He then requested her to stay"
110 "where she was, whilst he went to see who was within."
110 ""
110 "She sat down among the bushes within the gate, and Clare crept towards"
110 "the house. His absence lasted some considerable time, and when he"
110 "returned Tess was wildly anxious, not for herself, but for him. He had"
110 "found out from a boy that there was only an old woman in charge as"
110 "caretaker, and she only came there on fine days, from the hamlet near,"
110 "to open and shut the windows. She would come to shut them at sunset."
110 "“Now, we can get in through one of the lower windows, and rest there,”"
110 "said he."
110 ""
110 "Under his escort she went tardily forward to the main front, whose"
110 "shuttered windows, like sightless eyeballs, excluded the possibility of"
110 "watchers. The door was reached a few steps further, and one of the"
110 "windows beside it was open. Clare clambered in, and pulled Tess in"
110 "after him."
110 ""
110 "Except the hall, the rooms were all in darkness, and they ascended the"
110 "staircase. Up here also the shutters were tightly closed, the"
110 "ventilation being perfunctorily done, for this day at least, by opening"
110 "the hall-window in front and an upper window behind. Clare unlatched"

110 "shutters to the width of two or three inches. A shaft of dazzling"

110 "furniture, crimson damask hangings, and an enormous four-post bedstead,"
110 "along the head of which were carved running figures, apparently"
110 "Atalanta’s race."
110 ""
110 "“Rest at last!” said he, setting down his bag and the parcel of viands."
110 ""
110 "They remained in great quietness till the caretaker should have come to"
110 "shut the windows: as a precaution, putting themselves in total darkness"
110 "by barring the shutters as before, lest the woman should open the door"

110 "she came, but did not approach the wing they were in. They heard her"
110 "close the windows, fasten them, lock the door, and go away. Then Clare"
110 "again stole a chink of light from the window, and they shared another"
110 "meal, till by-and-by they were enveloped in the shades of night which"
110 "they had no candle to disperse."
110 ""
110 ""
110 "<CHAPTER LVIII>"
110 ""
110 "The night was strangely solemn and still. In the small hours she"
110 "whispered to him the whole story of how he had walked in his sleep with"
110 "her in his arms across the Froom stream, at the imminent risk of both"
110 "their lives, and laid her down in the stone coffin at the ruined abbey."
110 "He had never known of that till now."
110 ""
110 "“Why didn’t you tell me next day?” he said. “It might have prevented"
110 "much misunderstanding and woe.”"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t think of what’s past!” said she. “I am not going to think"
110 "outside of now. Why should we! Who knows what to-morrow has in store?”"
110 ""
110 "But it apparently had no sorrow. The morning was wet and foggy, and"
110 "Clare, rightly informed that the caretaker only opened the windows on"
110 "fine days, ventured to creep out of their chamber and explore the"
110 "house, leaving Tess asleep. There was no food on the premises, but"
110 "there was water, and he took advantage of the fog to emerge from the"
110 "mansion and fetch tea, bread, and butter from a shop in a little place"
110 "two miles beyond, as also a small tin kettle and spirit-lamp, that they"
110 "might get fire without smoke. His re-entry awoke her; and they"
110 "breakfasted on what he had brought."
110 ""
110 "They were indisposed to stir abroad, and the day passed, and the night"
110 "following, and the next, and next; till, almost without their being"
110 "aware, five days had slipped by in absolute seclusion, not a sight or"
110 "sound of a human being disturbing their peacefulness, such as it was."
110 "The changes of the weather were their only events, the birds of the New"
110 "Forest their only company. By tacit consent they hardly once spoke of"
110 "any incident of the past subsequent to their wedding-day. The gloomy"
110 "intervening time seemed to sink into chaos, over which the present and"
110 "prior times closed as if it never had been. Whenever he suggested that"
110 "they should leave their shelter, and go forwards towards Southampton or"
110 "London, she showed a strange unwillingness to move."
110 ""
110 "“Why should we put an end to all that’s sweet and lovely!” she"
110 "deprecated. “What must come will come.” And, looking through the"
110 "shutter-chink: “All is trouble outside there; inside here content.”"
110 ""
110 "He peeped out also. It was quite true; within was affection, union,"
110 "error forgiven: outside was the inexorable."
110 ""
110 "“And—and,” she said, pressing her cheek against his, “I fear that what"
110 "you think of me now may not last. I do not wish to outlive your present"
110 "feeling for me. I would rather not. I would rather be dead and buried"
110 "when the time comes for you to despise me, so that it may never be"
110 "known to me that you despised me.”"
110 ""
110 "“I cannot ever despise you.”"
110 ""
110 "“I also hope that. But considering what my life has been, I cannot see"
110 "why any man should, sooner or later, be able to help despising me...."
110 "How wickedly mad I was! Yet formerly I never could bear to hurt a fly"
110 "or a worm, and the sight of a bird in a cage used often to make me"
110 "cry.”"
110 ""
110 "They remained yet another day. In the night the dull sky cleared, and"
110 "the result was that the old caretaker at the cottage awoke early. The"
110 "brilliant sunrise made her unusually brisk; she decided to open the"
110 "contiguous mansion immediately, and to air it thoroughly on such a day."
110 "Thus it occurred that, having arrived and opened the lower rooms before"
110 "six o’clock, she ascended to the bedchambers, and was about to turn the"
110 "handle of the one wherein they lay. At that moment she fancied she"
110 "could hear the breathing of persons within. Her slippers and her"
110 "antiquity had rendered her progress a noiseless one so far, and she"
110 "made for instant retreat; then, deeming that her hearing might have"
110 "deceived her, she turned anew to the door and softly tried the handle."
110 "The lock was out of order, but a piece of furniture had been moved"
110 "forward on the inside, which prevented her opening the door more than"
110 "an inch or two. A stream of morning light through the shutter-chink"
110 "fell upon the faces of the pair, wrapped in profound slumber, Tess’s"
110 "lips being parted like a half-opened flower near his cheek. The"
110 "caretaker was so struck with their innocent appearance, and with the"
110 "elegance of Tess’s gown hanging across a chair, her silk stockings"
110 "beside it, the pretty parasol, and the other habits in which she had"
110 "arrived because she had none else, that her first indignation at the"
110 "effrontery of tramps and vagabonds gave way to a momentary"
110 "sentimentality over this genteel elopement, as it seemed. She closed"
110 "the door, and withdrew as softly as she had come, to go and consult"
110 "with her neighbours on the odd discovery."
110 ""
110 "Not more than a minute had elapsed after her withdrawal when Tess woke,"
110 "and then Clare. Both had a sense that something had disturbed them,"
110 "though they could not say what; and the uneasy feeling which it"
110 "engendered grew stronger. As soon as he was dressed he narrowly scanned"
110 "the lawn through the two or three inches of shutter-chink."
110 ""
110 "“I think we will leave at once,” said he. “It is a fine day. And I"
110 "cannot help fancying somebody is about the house. At any rate, the"
110 "woman will be sure to come to-day.”"
110 ""
110 "She passively assented, and putting the room in order, they took up the"
110 "few articles that belonged to them, and departed noiselessly. When they"
110 "had got into the Forest she turned to take a last look at the house."
110 ""
110 "“Ah, happy house—goodbye!” she said. “My life can only be a question of"
110 "a few weeks. Why should we not have stayed there?”"
110 ""
110 "“Don’t say it, Tess! We shall soon get out of this district altogether."
110 "We’ll continue our course as we’ve begun it, and keep straight north."
110 "Nobody will think of looking for us there. We shall be looked for at"
110 "the Wessex ports if we are sought at all. When we are in the north we"
110 "will get to a port and away.”"
110 ""
110 "Having thus persuaded her, the plan was pursued, and they kept a"
110 "bee-line northward. Their long repose at the manor-house lent them"
110 "walking power now; and towards mid-day they found that they were"
110 "approaching the steepled city of Melchester, which lay directly in"
110 "their way. He decided to rest her in a clump of trees during the"
110 "afternoon, and push onward under cover of darkness. At dusk Clare"
110 "purchased food as usual, and their night march began, the boundary"
110 "between Upper and Mid-Wessex being crossed about eight o’clock."
110 ""
110 "To walk across country without much regard to roads was not new to"
110 "Tess, and she showed her old agility in the performance. The"
110 "intercepting city, ancient Melchester, they were obliged to pass"
110 "through in order to take advantage of the town bridge for crossing a"
110 "large river that obstructed them. It was about midnight when they went"
110 "along the deserted streets, lighted fitfully by the few lamps, keeping"
110 "off the pavement that it might not echo their footsteps. The graceful"
110 "pile of cathedral architecture rose dimly on their left hand, but it"
110 "was lost upon them now. Once out of the town they followed the"
110 "turnpike-road, which after a few miles plunged across an open plain."
110 ""
110 "Though the sky was dense with cloud, a diffused light from some"
110 "fragment of a moon had hitherto helped them a little. But the moon had"
110 "now sunk, the clouds seemed to settle almost on their heads, and the"
110 "night grew as dark as a cave. However, they found their way along,"
110 "keeping as much on the turf as possible that their tread might not"
110 "resound, which it was easy to do, there being no hedge or fence of any"
110 "kind. All around was open loneliness and black solitude, over which a"
110 "stiff breeze blew."
110 ""
110 "They had proceeded thus gropingly two or three miles further when on a"
110 "sudden Clare became conscious of some vast erection close in his front,"
110 "rising sheer from the grass. They had almost struck themselves against"
110 "it."
110 ""
110 "“What monstrous place is this?” said Angel."
110 ""
110 "“It hums,” said she. “Hearken!”"
110 ""
110 "He listened. The wind, playing upon the edifice, produced a booming"
110 "tune, like the note of some gigantic one-stringed harp. No other sound"
110 "came from it, and lifting his hand and advancing a step or two, Clare"
110 "felt the vertical surface of the structure. It seemed to be of solid"
110 "stone, without joint or moulding. Carrying his fingers onward he found"
110 "that what he had come in contact with was a colossal rectangular"
110 "pillar; by stretching out his left hand he could feel a similar one"

110 "sky blacker, which had the semblance of a vast architrave uniting the"

110 "surfaces echoed their soft rustle; but they seemed to be still out of"
110 "doors. The place was roofless. Tess drew her breath fearfully, and"
110 "Angel, perplexed, said—"
110 ""
110 "“What can it be?”"
110 ""
110 "Feeling sideways they encountered another tower-like pillar, square and"
110 "uncompromising as the first; beyond it another and another. The place"
110 "was all doors and pillars, some connected above by continuous"
110 "architraves."
110 ""
110 "“A very Temple of the Winds,” he said."
110 ""
110 "The next pillar was isolated; others composed a trilithon; others were"
110 "prostrate, their flanks forming a causeway wide enough for a carriage;"
110 "and it was soon obvious that they made up a forest of monoliths grouped"

110 "this pavilion of the night till they stood in its midst."
110 ""
110 "“It is Stonehenge!” said Clare."
110 ""
110 "“The heathen temple, you mean?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes. Older than the centuries; older than the d’Urbervilles! Well,"
110 "what shall we do, darling? We may find shelter further on.”"
110 ""
110 "But Tess, really tired by this time, flung herself upon an oblong slab"
110 "that lay close at hand, and was sheltered from the wind by a pillar."

110 "warm and dry, in comforting contrast to the rough and chill grass"
110 "around, which had damped her skirts and shoes."
110 ""
110 "“I don’t want to go any further, Angel,” she said, stretching out her"
110 "hand for his. “Can’t we bide here?”"
110 ""
110 "“I fear not. This spot is visible for miles by day, although it does"
110 "not seem so now.”"
110 ""
110 "“One of my mother’s people was a shepherd hereabouts, now I think of"
110 "it. And you used to say at Talbothays that I was a heathen. So now I am"
110 "at home.”"
110 ""
110 "He knelt down beside her outstretched form, and put his lips upon hers."
110 ""
110 "“Sleepy are you, dear? I think you are lying on an altar.”"
110 ""
110 "“I like very much to be here,” she murmured. “It is so solemn and"
110 "lonely—after my great happiness—with nothing but the sky above my face."
110 "It seems as if there were no folk in the world but we two; and I wish"
110 "there were not—except ’Liza-Lu.”"
110 ""

110 ""
110 "“I will.”"
110 ""

110 "towering monoliths and trilithons began to be blackly defined."
110 ""
110 "“Did they sacrifice to God here?” asked she."
110 ""
110 "“No,” said he."
110 ""
110 "“Who to?”"
110 ""
110 "“I believe to the sun. That lofty stone set away by itself is in the"
110 "direction of the sun, which will presently rise behind it.”"
110 ""
110 "“This reminds me, dear,” she said. “You remember you never would"

110 ""
110 "He kissed her to avoid a reply at such a time."
110 ""

110 "Angel, who love each other so well?”"
110 ""
110 "Like a greater than himself, to the critical question at the critical"

110 "taciturnity, and hesitation which is usual just before day. The"
110 "eastward pillars and their architraves stood up blackly against the"

110 "quivering little pools in the cup-like hollows of the stones lay still."
110 "At the same time something seemed to move on the verge of the dip"
110 "eastward—a mere dot. It was the head of a man approaching them from the"
110 "hollow beyond the Sun-stone. Clare wished they had gone onward, but in"
110 "the circumstances decided to remain quiet. The figure came straight"
110 "towards the circle of pillars in which they were."
110 ""
110 "He heard something behind him, the brush of feet. Turning, he saw over"

110 ""
110 "“It is no use, sir,” he said. “There are sixteen of us on the Plain,"
110 "and the whole country is reared.”"
110 ""

110 ""

110 "peering under her eyelids and waking her."
110 ""
110 "“What is it, Angel?” she said, starting up. “Have they come for me?”"
110 ""
110 "“Yes, dearest,” he said. “They have come.”"
110 ""
110 "“It is as it should be,” she murmured. “Angel, I am almost glad—yes,"
110 "glad! This happiness could not have lasted. It was too much. I have had"
110 "enough; and now I shall not live for you to despise me!”"
110 ""
110 "She stood up, shook herself, and went forward, neither of the men"
110 "having moved."
110 ""
110 "“I am ready,” she said quietly."

110 "<CHAPTER LIX>"
110 ""
110 "The city of Wintoncester, that fine old city, aforetime capital of"
110 "Wessex, lay amidst its convex and concave downlands in all the"
110 "brightness and warmth of a July morning. The gabled brick, tile, and"
110 "freestone houses had almost dried off for the season their integument"
110 "of lichen, the streams in the meadows were low, and in the sloping High"
110 "Street, from the West Gateway to the mediæval cross, and from the"
110 "mediæval cross to the bridge, that leisurely dusting and sweeping was"
110 "in progress which usually ushers in an old-fashioned market-day."
110 ""
110 "From the western gate aforesaid the highway, as every Wintoncestrian"
110 "knows, ascends a long and regular incline of the exact length of a"
110 "measured mile, leaving the houses gradually behind. Up this road from"
110 "the precincts of the city two persons were walking rapidly, as if"
110 "unconscious of the trying ascent—unconscious through preoccupation and"
110 "not through buoyancy. They had emerged upon this road through a narrow,"
110 "barred wicket in a high wall a little lower down. They seemed anxious"
110 "to get out of the sight of the houses and of their kind, and this road"
110 "appeared to offer the quickest means of doing so. Though they were"
110 "young, they walked with bowed heads, which gait of grief the sun’s rays"
110 "smiled on pitilessly."
110 ""
110 "One of the pair was Angel Clare, the other a tall budding creature—half"
110 "girl, half woman—a spiritualized image of Tess, slighter than she, but"
110 "with the same beautiful eyes—Clare’s sister-in-law, ’Liza-Lu. Their"
110 "pale faces seemed to have shrunk to half their natural size. They moved"
110 "on hand in hand, and never spoke a word, the drooping of their heads"
110 "being that of Giotto’s “Two Apostles”."
110 ""
110 "When they had nearly reached the top of the great West Hill the clocks"
110 "in the town struck eight. Each gave a start at the notes, and, walking"
110 "onward yet a few steps, they reached the first milestone, standing"
110 "whitely on the green margin of the grass, and backed by the down, which"
110 "here was open to the road. They entered upon the turf, and, impelled by"
110 "a force that seemed to overrule their will, suddenly stood still,"
110 "turned, and waited in paralyzed suspense beside the stone."
110 ""
110 "The prospect from this summit was almost unlimited. In the valley"
110 "beneath lay the city they had just left, its more prominent buildings"
110 "showing as in an isometric drawing—among them the broad cathedral"
110 "tower, with its Norman windows and immense length of aisle and nave,"
110 "the spires of St Thomas’s, the pinnacled tower of the College, and,"
110 "more to the right, the tower and gables of the ancient hospice, where"
110 "to this day the pilgrim may receive his dole of bread and ale. Behind"
110 "the city swept the rotund upland of St Catherine’s Hill; further off,"
110 "landscape beyond landscape, till the horizon was lost in the radiance"
110 "of the sun hanging above it."
110 ""
110 "Against these far stretches of country rose, in front of the other city"
110 "edifices, a large red-brick building, with level gray roofs, and rows"
110 "of short barred windows bespeaking captivity, the whole contrasting"
110 "greatly by its formalism with the quaint irregularities of the Gothic"
110 "erections. It was somewhat disguised from the road in passing it by"
110 "yews and evergreen oaks, but it was visible enough up here. The wicket"
110 "from which the pair had lately emerged was in the wall of this"
110 "structure. From the middle of the building an ugly flat-topped"
110 "octagonal tower ascended against the east horizon, and viewed from this"
110 "spot, on its shady side and against the light, it seemed the one blot"
110 "on the city’s beauty. Yet it was with this blot, and not with the"
110 "beauty, that the two gazers were concerned."
110 ""
110 "Upon the cornice of the tower a tall staff was fixed. Their eyes were"
110 "riveted on it. A few minutes after the hour had struck something moved"
110 "slowly up the staff, and extended itself upon the breeze. It was a"
110 "black flag."
110 ""
110 "“Justice” was done, and the President of the Immortals, in Æschylean"
110 "phrase, had ended his sport with Tess. And the d’Urberville knights and"
110 "dames slept on in their tombs unknowing. The two speechless gazers bent"
110 "themselves down to the earth, as if in prayer, and remained thus a long"
110 "time, absolutely motionless: the flag continued to wave silently. As"
110 "soon as they had strength, they arose, joined hands again, and went on."
